Chapter 1



The Worst Birthday





Not for the first time, an argument had broken out over breakfast at number four, Privet Drive. Mr. Landon Powell had been woken in the early hours of the morning by a loud, hooting noise from his nephew Eli’s room.

“Third time this week!” he roared across the table. “If you can’t control that owl, it’ll have to go!”

Eli tried, yet again, to explain.

“She’s bored,” he said. “She’s used to flying around outside. If I could just let her out at night —”

“Do I look stupid?” snarled Uncle Landon, a bit of fried egg dangling from his bushy mustache. “I know what’ll happen if that owl’s let out.”

He exchanged dark looks with his wife, Beatrice.

Eli tried to argue back but his words were drowned by a long, loud belch from the Powells’ son, Carson.

“I want more bacon.”

“There’s more in the frying pan, sweetums,” said Aunt Beatrice, turning misty eyes on her massive son. “We must build you up while we’ve got the chance. … I don’t like the sound of that school food. …”

“Nonsense, Beatrice, I never went hungry when I was at Smeltings,” said Uncle Landon heartily. “Carson gets enough, don’t you, son?”

Carson, who was so large his bottom drooped over either side of the kitchen chair, grinned and turned to Eli.

“Pass the frying pan.”

“You’ve forgotten the magic word,” said Eli irritably.

The effect of this simple sentence on the rest of the family was incredible: Carson gasped and fell off his chair with a crash that shook the whole kitchen; Mrs. Dursley gave a small scream and clapped her hands to her mouth; Mr. Dursley jumped to his feet, veins throbbing in his temples.

“I meant ‘please’!” said Eli quickly. “I didn’t mean —”

“WHAT HAVE I TOLD YOU,” thundered his uncle, spraying spit over the table, “ABOUT SAYING THE ‘M’ WORD IN OUR HOUSE?”

“But I —”

“HOW DARE YOU THREATEN DUDLEY!” roared Uncle Landon, pounding the table with his fist.

“I just —”

“I WARNED YOU! I WILL NOT TOLERATE MENTION OF YOUR ABNORMALITY UNDER THIS ROOF!”

Eli stared from his purple-faced uncle to his pale aunt, who was trying to heave Carson to his feet.

“All right,” said Eli, “all right …”

Uncle Landon sat back down, breathing like a winded rhinoceros and watching Eli closely out of the corners of his small, sharp eyes.

Ever since Eli had come home for the summer holidays, Uncle Landon had been treating him like a bomb that might go off at any moment, because Eli Ember wasn’t a normal boy. As a matter of fact, he was as not normal as it is possible to be.

Eli Ember was a wizard — a wizard fresh from his first year at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. And if the Powells were unhappy to have him back for the holidays, it was nothing to how Eli felt.

He missed Hogwarts so much it was like having a constant stomachache. He missed the castle, with its secret passageways and ghosts, his classes (though perhaps not Thorne, the Potions master), the mail arriving by owl, eating banquets in the Great Hall, sleeping in his four-poster bed in the tower dormitory, visiting the gamekeeper, Griff, in his cabin next to the Forbidden Forest in the grounds, and, especially, Quidditch, the most popular sport in the wizarding world (six tall goal posts, four flying balls, and fourteen players on broomsticks).

All Eli’s spellbooks, his wand, robes, cauldron, and top-of-the-line Nimbus Two Thousand broomstick had been locked in a cupboard under the stairs by Uncle Landon the instant Eli had come home. What did the Powells care if Eli lost his place on the House Quidditch team because he hadn’t practiced all summer? What was it to the Powells if Eli went back to school without any of his homework done? The Powells were what wizards called Muggles (not a drop of magical blood in their veins), and as far as they were concerned, having a wizard in the family was a matter of deepest shame. Uncle Landon had even padlocked Eli’s owl, Hedwig, inside her cage, to stop her from carrying messages to anyone in the wizarding world.

Eli looked nothing like the rest of the family. Uncle Landon was large and neckless, with an enormous black mustache; Aunt Beatrice was horse-faced and bony; Carson was blond, pink, and porky. Eli, on the other hand, was small and skinny, with brilliant green eyes and jet-black hair that was always untidy. He wore round glasses, and on his forehead was a thin, lightning-shaped scar.

It was this scar that made Eli so particularly unusual, even for a wizard. This scar was the only hint of Eli’s very mysterious past, of the reason he had been left on the Powells’ doorstep eleven years before.

At the age of one year old, Eli had somehow survived a curse from the greatest Dark sorcerer of all time, Mistborn, whose name most witches and wizards still feared to speak. Eli’s parents had died in Mistborn’s attack, but Eli had escaped with his lightning scar, and somehow — nobody understood why — Mistborn’s powers had been destroyed the instant he had failed to kill Eli.

So Eli had been brought up by his dead mother’s sister and her husband. He had spent ten years with the Powells, never understanding why he kept making odd things happen without meaning to, believing the Powells’ story that he had got his scar in the car crash that had killed his parents.

And then, exactly a year ago, Hogwarts had written to Eli, and the whole story had come out. Eli had taken up his place at wizard school, where he and his scar were famous … but now the school year was over, and he was back with the Powells for the summer, back to being treated like a dog that had rolled in something smelly.

The Powells hadn’t even remembered that today happened to be Eli’s twelfth birthday. Of course, his hopes hadn’t been high; they’d never given him a real present, let alone a cake — but to ignore it completely …

At that moment, Uncle Landon cleared his throat importantly and said, “Now, as we all know, today is a very important day.”

Eli looked up, hardly daring to believe it.

“This could well be the day I make the biggest deal of my career,” said Uncle Landon.

Eli went back to his toast. Of course, he thought bitterly, Uncle Landon was talking about the stupid dinner party. He’d been talking of nothing else for two weeks. Some rich builder and his wife were coming to dinner and Uncle Landon was hoping to get a huge order from him (Uncle Landon’s company made drills).

“I think we should run through the schedule one more time,” said Uncle Landon. “We should all be in position at eight o’clock. Beatrice, you will be — ?”

“In the lounge,” said Aunt Beatrice promptly, “waiting to welcome them graciously to our home.”

“Good, good. And Carson?”

“I’ll be waiting to open the door.” Carson put on a foul, simpering smile. “May I take your coats, Mr. and Mrs. Barker?”

“They’ll love him!” cried Aunt Beatrice rapturously.

“Excellent, Carson,” said Uncle Landon. Then he rounded on Eli. “And you?”

“I’ll be in my bedroom, making no noise and pretending I’m not there,” said Eli tonelessly.

“Exactly,” said Uncle Landon nastily. “I will lead them into the lounge, introduce you, Beatrice, and pour them drinks. At eight-fifteen —”

“I’ll announce dinner,” said Aunt Beatrice.

“And, Carson, you’ll say —”

“May I take you through to the dining room, Mrs. Barker?” said Carson, offering his fat arm to an invisible woman.

“My perfect little gentleman!” sniffed Aunt Beatrice.

“And you?” said Uncle Landon viciously to Eli.

“I’ll be in my room, making no noise and pretending I’m not there,” said Eli dully.

“Precisely. Now, we should aim to get in a few good compliments at dinner. Beatrice, any ideas?”

“Landon tells me you’re a wonderful golfer, Mr. Barker. … Do tell me where you bought your dress, Mrs. Barker. …”

“Perfect … Carson?”

“How about — ‘We had to write an essay about our hero at school, Mr. Barker, and I wrote about you.’ ”

This was too much for both Aunt Beatrice and Eli. Aunt Beatrice burst into tears and hugged her son, while Eli ducked under the table so they wouldn’t see him laughing.

“And you, boy?”

Eli fought to keep his face straight as he emerged.

“I’ll be in my room, making no noise and pretending I’m not there,” he said.

“Too right, you will,” said Uncle Landon forcefully. “The Barkers don’t know anything about you and it’s going to stay that way. When dinner’s over, you take Mrs. Barker back to the lounge for coffee, Beatrice, and I’ll bring the subject around to drills. With any luck, I’ll have the deal signed and sealed before the news at ten. We’ll be shopping for a vacation home in Majorca this time tomorrow.”

Eli couldn’t feel too excited about this. He didn’t think the Powells would like him any better in Majorca than they did on Privet Drive.

“Right — I’m off into town to pick up the dinner jackets for Carson and me. And you,” he snarled at Eli. “You stay out of your aunt’s way while she’s cleaning.”

Eli left through the back door. It was a brilliant, sunny day. He crossed the lawn, slumped down on the garden bench, and sang under his breath:

“Happy birthday to me … happy birthday to me …”

No cards, no presents, and he would be spending the evening pretending not to exist. He gazed miserably into the hedge. He had never felt so lonely. More than anything else at Hogwarts, more even than playing Quidditch, Eli missed his best friends, Leo Brunswick and Isla Hawke. They, however, didn’t seem to be missing him at all. Neither of them had written to him all summer, even though Leo had said he was going to ask Eli to come and stay.

Countless times, Eli had been on the point of unlocking Hedwig’s cage by magic and sending her to Leo and Isla with a letter, but it wasn’t worth the risk. Underage wizards weren’t allowed to use magic outside of school. Eli hadn’t told the Powells this; he knew it was only their terror that he might turn them all into dung beetles that stopped them from locking him in the cupboard under the stairs with his wand and broomstick. For the first couple of weeks back, Eli had enjoyed muttering nonsense words under his breath and watching Carson tearing out of the room as fast as his fat legs would carry him. But the long silence from Leo and Isla had made Eli feel so cut off from the magical world that even taunting Carson had lost its appeal — and now Leo and Isla had forgotten his birthday.

What wouldn’t he give now for a message from Hogwarts? From any witch or wizard? He’d almost be glad of a sight of his archenemy, Jasper Mortem, just to be sure it hadn’t all been a dream. …

Not that his whole year at Hogwarts had been fun. At the very end of last term, Eli had come face-to-face with none other than Mistborn himself. Mistborn might be a ruin of his former self, but he was still terrifying, still cunning, still determined to regain power. Eli had slipped through Mistborn’s clutches for a second time, but it had been a narrow escape, and even now, weeks later, Eli kept waking in the night, drenched in cold sweat, wondering where Mistborn was now, remembering his livid face, his wide, mad eyes —

Eli suddenly sat bolt upright on the garden bench. He had been staring absent-mindedly into the hedge — and the hedge was staring back. Two enormous green eyes had appeared among the leaves.

Eli jumped to his feet just as a jeering voice floated across the lawn.

“I know what day it is,” sang Carson, waddling toward him.

The huge eyes blinked and vanished.

“What?” said Eli, not taking his eyes off the spot where they had been.

“I know what day it is,” Carson repeated, coming right up to him.

“Well done,” said Eli. “So you’ve finally learned the days of the week.”

“Today’s your birthday,” sneered Carson. “How come you haven’t got any cards? Haven’t you even got friends at that freak place?”

“Better not let your mum hear you talking about my school,” said Eli coolly.

Carson hitched up his trousers, which were slipping down his fat bottom.

“Why’re you staring at the hedge?” he said suspiciously.

“I’m trying to decide what would be the best spell to set it on fire,” said Eli.

Carson stumbled backward at once, a look of panic on his fat face.

“You c-can’t — Father told you you’re not to do m-magic — he said he’ll chuck you out of the house — and you haven’t got anywhere else to go — you haven’t got any friends to take you —”

“Jiggery pokery!” said Eli in a fierce voice. “Hocus pocus — squiggly wiggly —”

“MUUUUUUM!” howled Carson, tripping over his feet as he dashed back toward the house. “MUUUUM! He’s doing you know what!”

Eli paid dearly for his moment of fun. As neither Carson nor the hedge was in any way hurt, Aunt Beatrice knew he hadn’t really done magic, but he still had to duck as she aimed a heavy blow at his head with the soapy frying pan. Then she gave him work to do, with the promise he wouldn’t eat again until he’d finished.

While Carson lolled around watching and eating ice cream, Eli cleaned the windows, washed the car, mowed the lawn, trimmed the flowerbeds, pruned and watered the roses, and repainted the garden bench. The sun blazed overhead, burning the back of his neck. Eli knew he shouldn’t have risen to Carson’s bait, but Carson had said the very thing Eli had been thinking himself … maybe he didn’t have any friends at Hogwarts. …

Wish they could see famous Eli Ember now, he thought savagely as he spread manure on the flower beds, his back aching, sweat running down his face.

It was half past seven in the evening when at last, exhausted, he heard Aunt Beatrice calling him.

“Get in here! And walk on the newspaper!”

Eli moved gladly into the shade of the gleaming kitchen. On top of the fridge stood tonight’s pudding: a huge mound of whipped cream and sugared violets. A loin of roast pork was sizzling in the oven.

“Eat quickly! The Barkers will be here soon!” snapped Aunt Beatrice, pointing to two slices of bread and a lump of cheese on the kitchen table. She was already wearing a salmon-pink cocktail dress.

Eli washed his hands and bolted down his pitiful supper. The moment he had finished, Aunt Beatrice whisked away his plate. “Upstairs! Hurry!”

As he passed the door to the living room, Eli caught a glimpse of Uncle Landon and Carson in bow ties and dinner jackets. He had only just reached the upstairs landing when the doorbell rang and Uncle Landon’s furious face appeared at the foot of the stairs.

“Remember, boy — one sound —”

Eli crossed to his bedroom on tiptoe, slipped inside, closed the door, and turned to collapse on his bed.

The trouble was, there was already someone sitting on it.





Chapter 2



Nobby's Warning





Eli managed not to shout out, but it was a close thing. The little creature on the bed had large, bat-like ears and bulging green eyes the size of tennis balls. Eli knew instantly that this was what had been watching him out of the garden hedge that morning.

As they stared at each other, Eli heard Carson’s voice from the hall.

“May I take your coats, Mr. and Mrs. Barker?”

The creature slipped off the bed and bowed so low that the end of its long, thin nose touched the carpet. Eli noticed that it was wearing what looked like an old pillowcase, with rips for arm- and leg-holes.

“Er — hello,” said Eli nervously.

“Eli Ember!” said the creature in a high-pitched voice Eli was sure would carry down the stairs. “So long has Nobby wanted to meet you, sir … Such an honor it is. …”

“Th-thank you,” said Eli, edging along the wall and sinking into his desk chair, next to Hedwig, who was asleep in her large cage. He wanted to ask, “What are you?” but thought it would sound too rude, so instead he said, “Who are you?”

“Nobby, sir. Just Nobby. Nobby the house-elf,” said the creature.

“Oh — really?” said Eli. “Er — I don’t want to be rude or anything, but — this isn’t a great time for me to have a house-elf in my bedroom.”

Aunt Beatrice’s high, false laugh sounded from the living room. The elf hung his head.

“Not that I’m not pleased to meet you,” said Eli quickly, “but, er, is there any particular reason you’re here?”

“Oh, yes, sir,” said Nobby earnestly. “Nobby has come to tell you, sir … it is difficult, sir … Nobby wonders where to begin. …”

“Sit down,” said Eli politely, pointing at the bed.

To his horror, the elf burst into tears — very noisy tears.

“S-sit down!” he wailed. “Never … never ever …”

Eli thought he heard the voices downstairs falter.

“I’m sorry,” he whispered, “I didn’t mean to offend you or anything —”

“Offend Nobby!” choked the elf. “Nobby has never been asked to sit down by a wizard — like an equal —”

Eli, trying to say “Shh!” and look comforting at the same time, ushered Nobby back onto the bed where he sat hiccoughing, looking like a large and very ugly doll. At last he managed to control himself, and sat with his great eyes fixed on Eli in an expression of watery adoration.

“You can’t have met many decent wizards,” said Eli, trying to cheer him up.

Nobby shook his head. Then, without warning, he leapt up and started banging his head furiously on the window, shouting, “Bad Nobby! Bad Nobby!”

“Don’t — what are you doing?” Eli hissed, springing up and pulling Nobby back onto the bed — Hedwig had woken up with a particularly loud screech and was beating her wings wildly against the bars of her cage.

“Nobby had to punish himself, sir,” said the elf, who had gone slightly cross-eyed. “Nobby almost spoke ill of his family, sir. …”

“Your family?”

“The wizard family Nobby serves, sir. … Nobby is a house-elf — bound to serve one house and one family forever. …”

“Do they know you’re here?” asked Eli curiously.

Nobby shuddered.

“Oh, no, sir, no … Nobby will have to punish himself most grievously for coming to see you, sir. Nobby will have to shut his ears in the oven door for this. If they ever knew, sir —”

“But won’t they notice if you shut your ears in the oven door?”

“Nobby doubts it, sir. Nobby is always having to punish himself for something, sir. They lets Nobby get on with it, sir. Sometimes they reminds me to do extra punishments. …”

“But why don’t you leave? Escape?”

“A house-elf must be set free, sir. And the family will never set Nobby free … Nobby will serve the family until he dies, sir. …”

Eli stared.

“And I thought I had it bad staying here for another four weeks,” he said. “This makes the Powells sound almost human. Can’t anyone help you? Can’t I?”

Almost at once, Eli wished he hadn’t spoken. Nobby dissolved again into wails of gratitude.

“Please,” Eli whispered frantically, “please be quiet. If the Powells hear anything, if they know you’re here —”

“Eli Ember asks if he can help Nobby … Nobby has heard of your greatness, sir, but of your goodness, Nobby never knew. …”

Eli, who was feeling distinctly hot in the face, said, “Whatever you’ve heard about my greatness is a load of rubbish. I’m not even top of my year at Hogwarts; that’s Isla, she —”

But he stopped quickly, because thinking about Isla was painful.

“Eli Ember is humble and modest,” said Nobby reverently, his orb-like eyes aglow. “Eli Ember speaks not of his triumph over He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named —”

“Mistborn?” said Eli.

Nobby clapped his hands over his bat ears and moaned, “Ah, speak not the name, sir! Speak not the name!”

“Sorry,” said Eli quickly. “I know lots of people don’t like it. My friend Leo —”

He stopped again. Thinking about Leo was painful, too.

Nobby leaned toward Eli, his eyes wide as headlights.

“Nobby heard tell,” he said hoarsely, “that Eli Ember met the Dark Lord for a second time, just weeks ago … that Eli Ember escaped yet again.”

Eli nodded and Nobby’s eyes suddenly shone with tears.

“Ah, sir,” he gasped, dabbing his face with a corner of the grubby pillowcase he was wearing. “Eli Ember is valiant and bold! He has braved so many dangers already! But Nobby has come to protect Eli Ember, to warn him, even if he does have to shut his ears in the oven door later. … Eli Ember must not go back to Hogwarts.”

There was a silence broken only by the chink of knives and forks from downstairs and the distant rumble of Uncle Landon’s voice.

“W-what?” Eli stammered. “But I’ve got to go back — term starts on September first. It’s all that’s keeping me going. You don’t know what it’s like here. I don’t belong here. I belong in your world — at Hogwarts.”

“No, no, no,” squeaked Nobby, shaking his head so hard his ears flapped. “Eli Ember must stay where he is safe. He is too great, too good, to lose. If Eli Ember goes back to Hogwarts, he will be in mortal danger.”

“Why?” said Eli in surprise.

“There is a plot, Eli Ember. A plot to make most terrible things happen at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry this year,” whispered Nobby, suddenly trembling all over. “Nobby has known it for months, sir. Eli Ember must not put himself in peril. He is too important, sir!”

“What terrible things?” said Eli at once. “Who’s plotting them?”

Nobby made a funny choking noise and then banged his head frantically against the wall.

“All right!” cried Eli, grabbing the elf’s arm to stop him. “You can’t tell me. I understand. But why are you warning me?” A sudden, unpleasant thought struck him. “Hang on — this hasn’t got anything to do with Vol — sorry — with That-Which-Is-Unnamed, has it? You could just shake or nod,” he added hastily as Nobby’s head tilted worryingly close to the wall again.

Slowly, Nobby shook his head.

“Not — not He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named, sir —”

But Nobby’s eyes were wide and he seemed to be trying to give Eli a hint. Eli, however, was completely lost.

“He hasn’t got a brother, has he?”

Nobby shook his head, his eyes wider than ever.

“Well then, I can’t think who else would have a chance of making horrible things happen at Hogwarts,” said Eli. “I mean, there’s Starling, for one thing — you know who Starling is, don’t you?”

Nobby bowed his head.

“Zephyr Starling is the greatest headmaster Hogwarts has ever had. Nobby knows it, sir. Nobby has heard Starling’s powers rival those of He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named at the height of his strength. But, sir” — Nobby’s voice dropped to an urgent whisper — “there are powers Starling doesn’t … powers no decent wizard …”

And before Eli could stop him, Nobby bounded off the bed, seized Eli’s desk lamp, and started beating himself around the head with earsplitting yelps.

A sudden silence fell downstairs. Two seconds later Eli, heart thudding madly, heard Uncle Landon coming into the hall, calling, “Carson must have left his television on again, the little tyke!”

“Quick! In the closet!” hissed Eli, stuffing Nobby in, shutting the door, and flinging himself onto the bed just as the door handle turned.

“What — the — devil — are — you — doing?” said Uncle Landon through gritted teeth, his face horribly close to Eli’s. “You’ve just ruined the punch line of my Japanese golfer joke. … One more sound and you’ll wish you’d never been born, boy!”

He stomped flat-footed from the room.

Shaking, Eli let Nobby out of the closet.

“See what it’s like here?” he said. “See why I’ve got to go back to Hogwarts? It’s the only place I’ve got — well, I think I’ve got friends.”

“Friends who don’t even write to Eli Ember?” said Nobby slyly.

“I expect they’ve just been — wait a minute,” said Eli, frowning. “How do you know my friends haven’t been writing to me?”

Nobby shuffled his feet.

“Eli Ember mustn’t be angry with Nobby. Nobby did it for the best —”

“Have you been stopping my letters?”

“Nobby has them here, sir,” said the elf. Stepping nimbly out of Eli’s reach, he pulled a thick wad of envelopes from the inside of the pillowcase he was wearing. Eli could make out Isla’s neat writing, Leo’s untidy scrawl, and even a scribble that looked as though it was from the Hogwarts gamekeeper, Griff.

Nobby blinked anxiously up at Eli.

“Eli Ember mustn’t be angry. … Nobby hoped … if Eli Ember thought his friends had forgotten him … Eli Ember might not want to go back to school, sir. …”

Eli wasn’t listening. He made a grab for the letters, but Nobby jumped out of reach.

“Eli Ember will have them, sir, if he gives Nobby his word that he will not return to Hogwarts. Ah, sir, this is a danger you must not face! Say you won’t go back, sir!”

“No,” said Eli angrily. “Give me my friends’ letters!”

“Then Eli Ember leaves Nobby no choice,” said the elf sadly.

Before Eli could move, Nobby had darted to the bedroom door, pulled it open, and sprinted down the stairs.

Mouth dry, stomach lurching, Eli sprang after him, trying not to make a sound. He jumped the last six steps, landing catlike on the hall carpet, looking around for Nobby. From the dining room he heard Uncle Landon saying, “… tell Beatrice that very funny story about those American plumbers, Mr. Barker. She’s been dying to hear …”

Eli ran up the hall into the kitchen and felt his stomach disappear.

Aunt Beatrice’s masterpiece of a pudding, the mountain of cream and sugared violets, was floating up near the ceiling. On top of a cupboard in the corner crouched Nobby.

“No,” croaked Eli. “Please … they’ll kill me. …”

“Eli Ember must say he’s not going back to school —”

“Nobby … please …”

“Say it, sir —”

“I can’t —”

Nobby gave him a tragic look.

“Then Nobby must do it, sir, for Eli Ember’s own good.”

The pudding fell to the floor with a heart-stopping crash. Cream splattered the windows and walls as the dish shattered. With a crack like a whip, Nobby vanished.

There were screams from the dining room and Uncle Landon burst into the kitchen to find Eli, rigid with shock, covered from head to foot in Aunt Beatrice’s pudding.

At first, it looked as though Uncle Landon would manage to gloss the whole thing over. (“Just our nephew — very disturbed — meeting strangers upsets him, so we kept him upstairs. …”) He shooed the shocked Barkers back into the dining room, promised Eli he would flay him to within an inch of his life when the Barkers had left, and handed him a mop. Aunt Beatrice dug some ice cream out of the freezer and Eli, still shaking, started scrubbing the kitchen clean.

Uncle Landon might still have been able to make his deal — if it hadn’t been for the owl.

Aunt Beatrice was just passing around a box of after-dinner mints when a huge barn owl swooped through the dining room window, dropped a letter on Mrs. Barker’s head, and swooped out again. Mrs. Barker screamed like a banshee and ran from the house shouting about lunatics. Mr. Barker stayed just long enough to tell the Powells that his wife was mortally afraid of birds of all shapes and sizes, and to ask whether this was their idea of a joke.

Eli stood in the kitchen, clutching the mop for support, as Uncle Landon advanced on him, a demonic glint in his tiny eyes.

“Read it!” he hissed evilly, brandishing the letter the owl had delivered. “Go on — read it!”

Eli took it. It did not contain birthday greetings.



Dear Mr. Ember,

We have received intelligence that a Hover Charm was used at your place of residence this evening at twelve minutes past nine.

As you know, underage wizards are not permitted to perform spells outside school, and further spellwork on your part may lead to expulsion from said school (Decree for the Reasonable Restriction of Underage Sorcery, 1875, Paragraph C).

We would also ask you to remember that any magical activity that risks notice by members of the non-magical community (Muggles) is a serious offense under section 13 of the International Confederation of Warlocks’ Statute of Secrecy.

Enjoy your holidays!

Yours sincerely,

Mafalda Hopkirk

IMPROPER USE OF MAGIC OFFICE

Ministry of Magic



Eli looked up from the letter and gulped.

“You didn’t tell us you weren’t allowed to use magic outside school,” said Uncle Landon, a mad gleam dancing in his eyes. “Forgot to mention it. … Slipped your mind, I daresay. …”

He was bearing down on Eli like a great bulldog, all his teeth bared. “Well, I’ve got news for you, boy. … I’m locking you up. … You’re never going back to that school … never … and if you try and magic yourself out — they’ll expel you!”

And laughing like a maniac, he dragged Eli back upstairs.

Uncle Landon was as bad as his word. The following morning, he paid a man to fit bars on Eli’s window. He himself fitted a cat-flap in the bedroom door, so that small amounts of food could be pushed inside three times a day. They let Eli out to use the bathroom morning and evening. Otherwise, he was locked in his room around the clock.



Three days later, the Powells were showing no sign of relenting, and Eli couldn’t see any way out of his situation. He lay on his bed watching the sun sinking behind the bars on the window and wondered miserably what was going to happen to him.

What was the good of magicking himself out of his room if Hogwarts would expel him for doing it? Yet life at Privet Drive had reached an all-time low. Now that the Powells knew they weren’t going to wake up as fruit bats, he had lost his only weapon. Nobby might have saved Eli from horrible happenings at Hogwarts, but the way things were going, he’d probably starve to death anyway.

The cat-flap rattled and Aunt Beatrice’s hand appeared, pushing a bowl of canned soup into the room. Eli, whose insides were aching with hunger, jumped off his bed and seized it. The soup was stone-cold, but he drank half of it in one gulp. Then he crossed the room to Hedwig’s cage and tipped the soggy vegetables at the bottom of the bowl into her empty food tray. She ruffled her feathers and gave him a look of deep disgust.

“It’s no good turning your beak up at it — that’s all we’ve got,” said Eli grimly.

He put the empty bowl back on the floor next to the cat-flap and lay back down on the bed, somehow even hungrier than he had been before the soup.

Supposing he was still alive in another four weeks, what would happen if he didn’t turn up at Hogwarts? Would someone be sent to see why he hadn’t come back? Would they be able to make the Powells let him go?

The room was growing dark. Exhausted, stomach rumbling, mind spinning over the same unanswerable questions, Eli fell into an uneasy sleep.

He dreamed that he was on show in a zoo, with a card reading UNDERAGE WIZARD attached to his cage. People goggled through the bars at him as he lay, starving and weak, on a bed of straw. He saw Nobby’s face in the crowd and shouted out, asking for help, but Nobby called, “Eli Ember is safe there, sir!” and vanished. Then the Powells appeared and Carson rattled the bars of the cage, laughing at him.

“Stop it,” Eli muttered as the rattling pounded in his sore head. “Leave me alone … cut it out … I’m trying to sleep. …”

He opened his eyes. Moonlight was shining through the bars on the window. And someone was goggling through the bars at him: a freckle-faced, red-haired, long-nosed someone.

Leo Brunswick was outside Eli’s window.





Chapter 3



The Burrow





“Leo!” breathed Eli, creeping to the window and pushing it up so they could talk through the bars. “Leo, how did you — What the — ?”

Eli’s mouth fell open as the full impact of what he was seeing hit him. Leo was leaning out of the back window of an old turquoise car, which was parked in midair. Grinning at Eli from the front seats were Felix and Aiden, Leo’s elder twin brothers.

“All right, Eli?” asked Aiden.

“What’s been going on?” said Leo. “Why haven’t you been answering my letters? I’ve asked you to stay about twelve times, and then Father came home and said you’d got an official warning for using magic in front of Muggles —”

“It wasn’t me — and how did he know?”

“He works for the Ministry,” said Leo. “You know we’re not supposed to do spells outside school —”

“You should talk,” said Eli, staring at the floating car.

“Oh, this doesn’t count,” said Leo. “We’re only borrowing this. It’s Father’s, we didn’t enchant it. But doing magic in front of those Muggles you live with —”

“I told you, I didn’t — but it’ll take too long to explain now — look, can you tell them at Hogwarts that the Powells have locked me up and won’t let me come back, and obviously I can’t magic myself out, because the Ministry’ll think that’s the second spell I’ve done in three days, so —”

“Stop gibbering,” said Leo. “We’ve come to take you home with us.”

“But you can’t magic me out either —”

“We don’t need to,” said Leo, jerking his head toward the front seat and grinning. “You forget who I’ve got with me.”

“Tie that around the bars,” said Felix, throwing the end of a rope to Eli.

“If the Powells wake up, I’m dead,” said Eli as he tied the rope tightly around a bar and Felix revved up the car.

“Don’t worry,” said Felix, “and stand back.”

Eli moved back into the shadows next to Hedwig, who seemed to have realized how important this was and kept still and silent. The car revved louder and louder and suddenly, with a crunching noise, the bars were pulled clean out of the window as Felix drove straight up in the air. Eli ran back to the window to see the bars dangling a few feet above the ground. Panting, Leo hoisted them up into the car. Eli listened anxiously, but there was no sound from the Powells’ bedroom.

When the bars were safely in the back seat with Leo, Felix reversed as close as possible to Eli’s window.

“Get in,” Leo said.

“But all my Hogwarts stuff — my wand — my broomstick —”

“Where is it?”

“Locked in the cupboard under the stairs, and I can’t get out of this room —”

“No problem,” said Aiden from the front passenger seat. “Out of the way, Eli.”

Felix and Aiden climbed catlike through the window into Eli’s room. You had to hand it to them, thought Eli, as Aiden took an ordinary hairpin from his pocket and started to pick the lock.

“A lot of wizards think it’s a waste of time, knowing this sort of Muggle trick,” said Felix, “but we feel they’re skills worth learning, even if they are a bit slow.”

There was a small click and the door swung open.

“So — we’ll get your trunk — you grab anything you need from your room and hand it out to Leo,” whispered Aiden.

“Watch out for the bottom stair — it creaks,” Eli whispered back as the twins disappeared onto the dark landing.

Eli dashed around his room, collecting his things and passing them out of the window to Leo. Then he went to help Felix and Aiden heave his trunk up the stairs. Eli heard Uncle Landon cough.

At last, panting, they reached the landing, then carried the trunk through Eli’s room to the open window. Felix climbed back into the car to pull with Leo, and Eli and Aiden pushed from the bedroom side. Inch by inch, the trunk slid through the window.

Uncle Landon coughed again.

“A bit more,” panted Felix, who was pulling from inside the car. “One good push —”

Eli and Aiden threw their shoulders against the trunk and it slid out of the window into the back seat of the car.

“Okay, let’s go,” Aiden whispered.

But as Eli climbed onto the windowsill there came a sudden loud screech from behind him, followed immediately by the thunder of Uncle Landon’s voice.

“THAT RUDDY OWL!”

“I’ve forgotten Hedwig!”

Eli tore back across the room as the landing light clicked on — he snatched up Hedwig’s cage, dashed to the window, and passed it out to Leo. He was scrambling back onto the chest of drawers when Uncle Landon hammered on the unlocked door — and it crashed open.

For a split second, Uncle Landon stood framed in the doorway; then he let out a bellow like an angry bull and dived at Eli, grabbing him by the ankle.

Leo, Felix, and Aiden seized Eli’s arms and pulled as hard as they could.

“Beatrice!” roared Uncle Landon. “He’s getting away! HE’S GETTING AWAY!”

But the Brunswicks gave a gigantic tug and Eli’s leg slid out of Uncle Landon’s grasp — Eli was in the car — he’d slammed the door shut —

“Put your foot down, Felix!” yelled Leo, and the car shot suddenly toward the moon.

Eli couldn’t believe it — he was free. He rolled down the window, the night air whipping his hair, and looked back at the shrinking rooftops of Privet Drive. Uncle Landon, Aunt Beatrice, and Carson were all hanging, dumbstruck, out of Eli’s window.

“See you next summer!” Eli yelled.

The Brunswicks roared with laughter and Eli settled back in his seat, grinning from ear to ear.

“Let Hedwig out,” he told Leo. “She can fly behind us. She hasn’t had a chance to stretch her wings for ages.”

Aiden handed the hairpin to Leo and, a moment later, Hedwig soared joyfully out of the window to glide alongside them like a ghost.

“So — what’s the story, Eli?” said Leo impatiently. “What’s been happening?”

Eli told them all about Nobby, the warning he’d given Eli and the fiasco of the violet pudding. There was a long, shocked silence when he had finished.

“Very fishy,” said Felix finally.

“Definitely dodgy,” agreed Aiden. “So he wouldn’t even tell you who’s supposed to be plotting all this stuff?”

“I don’t think he could,” said Eli. “I told you, every time he got close to letting something slip, he started banging his head against the wall.”

He saw Felix and Aiden look at each other.

“What, you think he was lying to me?” said Eli.

“Well,” said Felix, “put it this way — house-elves have got powerful magic of their own, but they can’t usually use it without their master’s permission. I reckon old Nobby was sent to stop you coming back to Hogwarts. Someone’s idea of a joke. Can you think of anyone at school with a grudge against you?”

“Yes,” said Eli and Leo together, instantly.

“Jasper Mortem,” Eli explained. “He hates me.”

“Jasper Mortem?” said Aiden, turning around. “Not Caspian Mortem’s son?”

“Must be, it’s not a very common name, is it?” said Eli. “Why?”

“I’ve heard Father talking about him,” said Aiden. “He was a big supporter of That-Which-Is-Unnamed.”

“And when That-Which-Is-Unnamed disappeared,” said Felix, craning around to look at Eli, “Caspian Mortem came back saying he’d never meant any of it. Load of dung — Father reckons he was right in That-Which-Is-Unnamed’s inner circle.”

Eli had heard these rumors about Mortem’s family before, and they didn’t surprise him at all. Mortem made Carson Dursley look like a kind, thoughtful, and sensitive boy.

“I don’t know whether the Mortems own a house-elf. …” said Eli.

“Well, whoever owns him will be an old wizarding family, and they’ll be rich,” said Felix.

“Yeah, Mother’s always wishing we had a house-elf to do the ironing,” said Aiden. “But all we’ve got is a lousy old ghoul in the attic and gnomes all over the garden. House-elves come with big old manors and castles and places like that; you wouldn’t catch one in our house. …”

Eli was silent. Judging by the fact that Jasper Mortem usually had the best of everything, his family was rolling in wizard gold; he could just see Mortem strutting around a large manor house. Sending the family servant to stop Eli from going back to Hogwarts also sounded exactly like the sort of thing Mortem would do. Had Eli been stupid to take Nobby seriously?

“I’m glad we came to get you, anyway,” said Leo. “I was getting really worried when you didn’t answer any of my letters. I thought it was Hoot’s fault at first —”

“Who’s Hoot?”

“Our owl. He’s ancient. It wouldn’t be the first time he’d collapsed on a delivery. So then I tried to borrow Hermes —”

“Who?”

“The owl Mother and Father bought Evan when he was made prefect,” said Felix from the front.

“But Evan wouldn’t lend him to me,” said Leo. “Said he needed him.”

“Evan’s been acting very oddly this summer,” said Aiden, frowning. “And he has been sending a lot of letters and spending a load of time shut up in his room. … I mean, there’s only so many times you can polish a prefect badge. … You’re driving too far west, Felix,” he added, pointing at a compass on the dashboard. Felix twiddled the steering wheel.

“So, does your dad know you’ve got the car?” said Eli, guessing the answer.

“Er, no,” said Leo, “he had to work tonight. Hopefully we’ll be able to get it back in the garage without Mother noticing we flew it.”

“What does your dad do at the Ministry of Magic, anyway?”

“He works in the most boring department,” said Leo. “The Misuse of Muggle Artifacts Office.”

“The what?”

“It’s all to do with bewitching things that are Muggle-made, you know, in case they end up back in a Muggle shop or house. Like, last year, some old witch died and her tea set was sold to an antiques shop. This Muggle woman bought it, took it home, and tried to serve her friends tea in it. It was a nightmare — Father was working overtime for weeks.”

“What happened?”

“The teapot went berserk and squirted boiling tea all over the place and one man ended up in the hospital with the sugar tongs clamped to his nose. Father was going frantic — it’s only him and an old warlock called Perkins in the office — and they had to do Memory Charms and all sorts of stuff to cover it up —”

“But your dad — this car —”

Felix laughed. “Yeah, Father’s crazy about everything to do with Muggles; our shed’s full of Muggle stuff. He takes it apart, puts spells on it, and puts it back together again. If he raided our house he’d have to put himself under arrest. It drives Mother mad.”

“That’s the main road,” said Aiden, peering down through the windshield. “We’ll be there in ten minutes. … Just as well, it’s getting light. …”

A faint pinkish glow was visible along the horizon to the east.

Felix brought the car lower, and Eli saw a dark patchwork of fields and clumps of trees.

“We’re a little way outside the village,” said Aiden. “Ottery St. Catchpole.”

Lower and lower went the flying car. The edge of a brilliant red sun was now gleaming through the trees.

“Touchdown!” said Felix as, with a slight bump, they hit the ground. They had landed next to a tumbledown garage in a small yard, and Eli looked out for the first time at Leo’s house.

It looked as though it had once been a large stone pigpen, but extra rooms had been added here and there until it was several stories high and so crooked it looked as though it were held up by magic (which, Eli reminded himself, it probably was). Four or five chimneys were perched on top of the red roof. A lopsided sign stuck in the ground near the entrance read, THE BURROW. Around the front door lay a jumble of rubber boots and a very rusty cauldron. Several fat brown chickens were pecking their way around the yard.

“It’s not much,” said Leo.

“It’s wonderful,” said Eli happily, thinking of Privet Drive.

They got out of the car.

“Now, we’ll go upstairs really quietly,” said Felix, “and wait for Mother to call us for breakfast. Then, Leo, you come bounding downstairs going, ‘Mother, look who turned up in the night!’ and she’ll be all pleased to see Eli and no one need ever know we flew the car.”

“Right,” said Leo. “Come on, Eli, I sleep at the — at the top —”

Leo had gone a nasty greenish color, his eyes fixed on the house. The other three wheeled around.

Mrs. Brunswick was marching across the yard, scattering chickens, and for a short, plump, kind-faced woman, it was remarkable how much she looked like a saber-toothed tiger.

“Ah,” said Felix.

“Oh, dear,” said Aiden.

Mrs. Brunswick came to a halt in front of them, her hands on her hips, staring from one guilty face to the next. She was wearing a flowered apron with a wand sticking out of the pocket.

“So,” she said.

“ ’Morning, Mother,” said Aiden, in what he clearly thought was a jaunty, winning voice.

“Have you any idea how worried I’ve been?” said Mrs. Brunswick in a deadly whisper.

“Sorry, Mother, but see, we had to —”

All three of Mrs. Brunswick’s sons were taller than she was, but they cowered as her rage broke over them.

“Beds empty! No note! Car gone — could have crashed — out of my mind with worry — did you care? — never, as long as I’ve lived — you wait until your father gets home, we never had trouble like this from Max or Charlie or Evan —”

“Perfect Evan,” muttered Felix.

“YOU COULD DO WITH TAKING A LEAF OUT OF PERCY’S BOOK!” yelled Mrs. Brunswick, prodding a finger in Felix’s chest. “You could have died, you could have been seen, you could have lost your father his job —”

It seemed to go on for hours. Mrs. Brunswick had shouted herself hoarse before she turned on Eli, who backed away.

“I’m very pleased to see you, Eli, dear,” she said. “Come in and have some breakfast.”

She turned and walked back into the house and Eli, after a nervous glance at Leo, who nodded encouragingly, followed her.

The kitchen was small and rather cramped. There was a scrubbed wooden table and chairs in the middle, and Eli sat down on the edge of his seat, looking around. He had never been in a wizard house before.

The clock on the wall opposite him had only one hand and no numbers at all. Written around the edge were things like Time to make tea, Time to feed the chickens, and You’re late. Books were stacked three deep on the mantelpiece, books with titles like Charm Your Own Cheese, Enchantment in Baking, and One Minute Feasts — It’s Magic! And unless Eli’s ears were deceiving him, the old radio next to the sink had just announced that coming up was “Witching Hour, with the popular singing sorceress, Celestina Warbeck.”

Mrs. Brunswick was clattering around, cooking breakfast a little haphazardly, throwing dirty looks at her sons as she threw sausages into the frying pan. Every now and then she muttered things like “don’t know what you were thinking of,” and “never would have believed it.”

“I don’t blame you, dear,” she assured Eli, tipping eight or nine sausages onto his plate. “Arthur and I have been worried about you, too. Just last night we were saying we’d come and get you ourselves if you hadn’t written back to Leo by Friday. But really” (she was now adding three fried eggs to his plate), “flying an illegal car halfway across the country — anyone could have seen you —”

She flicked her wand casually at the dishes in the sink, which began to clean themselves, clinking gently in the background.

“It was cloudy, Mother!” said Felix.

“You keep your mouth closed while you’re eating!” Mrs. Brunswick snapped.

“They were starving him, Mother!” said Aiden.

“And you!” said Mrs. Brunswick, but it was with a slightly softened expression that she started cutting Eli bread and buttering it for him.

At that moment there was a diversion in the form of a small, redheaded figure in a long nightdress, who appeared in the kitchen, gave a small squeal, and ran out again.

“Lila,” said Leo in an undertone to Eli. “My sister. She’s been talking about you all summer.”

“Yeah, she’ll be wanting your autograph, Eli,” Felix said with a grin, but he caught his mother’s eye and bent his face over his plate without another word. Nothing more was said until all four plates were clean, which took a surprisingly short time.

“Blimey, I’m tired,” yawned Felix, setting down his knife and fork at last. “I think I’ll go to bed and —”

“You will not,” snapped Mrs. Brunswick. “It’s your own fault you’ve been up all night. You’re going to de-gnome the garden for me; they’re getting completely out of hand again —”

“Oh, Mother —”

“And you two,” she said, glaring at Leo and Felix. “You can go up to bed, dear,” she added to Eli. “You didn’t ask them to fly that wretched car —”

But Eli, who felt wide awake, said quickly, “I’ll help Leo. I’ve never seen a de-gnoming —”

“That’s very sweet of you, dear, but it’s dull work,” said Mrs. Brunswick. “Now, let’s see what Bright’s got to say on the subject —”

And she pulled a heavy book from the stack on the mantelpiece. Aiden groaned.

“Mother, we know how to de-gnome a garden —”

Eli looked at the cover of Mrs. Brunswick’s book. Written across it in fancy gold letters were the words Julian Bright’s Guide to Household Pests. There was a big photograph on the front of a very good-looking wizard with wavy blond hair and bright blue eyes. As always in the wizarding world, the photograph was moving; the wizard, who Eli supposed was Julian Bright, kept winking cheekily up at them all. Mrs. Brunswick beamed down at him.

“Oh, he is marvelous,” she said. “He knows his household pests, all right, it’s a wonderful book. …”

“Mother fancies him,” said Felix, in a very audible whisper.

“Don’t be so ridiculous, Felix,” said Mrs. Brunswick, her cheeks rather pink. “All right, if you think you know better than Bright, you can go and get on with it, and woe betide you if there’s a single gnome in that garden when I come out to inspect it.”

Yawning and grumbling, the Brunswicks slouched outside with Eli behind them. The garden was large, and in Eli’s eyes, exactly what a garden should be. The Powells wouldn’t have liked it — there were plenty of weeds, and the grass needed cutting — but there were gnarled trees all around the walls, plants Eli had never seen spilling from every flower bed, and a big green pond full of frogs.

“Muggles have garden gnomes, too, you know,” Eli told Leo as they crossed the lawn.

“Yeah, I’ve seen those things they think are gnomes,” said Leo, bent double with his head in a peony bush, “like fat little Santa Clauses with fishing rods. …”

There was a violent scuffling noise, the peony bush shuddered, and Leo straightened up. “This is a gnome,” he said grimly.

“Gerroff me! Gerroff me!” squealed the gnome.

It was certainly nothing like Santa Claus. It was small and leathery looking, with a large, knobby, bald head exactly like a potato. Leo held it at arm’s length as it kicked out at him with its horny little feet; he grasped it around the ankles and turned it upside down.

“This is what you have to do,” he said. He raised the gnome above his head (“Gerroff me!”) and started to swing it in great circles like a lasso. Seeing the shocked look on Eli’s face, Leo added, “It doesn’t hurt them — you’ve just got to make them really dizzy so they can’t find their way back to the gnomeholes.”

He let go of the gnome’s ankles: It flew twenty feet into the air and landed with a thud in the field over the hedge.

“Pitiful,” said Felix. “I bet I can get mine beyond that stump.”

Eli learned quickly not to feel too sorry for the gnomes. He decided just to drop the first one he caught over the hedge, but the gnome, sensing weakness, sank its razor-sharp teeth into Eli’s finger and he had a hard job shaking it off — until —

“Wow, Eli — that must’ve been fifty feet. …”

The air was soon thick with flying gnomes.

“See, they’re not too bright,” said Aiden, seizing five or six gnomes at once. “The moment they know the de-gnoming’s going on they storm up to have a look. You’d think they’d have learned by now just to stay put.”

Soon, the crowd of gnomes in the field started walking away in a straggling line, their little shoulders hunched.

“They’ll be back,” said Leo as they watched the gnomes disappear into the hedge on the other side of the field. “They love it here. … Father’s too soft with them; he thinks they’re funny. …”

Just then, the front door slammed.

“He’s back!” said Aiden. “Father’s home!”

They hurried through the garden and back into the house.

Mr. Brunswick was slumped in a kitchen chair with his glasses off and his eyes closed. He was a thin man, going bald, but the little hair he had was as red as any of his children’s. He was wearing long green robes, which were dusty and travel-worn.

“What a night,” he mumbled, groping for the teapot as they all sat down around him. “Nine raids. Nine! And old Darius Teller tried to put a hex on me when I had my back turned. …”

Mr. Brunswick took a long gulp of tea and sighed.

“Find anything, Father?” said Felix eagerly.

“All I got were a few shrinking door keys and a biting kettle,” yawned Mr. Brunswick. “There was some pretty nasty stuff that wasn’t my department, though. Gareth Hallow was taken away for questioning about some extremely odd ferrets, but that’s the Committee on Experimental Charms, thank goodness. …”

“Why would anyone bother making door keys shrink?” said Aiden.

“Just Muggle-baiting,” sighed Mr. Brunswick. “Sell them a key that keeps shrinking to nothing so they can never find it when they need it. … Of course, it’s very hard to convict anyone because no Muggle would admit their key keeps shrinking — they’ll insist they just keep losing it. Bless them, they’ll go to any lengths to ignore magic, even if it’s staring them in the face. … But the things our lot have taken to enchanting, you wouldn’t believe —”

“LIKE CARS, FOR INSTANCE?”

Mrs. Brunswick had appeared, holding a long poker like a sword. Mr. Brunswick’s eyes jerked open. He stared guiltily at his wife.

“C-cars, Amelia, dear?”

“Yes, Arthur, cars,” said Mrs. Brunswick, her eyes flashing. “Imagine a wizard buying a rusty old car and telling his wife all he wanted to do with it was take it apart to see how it worked, while really he was enchanting it to make it fly.”

Mr. Brunswick blinked.

“Well, dear, I think you’ll find that he would be quite within the law to do that, even if — er — he maybe would have done better to, um, tell his wife the truth. … There’s a loophole in the law, you’ll find. … As long as he wasn’t intending to fly the car, the fact that the car could fly wouldn’t —”

“Arthur Brunswick, you made sure there was a loophole when you wrote that law!” shouted Mrs. Brunswick. “Just so you could carry on tinkering with all that Muggle rubbish in your shed! And for your information, Eli arrived this morning in the car you weren’t intending to fly!”

“Eli?” said Mr. Brunswick blankly. “Eli who?”

He looked around, saw Eli, and jumped.

“Good lord, is it Eli Ember? Very pleased to meet you, Leo’s told us so much about —”

“Your sons flew that car to Eli’s house and back last night!” shouted Mrs. Brunswick. “What have you got to say about that, eh?”

“Did you really?” said Mr. Brunswick eagerly. “Did it go all right? I — I mean,” he faltered as sparks flew from Mrs. Brunswick’s eyes, “that — that was very wrong, boys — very wrong indeed. …”

“Let’s leave them to it,” Leo muttered to Eli as Mrs. Brunswick swelled like a bullfrog. “Come on, I’ll show you my bedroom.”

They slipped out of the kitchen and down a narrow passageway to an uneven staircase, which wound its way, zigzagging up through the house. On the third landing, a door stood ajar. Eli just caught sight of a pair of bright brown eyes staring at him before it closed with a snap.

“Lila,” said Leo. “You don’t know how weird it is for her to be this shy. She never shuts up normally —”

They climbed two more flights until they reached a door with peeling paint and a small plaque on it, saying RONALD’S ROOM.

Eli stepped in, his head almost touching the sloping ceiling, and blinked. It was like walking into a furnace: Nearly everything in Leo’s room seemed to be a violent shade of orange: the bedspread, the walls, even the ceiling. Then Eli realized that Leo had covered nearly every inch of the shabby wallpaper with posters of the same seven witches and wizards, all wearing bright orange robes, carrying broomsticks, and waving energetically.

“Your Quidditch team?” said Eli.

“The Chudley Cannons,” said Leo, pointing at the orange bedspread, which was emblazoned with two giant black C’s and a speeding cannonball. “Ninth in the league.”

Leo’s school spellbooks were stacked untidily in a corner, next to a pile of comics that all seemed to feature The Adventures of Martin Miggs, the Mad Muggle. Leo’s magic wand was lying on top of a fish tank full of frog spawn on the windowsill, next to his fat gray rat, Scabbers, who was snoozing in a patch of sun.

Eli stepped over a pack of Self-Shuffling playing cards on the floor and looked out of the tiny window. In the field far below he could see a gang of gnomes sneaking one by one back through the Brunswicks’ hedge. Then he turned to look at Leo, who was watching him almost nervously, as though waiting for his opinion.

“It’s a bit small,” said Leo quickly. “Not like that room you had with the Muggles. And I’m right underneath the ghoul in the attic; he’s always banging on the pipes and groaning. …”

But Eli, grinning widely, said, “This is the best house I’ve ever been in.”

Leo’s ears went pink.





Chapter 4



At Flourish and Blotts





Life at the Burrow was as different as possible from life on Privet Drive. The Powells liked everything neat and ordered; the Brunswicks’ house burst with the strange and unexpected. Eli got a shock the first time he looked in the mirror over the kitchen mantelpiece and it shouted, “Tuck your shirt in, scruffy!” The ghoul in the attic howled and dropped pipes whenever he felt things were getting too quiet, and small explosions from Felix and Aiden’s bedroom were considered perfectly normal. What Eli found most unusual about life at Leo’s, however, wasn’t the talking mirror or the clanking ghoul: It was the fact that everybody there seemed to like him.

Mrs. Brunswick fussed over the state of his socks and tried to force him to eat fourth helpings at every meal. Mr. Brunswick liked Eli to sit next to him at the dinner table so that he could bombard him with questions about life with Muggles, asking him to explain how things like plugs and the postal service worked.

“Fascinating!” he would say as Eli talked him through using a telephone. “Ingenious, really, how many ways Muggles have found of getting along without magic.”

Eli heard from Hogwarts one sunny morning about a week after he had arrived at the Burrow. He and Leo went down to breakfast to find Mr. and Mrs. Brunswick and Lila already sitting at the kitchen table. The moment she saw Eli, Lila accidentally knocked her porridge bowl to the floor with a loud clatter. Lila seemed very prone to knocking things over whenever Eli entered a room. She dived under the table to retrieve the bowl and emerged with her face glowing like the setting sun. Pretending he hadn’t noticed this, Eli sat down and took the toast Mrs. Brunswick offered him.

“Letters from school,” said Mr. Brunswick, passing Eli and Leo identical envelopes of yellowish parchment, addressed in green ink. “Starling already knows you’re here, Eli — doesn’t miss a trick, that man. You two’ve got them, too,” he added, as Felix and Aiden ambled in, still in their pajamas.

For a few minutes there was silence as they all read their letters. Eli’s told him to catch the Hogwarts Express as usual from King’s Cross station on September first. There was also a list of the new books he’d need for the coming year.



SECOND-YEAR STUDENTS WILL REQUIRE:

The Standard Book of Spells, Grade 2

by Cordelia Plover

Break with a Banshee by Julian Bright

Gadding with Ghouls by Julian Bright

Holidays with Hags by Julian Bright

Travels with Trolls by Julian Bright

Voyages with Vampires by Julian Bright

Wanderings with Werewolves by Julian Bright

Year with the Yeti by Julian Bright

Felix, who had finished his own list, peered over at Eli’s.

“You’ve been told to get all Bright’s books, too!” he said. “The new Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher must be a fan — bet it’s a witch.”

At this point, Felix caught his mother’s eye and quickly busied himself with the marmalade.

“That lot won’t come cheap,” said Aiden, with a quick look at his parents. “Bright’s books are really expensive. …”

“Well, we’ll manage,” said Mrs. Brunswick, but she looked worried. “I expect we’ll be able to pick up a lot of Lila’s things secondhand.”

“Oh, are you starting at Hogwarts this year?” Eli asked Lila.

She nodded, blushing to the roots of her flaming hair, and put her elbow in the butter dish. Fortunately no one saw this except Eli, because just then Leo’s elder brother Evan walked in. He was already dressed, his Hogwarts prefect badge pinned to his sweater vest.

“Morning, all,” said Evan briskly. “Lovely day.”

He sat down in the only remaining chair but leapt up again almost immediately, pulling from underneath him a molting, gray feather duster — at least, that was what Eli thought it was, until he saw that it was breathing.

“Hoot!” said Leo, taking the limp owl from Evan and extracting a letter from under its wing. “Finally — he’s got Isla’s answer. I wrote to her saying we were going to try and rescue you from the Powells.”

He carried Hoot to a perch just inside the back door and tried to stand him on it, but Hoot flopped straight off again so Leo laid him on the draining board instead, muttering, “Pathetic.” Then he ripped open Isla’s letter and read it out loud:

“ ‘Dear Leo, and Eli if you’re there,

“ ‘I hope everything went all right and that Eli is okay and that you didn’t do anything illegal to get him out, Leo, because that would get Eli into trouble, too. I’ve been really worried and if Eli is all right, will you please let me know at once, but perhaps it would be better if you used a different owl, because I think another delivery might finish your one off.

“ ‘I’m very busy with schoolwork, of course’ — How can she be?” said Leo in horror. “We’re on vacation! — ‘and we’re going to London next Wednesday to buy my new books. Why don’t we meet in Diagon Alley?

“ ‘Let me know what’s happening as soon as you can. Love from Isla.’ ”

“Well, that fits in nicely, we can go and get all your things then, too,” said Mrs. Brunswick, starting to clear the table. “What’re you all up to today?”

Eli, Leo, Felix, and Aiden were planning to go up the hill to a small paddock the Brunswicks owned. It was surrounded by trees that blocked it from view of the village below, meaning that they could practice Quidditch there, as long as they didn’t fly too high. They couldn’t use real Quidditch balls, which would have been hard to explain if they had escaped and flown away over the village; instead they threw apples for one another to catch. They took turns riding Eli’s Nimbus Two Thousand, which was easily the best broom; Leo’s old Shooting Star was often outstripped by passing butterflies.

Five minutes later they were marching up the hill, broomsticks over their shoulders. They had asked Evan if he wanted to join them, but he had said he was busy. Eli had only seen Evan at mealtimes so far; he stayed shut in his room the rest of the time.

“Wish I knew what he was up to,” said Felix, frowning. “He’s not himself. His exam results came the day before you did; twelve O.W.L.s and he hardly gloated at all.”

“Ordinary Wizarding Levels,” Aiden explained, seeing Eli’s puzzled look. “Max got twelve, too. If we’re not careful, we’ll have another Head Boy in the family. I don’t think I could stand the shame.”

Max was the oldest Brunswick brother. He and the next brother, Charlie, had already left Hogwarts. Eli had never met either of them, but knew that Charlie was in Romania studying dragons and Max in Egypt working for the wizard’s bank, Gringotts.

“Dunno how Mother and Father are going to afford all our school stuff this year,” said Aiden after a while. “Five sets of Bright books! And Lila needs robes and a wand and everything. …”

Eli said nothing. He felt a bit awkward. Stored in an underground vault at Gringotts in London was a small fortune that his parents had left him. Of course, it was only in the wizarding world that he had money; you couldn’t use Galleons, Sickles, and Knuts in Muggle shops. He had never mentioned his Gringotts bank account to the Powells; he didn’t think their horror of anything connected with magic would stretch to a large pile of gold.



Mrs. Brunswick woke them all early the following Wednesday. After a quick half a dozen bacon sandwiches each, they pulled on their coats and Mrs. Brunswick took a flowerpot off the kitchen mantelpiece and peered inside.

“We’re running low, Arthur,” she sighed. “We’ll have to buy some more today. … Ah well, guests first! After you, Eli dear!”

And she offered him the flowerpot.

Eli stared at them all watching him.

“W-what am I supposed to do?” he stammered.

“He’s never traveled by Floo powder,” said Leo suddenly. “Sorry, Eli, I forgot.”

“Never?” said Mr. Brunswick. “But how did you get to Diagon Alley to buy your school things last year?”

“I went on the Underground —”

“Really?” said Mr. Brunswick eagerly. “Were there escapators? How exactly —”

“Not now, Arthur,” said Mrs. Brunswick. “Floo powder’s a lot quicker, dear, but goodness me, if you’ve never used it before —”

“He’ll be all right, Mother,” said Felix. “Eli, watch us first.”

He took a pinch of glittering powder out of the flowerpot, stepped up to the fire, and threw the powder into the flames.

With a roar, the fire turned emerald green and rose higher than Felix, who stepped right into it, shouted, “Diagon Alley!” and vanished.

“You must speak clearly, dear,” Mrs. Brunswick told Eli as Aiden dipped his hand into the flowerpot. “And be sure to get out at the right grate. …”

“The right what?” said Eli nervously as the fire roared and whipped Aiden out of sight, too.

“Well, there are an awful lot of wizard fires to choose from, you know, but as long as you’ve spoken clearly —”

“He’ll be fine, Amelia, don’t fuss,” said Mr. Brunswick, helping himself to Floo powder, too.

“But, dear, if he got lost, how would we ever explain to his aunt and uncle?”

“They wouldn’t mind,” Eli reassured her. “Carson would think it was a brilliant joke if I got lost up a chimney, don’t worry about that —”

“Well … all right … you go after Arthur,” said Mrs. Brunswick. “Now, when you get into the fire, say where you’re going —”

“And keep your elbows tucked in,” Leo advised.

“And your eyes shut,” said Mrs. Brunswick. “The soot —”

“Don’t fidget,” said Leo. “Or you might well fall out of the wrong fireplace —”

“But don’t panic and get out too early; wait until you see Felix and Aiden.”

Trying hard to bear all this in mind, Eli took a pinch of Floo powder and walked to the edge of the fire. He took a deep breath, scattered the powder into the flames, and stepped forward; the fire felt like a warm breeze; he opened his mouth and immediately swallowed a lot of hot ash.

“D-Dia-gon Alley,” he coughed.

It felt as though he were being sucked down a giant drain. He seemed to be spinning very fast — the roaring in his ears was deafening — he tried to keep his eyes open but the whirl of green flames made him feel sick — something hard knocked his elbow and he tucked it in tightly, still spinning and spinning — now it felt as though cold hands were slapping his face — squinting through his glasses he saw a blurred stream of fireplaces and snatched glimpses of the rooms beyond — his bacon sandwiches were churning inside him — he closed his eyes again wishing it would stop, and then —

He fell, face forward, onto cold stone and felt the bridge of his glasses snap.

Dizzy and bruised, covered in soot, he got gingerly to his feet, holding his broken glasses up to his eyes. He was quite alone, but where he was, he had no idea. All he could tell was that he was standing in the stone fireplace of what looked like a large, dimly lit wizard’s shop — but nothing in here was ever likely to be on a Hogwarts school list.

A glass case nearby held a withered hand on a cushion, a bloodstained pack of cards, and a staring glass eye. Evil-looking masks stared down from the walls, an assortment of human bones lay upon the counter, and rusty, spiked instruments hung from the ceiling. Even worse, the dark, narrow street Eli could see through the dusty shop window was definitely not Diagon Alley.

The sooner he got out of here, the better. Nose still stinging where it had hit the hearth, Eli made his way swiftly and silently toward the door, but before he’d got halfway toward it, two people appeared on the other side of the glass — and one of them was the very last person Eli wanted to meet when he was lost, covered in soot, and wearing broken glasses: Jasper Mortem.

Eli looked quickly around and spotted a large black cabinet to his left; he shot inside it and pulled the doors closed, leaving a small crack to peer through. Seconds later, a bell clanged, and Mortem stepped into the shop.

The man who followed could only be Jasper’s father. He had the same pale, pointed face and identical cold, gray eyes. Mr. Mortem crossed the shop, looking lazily at the items on display, and rang a bell on the counter before turning to his son and saying, “Touch nothing, Jasper.”

Mortem, who had reached for the glass eye, said, “I thought you were going to buy me a present.”

“I said I would buy you a racing broom,” said his father, drumming his fingers on the counter.

“What’s the good of that if I’m not on the House team?” said Mortem, looking sulky and bad-tempered. “Eli Ember got a Nimbus Two Thousand last year. Special permission from Starling so he could play for Gryffindor. He’s not even that good, it’s just because he’s famous … famous for having a stupid scar on his forehead. …”

Mortem bent down to examine a shelf full of skulls.

“… everyone thinks he’s so smart, wonderful Ember with his scar and his broomstick —”

“You have told me this at least a dozen times already,” said Mr. Mortem, with a quelling look at his son. “And I would remind you that it is not — prudent — to appear less than fond of Eli Ember, not when most of our kind regard him as the hero who made the Dark Lord disappear — ah, Mr. Quill.”

A stooping man had appeared behind the counter, smoothing his greasy hair back from his face.

“Mr. Mortem, what a pleasure to see you again,” said Mr. Quill in a voice as oily as his hair. “Delighted — and young Master Mortem, too — charmed. How may I be of assistance? I must show you, just in today, and very reasonably priced —”

“I’m not buying today, Mr. Quill, but selling,” said Mr. Mortem.

“Selling?” The smile faded slightly from Mr. Quill’s face.

“You have heard, of course, that the Ministry is conducting more raids,” said Mr. Mortem, taking a roll of parchment from his inside pocket and unraveling it for Mr. Quill to read. “I have a few — ah — items at home that might embarrass me, if the Ministry were to call. …”

Mr. Quill fixed a pair of pince-nez to his nose and looked down the list.

“The Ministry wouldn’t presume to trouble you, sir, surely?”

Mr. Mortem’s lip curled.

“I have not been visited yet. The name Mortem still commands a certain respect, yet the Ministry grows ever more meddlesome. There are rumors about a new Muggle Protection Act — no doubt that flea-bitten, Muggle-loving fool Arthur Brunswick is behind it —”

Eli felt a hot surge of anger.

“— and as you see, certain of these poisons might make it appear —”

“I understand, sir, of course,” said Mr. Quill. “Let me see …”

“Can I have that?” interrupted Jasper, pointing at the withered hand on its cushion.

“Ah, the Hand of Glory!” said Mr. Quill, abandoning Mr. Mortem’s list and scurrying over to Jasper. “Insert a candle and it gives light only to the holder! Best friend of thieves and plunderers! Your son has fine taste, sir.”

“I hope my son will amount to more than a thief or a plunderer, Borgin,” said Mr. Mortem coldly, and Mr. Quill said quickly, “No offense, sir, no offense meant —”

“Though if his grades don’t pick up,” said Mr. Mortem, more coldly still, “that may indeed be all he is fit for —”

“It’s not my fault,” retorted Jasper. “The teachers all have favorites, that Isla Hawke —”

“I would have thought you’d be ashamed that a girl of no wizard family beat you in every exam,” snapped Mr. Mortem.

“Ha!” said Eli under his breath, pleased to see Jasper looking both abashed and angry.

“It’s the same all over,” said Mr. Quill, in his oily voice. “Wizard blood is counting for less everywhere —”

“Not with me,” said Mr. Mortem, his long nostrils flaring.

“No, sir, nor with me, sir,” said Mr. Quill, with a deep bow.

“In that case, perhaps we can return to my list,” said Mr. Mortem shortly. “I am in something of a hurry, Borgin, I have important business elsewhere today —”

They started to haggle. Eli watched nervously as Jasper drew nearer and nearer to his hiding place, examining the objects for sale. Jasper paused to examine a long coil of hangman’s rope and to read, smirking, the card propped on a magnificent necklace of opals, Caution: Do Not Touch. Cursed — Has Claimed the Lives of Nineteen Muggle Owners to Date.

Jasper turned away and saw the cabinet right in front of him. He walked forward — he stretched out his hand for the handle —

“Done,” said Mr. Mortem at the counter. “Come, Jasper —”

Eli wiped his forehead on his sleeve as Jasper turned away.

“Good day to you, Mr. Quill. I’ll expect you at the manor tomorrow to pick up the goods.”

The moment the door had closed, Mr. Quill dropped his oily manner.

“Good day yourself, Mister Mortem, and if the stories are true, you haven’t sold me half of what’s hidden in your manor. …”

Muttering darkly, Mr. Quill disappeared into a back room. Eli waited for a minute in case he came back, then, quietly as he could, slipped out of the cabinet, past the glass cases, and out of the shop door.

Clutching his broken glasses to his face, Eli stared around. He had emerged into a dingy alleyway that seemed to be made up entirely of shops devoted to the Dark Arts. The one he’d just left, Borgin and Burkes, looked like the largest, but opposite was a nasty window display of shrunken heads and, two doors down, a large cage was alive with gigantic black spiders. Two shabby-looking wizards were watching him from the shadow of a doorway, muttering to each other. Feeling jumpy, Eli set off, trying to hold his glasses on straight and hoping against hope he’d be able to find a way out of here.

An old wooden street sign hanging over a shop selling poisonous candles told him he was in Knockturn Alley. This didn’t help, as Eli had never heard of such a place. He supposed he hadn’t spoken clearly enough through his mouthful of ashes back in the Brunswicks’ fire. Trying to stay calm, he wondered what to do.

“Not lost are you, my dear?” said a voice in his ear, making him jump.

An aged witch stood in front of him, holding a tray of what looked horribly like whole human fingernails. She leered at him, showing mossy teeth. Eli backed away.

“I’m fine, thanks,” he said. “I’m just —”

“HARRY! What d’yeh think yer doin’ down there?”

Eli’s heart leapt. So did the witch; a load of fingernails cascaded down over her feet and she cursed as the massive form of Griff, the Hogwarts gamekeeper, came striding toward them, beetle-black eyes flashing over his great bristling beard.

“Griff!” Eli croaked in relief. “I was lost — Floo powder —”

Griff seized Eli by the scruff of the neck and pulled him away from the witch, knocking the tray right out of her hands. Her shrieks followed them all the way along the twisting alleyway out into bright sunlight. Eli saw a familiar, snow-white marble building in the distance — Gringotts Bank. Griff had steered him right into Diagon Alley.

“Yer a mess!” said Griff gruffly, brushing soot off Eli so forcefully he nearly knocked him into a barrel of dragon dung outside an apothecary. “Skulkin’ around Knockturn Alley, I dunno — dodgy place, Eli — don’ want no one ter see yeh down there —”

“I realized that,” said Eli, ducking as Griff made to brush him off again. “I told you, I was lost — what were you doing down there, anyway?”

“I was lookin’ fer a Flesh-Eatin’ Slug Repellent,” growled Griff. “They’re ruinin’ the school cabbages. Yer not on yer own?”

“I’m staying with the Brunswicks but we got separated,” Eli explained. “I’ve got to go and find them. …”

They set off together down the street.

“How come yeh never wrote back ter me?” said Griff as Eli jogged alongside him (he had to take three steps to every stride of Griff’s enormous boots). Eli explained all about Nobby and the Powells.

“Lousy Muggles,” growled Griff. “If I’d’ve known —”

“Eli! Eli! Over here!”

Eli looked up and saw Isla Hawke standing at the top of the white flight of steps to Gringotts. She ran down to meet them, her bushy brown hair flying behind her.

“What happened to your glasses? Hello, Griff — Oh, it’s wonderful to see you two again — Are you coming into Gringotts, Eli?”

“As soon as I’ve found the Brunswicks,” said Eli.

“Yeh won’t have long ter wait,” Griff said with a grin.

Eli and Isla looked around: Sprinting up the crowded street were Leo, Felix, Aiden, Evan, and Mr. Brunswick.

“Eli,” Mr. Brunswick panted. “We hoped you’d only gone one grate too far. …” He mopped his glistening bald patch. “Amelia’s frantic — she’s coming now —”

“Where did you come out?” Leo asked.

“Knockturn Alley,” said Griff grimly.

“Excellent!” said Felix and Aiden together.

“We’ve never been allowed in,” said Leo enviously.

“I should ruddy well think not,” growled Griff.

Mrs. Brunswick now came galloping into view, her handbag swinging wildly in one hand, Lila just clinging onto the other.

“Oh, Eli — oh, my dear — you could have been anywhere —”

Gasping for breath she pulled a large clothes brush out of her bag and began sweeping off the soot Griff hadn’t managed to beat away. Mr. Brunswick took Eli’s glasses, gave them a tap of his wand, and returned them, good as new.

“Well, gotta be off,” said Griff, who was having his hand wrung by Mrs. Brunswick (“Knockturn Alley! If you hadn’t found him, Griff!”). “See yer at Hogwarts!” And he strode away, head and shoulders taller than anyone else in the packed street.

“Guess who I saw in Borgin and Burkes?” Eli asked Leo and Isla as they climbed the Gringotts steps. “Mortem and his father.”

“Did Caspian Mortem buy anything?” said Mr. Brunswick sharply behind them.

“No, he was selling —”

“So he’s worried,” said Mr. Brunswick with grim satisfaction. “Oh, I’d love to get Caspian Mortem for something. …”

“You be careful, Arthur,” said Mrs. Brunswick sharply as they were bowed into the bank by a goblin at the door. “That family’s trouble. Don’t go biting off more than you can chew —”

“So you don’t think I’m a match for Caspian Mortem?” said Mr. Brunswick indignantly, but he was distracted almost at once by the sight of Isla’s parents, who were standing nervously at the counter that ran all along the great marble hall, waiting for Isla to introduce them.

“But you’re Muggles!” said Mr. Brunswick delightedly. “We must have a drink! What’s that you’ve got there? Oh, you’re changing Muggle money. Amelia, look!” He pointed excitedly at the ten-pound notes in Mr. Hawke’s hand.

“Meet you back here,” Leo said to Isla as the Brunswicks and Eli were led off to their underground vaults by another Gringotts goblin.

The vaults were reached by means of small, goblin-driven carts that sped along minature train tracks through the bank’s underground tunnels. Eli enjoyed the breakneck journey down to the Brunswicks’ vault, but felt dreadful, far worse than he had in Knock-turn Alley, when it was opened. There was a very small pile of silver Sickles inside, and just one gold Galleon. Mrs. Brunswick felt right into the corners before sweeping the whole lot into her bag. Eli felt even worse when they reached his vault. He tried to block the contents from view as he hastily shoved handfuls of coins into a leather bag.

Back outside on the marble steps, they all separated. Evan muttered vaguely about needing a new quill. Felix and Aiden had spotted their friend from Hogwarts, Jordan Lee. Mrs. Brunswick and Lila were going to a secondhand robe shop. Mr. Brunswick was insisting on taking the Hawkes off to the Leaky Cauldron for a drink.

“We’ll all meet at Flourish and Blotts in an hour to buy your schoolbooks,” said Mrs. Brunswick, setting off with Lila. “And not one step down Knockturn Alley!” she shouted at the twins’ retreating backs.

Eli, Leo, and Isla strolled off along the winding, cobbled street. The bag of gold, silver, and bronze jangling cheerfully in Eli’s pocket was clamoring to be spent, so he bought three large strawberry-and-peanut-butter ice creams, which they slurped happily as they wandered up the alley, examining the fascinating shop windows. Leo gazed longingly at a full set of Chudley Cannon robes in the windows of Quality Quidditch Supplies until Isla dragged them off to buy ink and parchment next door.
