In Gambol and Japes Wizarding Joke Shop, they met Felix, Aiden, and Jordan Lee, who were stocking up on Dr. Filibuster’s Fabulous Wet-Start, No-Heat Fireworks, and in a tiny junk shop full of broken wands, lopsided brass scales, and old cloaks covered in potion stains they found Evan, deeply immersed in a small and deeply boring book called Prefects Who Gained Power.

“A study of Hogwarts prefects and their later careers,” Leo read aloud off the back cover. “That sounds fascinating. …”

“Go away,” Evan snapped.

“ ’Course, he’s very ambitious, Evan, he’s got it all planned out. … He wants to be Minister of Magic …” Leo told Eli and Isla in an undertone as they left Evan to it.

An hour later, they headed for Flourish and Blotts. They were by no means the only ones making their way to the bookshop. As they approached it, they saw to their surprise a large crowd jostling outside the doors, trying to get in. The reason for this was proclaimed by a large banner stretched across the upper windows:





GILDEROY LOCKHART

will be signing copies of his autobiography

MAGICAL ME

today 12:30 p.m. to 4:30 p.m.





“We can actually meet him!” Isla squealed. “I mean, he’s written almost the whole booklist!”

The crowd seemed to be made up mostly of witches around Mrs. Brunswick’s age. A harassed-looking wizard stood at the door, saying, “Calmly, please, ladies. … Don’t push, there … mind the books, now. …”

Eli, Leo, and Isla squeezed inside. A long line wound right to the back of the shop, where Julian Bright was signing his books. They each grabbed a copy of The Standard Book of Spells, Grade 2 and sneaked up the line to where the rest of the Brunswicks were standing with Mr. and Mrs. Hawke.

“Oh, there you are, good,” said Mrs. Brunswick. She sounded breathless and kept patting her hair. “We’ll be able to see him in a minute. …”

Julian Bright came slowly into view, seated at a table surrounded by large pictures of his own face, all winking and flashing dazzlingly white teeth at the crowd. The real Bright was wearing robes of forget-me-not blue that exactly matched his eyes; his pointed wizard’s hat was set at a jaunty angle on his wavy hair.

A short, irritable-looking man was dancing around taking photographs with a large black camera that emitted puffs of purple smoke with every blinding flash.

“Out of the way, there,” he snarled at Leo, moving back to get a better shot. “This is for the Daily Prophet —”

“Big deal,” said Leo, rubbing his foot where the photographer had stepped on it.

Julian Bright heard him. He looked up. He saw Leo — and then he saw Eli. He stared. Then he leapt to his feet and positively shouted, “It can’t be Eli Ember?”

The crowd parted, whispering excitedly; Bright dived forward, seized Eli’s arm, and pulled him to the front. The crowd burst into applause. Eli’s face burned as Bright shook his hand for the photographer, who was clicking away madly, wafting thick smoke over the Brunswicks.

“Nice big smile, Eli,” said Bright, through his own gleaming teeth. “Together, you and I are worth the front page.”

When he finally let go of Eli’s hand, Eli could hardly feel his fingers. He tried to sidle back over to the Brunswicks, but Bright threw an arm around his shoulders and clamped him tightly to his side.

“Ladies and gentlemen,” he said loudly, waving for quiet. “What an extraordinary moment this is! The perfect moment for me to make a little announcement I’ve been sitting on for some time!

“When young Eli here stepped into Flourish and Blotts today, he only wanted to buy my autobiography — which I shall be happy to present him now, free of charge —” The crowd applauded again. “He had no idea,” Bright continued, giving Eli a little shake that made his glasses slip to the end of his nose, “that he would shortly be getting much, much more than my book, Magical Me. He and his schoolmates will, in fact, be getting the real magical me. Yes, ladies and gentlemen, I have great pleasure and pride in announcing that this September, I will be taking up the post of Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry!”

The crowd cheered and clapped and Eli found himself being presented with the entire works of Julian Bright. Staggering slightly under their weight, he managed to make his way out of the limelight to the edge of the room, where Lila was standing next to her new cauldron.

“You have these,” Eli mumbled to her, tipping the books into the cauldron. “I’ll buy my own —”

“Bet you loved that, didn’t you, Ember?” said a voice Eli had no trouble recognizing. He straightened up and found himself face-to-face with Jasper Mortem, who was wearing his usual sneer.

“Famous Eli Ember,” said Mortem. “Can’t even go into a bookshop without making the front page.”

“Leave him alone, he didn’t want all that!” said Lila. It was the first time she had spoken in front of Eli. She was glaring at Mortem.

“Ember, you’ve got yourself a girlfriend!” drawled Mortem. Lila went scarlet as Leo and Isla fought their way over, both clutching stacks of Bright’s books.

“Oh, it’s you,” said Leo, looking at Mortem as if he were something unpleasant on the sole of his shoe. “Bet you’re surprised to see Eli here, eh?”

“Not as surprised as I am to see you in a shop, Brunswick,” retorted Mortem. “I suppose your parents will go hungry for a month to pay for all those.”

Leo went as red as Lila. He dropped his books into the cauldron, too, and started toward Mortem, but Eli and Isla grabbed the back of his jacket.

“Leo!” said Mr. Brunswick, struggling over with Felix and Aiden. “What are you doing? It’s too crowded in here, let’s go outside.”

“Well, well, well — Arthur Brunswick.”

It was Mr. Mortem. He stood with his hand on Jasper’s shoulder, sneering in just the same way.

“Caspian,” said Mr. Brunswick, nodding coldly.

“Busy time at the Ministry, I hear,” said Mr. Mortem. “All those raids … I hope they’re paying you overtime?”

He reached into Lila’s cauldron and extracted, from amid the glossy Bright books, a very old, very battered copy of A Beginner’s Guide to Transfiguration.

“Obviously not,” Mr. Mortem said. “Dear me, what’s the use of being a disgrace to the name of wizard if they don’t even pay you well for it?”

Mr. Brunswick flushed darker than either Leo or Lila.

“We have a very different idea of what disgraces the name of wizard, Mortem,” he said.

“Clearly,” said Mr. Mortem, his pale eyes straying to Mr. and Mrs. Hawke, who were watching apprehensively. “The company you keep, Brunswick … and I thought your family could sink no lower —”

There was a thud of metal as Lila’s cauldron went flying; Mr. Brunswick had thrown himself at Mr. Mortem, knocking him backward into a bookshelf. Dozens of heavy spellbooks came thundering down on all their heads; there was a yell of, “Get him, Father!” from Felix or Aiden; Mrs. Brunswick was shrieking, “No, Arthur, no!”; the crowd stampeded backward, knocking more shelves over; “Gentlemen, please — please!” cried the assistant, and then, louder than all —

“Break it up, there, gents, break it up —”

Griff was wading toward them through the sea of books. In an instant he had pulled Mr. Brunswick and Mr. Mortem apart. Mr. Brunswick had a cut lip and Mr. Mortem had been hit in the eye by an Encyclopedia of Toadstools. He was still holding Lila’s old Transfiguration book. He thrust it at her, his eyes glittering with malice.

“Here, girl — take your book — it’s the best your father can give you —” Pulling himself out of Griff’s grip he beckoned to Jasper and swept from the shop.

“Yeh should’ve ignored him, Arthur,” said Griff, almost lifting Mr. Brunswick off his feet as he straightened his robes. “Rotten ter the core, the whole family, everyone knows that — no Mortem’s worth listenin’ ter — bad blood, that’s what it is — come on now — let’s get outta here.”

The assistant looked as though he wanted to stop them from leaving, but he barely came up to Griff’s waist and seemed to think better of it. They hurried up the street, the Hawkes shaking with fright and Mrs. Brunswick beside herself with fury.

“A fine example to set for your children … brawling in public … what Julian Bright must’ve thought —”

“He was pleased,” said Felix. “Didn’t you hear him as we were leaving? He was asking that bloke from the Daily Prophet if he’d be able to work the fight into his report — said it was all publicity —”

But it was a subdued group that headed back to the fireside in the Leaky Cauldron, where Eli, the Brunswicks, and all their shopping would be traveling back to the Burrow using Floo powder. They said good-bye to the Hawkes, who were leaving the pub for the Muggle street on the other side; Mr. Brunswick started to ask them how bus stops worked, but stopped quickly at the look on Mrs. Brunswick’s face.

Eli took off his glasses and put them safely in his pocket before helping himself to Floo powder. It definitely wasn’t his favorite way to travel.





Chapter 5



The Whomping Willow





The end of the summer vacation came too quickly for Eli’s liking. He was looking forward to getting back to Hogwarts, but his month at the Burrow had been the happiest of his life. It was difficult not to feel jealous of Leo when he thought of the Powells and the sort of welcome he could expect next time he turned up on Privet Drive.

On their last evening, Mrs. Brunswick conjured up a sumptuous dinner that included all of Eli’s favorite things, ending with a mouthwatering treacle pudding. Felix and Aiden rounded off the evening with a display of Filibuster fireworks; they filled the kitchen with red and blue stars that bounced from ceiling to wall for at least half an hour. Then it was time for a last mug of hot chocolate and bed.

It took a long while to get started next morning. They were up at dawn, but somehow they still seemed to have a great deal to do. Mrs. Brunswick dashed about in a bad mood looking for spare socks and quills; people kept colliding on the stairs, half-dressed with bits of toast in their hands; and Mr. Brunswick nearly broke his neck, tripping over a stray chicken as he crossed the yard carrying Lila’s trunk to the car.

Eli couldn’t see how eight people, six large trunks, two owls, and a rat were going to fit into one small Ford Anglia. He had reckoned, of course, without the special features that Mr. Brunswick had added.

“Not a word to Amelia,” he whispered to Eli as he opened the trunk and showed him how it had been magically expanded so that the luggage fitted easily.

When at last they were all in the car, Mrs. Brunswick glanced into the back seat, where Eli, Leo, Felix, Aiden, and Evan were all sitting comfortably side by side, and said, “Muggles do know more than we give them credit for, don’t they?” She and Lila got into the front seat, which had been stretched so that it resembled a park bench. “I mean, you’d never know it was this roomy from the outside, would you?”

Mr. Brunswick started up the engine and they trundled out of the yard, Eli turning back for a last look at the house. He barely had time to wonder when he’d see it again when they were back — Aiden had forgotten his box of Filibuster fireworks. Five minutes after that, they skidded to a halt in the yard so that Felix could run in for his broomstick. They had almost reached the highway when Lila shrieked that she’d left her diary. By the time she had clambered back into the car, they were running very late, and tempers were running high.

Mr. Brunswick glanced at his watch and then at his wife.

“Amelia, dear —”

“No, Arthur —”

“No one would see — this little button here is an Invisibility Booster I installed — that’d get us up in the air — then we fly above the clouds. We’d be there in ten minutes and no one would be any the wiser —”

“I said no, Arthur, not in broad daylight —”

They reached King’s Cross at a quarter to eleven. Mr. Brunswick dashed across the road to get trolleys for their trunks and they all hurried into the station.

Eli had caught the Hogwarts Express the previous year. The tricky part was getting onto platform nine and three-quarters, which wasn’t visible to the Muggle eye. What you had to do was walk through the solid barrier dividing platforms nine and ten. It didn’t hurt, but it had to be done carefully so that none of the Muggles noticed you vanishing.

“Evan first,” said Mrs. Brunswick, looking nervously at the clock overhead, which showed they had only five minutes to disappear casually through the barrier.

Evan strode briskly forward and vanished. Mr. Brunswick went next; Felix and Aiden followed.

“I’ll take Lila and you two come right after us,” Mrs. Brunswick told Eli and Leo, grabbing Lila’s hand and setting off. In the blink of an eye they were gone.

“Let’s go together, we’ve only got a minute,” Leo said to Eli.

Eli made sure that Hedwig’s cage was safely wedged on top of his trunk and wheeled his trolley around to face the barrier. He felt perfectly confident; this wasn’t nearly as uncomfortable as using Floo powder. Both of them bent low over the handles of their trolleys and walked purposefully toward the barrier, gathering speed. A few feet away from it, they broke into a run and —

CRASH.

Both trolleys hit the barrier and bounced backward; Leo’s trunk fell off with a loud thump, Eli was knocked off his feet, and Hedwig’s cage bounced onto the shiny floor, and she rolled away, shrieking indignantly; people all around them stared and a guard nearby yelled, “What in blazes d’you think you’re doing?”

“Lost control of the trolley,” Eli gasped, clutching his ribs as he got up. Leo ran to pick up Hedwig, who was causing such a scene that there was a lot of muttering about cruelty to animals from the surrounding crowd.

“Why can’t we get through?” Eli hissed to Leo.

“I dunno —”

Leo looked wildly around. A dozen curious people were still watching them.

“We’re going to miss the train,” Leo whispered. “I don’t understand why the gateway’s sealed itself —”

Eli looked up at the giant clock with a sickening feeling in the pit of his stomach. Ten seconds … nine seconds …

He wheeled his trolley forward cautiously until it was right against the barrier and pushed with all his might. The metal remained solid.

Three seconds … two seconds … one second …

“It’s gone,” said Leo, sounding stunned. “The train’s left. What if Mother and Father can’t get back through to us? Have you got any Muggle money?”

Eli gave a hollow laugh. “The Powells haven’t given me pocket money for about six years.”

Leo pressed his ear to the cold barrier.

“Can’t hear a thing,” he said tensely. “What’re we going to do? I don’t know how long it’ll take Mother and Father to get back to us.”

They looked around. People were still watching them, mainly because of Hedwig’s continuing screeches.

“I think we’d better go and wait by the car,” said Eli. “We’re attracting too much atten —”

“Eli!” said Leo, his eyes gleaming. “The car!”

“What about it?”

“We can fly the car to Hogwarts!”

“But I thought —”

“We’re stuck, right? And we’ve got to get to school, haven’t we? And even underage wizards are allowed to use magic if it’s a real emergency, section nineteen or something of the Restriction of Thingy —”

“But your mum and dad …” said Eli, pushing against the barrier again in the vain hope that it would give way. “How will they get home?”

“They don’t need the car!” said Leo impatiently. “They know how to Apparate! You know, just vanish and reappear at home! They only bother with Floo powder and the car because we’re all underage and we’re not allowed to Apparate yet. …”

Eli’s feeling of panic turned suddenly to excitement.

“Can you fly it?”

“No problem,” said Leo, wheeling his trolley around to face the exit. “C’mon, let’s go. If we hurry we’ll be able to follow the Hogwarts Express —”

And they marched off through the crowd of curious Muggles, out of the station and back onto the side road where the old Ford Anglia was parked.

Leo unlocked the cavernous trunk with a series of taps from his wand. They heaved their luggage back in, put Hedwig on the back seat, and got into the front.

“Check that no one’s watching,” said Leo, starting the ignition with another tap of his wand. Eli stuck his head out of the window: Traffic was rumbling along the main road ahead, but their street was empty.

“Okay,” he said.

Leo pressed a tiny silver button on the dashboard. The car around them vanished — and so did they. Eli could feel the seat vibrating beneath him, hear the engine, feel his hands on his knees and his glasses on his nose, but for all he could see, he had become a pair of eyeballs, floating a few feet above the ground in a dingy street full of parked cars.

“Let’s go,” said Leo’s voice from his right.

And the ground and the dirty buildings on either side fell away, dropping out of sight as the car rose; in seconds, the whole of London lay, smoky and glittering, below them.

Then there was a popping noise and the car, Eli, and Leo reappeared.

“Uh-oh,” said Leo, jabbing at the Invisibility Booster. “It’s faulty —”

Both of them pummeled it. The car vanished. Then it flickered back again.

“Hold on!” Leo yelled, and he slammed his foot on the accelerator; they shot straight into the low, woolly clouds and everything turned dull and foggy.

“Now what?” said Eli, blinking at the solid mass of cloud pressing in on them from all sides.

“We need to see the train to know what direction to go in,” said Leo.

“Dip back down again — quickly —”

They dropped back beneath the clouds and twisted around in their seats, squinting at the ground.

“I can see it!” Eli yelled. “Right ahead — there!”

The Hogwarts Express was streaking along below them like a scarlet snake.

“Due north,” said Leo, checking the compass on the dashboard. “Okay, we’ll just have to check on it every half hour or so — hold on —”

And they shot up through the clouds. A minute later, they burst out into a blaze of sunlight.

It was a different world. The wheels of the car skimmed the sea of fluffy cloud, the sky a bright, endless blue under the blinding white sun.

“All we’ve got to worry about now are airplanes,” said Leo.

They looked at each other and started to laugh; for a long time, they couldn’t stop.

It was as though they had been plunged into a fabulous dream. This, thought Eli, was surely the only way to travel — past swirls and turrets of snowy cloud, in a car full of hot, bright sunlight, with a fat pack of toffees in the glove compartment, and the prospect of seeing Felix’s and Aiden’s jealous faces when they landed smoothly and spectacularly on the sweeping lawn in front of Hogwarts castle.

They made regular checks on the train as they flew farther and farther north, each dip beneath the clouds showing them a different view. London was soon far behind them, replaced by neat green fields that gave way in turn to wide, purplish moors, a great city alive with cars like multicolored ants, villages with tiny toy churches.

Several uneventful hours later, however, Eli had to admit that some of the fun was wearing off. The toffees had made them extremely thirsty and they had nothing to drink. He and Leo had pulled off their sweaters, but Eli’s T-shirt was sticking to the back of his seat and his glasses kept sliding down to the end of his sweaty nose. He had stopped noticing the fantastic cloud shapes now and was thinking longingly of the train miles below, where you could buy ice-cold pumpkin juice from a trolley pushed by a plump witch. Why hadn’t they been able to get onto platform nine and three-quarters?

“Can’t be much further, can it?” croaked Leo, hours later still, as the sun started to sink into their floor of cloud, staining it a deep pink. “Ready for another check on the train?”

It was still right below them, winding its way past a snowcapped mountain. It was much darker beneath the canopy of clouds.

Leo put his foot on the accelerator and drove them upward again, but as he did so, the engine began to whine.

Eli and Leo exchanged nervous glances.

“It’s probably just tired,” said Leo. “It’s never been this far before. …”

And they both pretended not to notice the whining growing louder and louder as the sky became steadily darker. Stars were blossoming in the blackness. Eli pulled his sweater back on, trying to ignore the way the windshield wipers were now waving feebly, as though in protest.

“Not far,” said Leo, more to the car than to Eli, “not far now,” and he patted the dashboard nervously.

When they flew back beneath the clouds a little while later, they had to squint through the darkness for a landmark they knew.

“There!” Eli shouted, making Leo and Hedwig jump. “Straight ahead!”

Silhouetted on the dark horizon, high on the cliff over the lake, stood the many turrets and towers of Hogwarts castle.

But the car had begun to shudder and was losing speed.

“Come on,” Leo said cajolingly, giving the steering wheel a little shake, “nearly there, come on —”

The engine groaned. Narrow jets of steam were issuing from under the hood. Eli found himself gripping the edges of his seat very hard as they flew toward the lake.

The car gave a nasty wobble. Glancing out of his window, Eli saw the smooth, black, glassy surface of the water, a mile below. Leo’s knuckles were white on the steering wheel. The car wobbled again.

“Come on,” Leo muttered.

They were over the lake — the castle was right ahead — Leo put his foot down.

There was a loud clunk, a splutter, and the engine died completely.

“Uh-oh,” said Leo, into the silence.

The nose of the car dropped. They were falling, gathering speed, heading straight for the solid castle wall.

“Noooooo!” Leo yelled, swinging the steering wheel around; they missed the dark stone wall by inches as the car turned in a great arc, soaring over the dark greenhouses, then the vegetable patch, and then out over the black lawns, losing altitude all the time.

Leo let go of the steering wheel completely and pulled his wand out of his back pocket —

“STOP! STOP!” he yelled, whacking the dashboard and the windshield, but they were still plummeting, the ground flying up toward them —

“WATCH OUT FOR THAT TREE!” Eli bellowed, lunging for the steering wheel, but too late —

CRUNCH.

With an earsplitting bang of metal on wood, they hit the thick tree trunk and dropped to the ground with a heavy jolt. Steam was billowing from under the crumpled hood; Hedwig was shrieking in terror; a golf-ball-sized lump was throbbing on Eli’s head where he had hit the windshield; and to his right, Leo let out a low, despairing groan.

“Are you okay?” Eli said urgently.

“My wand,” said Leo, in a shaky voice. “Look at my wand —”

It had snapped, almost in two; the tip was dangling limply, held on by a few splinters.

Eli opened his mouth to say he was sure they’d be able to mend it up at the school, but he never even got started. At that very moment, something hit his side of the car with the force of a charging bull, sending him lurching sideways into Leo, just as an equally heavy blow hit the roof.

“What’s happen — ?”

Leo gasped, staring through the windshield, and Eli looked around just in time to see a branch as thick as a python smash into it. The tree they had hit was attacking them. Its trunk was bent almost double, and its gnarled boughs were pummeling every inch of the car it could reach.

“Aaargh!” said Leo as another twisted limb punched a large dent into his door; the windshield was now trembling under a hail of blows from knuckle-like twigs and a branch as thick as a battering ram was pounding furiously on the roof, which seemed to be caving —

“Run for it!” Leo shouted, throwing his full weight against his door, but next second he had been knocked backward into Eli’s lap by a vicious uppercut from another branch.

“We’re done for!” he moaned as the ceiling sagged, but suddenly the floor of the car was vibrating — the engine had restarted.

“Reverse!” Eli yelled, and the car shot backward; the tree was still trying to hit them; they could hear its roots creaking as it almost ripped itself up, lashing out at them as they sped out of reach.

“That,” panted Leo, “was close. Well done, car —”

The car, however, had reached the end of its tether. With two sharp clunks, the doors flew open and Eli felt his seat tip sideways: Next thing he knew he was sprawled on the damp ground. Loud thuds told him that the car was ejecting their luggage from the trunk; Hedwig’s cage flew through the air and burst open; she rose out of it with an angry screech and sped off toward the castle without a backward look. Then, dented, scratched, and steaming, the car rumbled off into the darkness, its rear lights blazing angrily.

“Come back!” Leo yelled after it, brandishing his broken wand. “Father’ll kill me!”

But the car disappeared from view with one last snort from its exhaust.

“Can you believe our luck?” said Leo miserably, bending down to pick up Scabbers. “Of all the trees we could’ve hit, we had to get one that hits back.”

He glanced over his shoulder at the ancient tree, which was still flailing its branches threateningly.

“Come on,” said Eli wearily, “we’d better get up to the school. …”

It wasn’t at all the triumphant arrival they had pictured. Stiff, cold, and bruised, they seized the ends of their trunks and began dragging them up the grassy slope, toward the great oak front doors.

“I think the feast’s already started,” said Leo, dropping his trunk at the foot of the front steps and crossing quietly to look through a brightly lit window. “Hey — Eli — come and look — it’s the Sorting!”

Eli hurried over and, together, he and Leo peered in at the Great Hall.

Innumerable candles were hovering in midair over four long, crowded tables, making the golden plates and goblets sparkle. Overhead, the bewitched ceiling, which always mirrored the sky outside, sparkled with stars.

Through the forest of pointed black Hogwarts hats, Eli saw a long line of scared-looking first years filing into the Hall. Lila was among them, easily visible because of her vivid Brunswick hair. Meanwhile, Professor Falconer, a bespectacled witch with her hair in a tight bun, was placing the famous Hogwarts Sorting Hat on a stool before the newcomers.

Every year, this aged old hat, patched, frayed, and dirty, sorted new students into the four Hogwarts houses (Gryffindor, Hufflepuff, Ravenclaw, and Slytherin). Eli well remembered putting it on, exactly one year ago, and waiting, petrified, for its decision as it muttered aloud in his ear. For a few horrible seconds he had feared that the hat was going to put him in Slytherin, the House that had turned out more Dark witches and wizards than any other — but he had ended up in Gryffindor, along with Leo, Isla, and the rest of the Brunswicks. Last term, Eli and Leo had helped Gryffindor win the House Championship, beating Slytherin for the first time in seven years.

A very small, mousy-haired boy had been called forward to place the hat on his head. Eli’s eyes wandered past him to where Professor Starling, the headmaster, sat watching the Sorting from the staff table, his long silver beard and half-moon glasses shining brightly in the candlelight. Several seats along, Eli saw Julian Bright, dressed in robes of aquamarine. And there at the end was Griff, huge and hairy, drinking deeply from his goblet.

“Hang on …” Eli muttered to Leo. “There’s an empty chair at the staff table. … Where’s Thorne?”

Professor Severus Thorne was Eli’s least favorite teacher. Eli also happened to be Thorne’s least favorite student. Cruel, sarcastic, and disliked by everybody except the students from his own House (Slytherin), Thorne taught Potions.

“Maybe he’s ill!” said Leo hopefully.

“Maybe he’s left,” said Eli, “because he missed out on the Defense Against the Dark Arts job again!”

“Or he might have been sacked!” said Leo enthusiastically. “I mean, everyone hates him —”

“Or maybe,” said a very cold voice right behind them, “he’s waiting to hear why you two didn’t arrive on the school train.”

Eli spun around. There, his black robes rippling in a cold breeze, stood Severus Thorne. He was a thin man with sallow skin, a hooked nose, and greasy, shoulder-length black hair, and at this moment, he was smiling in a way that told Eli he and Leo were in very deep trouble.

“Follow me,” said Thorne.

Not daring even to look at each other, Eli and Leo followed Thorne up the steps into the vast, echoing entrance hall, which was lit with flaming torches. A delicious smell of food was wafting from the Great Hall, but Thorne led them away from the warmth and light, down a narrow stone staircase that led into the dungeons.

“In!” he said, opening a door halfway down the cold passageway and pointing.

They entered Thorne’s office, shivering. The shadowy walls were lined with shelves of large glass jars, in which floated all manner of revolting things Eli didn’t really want to know the name of at the moment. The fireplace was dark and empty. Thorne closed the door and turned to look at them.

“So,” he said softly, “the train isn’t good enough for the famous Eli Ember and his faithful sidekick, Brunswick. Wanted to arrive with a bang, did we, boys?”

“No, sir, it was the barrier at King’s Cross, it —”

“Silence!” said Thorne coldly. “What have you done with the car?”

Leo gulped. This wasn’t the first time Thorne had given Eli the impression of being able to read minds. But a moment later, he understood, as Thorne unrolled today’s issue of the Evening Prophet.

“You were seen,” he hissed, showing them the headline: FLYING FORD ANGLIA MYSTIFIES MUGGLES. He began to read aloud: “Two Muggles in London, convinced they saw an old car flying over the Post Office tower … at noon in Norfolk, Mrs. Betty Quail, while hanging out her washing … Mr. Fergus Gale, of Peebles, reported to police … Six or seven Muggles in all. I believe your father works in the Misuse of Muggle Artifacts Office?” he said, looking up at Leo and smiling still more nastily. “Dear, dear … his own son …”

Eli felt as though he’d just been walloped in the stomach by one of the mad tree’s larger branches. If anyone found out Mr. Brunswick had bewitched the car … he hadn’t thought of that. …

“I noticed, in my search of the park, that considerable damage seems to have been done to a very valuable Whomping Willow,” Thorne went on.

“That tree did more damage to us than we —” Leo blurted out.

“Silence!” snapped Thorne again. “Most unfortunately, you are not in my House and the decision to expel you does not rest with me. I shall go and fetch the people who do have that happy power. You will wait here.”

Eli and Leo stared at each other, white-faced. Eli didn’t feel hungry anymore. He now felt extremely sick. He tried not to look at a large, slimy something suspended in green liquid on a shelf behind Thorne’s desk. If Thorne had gone to fetch Professor Falconer, head of Gryffindor House, they were hardly any better off. She might be fairer than Thorne, but she was still extremely strict.

Ten minutes later, Thorne returned, and sure enough it was Professor Falconer who accompanied him. Eli had seen Professor Falconer angry on several occasions, but either he had forgotten just how thin her mouth could go, or he had never seen her this angry before. She raised her wand the moment she entered; Eli and Leo both flinched, but she merely pointed it at the empty fireplace, where flames suddenly erupted.

“Sit,” she said, and they both backed into chairs by the fire.

“Explain,” she said, her glasses glinting ominously.

Leo launched into the story, starting with the barrier at the station refusing to let them through.

“— so we had no choice, Professor, we couldn’t get on the train.”

“Why didn’t you send us a letter by owl? I believe you have an owl?” Professor Falconer said coldly to Eli.

Eli gaped at her. Now she’d said it, that seemed the obvious thing to have done.

“I — I didn’t think —”

“That,” said Professor Falconer, “is obvious.”

There was a knock on the office door and Thorne, now looking happier than ever, opened it. There stood the headmaster, Professor Starling.

Eli’s whole body went numb. Starling was looking unusually grave. He stared down his very crooked nose at them, and Eli suddenly found himself wishing he and Leo were still being beaten up by the Whomping Willow.

There was a long silence. Then Starling said, “Please explain why you did this.”

It would have been better if he had shouted. Eli hated the disappointment in his voice. For some reason, he was unable to look Starling in the eyes, and spoke instead to his knees. He told Starling everything except that Mr. Brunswick owned the bewitched car, making it sound as though he and Leo had happened to find a flying car parked outside the station. He knew Starling would see through this at once, but Starling asked no questions about the car. When Eli had finished, he merely continued to peer at them through his spectacles.

“We’ll go and get our stuff,” said Leo in a hopeless sort of voice.

“What are you talking about, Brunswick?” barked Professor Falconer.

“Well, you’re expelling us, aren’t you?” said Leo.

Eli looked quickly at Starling.

“Not today, Mr. Brunswick,” said Starling. “But I must impress upon both of you the seriousness of what you have done. I will be writing to both your families tonight. I must also warn you that if you do anything like this again, I will have no choice but to expel you.”

Thorne looked as though Christmas had been canceled. He cleared his throat and said, “Professor Starling, these boys have flouted the Decree for the Restriction of Underage Wizardry, caused serious damage to an old and valuable tree — surely acts of this nature —”

“It will be for Professor Falconer to decide on these boys’ punishments, Severus,” said Starling calmly. “They are in her House and are therefore her responsibility.” He turned to Professor Falconer. “I must go back to the feast, Helena, I’ve got to give out a few notices. Come, Severus, there’s a delicious-looking custard tart I want to sample —”

Thorne shot a look of pure venom at Eli and Leo as he allowed himself to be swept out of his office, leaving them alone with Professor Falconer, who was still eyeing them like a wrathful eagle.

“You’d better get along to the hospital wing, Brunswick, you’re bleeding.”

“Not much,” said Leo, hastily wiping the cut over his eye with his sleeve. “Professor, I wanted to watch my sister being Sorted —”

“The Sorting Ceremony is over,” said Professor Falconer. “Your sister is also in Gryffindor.”

“Oh, good,” said Leo.

“And speaking of Gryffindor —” Professor Falconer said sharply, but Eli cut in: “Professor, when we took the car, term hadn’t started, so — so Gryffindor shouldn’t really have points taken from it — should it?” he finished, watching her anxiously.

Professor Falconer gave him a piercing look, but he was sure she had almost smiled. Her mouth looked less thin, anyway.

“I will not take any points from Gryffindor,” she said, and Eli’s heart lightened considerably. “But you will both get a detention.”

It was better than Eli had expected. As for Starling’s writing to the Powells, that was nothing. Eli knew perfectly well they’d just be disappointed that the Whomping Willow hadn’t squashed him flat.

Professor Falconer raised her wand again and pointed it at Thorne’s desk. A large plate of sandwiches, two silver goblets, and a jug of iced pumpkin juice appeared with a pop.

“You will eat in here and then go straight up to your dormitory,” she said. “I must also return to the feast.”

When the door had closed behind her, Leo let out a long, low whistle.

“I thought we’d had it,” he said, grabbing a sandwich.

“So did I,” said Eli, taking one, too.

“Can you believe our luck, though?” said Leo thickly through a mouthful of chicken and ham. “Felix and Aiden must’ve flown that car five or six times and no Muggle ever saw them.” He swallowed and took another huge bite. “Why couldn’t we get through the barrier?”

Eli shrugged. “We’ll have to watch our step from now on, though,” he said, taking a grateful swig of pumpkin juice. “Wish we could’ve gone up to the feast. …”

“She didn’t want us showing off,” said Leo sagely. “Doesn’t want people to think it’s clever, arriving by flying car.”

When they had eaten as many sandwiches as they could (the plate kept refilling itself), they rose and left the office, treading the familiar path to Gryffindor Tower. The castle was quiet; it seemed that the feast was over. They walked past muttering portraits and creaking suits of armor, and climbed narrow flights of stone stairs, until at last they reached the passage where the secret entrance to Gryffindor Tower was hidden, behind an oil painting of a very fat woman in a pink silk dress.

“Password?” she said as they approached.

“Er —” said Eli.

They didn’t know the new year’s password, not having met a Gryffindor prefect yet, but help came almost immediately; they heard hurrying feet behind them and turned to see Isla dashing toward them.

“There you are! Where have you been? The most ridiculous rumors — someone said you’d been expelled for crashing a flying car —”

“Well, we haven’t been expelled,” Eli assured her.

“You’re not telling me you did fly here?” said Isla, sounding almost as severe as Professor Falconer.

“Skip the lecture,” said Leo impatiently, “and tell us the new password.”

“It’s ‘wattlebird,’ ” said Isla impatiently, “but that’s not the point —”

Her words were cut short, however, as the portrait of the fat lady swung open and there was a sudden storm of clapping. It looked as though the whole of Gryffindor House was still awake, packed into the circular common room, standing on the lopsided tables and squashy armchairs, waiting for them to arrive. Arms reached through the portrait hole to pull Eli and Leo inside, leaving Isla to scramble in after them.

“Brilliant!” yelled Jordan Lee. “Inspired! What an entrance! Flying a car right into the Whomping Willow, people’ll be talking about that one for years —”

“Good for you,” said a fifth year Eli had never spoken to; someone was patting him on the back as though he’d just won a marathon; Felix and Aiden pushed their way to the front of the crowd and said together, “Why couldn’t we’ve come in the car, eh?” Leo was scarlet in the face, grinning embarrassedly, but Eli could see one person who didn’t look happy at all. Evan was visible over the heads of some excited first years, and he seemed to be trying to get near enough to start telling them off. Eli nudged Leo in the ribs and nodded in Evan’s direction. Leo got the point at once.

“Got to get upstairs — bit tired,” he said, and the two of them started pushing their way toward the door on the other side of the room, which led to a spiral staircase and the dormitories.

“ ’Night,” Eli called back to Isla, who was wearing a scowl just like Evan’s.

They managed to get to the other side of the common room, still having their backs slapped, and gained the peace of the staircase. They hurried up it, right to the top, and at last reached the door of their old dormitory, which now had a sign on it saying SECOND YEARS. They entered the familiar, circular room, with its five four-posters hung with red velvet and its high, narrow windows. Their trunks had been brought up for them and stood at the ends of their beds.

Leo grinned guiltily at Eli.

“I know I shouldn’t’ve enjoyed that or anything, but —”

The dormitory door flew open and in came the other second year Gryffindor boys, Liam Finnegan, Dean Hunter, and Nolan Broadfoot.

“Unbelievable!” beamed Liam.

“Cool,” said Dean.

“Amazing,” said Nolan, awestruck.

Eli couldn’t help it. He grinned, too.





Chapter 6



Julian Bright





The next day, however, Eli barely grinned once. Things started to go downhill from breakfast in the Great Hall. The four long House tables were laden with tureens of porridge, plates of kippers, mountains of toast, and dishes of eggs and bacon, beneath the enchanted ceiling (today, a dull, cloudy gray). Eli and Leo sat down at the Gryffindor table next to Isla, who had her copy of Voyages with Vampires propped open against a milk jug. There was a slight stiffness in the way she said “ ’Morning,” which told Eli that she was still disapproving of the way they had arrived. Nolan Broadfoot, on the other hand, greeted them cheerfully. Nolan was a round-faced and accident-prone boy with the worst memory of anyone Eli had ever met.

“Mail’s due any minute — I think Gran’s sending a few things I forgot.”

Eli had only just started his porridge when, sure enough, there was a rushing sound overhead and a hundred or so owls streamed in, circling the hall and dropping letters and packages into the chattering crowd. A big, lumpy package bounced off Nolan’s head and, a second later, something large and gray fell into Isla’s jug, spraying them all with milk and feathers.

“Hoot!” said Leo, pulling the bedraggled owl out by the feet. Hoot slumped, unconscious, onto the table, his legs in the air and a damp red envelope in his beak.

“Oh, no —” Leo gasped.

“It’s all right, he’s still alive,” said Isla, prodding Hoot gently with the tip of her finger.

“It’s not that — it’s that.”

Leo was pointing at the red envelope. It looked quite ordinary to Eli, but Leo and Nolan were both looking at it as though they expected it to explode.

“What’s the matter?” said Eli.

“She’s — she’s sent me a Howler,” said Leo faintly.

“You’d better open it, Leo,” said Nolan in a timid whisper. “It’ll be worse if you don’t. My gran sent me one once, and I ignored it and” — he gulped — “it was horrible.”

Eli looked from their petrified faces to the red envelope.

“What’s a Howler?” he said.

But Leo’s whole attention was fixed on the letter, which had begun to smoke at the corners.

“Open it,” Nolan urged. “It’ll all be over in a few minutes —”

Leo stretched out a shaking hand, eased the envelope from Hoot’s beak, and slit it open. Nolan stuffed his fingers in his ears. A split second later, Eli knew why. He thought for a moment it had exploded; a roar of sound filled the huge hall, shaking dust from the ceiling.

“— STEALING THE CAR, I WOULDN’T HAVE BEEN SURPRISED IF THEY’D EXPELLED YOU, YOU WAIT TILL I GET HOLD OF YOU, I DON’T SUPPOSE YOU STOPPED TO THINK WHAT YOUR FATHER AND I WENT THROUGH WHEN WE SAW IT WAS GONE —”

Mrs. Brunswick’s yells, a hundred times louder than usual, made the plates and spoons rattle on the table, and echoed deafeningly off the stone walls. People throughout the hall were swiveling around to see who had received the Howler, and Leo sank so low in his chair that only his crimson forehead could be seen.

“— LETTER FROM DUMBLEDORE LAST NIGHT, I THOUGHT YOUR FATHER WOULD DIE OF SHAME, WE DIDN’T BRING YOU UP TO BEHAVE LIKE THIS, YOU AND HARRY COULD BOTH HAVE DIED —”

Eli had been wondering when his name was going to crop up. He tried very hard to look as though he couldn’t hear the voice that was making his eardrums throb.

“— ABSOLUTELY DISGUSTED — YOUR FATHER’S FACING AN INQUIRY AT WORK, IT’S ENTIRELY YOUR FAULT AND IF YOU PUT ANOTHER TOE OUT OF LINE WE’LL BRING YOU STRAIGHT BACK HOME.”

A ringing silence fell. The red envelope, which had dropped from Leo’s hand, burst into flames and curled into ashes. Eli and Leo sat stunned, as though a tidal wave had just passed over them. A few people laughed and, gradually, a babble of talk broke out again.

Isla closed Voyages with Vampires and looked down at the top of Leo’s head.

“Well, I don’t know what you expected, Leo, but you —”

“Don’t tell me I deserved it,” snapped Leo.

Eli pushed his porridge away. His insides were burning with guilt. Mr. Brunswick was facing an inquiry at work. After all Mr. and Mrs. Brunswick had done for him over the summer …

But he had no time to dwell on this; Professor Falconer was moving along the Gryffindor table, handing out course schedules. Eli took his and saw that they had double Herbology with the Hufflepuffs first.

Eli, Leo, and Isla left the castle together, crossed the vegetable patch, and made for the greenhouses, where the magical plants were kept. At least the Howler had done one good thing: Isla seemed to think they had now been punished enough and was being perfectly friendly again.

As they neared the greenhouses they saw the rest of the class standing outside, waiting for Professor Sage. Eli, Leo, and Isla had only just joined them when she came striding into view across the lawn, accompanied by Julian Bright. Professor Sage’s arms were full of bandages, and with another twinge of guilt, Eli spotted the Whomping Willow in the distance, several of its branches now in slings.

Professor Sage was a squat little witch who wore a patched hat over her flyaway hair; there was usually a large amount of earth on her clothes and her fingernails would have made Aunt Beatrice faint. Julian Bright, however, was immaculate in sweeping robes of turquoise, his golden hair shining under a perfectly positioned turquoise hat with gold trimming.

“Oh, hello there!” he called, beaming around at the assembled students. “Just been showing Professor Sage the right way to doctor a Whomping Willow! But I don’t want you running away with the idea that I’m better at Herbology than she is! I just happen to have met several of these exotic plants on my travels …”

“Greenhouse three today, chaps!” said Professor Sage, who was looking distinctly disgruntled, not at all her usual cheerful self.

There was a murmur of interest. They had only ever worked in greenhouse one before — greenhouse three housed far more interesting and dangerous plants. Professor Sage took a large key from her belt and unlocked the door. Eli caught a whiff of damp earth and fertilizer mingling with the heavy perfume of some giant, umbrella-sized flowers dangling from the ceiling. He was about to follow Leo and Isla inside when Bright’s hand shot out.

“Eli! I’ve been wanting a word — you don’t mind if he’s a couple of minutes late, do you, Professor Sage?”

Judging by Professor Sage’s scowl, she did mind, but Bright said, “That’s the ticket,” and closed the greenhouse door in her face.

“Eli,” said Bright, his large white teeth gleaming in the sunlight as he shook his head. “Eli, Eli, Eli.”

Completely nonplussed, Eli said nothing.

“When I heard — well, of course, it was all my fault. Could have kicked myself.”

Eli had no idea what he was talking about. He was about to say so when Bright went on, “Don’t know when I’ve been more shocked. Flying a car to Hogwarts! Well, of course, I knew at once why you’d done it. Stood out a mile. Eli, Eli, Eli.”

It was remarkable how he could show every one of those brilliant teeth even when he wasn’t talking.

“Gave you a taste for publicity, didn’t I?” said Bright. “Gave you the bug. You got onto the front page of the paper with me and you couldn’t wait to do it again.”

“Oh, no, Professor, see —”

“Eli, Eli, Eli,” said Bright, reaching out and grasping his shoulder. “I understand. Natural to want a bit more once you’ve had that first taste — and I blame myself for giving you that, because it was bound to go to your head — but see here, young man, you can’t start flying cars to try and get yourself noticed. Just calm down, all right? Plenty of time for all that when you’re older. Yes, yes, I know what you’re thinking! ‘It’s all right for him, he’s an internationally famous wizard already!’ But when I was twelve, I was just as much of a nobody as you are now. In fact, I’d say I was even more of a nobody! I mean, a few people have heard of you, haven’t they? All that business with He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named!” He glanced at the lightning scar on Eli’s forehead. “I know, I know — it’s not quite as good as winning Witch Weekly’s Most-Charming-Smile Award five times in a row, as I have — but it’s a start, Eli, it’s a start.”

He gave Eli a hearty wink and strode off. Eli stood stunned for a few seconds, then, remembering he was supposed to be in the greenhouse, he opened the door and slid inside.

Professor Sage was standing behind a trestle bench in the center of the greenhouse. About twenty pairs of different-colored ear-muffs were lying on the bench. When Eli had taken his place between Leo and Isla, she said, “We’ll be repotting Mandrakes today. Now, who can tell me the properties of the Mandrake?”

To nobody’s surprise, Isla’s hand was first into the air.

“Mandrake, or Mandragora, is a powerful restorative,” said Isla, sounding as usual as though she had swallowed the textbook. “It is used to return people who have been transfigured or cursed to their original state.”

“Excellent. Ten points to Gryffindor,” said Professor Sage. “The Mandrake forms an essential part of most antidotes. It is also, however, dangerous. Who can tell me why?”

Isla’s hand narrowly missed Eli’s glasses as it shot up again.

“The cry of the Mandrake is fatal to anyone who hears it,” she said promptly.

“Precisely. Take another ten points,” said Professor Sage. “Now, the Mandrakes we have here are still very young.”

She pointed to a row of deep trays as she spoke, and everyone shuffled forward for a better look. A hundred or so tufty little plants, purplish green in color, were growing there in rows. They looked quite unremarkable to Eli, who didn’t have the slightest idea what Isla meant by the “cry” of the Mandrake.

“Everyone take a pair of earmuffs,” said Professor Sage.

There was a scramble as everyone tried to seize a pair that wasn’t pink and fluffy.

“When I tell you to put them on, make sure your ears are completely covered,” said Professor Sage. “When it is safe to remove them, I will give you the thumbs-up. Right — earmuffs on.”

Eli snapped the earmuffs over his ears. They shut out sound completely. Professor Sage put the pink, fluffy pair over her own ears, rolled up the sleeves of her robes, grasped one of the tufty plants firmly, and pulled hard.

Eli let out a gasp of surprise that no one could hear.

Instead of roots, a small, muddy, and extremely ugly baby popped out of the earth. The leaves were growing right out of his head. He had pale green, mottled skin, and was clearly bawling at the top of his lungs.

Professor Sage took a large plant pot from under the table and plunged the Mandrake into it, burying him in dark, damp compost until only the tufted leaves were visible. Professor Sage dusted off her hands, gave them all the thumbs-up, and removed her own earmuffs.

“As our Mandrakes are only seedlings, their cries won’t kill yet,” she said calmly as though she’d just done nothing more exciting than water a begonia. “However, they will knock you out for several hours, and as I’m sure none of you want to miss your first day back, make sure your earmuffs are securely in place while you work. I will attract your attention when it is time to pack up.

“Four to a tray — there is a large supply of pots here — compost in the sacks over there — and be careful of the Venomous Tentacula, it’s teething.”

She gave a sharp slap to a spiky, dark red plant as she spoke, making it draw in the long feelers that had been inching sneakily over her shoulder.

Eli, Leo, and Isla were joined at their tray by a curly-haired Hufflepuff boy Eli knew by sight but had never spoken to.

“Justin Winge,” he said brightly, shaking Eli by the hand. “Know who you are, of course, the famous Eli Ember. … And you’re Isla Hawke — always top in everything” (Isla beamed as she had her hand shaken too) “— and Leo Brunswick. Wasn’t that your flying car?”

Leo didn’t smile. The Howler was obviously still on his mind.

“That Bright’s something, isn’t he?” said Justin happily as they began filling their plant pots with dragon dung compost. “Awfully brave chap. Have you read his books? I’d have died of fear if I’d been cornered in a telephone booth by a werewolf, but he stayed cool and — zap — just fantastic.

“My name was down for Eton, you know. I can’t tell you how glad I am I came here instead. Of course, Mother was slightly disappointed, but since I made her read Bright’s books I think she’s begun to see how useful it’ll be to have a fully trained wizard in the family. …”

After that they didn’t have much chance to talk. Their earmuffs were back on and they needed to concentrate on the Mandrakes. Professor Sage had made it look extremely easy, but it wasn’t. The Mandrakes didn’t like coming out of the earth, but didn’t seem to want to go back into it either. They squirmed, kicked, flailed their sharp little fists, and gnashed their teeth; Eli spent ten whole minutes trying to squash a particularly fat one into a pot.

By the end of the class, Eli, like everyone else, was sweaty, aching, and covered in earth. Everyone traipsed back to the castle for a quick wash and then the Gryffindors hurried off to Transfiguration.

Professor Falconer’s classes were always hard work, but today was especially difficult. Everything Eli had learned last year seemed to have leaked out of his head during the summer. He was supposed to be turning a beetle into a button, but all he managed to do was give his beetle a lot of exercise as it scuttled over the desktop avoiding his wand.

Leo was having far worse problems. He had patched up his wand with some borrowed Spellotape, but it seemed to be damaged beyond repair. It kept crackling and sparking at odd moments, and every time Leo tried to transfigure his beetle it engulfed him in thick gray smoke that smelled of rotten eggs. Unable to see what he was doing, Leo accidentally squashed his beetle with his elbow and had to ask for a new one. Professor Falconer wasn’t pleased.

Eli was relieved to hear the lunch bell. His brain felt like a wrung sponge. Everyone filed out of the classroom except him and Leo, who was whacking his wand furiously on the desk.

“Stupid — useless — thing —”

“Write home for another one,” Eli suggested as the wand let off a volley of bangs like a firecracker.

“Oh, yeah, and get another Howler back,” said Leo, stuffing the now hissing wand into his bag. “ ‘It’s your own fault your wand got snapped —’ ”

They went down to lunch, where Leo’s mood was not improved by Isla’s showing them the handful of perfect coat buttons she had produced in Transfiguration.

“What’ve we got this afternoon?” said Eli, hastily changing the subject.

“Defense Against the Dark Arts,” said Isla at once.

“Why,” demanded Leo, seizing her schedule, “have you outlined all Bright’s lessons in little hearts?”

Isla snatched the schedule back, blushing furiously.

They finished lunch and went outside into the overcast courtyard. Isla sat down on a stone step and buried her nose in Voyages with Vampires again. Eli and Leo stood talking about Quidditch for several minutes before Eli became aware that he was being closely watched. Looking up, he saw the very small, mousy-haired boy he’d seen trying on the Sorting Hat last night staring at Eli as though transfixed. He was clutching what looked like an ordinary Muggle camera, and the moment Eli looked at him, he went bright red.

“All right, Eli? I’m — I’m Colin Ripple,” he said breathlessly, taking a tentative step forward. “I’m in Gryffindor, too. D’you think — would it be all right if — can I have a picture?” he said, raising the camera hopefully.

“A picture?” Eli repeated blankly.

“So I can prove I’ve met you,” said Colin Ripple eagerly, edging further forward. “I know all about you. Everyone’s told me. About how you survived when That-Which-Is-Unnamed tried to kill you and how he disappeared and everything and how you’ve still got a lightning scar on your forehead” (his eyes raked Eli’s hairline) “and a boy in my dormitory said if I develop the film in the right potion, the pictures’ll move.” Colin drew a great shuddering breath of excitement and said, “It’s amazing here, isn’t it? I never knew all the odd stuff I could do was magic till I got the letter from Hogwarts. My dad’s a milkman, he couldn’t believe it either. So I’m taking loads of pictures to send home to him. And it’d be really good if I had one of you” — he looked imploringly at Eli — “maybe your friend could take it and I could stand next to you? And then, could you sign it?”

“Signed photos? You’re giving out signed photos, Ember?”

Loud and scathing, Jasper Mortem’s voice echoed around the courtyard. He had stopped right behind Colin, flanked, as he always was at Hogwarts, by his large and thuggish cronies, Lobbe and Gore.

“Everyone line up!” Mortem roared to the crowd. “Eli Ember’s giving out signed photos!”

“No, I’m not,” said Eli angrily, his fists clenching. “Shut up, Mortem.”

“You’re just jealous,” piped up Colin, whose entire body was about as thick as Lobbe’s neck.

“Jealous?” said Mortem, who didn’t need to shout anymore: Half the courtyard was listening in. “Of what? I don’t want a foul scar right across my head, thanks. I don’t think getting your head cut open makes you that special, myself.”

Lobbe and Gore were sniggering stupidly.

“Eat slugs, Mortem,” said Leo angrily. Lobbe stopped laughing and started rubbing his knuckles in a menacing way.

“Be careful, Brunswick,” sneered Mortem. “You don’t want to start any trouble or your mommy’ll have to come and take you away from school.” He put on a shrill, piercing voice. “If you put another toe out of line —”

A knot of Slytherin fifth years nearby laughed loudly at this.

“Brunswick would like a signed photo, Ember,” smirked Mortem. “It’d be worth more than his family’s whole house —”

Leo whipped out his Spellotaped wand, but Isla shut Voyages with Vampires with a snap and whispered, “Look out!”

“What’s all this, what’s all this?” Julian Bright was striding toward them, his turquoise robes swirling behind him. “Who’s giving out signed photos?”

Eli started to speak but he was cut short as Bright flung an arm around his shoulders and thundered jovially, “Shouldn’t have asked! We meet again, Eli!”

Pinned to Bright’s side and burning with humiliation, Eli saw Mortem slide smirking back into the crowd.

“Come on then, Mr. Ripple,” said Bright, beaming at Colin. “A double portrait, can’t do better than that, and we’ll both sign it for you.”

Colin fumbled for his camera and took the picture as the bell rang behind them, signaling the start of afternoon classes.

“Off you go, move along there,” Bright called to the crowd, and he set off back to the castle with Eli, who was wishing he knew a good Vanishing Spell, still clasped to his side.

“A word to the wise, Eli,” said Bright paternally as they entered the building through a side door. “I covered up for you back there with young Ripple — if he was photographing me, too, your schoolmates won’t think you’re setting yourself up so much. …”

Deaf to Eli’s stammers, Bright swept him down a corridor lined with staring students and up a staircase.

“Let me just say that handing out signed pictures at this stage of your career isn’t sensible — looks a tad bigheaded, Eli, to be frank. There may well come a time when, like me, you’ll need to keep a stack handy wherever you go, but” — he gave a little chortle — “I don’t think you’re quite there yet.”

They had reached Bright’s classroom and he let Eli go at last. Eli yanked his robes straight and headed for a seat at the very back of the class, where he busied himself with piling all seven of Bright’s books in front of him, so that he could avoid looking at the real thing.

The rest of the class came clattering in, and Leo and Isla sat down on either side of Eli.

“You could’ve fried an egg on your face,” said Leo. “You’d better hope Ripple doesn’t meet Lila, or they’ll be starting a Eli Ember fan club.”

“Shut up,” snapped Eli. The last thing he needed was for Bright to hear the phrase “Eli Ember fan club.”

When the whole class was seated, Bright cleared his throat loudly and silence fell. He reached forward, picked up Nolan Broadfoot’s copy of Travels with Trolls, and held it up to show his own, winking portrait on the front.

“Me,” he said, pointing at it and winking as well. “Julian Bright, Order of Merlin, Third Class, Honorary Member of the Dark Force Defense League, and five-time winner of Witch Weekly’s Most-Charming-Smile Award — but I don’t talk about that. I didn’t get rid of the Bandon Banshee by smiling at her!”

He waited for them to laugh; a few people smiled weakly.

“I see you’ve all bought a complete set of my books — well done. I thought we’d start today with a little quiz. Nothing to worry about — just to check how well you’ve read them, how much you’ve taken in —”

When he had handed out the test papers he returned to the front of the class and said, “You have thirty minutes — start — now!”

Eli looked down at his paper and read:



1. What is Julian Bright’s favorite color?

2. What is Julian Bright’s secret ambition?

3. What, in your opinion, is Julian Bright’s

greatest achievement to date?



On and on it went, over three sides of paper, right down to:



54. When is Julian Bright’s birthday, and what

would his ideal gift be?



Half an hour later, Bright collected the papers and rifled through them in front of the class.

“Tut, tut — hardly any of you remembered that my favorite color is lilac. I say so in Year with the Yeti. And a few of you need to read Wanderings with Werewolves more carefully — I clearly state in chapter twelve that my ideal birthday gift would be harmony between all magic and non-magic peoples — though I wouldn’t say no to a large bottle of Ogden’s Old Firewhisky!”

He gave them another roguish wink. Leo was now staring at Bright with an expression of disbelief on his face; Liam Finnegan and Dean Hunter, who were sitting in front, were shaking with silent laughter. Isla, on the other hand, was listening to Bright with rapt attention and gave a start when he mentioned her name.

“… but Miss Isla Hawke knew my secret ambition is to rid the world of evil and market my own range of hair-care potions — good girl! In fact” — he flipped her paper over — “full marks! Where is Miss Isla Hawke?”

Isla raised a trembling hand.

“Excellent!” beamed Bright. “Quite excellent! Take ten points for Gryffindor! And so — to business —”

He bent down behind his desk and lifted a large, covered cage onto it.

“Now — be warned! It is my job to arm you against the foulest creatures known to wizardkind! You may find yourselves facing your worst fears in this room. Know only that no harm can befall you whilst I am here. All I ask is that you remain calm.”

In spite of himself, Eli leaned around his pile of books for a better look at the cage. Bright placed a hand on the cover. Dean and Liam had stopped laughing now. Nolan was cowering in his front row seat.

“I must ask you not to scream,” said Bright in a low voice. “It might provoke them.”

As the whole class held its breath, Bright whipped off the cover.

“Yes,” he said dramatically. “Freshly caught Cornish pixies.”

Liam Finnegan couldn’t control himself. He let out a snort of laughter that even Bright couldn’t mistake for a scream of terror.

“Yes?” He smiled at Liam.

“Well, they’re not — they’re not very — dangerous, are they?” Liam choked.

“Don’t be so sure!” said Bright, waggling a finger annoyingly at Liam. “Devilish tricky little blighters they can be!”

The pixies were electric blue and about eight inches high, with pointed faces and voices so shrill it was like listening to a lot of budgies arguing. The moment the cover had been removed, they had started jabbering and rocketing around, rattling the bars and making bizarre faces at the people nearest them.

“Right, then,” Bright said loudly. “Let’s see what you make of them!” And he opened the cage.

It was pandemonium. The pixies shot in every direction like rockets. Two of them seized Nolan by the ears and lifted him into the air. Several shot straight through the window, showering the back row with broken glass. The rest proceeded to wreck the classroom more effectively than a rampaging rhino. They grabbed ink bottles and sprayed the class with them, shredded books and papers, tore pictures from the walls, up-ended the waste basket, grabbed bags and books and threw them out of the smashed window; within minutes, half the class was sheltering under desks and Nolan was swinging from the iron chandelier in the ceiling.

“Come on now — round them up, round them up, they’re only pixies,” Bright shouted.

He rolled up his sleeves, brandished his wand, and bellowed, “Peskipiksi Pesternomi!”

It had absolutely no effect; one of the pixies seized his wand and threw it out of the window, too. Bright gulped and dived under his own desk, narrowly avoiding being squashed by Nolan, who fell a second later as the chandelier gave way.

The bell rang and there was a mad rush toward the exit. In the relative calm that followed, Bright straightened up, caught sight of Eli, Leo, and Isla, who were almost at the door, and said, “Well, I’ll ask you three to just nip the rest of them back into their cage.” He swept past them and shut the door quickly behind him.

“Can you believe him?” roared Leo as one of the remaining pixies bit him painfully on the ear.

“He just wants to give us some hands-on experience,” said Isla, immobilizing two pixies at once with a clever Freezing Charm and stuffing them back into their cage.

“Hands on?” said Eli, who was trying to grab a pixie dancing out of reach with its tongue out. “Isla, he didn’t have a clue what he was doing —”

“Rubbish,” said Isla. “You’ve read his books — look at all those amazing things he’s done —”

“He says he’s done,” Leo muttered.





Chapter 7



Mudbloods and Murmurs





Eli spent a lot of time over the next few days dodging out of sight whenever he saw Julian Bright coming down a corridor. Harder to avoid was Colin Ripple, who seemed to have memorized Eli’s schedule. Nothing seemed to give Colin a bigger thrill than to say, “All right, Eli?” six or seven times a day and hear, “Hello, Colin,” back, however exasperated Eli sounded when he said it.

Hedwig was still angry with Eli about the disasterous car journey and Leo’s wand was still malfunctioning, surpassing itself on Friday morning by shooting out of Leo’s hand in Charms and hitting tiny old Professor Twig squarely between the eyes, creating a large, throbbing green boil where it had struck. So with one thing and another, Eli was quite glad to reach the weekend. He, Leo, and Isla were planning to visit Griff on Saturday morning. Eli, however, was shaken awake several hours earlier than he would have liked by Dorian Ash, Captain of the Gryffindor Quidditch team.

“Whassamatter?” said Eli groggily.

“Quidditch practice!” said Ash. “Come on!”

Eli squinted at the window. There was a thin mist hanging across the pink-and-gold sky. Now that he was awake, he couldn’t understand how he could have slept through the racket the birds were making.

“Dorian,” Eli croaked. “It’s the crack of dawn.”

“Exactly,” said Ash. He was a tall and burly sixth year and, at the moment, his eyes were gleaming with a crazed enthusiasm. “It’s part of our new training program. Come on, grab your broom, and let’s go,” said Ash heartily. “None of the other teams have started training yet; we’re going to be first off the mark this year —”

Yawning and shivering slightly, Eli climbed out of bed and tried to find his Quidditch robes.

“Good man,” said Ash. “Meet you on the field in fifteen minutes.”

When he’d found his scarlet team robes and pulled on his cloak for warmth, Eli scribbled a note to Leo explaining where he’d gone and went down the spiral staircase to the common room, his Nimbus Two Thousand on his shoulder. He had just reached the portrait hole when there was a clatter behind him and Colin Ripple came dashing down the spiral staircase, his camera swinging madly around his neck and something clutched in his hand.

“I heard someone saying your name on the stairs, Eli! Look what I’ve got here! I’ve had it developed, I wanted to show you —”

Eli looked bemusedly at the photograph Colin was brandishing under his nose.

A moving, black-and-white Bright was tugging hard on an arm Eli recognized as his own. He was pleased to see that his photographic self was putting up a good fight and refusing to be dragged into view. As Eli watched, Bright gave up and slumped, panting, against the white edge of the picture.

“Will you sign it?” said Colin eagerly.

“No,” said Eli flatly, glancing around to check that the room was really deserted. “Sorry, Colin, I’m in a hurry — Quidditch practice —”

He climbed through the portrait hole.

“Oh, wow! Wait for me! I’ve never watched a Quidditch game before!”

Colin scrambled through the hole after him.

“It’ll be really boring,” Eli said quickly, but Colin ignored him, his face shining with excitement.

“You were the youngest House player in a hundred years, weren’t you, Eli? Weren’t you?” said Colin, trotting alongside him. “You must be brilliant. I’ve never flown. Is it easy? Is that your own broom? Is that the best one there is?”

Eli didn’t know how to get rid of him. It was like having an extremely talkative shadow.

“I don’t really understand Quidditch,” said Colin breathlessly. “Is it true there are four balls? And two of them fly around trying to knock people off their brooms?”

“Yes,” said Eli heavily, resigned to explaining the complicated rules of Quidditch. “They’re called Bludgers. There are two Beaters on each team who carry clubs to beat the Bludgers away from their side. Felix and Aiden Brunswick are the Gryffindor Beaters.”

“And what are the other balls for?” Colin asked, tripping down a couple of steps because he was gazing open-mouthed at Eli.

“Well, the Quaffle — that’s the biggish red one — is the one that scores goals. Three Chasers on each team throw the Quaffle to each other and try and get it through the goal posts at the end of the pitch — they’re three long poles with hoops on the end.”

“And the fourth ball —”

“— is the Golden Snitch,” said Eli, “and it’s very small, very fast, and difficult to catch. But that’s what the Seeker’s got to do, because a game of Quidditch doesn’t end until the Snitch has been caught. And whichever team’s Seeker gets the Snitch earns his team an extra hundred and fifty points.”

“And you’re the Gryffindor Seeker, aren’t you?” said Colin in awe.

“Yes,” said Eli as they left the castle and started across the dew-drenched grass. “And there’s the Keeper, too. He guards the goal posts. That’s it, really.”

But Colin didn’t stop questioning Eli all the way down the sloping lawns to the Quidditch field, and Eli only shook him off when he reached the changing rooms; Colin called after him in a piping voice, “I’ll go and get a good seat, Eli!” and hurried off to the stands.

The rest of the Gryffindor team were already in the changing room. Ash was the only person who looked truly awake. Felix and Aiden Brunswick were sitting, puffy-eyed and tousle-haired, next to fourth year Alicia Windy, who seemed to be nodding off against the wall behind her. Her fellow Chasers, Katie Starr and Angelica Monroe, were yawning side by side opposite them.

“There you are, Eli, what kept you?” said Ash briskly. “Now, I wanted a quick talk with you all before we actually get onto the field, because I spent the summer devising a whole new training program, which I really think will make all the difference. …”

Ash was holding up a large diagram of a Quidditch field, on which were drawn many lines, arrows, and crosses in different-colored inks. He took out his wand, tapped the board, and the arrows began to wiggle over the diagram like caterpillars. As Ash launched into a speech about his new tactics, Felix Brunswick’s head drooped right onto Alicia Windy’s shoulder and he began to snore.

The first board took nearly twenty minutes to explain, but there was another board under that, and a third under that one. Eli sank into a stupor as Ash droned on and on.

“So,” said Ash, at long last, jerking Eli from a wistful fantasy about what he could be eating for breakfast at this very moment up at the castle. “Is that clear? Any questions?”

“I’ve got a question, Dorian,” said Aiden, who had woken with a start. “Why couldn’t you have told us all this yesterday when we were awake?”

Ash wasn’t pleased.

“Now, listen here, you lot,” he said, glowering at them all. “We should have won the Quidditch Cup last year. We’re easily the best team. But unfortunately — owing to circumstances beyond our control —”

Eli shifted guiltily in his seat. He had been unconscious in the hospital wing for the final match of the previous year, meaning that Gryffindor had been a player short and had suffered their worst defeat in three hundred years.

Ash took a moment to regain control of himself. Their last defeat was clearly still torturing him.

“So this year, we train harder than ever before. … Okay, let’s go and put our new theories into practice!” Ash shouted, seizing his broomstick and leading the way out of the locker rooms. Stiff-legged and still yawning, his team followed.

They had been in the locker room so long that the sun was up completely now, although remnants of mist hung over the grass in the stadium. As Eli walked onto the field, he saw Leo and Isla sitting in the stands.

“Aren’t you finished yet?” called Leo incredulously.

“Haven’t even started,” said Eli, looking jealously at the toast and marmalade Leo and Isla had brought out of the Great Hall. “Ash’s been teaching us new moves.”

He mounted his broomstick and kicked at the ground, soaring up into the air. The cool morning air whipped his face, waking him far more effectively than Ash’s long talk. It felt wonderful to be back on the Quidditch field. He soared right around the stadium at full speed, racing Felix and Aiden.

“What’s that funny clicking noise?” called Felix as they hurtled around the corner.

Eli looked into the stands. Colin was sitting in one of the highest seats, his camera raised, taking picture after picture, the sound strangely magnified in the deserted stadium.

“Look this way, Eli! This way!” he cried shrilly.

“Who’s that?” said Felix.

“No idea,” Eli lied, putting on a spurt of speed that took him as far away as possible from Colin.

“What’s going on?” said Ash, frowning, as he skimmed through the air toward them. “Why’s that first year taking pictures? I don’t like it. He could be a Slytherin spy, trying to find out about our new training program.”

“He’s in Gryffindor,” said Eli quickly.

“And the Slytherins don’t need a spy, Dorian,” said Aiden.

“What makes you say that?” said Ash testily.

“Because they’re here in person,” said Aiden, pointing.

Several people in green robes were walking onto the field, broomsticks in their hands.

“I don’t believe it!” Ash hissed in outrage. “I booked the field for today! We’ll see about this!”

Ash shot toward the ground, landing rather harder than he meant to in his anger, staggering slightly as he dismounted. Eli, Felix, and Aiden followed.

“Flint!” Ash bellowed at the Slytherin Captain. “This is our practice time! We got up specially! You can clear off now!”

Marcus Flint was even larger than Ash. He had a look of trollish cunning on his face as he replied, “Plenty of room for all of us, Ash.”

Angelica, Alicia, and Katie had come over, too. There were no girls on the Slytherin team, who stood shoulder to shoulder, facing the Gryffindors, leering to a man.

“But I booked the field!” said Ash, positively spitting with rage. “I booked it!”

“Ah,” said Flint. “But I’ve got a specially signed note here from Professor Thorne. ‘I, Professor S. Thorne, give the Slytherin team permission to practice today on the Quidditch field owing to the need to train their new Seeker.’ ”

“You’ve got a new Seeker?” said Ash, distracted. “Where?”

And from behind the six large figures before them came a seventh, smaller boy, smirking all over his pale, pointed face. It was Jasper Mortem.

“Aren’t you Caspian Mortem’s son?” said Felix, looking at Mortem with dislike.

“Funny you should mention Jasper’s father,” said Flint as the whole Slytherin team smiled still more broadly. “Let me show you the generous gift he’s made to the Slytherin team.”

All seven of them held out their broomsticks. Seven highly polished, brand-new handles and seven sets of fine gold lettering spelling the words Nimbus Two Thousand and One gleamed under the Gryffindors’ noses in the early morning sun.

“Very latest model. Only came out last month,” said Flint carelessly, flicking a speck of dust from the end of his own. “I believe it outstrips the old Two Thousand series by a considerable amount. As for the old Cleansweeps” — he smiled nastily at Felix and Aiden, who were both clutching Cleansweep Fives — “sweeps the board with them.”

None of the Gryffindor team could think of anything to say for a moment. Mortem was smirking so broadly his cold eyes were reduced to slits.

“Oh, look,” said Flint. “A field invasion.”

Leo and Isla were crossing the grass to see what was going on.

“What’s happening?” Leo asked Eli. “Why aren’t you playing? And what’s he doing here?”

He was looking at Mortem, taking in his Slytherin Quidditch robes.

“I’m the new Slytherin Seeker, Brunswick,” said Mortem, smugly. “Everyone’s just been admiring the brooms my father’s bought our team.”

Leo gaped, openmouthed, at the seven superb broomsticks in front of him.

“Good, aren’t they?” said Mortem smoothly. “But perhaps the Gryffindor team will be able to raise some gold and get new brooms, too. You could raffle off those Cleansweep Fives; I expect a museum would bid for them.”

The Slytherin team howled with laughter.

“At least no one on the Gryffindor team had to buy their way in,” said Isla sharply. “They got in on pure talent.”

The smug look on Mortem’s face flickered.

“No one asked your opinion, you filthy little Mudblood,” he spat.

Eli knew at once that Mortem had said something really bad because there was an instant uproar at his words. Flint had to dive in front of Mortem to stop Felix and Aiden jumping on him, Alicia shrieked, “How dare you!”, and Leo plunged his hand into his robes, pulled out his wand, yelling, “You’ll pay for that one, Mortem!” and pointed it furiously under Flint’s arm at Mortem’s face.

A loud bang echoed around the stadium and a jet of green light shot out of the wrong end of Leo’s wand, hitting him in the stomach and sending him reeling backward onto the grass.

“Leo! Leo! Are you all right?” squealed Isla.

Leo opened his mouth to speak, but no words came out. Instead he gave an almighty belch and several slugs dribbled out of his mouth onto his lap.

The Slytherin team were paralyzed with laughter. Flint was doubled up, hanging onto his new broomstick for support. Mortem was on all fours, banging the ground with his fist. The Gryffindors were gathered around Leo, who kept belching large, glistening slugs. Nobody seemed to want to touch him.

“We’d better get him to Griff’s, it’s nearest,” said Eli to Isla, who nodded bravely, and the pair of them pulled Leo up by the arms.

“What happened, Eli? What happened? Is he ill? But you can cure him, can’t you?” Colin had run down from his seat and was now dancing alongside them as they left the field. Leo gave a huge heave and more slugs dribbled down his front.

“Oooh,” said Colin, fascinated and raising his camera. “Can you hold him still, Eli?”

“Get out of the way, Colin!” said Eli angrily. He and Isla supported Leo out of the stadium and across the grounds toward the edge of the forest.

“Nearly there, Leo,” said Isla as the gamekeeper’s cabin came into view. “You’ll be all right in a minute — almost there —”

They were within twenty feet of Griff’s house when the front door opened, but it wasn’t Griff who emerged. Julian Bright, wearing robes of palest mauve today, came striding out.
