“Quick, behind here,” Eli hissed, dragging Leo behind a nearby bush. Isla followed, somewhat reluctantly.

“It’s a simple matter if you know what you’re doing!” Bright was saying loudly to Griff. “If you need help, you know where I am! I’ll let you have a copy of my book. I’m surprised you haven’t already got one — I’ll sign one tonight and send it over. Well, good-bye!” And he strode away toward the castle.

Eli waited until Bright was out of sight, then pulled Leo out of the bush and up to Griff’s front door. They knocked urgently.

Griff appeared at once, looking very grumpy, but his expression brightened when he saw who it was.

“Bin wonderin’ when you’d come ter see me — come in, come in — thought you mighta bin Professor Bright back again —”

Eli and Isla supported Leo over the threshold into the one-roomed cabin, which had an enormous bed in one corner, a fire crackling merrily in the other. Griff didn’t seem perturbed by Leo’s slug problem, which Eli hastily explained as he lowered Leo into a chair.

“Better out than in,” he said cheerfully, plunking a large copper basin in front of him. “Get ’em all up, Leo.”

“I don’t think there’s anything to do except wait for it to stop,” said Isla anxiously, watching Leo bend over the basin. “That’s a difficult curse to work at the best of times, but with a broken wand —”

Griff was bustling around making them tea. His boarhound, Fang, was slobbering over Eli.

“What did Bright want with you, Griff?” Eli asked, scratching Fang’s ears.

“Givin’ me advice on gettin’ kelpies out of a well,” growled Griff, moving a half-plucked rooster off his scrubbed table and setting down the teapot. “Like I don’ know. An’ bangin’ on about some banshee he banished. If one word of it was true, I’ll eat my kettle.”

It was most unlike Griff to criticize a Hogwarts teacher, and Eli looked at him in surprise. Isla, however, said in a voice somewhat higher than usual, “I think you’re being a bit unfair. Professor Starling obviously thought he was the best man for the job —”

“He was the on’y man for the job,” said Griff, offering them a plate of treacle toffee, while Leo coughed squelchily into his basin. “An’ I mean the on’y one. Gettin’ very difficult ter find anyone fer the Dark Arts job. People aren’t too keen ter take it on, see. They’re startin’ ter think it’s jinxed. No one’s lasted long fer a while now. So tell me,” said Griff, jerking his head at Leo. “Who was he tryin’ ter curse?”

“Mortem called Isla something — it must’ve been really bad, because everyone went wild.”

“It was bad,” said Leo hoarsely, emerging over the tabletop looking pale and sweaty. “Mortem called her ‘Mudblood,’ Griff —”

Leo dived out of sight again as a fresh wave of slugs made their appearance. Griff looked outraged.

“He didn’!” he growled at Isla.

“He did,” she said. “But I don’t know what it means. I could tell it was really rude, of course —”

“It’s about the most insulting thing he could think of,” gasped Leo, coming back up. “Mudblood’s a really foul name for someone who is Muggle-born — you know, non-magic parents. There are some wizards — like Mortem’s family — who think they’re better than everyone else because they’re what people call pure-blood.” He gave a small burp, and a single slug fell into his outstretched hand. He threw it into the basin and continued, “I mean, the rest of us know it doesn’t make any difference at all. Look at Nolan Broadfoot — he’s pure-blood and he can hardly stand a cauldron the right way up.”

“An’ they haven’t invented a spell our Isla can’ do,” said Griff proudly, making Isla go a brilliant shade of magenta.

“It’s a disgusting thing to call someone,” said Leo, wiping his sweaty brow with a shaking hand. “Dirty blood, see. Common blood. It’s ridiculous. Most wizards these days are half-blood anyway. If we hadn’t married Muggles we’d’ve died out.”

He retched and ducked out of sight again.

“Well, I don’ blame yeh fer tryin’ ter curse him, Leo,” said Griff loudly over the thuds of more slugs hitting the basin. “Bu’ maybe it was a good thing yer wand backfired. ’Spect Caspian Mortem would’ve come marchin’ up ter school if yeh’d cursed his son. Least yer not in trouble.”

Eli would have pointed out that trouble didn’t come much worse than having slugs pouring out of your mouth, but he couldn’t; Griff’s treacle toffee had cemented his jaws together.

“Eli,” said Griff abruptly as though struck by a sudden thought. “Gotta bone ter pick with yeh. I’ve heard you’ve bin givin’ out signed photos. How come I haven’t got one?”

Furious, Eli wrenched his teeth apart.

“I have not been giving out signed photos,” he said hotly. “If Bright’s still spreading that around —”

But then he saw that Griff was laughing.

“I’m on’y jokin’,” he said, patting Eli genially on the back and sending him face first into the table. “I knew yeh hadn’t really. I told Bright yeh didn’ need teh. Yer more famous than him without tryin’.”

“Bet he didn’t like that,” said Eli, sitting up and rubbing his chin.

“Don’ think he did,” said Griff, his eyes twinkling. “An’ then I told him I’d never read one o’ his books an’ he decided ter go. Treacle toffee, Leo?” he added as Leo reappeared.

“No thanks,” said Leo weakly. “Better not risk it.”

“Come an’ see what I’ve bin growin’,” said Griff as Eli and Isla finished the last of their tea.

In the small vegetable patch behind Griff’s house were a dozen of the largest pumpkins Eli had ever seen. Each was the size of a large boulder.

“Gettin’ on well, aren’t they?” said Griff happily. “Fer the Halloween feast … should be big enough by then.”

“What’ve you been feeding them?” said Eli.

Griff looked over his shoulder to check that they were alone.

“Well, I’ve bin givin’ them — you know — a bit o’ help —”

Eli noticed Griff’s flowery pink umbrella leaning against the back wall of the cabin. Eli had had reason to believe before now that this umbrella was not all it looked; in fact, he had the strong impression that Griff’s old school wand was concealed inside it. Griff wasn’t supposed to use magic. He had been expelled from Hogwarts in his third year, but Eli had never found out why — any mention of the matter and Griff would clear his throat loudly and become mysteriously deaf until the subject was changed.

“An Engorgement Charm, I suppose?” said Isla, halfway between disapproval and amusement. “Well, you’ve done a good job on them.”

“That’s what yer little sister said,” said Griff, nodding at Leo. “Met her jus’ yesterday.” Griff looked sideways at Eli, his beard twitching. “Said she was jus’ lookin’ round the grounds, but I reckon she was hopin’ she might run inter someone else at my house.” He winked at Eli. “If yeh ask me, she wouldn’ say no ter a signed —”

“Oh, shut up,” said Eli. Leo snorted with laughter and the ground was sprayed with slugs.

“Watch it!” Griff roared, pulling Leo away from his precious pumpkins.

It was nearly lunchtime and as Eli had only had one bit of treacle toffee since dawn, he was keen to go back to school to eat. They said good-bye to Griff and walked back up to the castle, Leo hiccoughing occasionally, but only bringing up two very small slugs.

They had barely set foot in the cool entrance hall when a voice rang out, “There you are, Ember — Brunswick.” Professor Falconer was walking toward them, looking stern. “You will both do your detentions this evening.”

“What’re we doing, Professor?” said Leo, nervously suppressing a burp.

“You will be polishing the silver in the trophy room with Mr. Acheron,” said Professor Falconer. “And no magic, Brunswick — elbow grease.”

Leo gulped. Argus Acheron, the caretaker, was loathed by every student in the school.

“And you, Ember, will be helping Professor Bright answer his fan mail,” said Professor Falconer.

“Oh n — Professor, can’t I go and do the trophy room, too?” said Eli desperately.

“Certainly not,” said Professor Falconer, raising her eyebrows. “Professor Bright requested you particularly. Eight o’clock sharp, both of you.”

Eli and Leo slouched into the Great Hall in states of deepest gloom, Isla behind them, wearing a well-you-did-break-school-rules sort of expression. Eli didn’t enjoy his shepherd’s pie as much as he’d thought. Both he and Leo felt they’d got the worse deal.

“Acheron’ll have me there all night,” said Leo heavily. “No magic! There must be about a hundred cups in that room. I’m no good at Muggle cleaning.”

“I’d swap anytime,” said Eli hollowly. “I’ve had loads of practice with the Powells. Answering Bright’s fan mail … he’ll be a nightmare. …”

Saturday afternoon seemed to melt away, and in what seemed like no time, it was five minutes to eight, and Eli was dragging his feet along the second-floor corridor to Bright’s office. He gritted his teeth and knocked.

The door flew open at once. Bright beamed down at him.

“Ah, here’s the scalawag!” he said. “Come in, Eli, come in —”

Shining brightly on the walls by the light of many candles were countless framed photographs of Bright. He had even signed a few of them. Another large pile lay on his desk.

“You can address the envelopes!” Bright told Eli, as though this was a huge treat. “This first one’s to Gladys Bumble, bless her — huge fan of mine —”

The minutes snailed by. Eli let Bright’s voice wash over him, occasionally saying, “Mmm” and “Right” and “Yeah.” Now and then he caught a phrase like, “Fame’s a fickle friend, Eli,” or “Celebrity is as celebrity does, remember that.”

The candles burned lower and lower, making the light dance over the many moving faces of Bright watching him. Eli moved his aching hand over what felt like the thousandth envelope, writing out Veronica Birch’s address. It must be nearly time to leave, Eli thought miserably, please let it be nearly time. …

And then he heard something — something quite apart from the spitting of the dying candles and Bright’s prattle about his fans.

It was a voice, a voice to chill the bone marrow, a voice of breathtaking, ice-cold venom.

“Come … come to me. … Let me rip you. … Let me tear you. … Let me kill you. …”

Eli gave a huge jump and a large lilac blot appeared on Veronica Birch’s street.

“What?” he said loudly.

“I know!” said Bright. “Six solid months at the top of the best-seller list! Broke all records!”

“No,” said Eli frantically. “That voice!”

“Sorry?” said Bright, looking puzzled. “What voice?”

“That — that voice that said — didn’t you hear it?”

Bright was looking at Eli in high astonishment.

“What are you talking about, Eli? Perhaps you’re getting a little drowsy? Great Scott — look at the time! “We’ve been here nearly four hours! I’d never have believed it — the time’s flown, hasn’t it?”

Eli didn’t answer. He was straining his ears to hear the voice again, but there was no sound now except for Bright telling him he mustn’t expect a treat like this every time he got detention. Feeling dazed, Eli left.

It was so late that the Gryffindor common room was almost empty. Eli went straight up to the dormitory. Leo wasn’t back yet. Eli pulled on his pajamas, got into bed, and waited. Half an hour later, Leo arrived, nursing his right arm and bringing a strong smell of polish into the darkened room.

“My muscles have all seized up,” he groaned, sinking on his bed. “Fourteen times he made me buff up that Quidditch Cup before he was satisfied. And then I had another slug attack all over a Special Award for Services to the School. Took ages to get the slime off. … How was it with Bright?”

Keeping his voice low so as not to wake Nolan, Dean, and Liam, Eli told Leo exactly what he had heard.

“And Bright said he couldn’t hear it?” said Leo. Eli could see him frowning in the moonlight. “D’you think he was lying? But I don’t get it — even someone invisible would’ve had to open the door.”

“I know,” said Eli, lying back in his four-poster and staring at the canopy above him. “I don’t get it either.”





Chapter 8



The Deathday Party





October arrived, spreading a damp chill over the grounds and into the castle. Madam Fern, the nurse, was kept busy by a sudden spate of colds among the staff and students. Her Pepperup Potion worked instantly, though it left the drinker smoking at the ears for several hours afterward. Lila Brunswick, who had been looking pale, was bullied into taking some by Evan. The steam pouring from under her vivid hair gave the impression that her whole head was on fire.

Raindrops the size of bullets thundered on the castle windows for days on end; the lake rose, the flower beds turned into muddy streams, and Griff’s pumpkins swelled to the size of garden sheds. Dorian Ash’s enthusiasm for regular training sessions, however, was not dampened, which was why Eli was to be found, late one stormy Saturday afternoon a few days before Halloween, returning to Gryffindor Tower, drenched to the skin and splattered with mud. Even aside from the rain and wind it hadn’t been a happy practice session. Felix and Aiden, who had been spying on the Slytherin team, had seen for themselves the speed of those new Nimbus Two Thousand and Ones. They reported that the Slytherin team was no more than seven greenish blurs, shooting through the air like missiles.

As Eli squelched along the deserted corridor he came across somebody who looked just as preoccupied as he was. Barely Headless Nate, the ghost of Gryffindor Tower, was staring morosely out of a window, muttering under his breath, “… don’t fulfill their requirements … half an inch, if that …”

“Hello, Nick,” said Eli.

“Hello, hello,” said Barely Headless Nate, starting and looking round. He wore a dashing, plumed hat on his long curly hair, and a tunic with a ruff, which concealed the fact that his neck was almost completely severed. He was pale as smoke, and Eli could see right through him to the dark sky and torrential rain outside.

“You look troubled, young Ember,” said Nick, folding a transparent letter as he spoke and tucking it inside his doublet.

“So do you,” said Eli.

“Ah,” Barely Headless Nate waved an elegant hand, “a matter of no importance. … It’s not as though I really wanted to join. … Thought I’d apply, but apparently I ‘don’t fulfill requirements’ —”

In spite of his airy tone, there was a look of great bitterness on his face.

“But you would think, wouldn’t you,” he erupted suddenly, pulling the letter back out of his pocket, “that getting hit forty-five times in the neck with a blunt axe would qualify you to join the Headless Hunt?”

“Oh — yes,” said Eli, who was obviously supposed to agree.

“I mean, nobody wishes more than I do that it had all been quick and clean, and my head had come off properly, I mean, it would have saved me a great deal of pain and ridicule. However —” Barely Headless Nate shook his letter open and read furiously:



“ ‘We can only accept huntsmen whose heads have parted company with their bodies. You will appreciate that it would be impossible otherwise for members to participate in hunt activities such as Horseback Head-Juggling and Head Polo. It is with the greatest regret, therefore, that I must inform you that you do not fulfill our requirements. With very best wishes, Sir Reginald Daring-More.’ ”



Fuming, Barely Headless Nate stuffed the letter away.

“Half an inch of skin and sinew holding my neck on, Eli! Most people would think that’s good and beheaded, but oh, no, it’s not enough for Sir Properly Decapitated-Podmore.”

Barely Headless Nate took several deep breaths and then said, in a far calmer tone, “So — what’s bothering you? Anything I can do?”

“No,” said Eli. “Not unless you know where we can get seven free Nimbus Two Thousand and Ones for our match against Sly —”

The rest of Eli’s sentence was drowned out by a high-pitched mewling from somewhere near his ankles. He looked down and found himself gazing into a pair of lamp-like yellow eyes. It was Mrs. Whiskers, the skeletal gray cat who was used by the caretaker, Argus Acheron, as a sort of deputy in his endless battle against students.

“You’d better get out of here, Eli,” said Nick quickly. “Acheron isn’t in a good mood — he’s got the flu and some third years accidentally plastered frog brains all over the ceiling in dungeon five. He’s been cleaning all morning, and if he sees you dripping mud all over the place —”

“Right,” said Eli, backing away from the accusing stare of Mrs. Whiskers, but not quickly enough. Drawn to the spot by the mysterious power that seemed to connect him with his foul cat, Argus Acheron burst suddenly through a tapestry to Eli’s right, wheezing and looking wildly about for the rule-breaker. There was a thick tartan scarf bound around his head, and his nose was unusually purple.

“Filth!” he shouted, his jowls aquiver, his eyes popping alarmingly as he pointed at the muddy puddle that had dripped from Eli’s Quidditch robes. “Mess and muck everywhere! I’ve had enough of it, I tell you! Follow me, Ember!”

So Eli waved a gloomy good-bye to Barely Headless Nate and followed Acheron back downstairs, doubling the number of muddy footprints on the floor.

Eli had never been inside Acheron’s office before; it was a place most students avoided. The room was dingy and windowless, lit by a single oil lamp dangling from the low ceiling. A faint smell of fried fish lingered about the place. Ashen filing cabinets stood around the walls; from their labels, Eli could see that they contained details of every pupil Acheron had ever punished. Felix and Aiden Brunswick had an entire drawer to themselves. A highly polished collection of chains and manacles hung on the wall behind Acheron’s desk. It was common knowledge that he was always begging Starling to let him suspend students by their ankles from the ceiling.

Acheron grabbed a quill from a pot on his desk and began shuffling around looking for parchment.

“Dung,” he muttered furiously, “great sizzling dragon bogies … frog brains … rat intestines … I’ve had enough of it … make an example … where’s the form … yes …”

He retrieved a large roll of parchment from his desk drawer and stretched it out in front of him, dipping his long black quill into the ink pot.

“Name … Eli Ember. Crime …”

“It was only a bit of mud!” said Eli.

“It’s only a bit of mud to you, boy, but to me it’s an extra hour scrubbing!” shouted Acheron, a drip shivering unpleasantly at the end of his bulbous nose. “Crime … befouling the castle … suggested sentence …”

Dabbing at his streaming nose, Acheron squinted unpleasantly at Eli, who waited with bated breath for his sentence to fall.

But as Acheron lowered his quill, there was a great BANG! on the ceiling of the office, which made the oil lamp rattle.

“PEEVES!” Acheron roared, flinging down his quill in a transport of rage. “I’ll have you this time, I’ll have you!”

And without a backward glance at Eli, Acheron ran flat-footed from the office, Mrs. Whiskers streaking alongside him.

Banshee was the school poltergeist, a grinning, airborne menace who lived to cause havoc and distress. Eli didn’t much like Banshee, but couldn’t help feeling grateful for his timing. Hopefully, whatever Banshee had done (and it sounded as though he’d wrecked something very big this time) would distract Acheron from Eli.

Thinking that he should probably wait for Acheron to come back, Eli sank into a moth-eaten chair next to the desk. There was only one thing on it apart from his half-completed form: a large, glossy, purple envelope with silver lettering on the front. With a quick glance at the door to check that Acheron wasn’t on his way back, Eli picked up the envelope and read:





KWIKSPELL

A Correspondence Course in Beginners’ Magic





Intrigued, Eli flicked the envelope open and pulled out the sheaf of parchment inside. More curly silver writing on the front page said:



Feel out of step in the world of modern magic? Find yourself making excuses not to perform simple spells? Ever been taunted for your woeful wandwork?

There is an answer!



Kwikspell is an all-new, fail-safe, quick-result, easy-learn course. Hundreds of witches and wizards have benefited from the Kwikspell method!



Madam Z. Nettles of Topsham writes:

“I had no memory for incantations and my potions were a family joke! Now, after a Kwikspell course, I am the center of attention at parties and friends beg for the recipe of my Scintillation Solution!”



Warlock D. J. Prod of Didsbury says:

“My wife used to sneer at my feeble charms, but one month into your fabulous Kwikspell course and I succeeded in turning her into a yak!

Thank you, Kwikspell!”



Fascinated, Eli thumbed through the rest of the envelope’s contents. Why on earth did Acheron want a Kwikspell course? Did this mean he wasn’t a proper wizard? Eli was just reading “Lesson One: Holding Your Wand (Some Useful Tips)” when shuffling footsteps outside told him Acheron was coming back. Stuffing the parchment back into the envelope, Eli threw it back onto the desk just as the door opened.

Acheron was looking triumphant.

“That vanishing cabinet was extremely valuable!” he was saying gleefully to Mrs. Whiskers. “We’ll have Banshee out this time, my sweet —”

His eyes fell on Eli and then darted to the Kwikspell envelope, which, Eli realized too late, was lying two feet away from where it had started.

Acheron’s pasty face went brick red. Eli braced himself for a tidal wave of fury. Acheron hobbled across to his desk, snatched up the envelope, and threw it into a drawer.

“Have you — did you read — ?” he sputtered.

“No,” Eli lied quickly.

Acheron’s knobbly hands were twisting together.

“If I thought you’d read my private — not that it’s mine — for a friend — be that as it may — however —”

Eli was staring at him, alarmed; Acheron had never looked madder. His eyes were popping, a tic was going in one of his pouchy cheeks, and the tartan scarf didn’t help.

“Very well — go — and don’t breathe a word — not that — however, if you didn’t read — go now, I have to write up Banshee’ report — go —”

Amazed at his luck, Eli sped out of the office, up the corridor, and back upstairs. To escape from Acheron’s office without punishment was probably some kind of school record.

“Eli! Eli! Did it work?”

Barely Headless Nate came gliding out of a classroom. Behind him, Eli could see the wreckage of a large black-and-gold cabinet that appeared to have been dropped from a great height.

“I persuaded Banshee to crash it right over Acheron’s office,” said Nick eagerly. “Thought it might distract him —”

“Was that you?” said Eli gratefully. “Yeah, it worked, I didn’t even get detention. Thanks, Nick!”

They set off up the corridor together. Barely Headless Nate, Eli noticed, was still holding Sir Reginald’s rejection letter.

“I wish there was something I could do for you about the Headless Hunt,” Eli said.

Barely Headless Nate stopped in his tracks and Eli walked right through him. He wished he hadn’t; it was like stepping through an icy shower.

“But there is something you could do for me,” said Nick excitedly. “Eli — would I be asking too much — but no, you wouldn’t want —”

“What is it?” said Eli.

“Well, this Halloween will be my five hundredth deathday,” said Barely Headless Nate, drawing himself up and looking dignified.

“Oh,” said Eli, not sure whether he should look sorry or happy about this. “Right.”

“I’m holding a party down in one of the roomier dungeons. Friends will be coming from all over the country. It would be such an honor if you would attend. Mr. Brunswick and Miss Hawke would be most welcome, too, of course — but I daresay you’d rather go to the school feast?” He watched Eli on tenterhooks.

“No,” said Eli quickly, “I’ll come —”

“My dear boy! Eli Ember, at my deathday party! And” — he hesitated, looking excited — “do you think you could possibly mention to Sir Reginald how very frightening and impressive you find me?”

“Of — of course,” said Eli.

Barely Headless Nate beamed at him.



“A deathday party?” said Isla keenly when Eli had changed at last and joined her and Leo in the common room. “I bet there aren’t many living people who can say they’ve been to one of those — it’ll be fascinating!”

“Why would anyone want to celebrate the day they died?” said Leo, who was halfway through his Potions homework and grumpy. “Sounds dead depressing to me. …”

Rain was still lashing the windows, which were now inky black, but inside all looked bright and cheerful. The firelight glowed over the countless squashy armchairs where people sat reading, talking, doing homework or, in the case of Felix and Aiden Brunswick, trying to find out what would happen if you fed a Filibuster firework to a salamander. Felix had “rescued” the brilliant orange, fire-dwelling lizard from a Care of Magical Creatures class and it was now smoldering gently on a table surrounded by a knot of curious people.

Eli was at the point of telling Leo and Isla about Acheron and the Kwikspell course when the salamander suddenly whizzed into the air, emitting loud sparks and bangs as it whirled wildly round the room. The sight of Evan bellowing himself hoarse at Felix and Aiden, the spectacular display of tangerine stars showering from the salamander’s mouth, and its escape into the fire, with accompanying explosions, drove both Acheron and the Kwikspell envelope from Eli’s mind.



By the time Halloween arrived, Eli was regretting his rash promise to go to the deathday party. The rest of the school was happily anticipating their Halloween feast; the Great Hall had been decorated with the usual live bats, Griff’s vast pumpkins had been carved into lanterns large enough for three men to sit in, and there were rumors that Starling had booked a troupe of dancing skeletons for the entertainment.

“A promise is a promise,” Isla reminded Eli bossily. “You said you’d go to the deathday party.”

So at seven o’clock, Eli, Leo, and Isla walked straight past the doorway to the packed Great Hall, which was glittering invitingly with gold plates and candles, and directed their steps instead toward the dungeons.

The passageway leading to Barely Headless Nate’s party had been lined with candles, too, though the effect was far from cheerful: These were long, thin, jet-black tapers, all burning bright blue, casting a dim, ghostly light even over their own living faces. The temperature dropped with every step they took. As Eli shivered and drew his robes tightly around him, he heard what sounded like a thousand fingernails scraping an enormous blackboard.

“Is that supposed to be music?” Leo whispered. They turned a corner and saw Barely Headless Nate standing at a doorway hung with black velvet drapes.

“My dear friends,” he said mournfully. “Welcome, welcome … so pleased you could come. …”

He swept off his plumed hat and bowed them inside.

It was an incredible sight. The dungeon was full of hundreds of pearly-white, translucent people, mostly drifting around a crowded dance floor, waltzing to the dreadful, quavering sound of thirty musical saws, played by an orchestra on a raised, black-draped platform. A chandelier overhead blazed midnight-blue with a thousand more black candles. Their breath rose in a mist before them; it was like stepping into a freezer.

“Shall we have a look around?” Eli suggested, wanting to warm up his feet.

“Careful not to walk through anyone,” said Leo nervously, and they set off around the edge of the dance floor. They passed a group of gloomy nuns, a ragged man wearing chains, and the Fat Friar, a cheerful Hufflepuff ghost, who was talking to a knight with an arrow sticking out of his forehead. Eli wasn’t surprised to see that the Bloody Baron, a gaunt, staring Slytherin ghost covered in silver bloodstains, was being given a wide berth by the other ghosts.

“Oh, no,” said Isla, stopping abruptly. “Turn back, turn back, I don’t want to talk to Wailing Wren —”

“Who?” said Eli as they backtracked quickly.

“She haunts one of the toilets in the girls’ bathroom on the first floor,” said Isla.

“She haunts a toilet?”

“Yes. It’s been out-of-order all year because she keeps having tantrums and flooding the place. I never went in there anyway if I could avoid it; it’s awful trying to have a pee with her wailing at you —”

“Look, food!” said Leo.

On the other side of the dungeon was a long table, also covered in black velvet. They approached it eagerly but next moment had stopped in their tracks, horrified. The smell was quite disgusting. Large, rotten fish were laid on handsome silver platters; cakes, burned charcoal-black, were heaped on salvers; there was a great maggoty haggis, a slab of cheese covered in furry green mold and, in pride of place, an enormous gray cake in the shape of a tombstone, with tar-like icing forming the words,





SIR NICHOLAS DE MIMSY-PORPINGTON

DIED 31ST OCTOBER, 1492





Eli watched, amazed, as a portly ghost approached the table, crouched low, and walked through it, his mouth held wide so that it passed through one of the stinking salmon.

“Can you taste it if you walk through it?” Eli asked him.

“Almost,” said the ghost sadly, and he drifted away.

“I expect they’ve let it rot to give it a stronger flavor,” said Isla knowledgeably, pinching her nose and leaning closer to look at the putrid haggis.

“Can we move? I feel sick,” said Leo.

They had barely turned around, however, when a little man swooped suddenly from under the table and came to a halt in midair before them.

“Hello, Banshee,” said Eli cautiously.

Unlike the ghosts around them, Banshee the Poltergeist was the very reverse of pale and transparent. He was wearing a bright orange party hat, a revolving bow tie, and a broad grin on his wide, wicked face.

“Nibbles?” he said sweetly, offering them a bowl of peanuts covered in fungus.

“No thanks,” said Isla.

“Heard you talking about poor Wren,” said Banshee, his eyes dancing. “Rude you was about poor Wren.” He took a deep breath and bellowed, “OY! MYRTLE!”

“Oh, no, Banshee, don’t tell her what I said, she’ll be really upset,” Isla whispered frantically. “I didn’t mean it, I don’t mind her — er, hello, Wren.”

The squat ghost of a girl had glided over. She had the glummest face Eli had ever seen, half-hidden behind lank hair and thick, pearly spectacles.

“What?” she said sulkily.

“How are you, Wren?” said Isla in a falsely bright voice. “It’s nice to see you out of the toilet.”

Wren sniffed.

“Miss Hawke was just talking about you —” said Banshee slyly in Wren’s ear.

“Just saying — saying — how nice you look tonight,” said Isla, glaring at Banshee.

Wren eyed Isla suspiciously.

“You’re making fun of me,” she said, silver tears welling rapidly in her small, see-through eyes.

“No — honestly — didn’t I just say how nice Wren’s looking?” said Isla, nudging Eli and Leo painfully in the ribs.

“Oh, yeah —”

“She did —”

“Don’t lie to me,” Wren gasped, tears now flooding down her face, while Banshee chuckled happily over her shoulder. “D’you think I don’t know what people call me behind my back? Fat Wren! Ugly Wren! Miserable, moaning, moping Wren!”

“You’ve forgotten pimply,” Banshee hissed in her ear.

Wailing Wren burst into anguished sobs and fled from the dungeon. Banshee shot after her, pelting her with moldy peanuts, yelling, “Pimply! Pimply!”

“Oh, dear,” said Isla sadly.

Barely Headless Nate now drifted toward them through the crowd.

“Enjoying yourselves?”

“Oh, yes,” they lied.

“Not a bad turnout,” said Barely Headless Nate proudly. “The Wailing Widow came all the way up from Kent. … It’s nearly time for my speech, I’d better go and warn the orchestra. …”

The orchestra, however, stopped playing at that very moment. They, and everyone else in the dungeon, fell silent, looking around in excitement, as a hunting horn sounded.

“Oh, here we go,” said Barely Headless Nate bitterly.

Through the dungeon wall burst a dozen ghost horses, each ridden by a headless horseman. The assembly clapped wildly; Eli started to clap, too, but stopped quickly at the sight of Nick’s face.

The horses galloped into the middle of the dance floor and halted, rearing and plunging. At the front of the pack was a large ghost who held his bearded head under his arm, from which position he was blowing the horn. The ghost leapt down, lifted his head high in the air so he could see over the crowd (everyone laughed), and strode over to Barely Headless Nate, squashing his head back onto his neck.

“Nick!” he roared. “How are you? Head still hanging in there?”

He gave a hearty guffaw and clapped Barely Headless Nate on the shoulder.

“Welcome, Patrick,” said Nick stiffly.

“Live ’uns!” said Sir Reginald, spotting Eli, Leo, and Isla and giving a huge, fake jump of astonishment, so that his head fell off again (the crowd howled with laughter).

“Very amusing,” said Barely Headless Nate darkly.

“Don’t mind Nick!” shouted Sir Reginald’s head from the floor. “Still upset we won’t let him join the Hunt! But I mean to say — look at the fellow —”

“I think,” said Eli hurriedly, at a meaningful look from Nick, “Nick’s very — frightening and — er —”

“Ha!” yelled Sir Reginald’s head. “Bet he asked you to say that!”

“If I could have everyone’s attention, it’s time for my speech!” said Barely Headless Nate loudly, striding toward the podium and climbing into an icy blue spotlight.

“My late lamented lords, ladies, and gentlemen, it is my great sorrow …”

But nobody heard much more. Sir Reginald and the rest of the Headless Hunt had just started a game of Head Hockey and the crowd were turning to watch. Barely Headless Nate tried vainly to recapture his audience, but gave up as Sir Reginald’s head went sailing past him to loud cheers.

Eli was very cold by now, not to mention hungry.

“I can’t stand much more of this,” Leo muttered, his teeth chattering, as the orchestra ground back into action and the ghosts swept back onto the dance floor.

“Let’s go,” Eli agreed.

They backed toward the door, nodding and beaming at anyone who looked at them, and a minute later were hurrying back up the passageway full of black candles.

“Pudding might not be finished yet,” said Leo hopefully, leading the way toward the steps to the entrance hall.

And then Eli heard it.

“… rip … tear … kill …”

It was the same voice, the same cold, murderous voice he had heard in Bright’s office.

He stumbled to a halt, clutching at the stone wall, listening with all his might, looking around, squinting up and down the dimly lit passageway.

“Eli, what’re you — ?”

“It’s that voice again — shut up a minute —”

“… soo hungry … for so long …”

“Listen!” said Eli urgently, and Leo and Isla froze, watching him.

“… kill … time to kill …”

The voice was growing fainter. Eli was sure it was moving away — moving upward. A mixture of fear and excitement gripped him as he stared at the dark ceiling; how could it be moving upward? Was it a phantom, to whom stone ceilings didn’t matter?

“This way,” he shouted, and he began to run, up the stairs, into the entrance hall. It was no good hoping to hear anything here, the babble of talk from the Halloween feast was echoing out of the Great Hall. Eli sprinted up the marble staircase to the first floor, Leo and Isla clattering behind him.

“Eli, what’re we —”

“SHH!”

Eli strained his ears. Distantly, from the floor above, and growing fainter still, he heard the voice: “… I smell blood. … I SMELL BLOOD!”

His stomach lurched —

“It’s going to kill someone!” he shouted, and ignoring Leo’s and Isla’s bewildered faces, he ran up the next flight of steps three at a time, trying to listen over his own pounding footsteps —

Eli hurtled around the whole of the second floor, Leo and Isla panting behind him, not stopping until they turned a corner into the last, deserted passage.

“Eli, what was that all about?” said Leo, wiping sweat off his face. “I couldn’t hear anything. …”

But Isla gave a sudden gasp, pointing down the corridor.

“Look!”

Something was shining on the wall ahead. They approached slowly, squinting through the darkness. Foot-high words had been daubed on the wall between two windows, shimmering in the light cast by the flaming torches.





THE CHAMBER OF SECRETS HAS BEEN

OPENED. ENEMIES OF THE HEIR, BEWARE.





“What’s that thing — hanging underneath?” said Leo, a slight quiver in his voice.

As they edged nearer, Eli almost slipped — there was a large puddle of water on the floor; Leo and Isla grabbed him, and they inched toward the message, eyes fixed on a dark shadow beneath it. All three of them realized what it was at once, and leapt backward with a splash.

Mrs. Whiskers, the caretaker’s cat, was hanging by her tail from the torch bracket. She was stiff as a board, her eyes wide and staring.

For a few seconds, they didn’t move. Then Leo said, “Let’s get out of here.”

“Shouldn’t we try and help —” Eli began awkwardly.

“Trust me,” said Leo. “We don’t want to be found here.”

But it was too late. A rumble, as though of distant thunder, told them that the feast had just ended. From either end of the corridor where they stood came the sound of hundreds of feet climbing the stairs, and the loud, happy talk of well-fed people; next moment, students were crashing into the passage from both ends.

The chatter, the bustle, the noise died suddenly as the people in front spotted the hanging cat. Eli, Leo, and Isla stood alone, in the middle of the corridor, as silence fell among the mass of students pressing forward to see the grisly sight.

Then someone shouted through the quiet.

“Enemies of the Heir, beware! You’ll be next, Mudbloods!”

It was Jasper Mortem. He had pushed to the front of the crowd, his cold eyes alive, his usually bloodless face flushed, as he grinned at the sight of the hanging, immobile cat.





Chapter 9



The Writing on the Wall





“What’s going on here? What’s going on?”

Attracted no doubt by Mortem’s shout, Argus Acheron came shouldering his way through the crowd. Then he saw Mrs. Whiskers and fell back, clutching his face in horror.

“My cat! My cat! What’s happened to Mrs. Whiskers?” he shrieked.

And his popping eyes fell on Eli.

“You!” he screeched. “You! You’ve murdered my cat! You’ve killed her! I’ll kill you! I’ll —”

“Argus!”

Starling had arrived on the scene, followed by a number of other teachers. In seconds, he had swept past Eli, Leo, and Isla and detached Mrs. Whiskers from the torch bracket.

“Come with me, Argus,” he said to Acheron. “You, too, Mr. Ember, Mr. Brunswick, Miss Hawke.”

Bright stepped forward eagerly.

“My office is nearest, Headmaster — just upstairs — please feel free —”

“Thank you, Julian,” said Starling.

The silent crowd parted to let them pass. Bright, looking excited and important, hurried after Starling; so did Professors Falconer and Thorne.

As they entered Bright’s darkened office there was a flurry of movement across the walls; Eli saw several of the Brights in the pictures dodging out of sight, their hair in rollers. The real Bright lit the candles on his desk and stood back. Starling laid Mrs. Whiskers on the polished surface and began to examine her. Eli, Leo, and Isla exchanged tense looks and sank into chairs outside the pool of candlelight, watching.

The tip of Starling’s long, crooked nose was barely an inch from Mrs. Whiskers’s fur. He was looking at her closely through his half-moon spectacles, his long fingers gently prodding and poking. Professor Falconer was bent almost as close, her eyes narrowed. Thorne loomed behind them, half in shadow, wearing a most peculiar expression: It was as though he was trying hard not to smile. And Bright was hovering around all of them, making suggestions.

“It was definitely a curse that killed her — probably the Trans-mogrifian Torture — I’ve seen it used many times, so unlucky I wasn’t there, I know the very countercurse that would have saved her. …”

Bright’s comments were punctuated by Acheron’s dry, racking sobs. He was slumped in a chair by the desk, unable to look at Mrs. Whiskers, his face in his hands. Much as he detested Acheron, Eli couldn’t help feeling a bit sorry for him, though not nearly as sorry as he felt for himself. If Starling believed Acheron, he would be expelled for sure.

Starling was now muttering strange words under his breath and tapping Mrs. Whiskers with his wand but nothing happened: She continued to look as though she had been recently stuffed.

“… I remember something very similar happening in Oua-gadogou,” said Bright, “a series of attacks, the full story’s in my autobiography, I was able to provide the townsfolk with various amulets, which cleared the matter up at once. …”

The photographs of Bright on the walls were all nodding in agreement as he talked. One of them had forgotten to remove his hair net.

At last Starling straightened up.

“She’s not dead, Argus,” he said softly.

Bright stopped abruptly in the middle of counting the number of murders he had prevented.

“Not dead?” choked Acheron, looking through his fingers at Mrs. Whiskers. “But why’s she all — all stiff and frozen?”

“She has been Petrified,” said Starling (“Ah! I thought so!” said Bright). “But how, I cannot say. …”

“Ask him!” shrieked Acheron, turning his blotched and tearstained face to Eli.

“No second year could have done this,” said Starling firmly. “It would take Dark Magic of the most advanced —”

“He did it, he did it!” Acheron spat, his pouchy face purpling. “You saw what he wrote on the wall! He found — in my office — he knows I’m a — I’m a —” Acheron’s face worked horribly. “He knows I’m a Squib!” he finished.

“I never touched Mrs. Whiskers!” Eli said loudly, uncomfortably aware of everyone looking at him, including all the Brights on the walls. “And I don’t even know what a Squib is.”

“Rubbish!” snarled Acheron. “He saw my Kwikspell letter!”

“If I might speak, Headmaster,” said Thorne from the shadows, and Eli’s sense of foreboding increased; he was sure nothing Thorne had to say was going to do him any good.

“Ember and his friends may have simply been in the wrong place at the wrong time,” he said, a slight sneer curling his mouth as though he doubted it. “But we do have a set of suspicious circumstances here. Why was he in the upstairs corridor at all? Why wasn’t he at the Halloween feast?”

Eli, Leo and Isla all launched into an explanation about the deathday party. “… there were hundreds of ghosts, they’ll tell you we were there —”

“But why not join the feast afterward?” said Thorne, his black eyes glittering in the candlelight. “Why go up to that corridor?”

Leo and Isla looked at Eli.

“Because — because —” Eli said, his heart thumping very fast; something told him it would sound very far-fetched if he told them he had been led there by a bodiless voice no one but he could hear, “because we were tired and wanted to go to bed,” he said.

“Without any supper?” said Thorne, a triumphant smile flickering across his gaunt face. “I didn’t think ghosts provided food fit for living people at their parties.”

“We weren’t hungry,” said Leo loudly as his stomach gave a huge rumble.

Thorne’s nasty smile widened.

“I suggest, Headmaster, that Ember is not being entirely truthful,” he said. “It might be a good idea if he were deprived of certain privileges until he is ready to tell us the whole story. I personally feel he should be taken off the Gryffindor Quidditch team until he is ready to be honest.”

“Really, Severus,” said Professor Falconer sharply, “I see no reason to stop the boy playing Quidditch. This cat wasn’t hit over the head with a broomstick. There is no evidence at all that Ember has done anything wrong.”

Starling was giving Eli a searching look. His twinkling light-blue gaze made Eli feel as though he were being X-rayed.

“Innocent until proven guilty, Severus,” he said firmly.

Thorne looked furious. So did Acheron.

“My cat has been Petrified!” he shrieked, his eyes popping. “I want to see some punishment!”

“We will be able to cure her, Argus,” said Starling patiently. “Professer Sprout recently managed to procure some Mandrakes. As soon as they have reached their full size, I will have a potion made that will revive Mrs. Whiskers.”

“I’ll make it,” Bright butted in. “I must have done it a hundred times. I could whip up a Mandrake Restorative Draught in my sleep —”

“Excuse me,” said Thorne icily. “But I believe I am the Potions master at this school.”

There was a very awkward pause.

“You may go,” Starling said to Eli, Leo, and Isla.

They went, as quickly as they could without actually running. When they were a floor up from Bright’s office, they turned into an empty classroom and closed the door quietly behind them. Eli squinted at his friends’ darkened faces.

“D’you think I should have told them about that voice I heard?”

“No,” said Leo, without hesitation. “Hearing voices no one else can hear isn’t a good sign, even in the wizarding world.”

Something in Leo’s voice made Eli ask, “You do believe me, don’t you?”

“ ’Course I do,” said Leo quickly. “But — you must admit it’s weird. …”

“I know it’s weird,” said Eli. “The whole thing’s weird. What was that writing on the wall about? ‘The Chamber Has Been Opened’. … What’s that supposed to mean?”

“You know, it rings a sort of bell,” said Leo slowly. “I think someone told me a story about a secret chamber at Hogwarts once … might’ve been Max. …”

“And what on earth’s a Squib?” said Eli.

To his surprise, Leo stifled a snigger.

“Well — it’s not funny really — but as it’s Acheron,” he said. “A Squib is someone who was born into a wizarding family but hasn’t got any magic powers. Kind of the opposite of Muggle-born wizards, but Squibs are quite unusual. If Acheron’s trying to learn magic from a Kwikspell course, I reckon he must be a Squib. It would explain a lot. Like why he hates students so much.” Leo gave a satisfied smile. “He’s bitter.”

A clock chimed somewhere.

“Midnight,” said Eli. “We’d better get to bed before Thorne comes along and tries to frame us for something else.”



* * *



For a few days, the school could talk of little else but the attack on Mrs. Whiskers. Acheron kept it fresh in everyone’s minds by pacing the spot where she had been attacked, as though he thought the attacker might come back. Eli had seen him scrubbing the message on the wall with Mrs. Skower’s All-Purpose Magical Mess Remover, but to no effect; the words still gleamed as brightly as ever on the stone. When Acheron wasn’t guarding the scene of the crime, he was skulking red-eyed through the corridors, lunging out at unsuspecting students and trying to put them in detention for things like “breathing loudly” and “looking happy.”

Lila Brunswick seemed very disturbed by Mrs. Whiskers’s fate. According to Leo, she was a great cat lover.

“But you haven’t really got to know Mrs. Whiskers,” Leo told her bracingly. “Honestly, we’re much better off without her.” Lila’s lip trembled. “Stuff like this doesn’t often happen at Hogwarts,” Leo assured her. “They’ll catch the maniac who did it and have him out of here in no time. I just hope he’s got time to Petrify Acheron before he’s expelled. I’m only joking —” Leo added hastily as Lila blanched.

The attack had also had an effect on Isla. It was quite usual for Isla to spend a lot of time reading, but she was now doing almost nothing else. Nor could Eli and Leo get much response from her when they asked what she was up to, and not until the following Wednesday did they find out.

Eli had been held back in Potions, where Thorne had made him stay behind to scrape tubeworms off the desks. After a hurried lunch, he went upstairs to meet Leo in the library, and saw Justin Winge, the Hufflepuff boy from Herbology, coming toward him. Eli had just opened his mouth to say hello when Justin caught sight of him, turned abruptly, and sped off in the opposite direction.

Eli found Leo at the back of the library, measuring his History of Magic homework. Professor Shades had asked for a three-foot-long composition on “The Medieval Assembly of European Wizards.”

“I don’t believe it, I’m still eight inches short. …” said Leo furiously, letting go of his parchment, which sprang back into a roll. “And Isla’s done four feet seven inches and her writing’s tiny.”

“Where is she?” asked Eli, grabbing the tape measure and unrolling his own homework.

“Somewhere over there,” said Leo, pointing along the shelves. “Looking for another book. I think she’s trying to read the whole library before Christmas.”

Eli told Leo about Justin Winge running away from him.

“Dunno why you care. I thought he was a bit of an idiot,” said Leo, scribbling away, making his writing as large as possible. “All that junk about Bright being so great —”

Isla emerged from between the bookshelves. She looked irritable and at last seemed ready to talk to them.

“All the copies of Hogwarts, A History have been taken out,” she said, sitting down next to Eli and Leo. “And there’s a two-week waiting list. I wish I hadn’t left my copy at home, but I couldn’t fit it in my trunk with all the Bright books.”

“Why do you want it?” said Eli.

“The same reason everyone else wants it,” said Isla, “to read up on the legend of the Chamber of Secrets.”

“What’s that?” said Eli quickly.

“That’s just it. I can’t remember,” said Isla, biting her lip. “And I can’t find the story anywhere else —”

“Isla, let me read your composition,” said Leo desperately, checking his watch.

“No, I won’t,” said Isla, suddenly severe. “You’ve had ten days to finish it —”

“I only need another two inches, come on —”

The bell rang. Leo and Isla led the way to History of Magic, bickering.

History of Magic was the dullest subject on their schedule. Professor Shades, who taught it, was their only ghost teacher, and the most exciting thing that ever happened in his classes was his entering the room through the blackboard. Ancient and shriveled, many people said he hadn’t noticed he was dead. He had simply got up to teach one day and left his body behind him in an armchair in front of the staffroom fire; his routine had not varied in the slightest since.

Today was as boring as ever. Professor Shades opened his notes and began to read in a flat drone like an old vacuum cleaner until nearly everyone in the class was in a deep stupor, occasionally coming to long enough to copy down a name or date, then falling asleep again. He had been speaking for half an hour when something happened that had never happened before. Isla put up her hand.

Professor Shades, glancing up in the middle of a deadly dull lecture on the International Warlock Convention of 1289, looked amazed.

“Miss — er — ?”

“Hawke, Professor. I was wondering if you could tell us anything about the Chamber of Secrets,” said Isla in a clear voice.

Dean Hunter, who had been sitting with his mouth hanging open, gazing out of the window, jerked out of his trance; Lilac Beige’s head came up off her arms and Nolan Broadfoot’s elbow slipped off his desk.

Professor Shades blinked.

“My subject is History of Magic,” he said in his dry, wheezy voice. “I deal with, facts, Miss Hawke, not myths and legends.” He cleared his throat with a small noise like chalk snapping and continued, “In September of that year, a subcommittee of Sardinian sorcerers —”

He stuttered to a halt. Isla’s hand was waving in the air again.

“Miss Grant?”

“Please, sir, don’t legends always have a basis in fact?”

Professor Shades was looking at her in such amazement, Eli was sure no student had ever interrupted him before, alive or dead.

“Well,” said Professor Shades slowly, “yes, one could argue that, I suppose.” He peered at Isla as though he had never seen a student properly before. “However, the legend of which you speak is such a very sensational, even ludicrous tale —”

But the whole class was now hanging on Professor Shades’s every word. He looked dimly at them all, every face turned to his. Eli could tell he was completely thrown by such an unusual show of interest.

“Oh, very well,” he said slowly. “Let me see … the Chamber of Secrets …

“You all know, of course, that Hogwarts was founded over a thousand years ago — the precise date is uncertain — by the four greatest witches and wizards of the age. The four school Houses are named after them: Godric Gryffindor, Helga Hufflepuff, Rowena Ravenclaw, and Salvatore Serpentine. They built this castle together, far from prying Muggle eyes, for it was an age when magic was feared by common people, and witches and wizards suffered much persecution.”

He paused, gazed blearily around the room, and continued.

“For a few years, the founders worked in harmony together, seeking out youngsters who showed signs of magic and bringing them to the castle to be educated. But then disagreements sprang up between them. A rift began to grow between Slytherin and the others. Slytherin wished to be more selective about the students admitted to Hogwarts. He believed that magical learning should be kept within all-magic families. He disliked taking students of Muggle parentage, believing them to be untrustworthy. After a while, there was a serious argument on the subject between Slytherin and Gryffindor, and Slytherin left the school.”

Professor Shades paused again, pursing his lips, looking like a wrinkled old tortoise.

“Reliable historical sources tell us this much,” he said. “But these honest facts have been obscured by the fanciful legend of the Chamber of Secrets. The story goes that Slytherin had built a hidden chamber in the castle, of which the other founders knew nothing.

“Slytherin, according to the legend, sealed the Chamber of Secrets so that none would be able to open it until his own true heir arrived at the school. The heir alone would be able to unseal the Chamber of Secrets, unleash the horror within, and use it to purge the school of all who were unworthy to study magic.”

There was silence as he finished telling the story, but it wasn’t the usual, sleepy silence that filled Professor Shades’s classes. There was unease in the air as everyone continued to watch him, hoping for more. Professor Shades looked faintly annoyed.

“The whole thing is arrant nonsense, of course,” he said. “Naturally, the school has been searched for evidence of such a chamber, many times, by the most learned witches and wizards. It does not exist. A tale told to frighten the gullible.”

Isla’s hand was back in the air.

“Sir — what exactly do you mean by the ‘horror within’ the Chamber?”

“That is believed to be some sort of monster, which the Heir of Slytherin alone can control,” said Professor Shades in his dry, reedy voice.

The class exchanged nervous looks.

“I tell you, the thing does not exist,” said Professor Shades, shuffling his notes. “There is no Chamber and no monster.”

“But, sir,” said Liam Finnegan, “if the Chamber can only be opened by Slytherin’s true heir, no one else would be able to find it, would they?”

“Nonsense, O’Flaherty,” said Professor Shades in an aggravated tone. “If a long succession of Hogwarts headmasters and headmistresses haven’t found the thing —”

“But, Professor,” piped up Parvati Calla, “you’d probably have to use Dark Magic to open it —”

“Just because a wizard doesn’t use Dark Magic doesn’t mean he can’t, Miss Pennyfeather,” snapped Professor Shades. “I repeat, if the likes of Starling —”

“But maybe you’ve got to be related to Slytherin, so Starling couldn’t —” began Dean Hunter, but Professor Shades had had enough.

“That will do,” he said sharply. “It is a myth! It does not exist! There is not a shred of evidence that Slytherin ever built so much as a secret broom cupboard! I regret telling you such a foolish story! We will return, if you please, to history, to solid, believable, verifiable fact!”

And within five minutes, the class had sunk back into its usual torpor.



“I always knew Salvatore Serpentine was a twisted old loony,” Leo told Eli and Isla as they fought their way through the teeming corridors at the end of the lesson to drop off their bags before dinner. “But I never knew he started all this pure-blood stuff. I wouldn’t be in his House if you paid me. Honestly, if the Sorting Hat had tried to put me in Slytherin, I’d’ve got the train straight back home. …”

Isla nodded fervently, but Eli didn’t say anything. His stomach had just dropped unpleasantly.

Eli had never told Leo and Isla that the Sorting Hat had seriously considered putting him in Slytherin. He could remember, as though it were yesterday, the small voice that had spoken in his ear when he’d placed the hat on his head a year before: You could be great, you know, it’s all here in your head, and Slytherin would help you on the way to greatness, no doubt about that. …

But Eli, who had already heard of Slytherin House’s reputation for turning out Dark wizards, had thought desperately, Not Slytherin! and the hat had said, Oh, well, if you’re sure … better be Gryffindor. …

As they were shunted along in the throng, Colin Ripple went past.

“Hiya, Eli!”

“Hullo, Colin,” said Eli automatically.

“Eli — Eli — a boy in my class has been saying you’re —”

But Colin was so small he couldn’t fight against the tide of people bearing him toward the Great Hall; they heard him squeak, “See you, Eli!” and he was gone.

“What’s a boy in his class saying about you?” Isla wondered.

“That I’m Slytherin’s heir, I expect,” said Eli, his stomach dropping another inch or so as he suddenly remembered the way Justin Winge had run away from him at lunchtime.

“People here’ll believe anything,” said Leo in disgust.

The crowd thinned and they were able to climb the next staircase without difficulty.

“D’you really think there’s a Chamber of Secrets?” Leo asked Isla.

“I don’t know,” she said, frowning. “Starling couldn’t cure Mrs. Whiskers, and that makes me think that whatever attacked her might not be — well — human.”

As she spoke, they turned a corner and found themselves at the end of the very corridor where the attack had happened. They stopped and looked. The scene was just as it had been that night, except that there was no stiff cat hanging from the torch bracket, and an empty chair stood against the wall bearing the message “The Chamber of Secrets Has Been Opened.”

“That’s where Acheron has been keeping guard,” Leo muttered.

They looked at each other. The corridor was deserted.

“Can’t hurt to have a poke around,” said Eli, dropping his bag and getting to his hands and knees so that he could crawl along, searching for clues.

“Scorch marks!” he said. “Here — and here —”

“Come and look at this!” said Isla. “This is funny. …”

Eli got up and crossed to the window next to the message on the wall. Isla was pointing at the topmost pane, where around twenty spiders were scuttling, apparently fighting to get through a small crack. A long, silvery thread was dangling like a rope, as though they had all climbed it in their hurry to get outside.

“Have you ever seen spiders act like that?” said Isla wonderingly.

“No,” said Eli, “have you, Leo? Leo?”

He looked over his shoulder. Leo was standing well back and seemed to be fighting the impulse to run.

“What’s up?” said Eli.

“I — don’t — like — spiders,” said Leo tensely.

“I never knew that,” said Isla, looking at Leo in surprise. “You’ve used spiders in Potions loads of times. …”

“I don’t mind them dead,” said Leo, who was carefully looking anywhere but at the window. “I just don’t like the way they move. …”

Isla giggled.

“It’s not funny,” said Leo, fiercely. “If you must know, when I was three, Felix turned my — my teddy bear into a great big filthy spider because I broke his toy broomstick. … You wouldn’t like them either if you’d been holding your bear and suddenly it had too many legs and …”

He broke off, shuddering. Isla was obviously still trying not to laugh. Feeling they had better get off the subject, Eli said, “Remember all that water on the floor? Where did that come from? Someone’s mopped it up.”

“It was about here,” said Leo, recovering himself to walk a few paces past Acheron’s chair and pointing. “Level with this door.”

He reached for the brass doorknob but suddenly withdrew his hand as though he’d been burned.

“What’s the matter?” said Eli.

“Can’t go in there,” said Leo gruffly. “That’s a girls’ toilet.”

“Oh, Leo, there won’t be anyone in there,” said Isla, standing up and coming over. “That’s Wailing Wren’s place. Come on, let’s have a look.”

And ignoring the large OUT OF ORDER sign, she opened the door.

It was the gloomiest, most depressing bathroom Eli had ever set foot in. Under a large, cracked, and spotted mirror were a row of chipped sinks. The floor was damp and reflected the dull light given off by the stubs of a few candles, burning low in their holders; the wooden doors to the stalls were flaking and scratched and one of them was dangling off its hinges.

Isla put her fingers to her lips and set off toward the end stall. When she reached it she said, “Hello, Wren, how are you?”

Eli and Leo went to look. Wailing Wren was floating above the tank of the toilet, picking a spot on her chin.

“This is a girls’ bathroom,” she said, eyeing Leo and Eli suspiciously. “They’re not girls.”

“No,” Isla agreed. “I just wanted to show them how — er — nice it is in here.”

She waved vaguely at the dirty old mirror and the damp floor.

“Ask her if she saw anything,” Eli mouthed at Isla.

“What are you whispering?” said Wren, staring at him.

“Nothing,” said Eli quickly. “We wanted to ask —”

“I wish people would stop talking behind my back!” said Wren, in a voice choked with tears. “I do have feelings, you know, even if I am dead —”

“Wren, no one wants to upset you,” said Isla. “Eli only —”

“No one wants to upset me! That’s a good one!” howled Wren. “My life was nothing but misery at this place and now people come along ruining my death!”

“We wanted to ask you if you’ve seen anything funny lately,” said Isla quickly. “Because a cat was attacked right outside your front door on Halloween.”

“Did you see anyone near here that night?” said Eli.

“I wasn’t paying attention,” said Wren dramatically. “Banshee upset me so much I came in here and tried to kill myself. Then, of course, I remembered that I’m — that I’m —”

“Already dead,” said Leo helpfully.

Wren gave a tragic sob, rose up in the air, turned over, and dived headfirst into the toilet, splashing water all over them and vanishing from sight, although from the direction of her muffled sobs, she had come to rest somewhere in the U-bend.

Eli and Leo stood with their mouths open, but Isla shrugged wearily and said, “Honestly, that was almost cheerful for Wren. … Come on, let’s go.”

Eli had barely closed the door on Wren’s gurgling sobs when a loud voice made all three of them jump.

“RON!”

Evan Brunswick had stopped dead at the head of the stairs, prefect badge agleam, an expression of complete shock on his face.

“That’s a girls’ bathroom!” he gasped. “What were you — ?”

“Just having a look around,” Leo shrugged. “Clues, you know —”

Evan swelled in a manner that reminded Eli forcefully of Mrs. Brunswick.

“Get — away — from — there —” Evan said, striding toward them and starting to bustle them along, flapping his arms. “Don’t you care what this looks like? Coming back here while everyone’s at dinner —”

“Why shouldn’t we be here?” said Leo hotly, stopping short and glaring at Evan. “Listen, we never laid a finger on that cat!”

“That’s what I told Lila,” said Evan fiercely, “but she still seems to think you’re going to be expelled, I’ve never seen her so upset, crying her eyes out, you might think of her, all the first years are thoroughly overexcited by this business —”

“You don’t care about Lila,” said Leo, whose ears were now reddening. “You’re just worried I’m going to mess up your chances of being Head Boy —”

“Five points from Gryffindor!” Evan said tersely, fingering his prefect badge. “And I hope it teaches you a lesson! No more detective work, or I’ll write to Mother!”

And he strode off, the back of his neck as red as Leo’s ears.



Eli, Leo, and Isla chose seats as far as possible from Evan in the common room that night. Leo was still in a very bad temper and kept blotting his Charms homework. When he reached absently for his wand to remove the smudges, it ignited the parchment. Fuming almost as much as his homework, Leo slammed The Standard Book of Spells, Grade 2 shut. To Eli’s surprise, Isla followed suit.

“Who can it be, though?” she said in a quiet voice, as though continuing a conversation they had just been having. “Who’d want to frighten all the Squibs and Muggle-borns out of Hogwarts?”

“Let’s think,” said Leo in mock puzzlement. “Who do we know who thinks Muggle-borns are scum?”

He looked at Isla. Isla looked back, unconvinced.

“If you’re talking about Mortem —”

“Of course I am!” said Leo. “You heard him — ‘You’ll be next, Mudbloods!’ — come on, you’ve only got to look at his foul rat face to know it’s him —”

“Mortem, the Heir of Slytherin?” said Isla skeptically.

“Look at his family,” said Eli, closing his books, too. “The whole lot of them have been in Slytherin; he’s always boasting about it. They could easily be Slytherin’s descendants. His father’s definitely evil enough.”

“They could’ve had the key to the Chamber of Secrets for centuries!” said Leo. “Handing it down, father to son. …”

“Well,” said Isla cautiously, “I suppose it’s possible. …”

“But how do we prove it?” said Eli darkly.

“There might be a way,” said Isla slowly, dropping her voice still further with a quick glance across the room at Evan. “Of course, it would be difficult. And dangerous, very dangerous. We’d be breaking about fifty school rules, I expect —”

“If, in a month or so, you feel like explaining, you will let us know, won’t you?” said Leo irritably.

“All right,” said Isla coldly. “What we’d need to do is to get inside the Slytherin common room and ask Mortem a few questions without him realizing it’s us.”

“But that’s impossible,” Eli said as Leo laughed.

“No, it’s not,” said Isla. “All we’d need would be some Polyjuice Potion.”

“What’s that?” said Leo and Eli together.

“Thorne mentioned it in class a few weeks ago —”

“D’you think we’ve got nothing better to do in Potions than listen to Thorne?” muttered Leo.

“It transforms you into somebody else. Think about it! We could change into three of the Slytherins. No one would know it was us. Mortem would probably tell us anything. He’s probably boasting about it in the Slytherin common room right now, if only we could hear him.”

“This Polyjuice stuff sounds a bit dodgy to me,” said Leo, frowning. “What if we were stuck looking like three of the Slytherins forever?”

“It wears off after a while,” said Isla, waving her hand impatiently. “But getting hold of the recipe will be very difficult. Thorne said it was in a book called Moste Potente Potions and it’s bound to be in the Restricted Section of the library.”

There was only one way to get out a book from the Restricted Section: You needed a signed note of permission from a teacher.

“Hard to see why we’d want the book, really,” said Leo, “if we weren’t going to try and make one of the potions.”

“I think,” said Isla, “that if we made it sound as though we were just interested in the theory, we might stand a chance. …”

“Oh, come on, no teacher’s going to fall for that,” said Leo. “They’d have to be really thick. …”





Chapter 10



The Rogue Bludger





Since the disastrous episode of the pixies, Professor Bright had not brought live creatures to class. Instead, he read passages from his books to them, and sometimes reenacted some of the more dramatic bits. He usually picked Eli to help him with these reconstructions; so far, Eli had been forced to play a simple Transylvanian villager whom Bright had cured of a Babbling Curse, a yeti with a head cold, and a vampire who had been unable to eat anything except lettuce since Bright had dealt with him.

Eli was hauled to the front of the class during their very next Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson, this time acting a werewolf. If he hadn’t had a very good reason for keeping Bright in a good mood, he would have refused to do it.

“Nice loud howl, Eli — exactly — and then, if you’ll believe it, I pounced — like this — slammed him to the floor — thus — with one hand, I managed to hold him down — with my other, I put my wand to his throat — I then screwed up my remaining strength and performed the immensely complex Homorphus Charm — he let out a piteous moan — go on, Eli — higher than that — good — the fur vanished — the fangs shrank — and he turned back into a man. Simple, yet effective — and another village will remember me forever as the hero who delivered them from the monthly terror of werewolf attacks.”

The bell rang and Bright got to his feet.

“Homework — compose a poem about my defeat of the Wagga Wagga Werewolf! Signed copies of Magical Me to the author of the best one!”

The class began to leave. Eli returned to the back of the room, where Leo and Isla were waiting.

“Ready?” Eli muttered.

“Wait till everyone’s gone,” said Isla nervously. “All right …”

She approached Bright’s desk, a piece of paper clutched tightly in her hand, Eli and Leo right behind her.

“Er — Professor Bright?” Isla stammered. “I wanted to — to get this book out of the library. Just for background reading.” She held out the piece of paper, her hand shaking slightly. “But the thing is, it’s in the Restricted Section of the library, so I need a teacher to sign for it — I’m sure it would help me understand what you say in Gadding with Ghouls about slow-acting venoms —”

“Ah, Gadding with Ghouls!” said Bright, taking the note from Isla and smiling widely at her. “Possibly my very favorite book. You enjoyed it?”

“Oh, yes,” said Isla eagerly. “So clever, the way you trapped that last one with the tea-strainer —”

“Well, I’m sure no one will mind me giving the best student of the year a little extra help,” said Bright warmly, and he pulled out an enormous peacock quill. “Yes, nice, isn’t it?” he said, misreading the revolted look on Leo’s face. “I usually save it for book signings.”

He scrawled an enormous loopy signature on the note and handed it back to Isla.

“So, Eli,” said Bright, while Isla folded the note with fumbling fingers and slipped it into her bag. “Tomorrow’s the first Quidditch match of the season, I believe? Gryffindor against Slytherin, is it not? I hear you’re a useful player. I was a Seeker, too. I was asked to try for the National Squad, but preferred to dedicate my life to the eradication of the Dark Forces. Still, if ever you feel the need for a little private training, don’t hesitate to ask. Always happy to pass on my expertise to less able players. …”

Eli made an indistinct noise in his throat and then hurried off after Leo and Isla.

“I don’t believe it,” he said as the three of them examined the signature on the note. “He didn’t even look at the book we wanted.”

“That’s because he’s a brainless git,” said Leo. “But who cares, we’ve got what we needed —”

“He is not a brainless git,” said Isla shrilly as they half ran toward the library.

“Just because he said you were the best student of the year —”

They dropped their voices as they entered the muffled stillness of the library. Madam Quill, the librarian, was a thin, irritable woman who looked like an underfed vulture.

“Moste Potente Potions?” she repeated suspiciously, trying to take the note from Isla; but Isla wouldn’t let go.

“I was wondering if I could keep it,” she said breathlessly.

“Oh, come on,” said Leo, wrenching it from her grasp and thrusting it at Madam Quill. “We’ll get you another autograph. Bright’ll sign anything if it stands still long enough.”

Madam Quill held the note up to the light, as though determined to detect a forgery, but it passed the test. She stalked away between the lofty shelves and returned several minutes later carrying a large and moldy-looking book. Isla put it carefully into her bag and they left, trying not to walk too quickly or look too guilty.

Five minutes later, they were barricaded in Wailing Wren’s out-of-order bathroom once again. Isla had overridden Leo’s objections by pointing out that it was the last place anyone in their right minds would go, so they were guaranteed some privacy. Wailing Wren was crying noisily in her stall, but they were ignoring her, and she them.

Isla opened Moste Potente Potions carefully, and the three of them bent over the damp-spotted pages. It was clear from a glance why it belonged in the Restricted Section. Some of the potions had effects almost too gruesome to think about, and there were some very unpleasant illustrations, which included a man who seemed to have been turned inside out and a witch sprouting several extra pairs of arms out of her head.

“Here it is,” said Isla excitedly as she found the page headed The Polyjuice Potion. It was decorated with drawings of people halfway through transforming into other people. Eli sincerely hoped the artist had imagined the looks of intense pain on their faces.

“This is the most complicated potion I’ve ever seen,” said Isla as they scanned the recipe. “Lacewing flies, leeches, fluxweed, and knotgrass,” she murmured, running her finger down the list of ingredients. “Well, they’re easy enough, they’re in the student store-cupboard, we can help ourselves. … Oooh, look, powdered horn of a bicorn — don’t know where we’re going to get that — shredded skin of a boomslang — that’ll be tricky, too — and of course a bit of whoever we want to change into.”

“Excuse me?” said Leo sharply. “What d’you mean, a bit of whoever we’re changing into? I’m drinking nothing with Lobbe’s toenails in it —”

Isla continued as though she hadn’t heard him.

“We don’t have to worry about that yet, though, because we add those bits last. …”

Leo turned, speechless, to Eli, who had another worry.

“D’you realize how much we’re going to have to steal, Isla? Shredded skin of a boomslang, that’s definitely not in the students’ cupboard. What’re we going to do, break into Thorne’s private stores? I don’t know if this is a good idea. …”

Isla shut the book with a snap.

“Well, if you two are going to chicken out, fine,” she said. There were bright pink patches on her cheeks and her eyes were brighter than usual. “I don’t want to break rules, you know. I think threatening Muggle-borns is far worse than brewing up a difficult potion. But if you don’t want to find out if it’s Mortem, I’ll go straight to Madam Quill now and hand the book back in —”

“I never thought I’d see the day when you’d be persuading us to break rules,” said Leo. “All right, we’ll do it. But not toenails, okay?”

“How long will it take to make, anyway?” said Eli as Isla, looking happier, opened the book again.

“Well, since the fluxweed has got to be picked at the full moon and the lacewings have got to be stewed for twenty-one days … I’d say it’d be ready in about a month, if we can get all the ingredients.”

“A month?” said Leo. “Mortem could have attacked half the Muggle-borns in the school by then!” But Isla’s eyes narrowed dangerously again, and he added swiftly, “But it’s the best plan we’ve got, so full steam ahead, I say.”

However, while Isla was checking that the coast was clear for them to leave the bathroom, Leo muttered to Eli, “It’ll be a lot less hassle if you can just knock Mortem off his broom tomorrow.”



Eli woke early on Saturday morning and lay for a while thinking about the coming Quidditch match. He was nervous, mainly at the thought of what Ash would say if Gryffindor lost, but also at the idea of facing a team mounted on the fastest racing brooms gold could buy. He had never wanted to beat Slytherin so badly. After half an hour of lying there with his insides churning, he got up, dressed, and went down to breakfast early, where he found the rest of the Gryffindor team huddled at the long, empty table, all looking uptight and not speaking much.

As eleven o’clock approached, the whole school started to make its way down to the Quidditch stadium. It was a muggy sort of day with a hint of thunder in the air. Leo and Isla came hurrying over to wish Eli good luck as he entered the locker rooms. The team pulled on their scarlet Gryffindor robes, then sat down to listen to Ash’s usual pre-match pep talk.

“Slytherin has better brooms than us,” he began. “No point denying it. But we’ve got better people on our brooms. We’ve trained harder than they have, we’ve been flying in all weathers —” (“Too true,” muttered Aiden Brunswick. “I haven’t been properly dry since August”) “— and we’re going to make them rue the day they let that little bit of slime, Mortem, buy his way onto their team.”

Chest heaving with emotion, Ash turned to Eli.

“It’ll be down to you, Eli, to show them that a Seeker has to have something more than a rich father. Get to that Snitch before Mortem or die trying, Eli, because we’ve got to win today, we’ve got to.”

“So no pressure, Eli,” said Felix, winking at him.

As they walked out onto the field, a roar of noise greeted them; mainly cheers, because Ravenclaw and Hufflepuff were anxious to see Slytherin beaten, but the Slytherins in the crowd made their boos and hisses heard, too. Madam Feather, the Quidditch teacher, asked Flint and Ash to shake hands, which they did, giving each other threatening stares and gripping rather harder than was necessary.

“On my whistle,” said Madam Feather. “Three … two … one …”

With a roar from the crowd to speed them upward, the fourteen players rose toward the leaden sky. Eli flew higher than any of them, squinting around for the Snitch.

“All right there, Scarhead?” yelled Mortem, shooting underneath him as though to show off the speed of his broom.

Eli had no time to reply. At that very moment, a heavy black Bludger came pelting toward him; he avoided it so narrowly that he felt it ruffle his hair as it passed.

“Close one, Eli!” said Aiden, streaking past him with his club in his hand, ready to knock the Bludger back toward a Slytherin. Eli saw Aiden give the Bludger a powerful whack in the direction of Adrian Gust, but the Bludger changed direction in midair and shot straight for Eli again.

Eli dropped quickly to avoid it, and Aiden managed to hit it hard toward Mortem. Once again, the Bludger swerved like a boomerang and shot at Eli’s head.

Eli put on a burst of speed and zoomed toward the other end of the field. He could hear the Bludger whistling along behind him. What was going on? Bludgers never concentrated on one player like this; it was their job to try and unseat as many people as possible. …

Felix Brunswick was waiting for the Bludger at the other end. Eli ducked as Felix swung at the Bludger with all his might; the Bludger was knocked off course.

“Gotcha!” Felix yelled happily, but he was wrong; as though it was magnetically attracted to Eli, the Bludger pelted after him once more and Eli was forced to fly off at full speed.

It had started to rain; Eli felt heavy drops fall onto his face, splattering onto his glasses. He didn’t have a clue what was going on in the rest of the game until he heard Jordan Lee, who was commentating, say, “Slytherin lead, sixty points to zero —”

The Slytherins’ superior brooms were clearly doing their jobs, and meanwhile the mad Bludger was doing all it could to knock Eli out of the air. Felix and Aiden were now flying so close to him on either side that Eli could see nothing at all except their flailing arms and had no chance to look for the Snitch, let alone catch it.

“Someone’s — tampered — with — this — Bludger —” Felix grunted, swinging his bat with all his might at it as it launched a new attack on Eli.

“We need time out,” said Aiden, trying to signal to Ash and stop the Bludger breaking Eli’s nose at the same time.

Ash had obviously got the message. Madam Feather’s whistle rang out and Eli, Felix, and Aiden dived for the ground, still trying to avoid the mad Bludger.
