“What’s going on?” said Ash as the Gryffindor team huddled together, while Slytherins in the crowd jeered. “We’re being flattened. Felix, Aiden, where were you when that Bludger stopped Angelica scoring?”

“We were twenty feet above her, stopping the other Bludger from murdering Eli, Dorian,” said Aiden angrily. “Someone’s fixed it — it won’t leave Eli alone. It hasn’t gone for anyone else all game. The Slytherins must have done something to it.”

“But the Bludgers have been locked in Madam Feather’s office since our last practice, and there was nothing wrong with them then. …” said Ash, anxiously.

Madam Feather was walking toward them. Over her shoulder, Eli could see the Slytherin team jeering and pointing in his direction.

“Listen,” said Eli as she came nearer and nearer, “with you two flying around me all the time the only way I’m going to catch the Snitch is if it flies up my sleeve. Go back to the rest of the team and let me deal with the rogue one.”

“Don’t be thick,” said Felix. “It’ll take your head off.”

Ash was looking from Eli to the Brunswicks.

“Dorian, this is insane,” said Alicia Windy angrily. “You can’t let Eli deal with that thing on his own. Let’s ask for an inquiry —”

“If we stop now, we’ll have to forfeit the match!” said Eli. “And we’re not losing to Slytherin just because of a crazy Bludger! Come on, Dorian, tell them to leave me alone!”

“This is all your fault,” Aiden said angrily to Ash. “ ‘Get the Snitch or die trying,’ what a stupid thing to tell him —”

Madam Feather had joined them.

“Ready to resume play?” she asked Ash.

Ash looked at the determined look on Eli’s face.

“All right,” he said. “Felix, Aiden, you heard Eli — leave him alone and let him deal with the Bludger on his own.”

The rain was falling more heavily now. On Madam Feather’s whistle, Eli kicked hard into the air and heard the telltale whoosh of the Bludger behind him. Higher and higher Eli climbed; he looped and swooped, spiraled, zigzagged, and rolled. Slightly dizzy, he nevertheless kept his eyes wide open, rain was speckling his glasses and ran up his nostrils as he hung upside down, avoiding another fierce dive from the Bludger. He could hear laughter from the crowd; he knew he must look very stupid, but the rogue Bludger was heavy and couldn’t change direction as quickly as Eli could; he began a kind of roller-coaster ride around the edges of the stadium, squinting through the silver sheets of rain to the Gryffindor goal posts, where Adrian Gust was trying to get past Ash —

A whistling in Eli’s ear told him the Bludger had just missed him again; he turned right over and sped in the opposite direction.

“Training for the ballet, Ember?” yelled Mortem as Eli was forced to do a stupid kind of twirl in midair to dodge the Bludger, and he fled, the Bludger trailing a few feet behind him; and then, glaring back at Mortem in hatred, he saw it — the Golden Snitch. It was hovering inches above Mortem’s left ear — and Mortem, busy laughing at Eli, hadn’t seen it.

For an agonizing moment, Eli hung in midair, not daring to speed toward Mortem in case he looked up and saw the Snitch.

WHAM.

He had stayed still a second too long. The Bludger had hit him at last, smashed into his elbow, and Eli felt his arm break. Dimly, dazed by the searing pain in his arm, he slid sideways on his rain-drenched broom, one knee still crooked over it, his right arm dangling useless at his side — the Bludger came pelting back for a second attack, this time aiming at his face — Eli swerved out of the way, one idea firmly lodged in his numb brain: get to Mortem.

Through a haze of rain and pain he dived for the shimmering, sneering face below him and saw its eyes widen with fear: Mortem thought Eli was attacking him.

“What the —” he gasped, careening out of Eli’s way.

Eli took his remaining hand off his broom and made a wild snatch; he felt his fingers close on the cold Snitch but was now only gripping the broom with his legs, and there was a yell from the crowd below as he headed straight for the ground, trying hard not to pass out.

With a splattering thud he hit the mud and rolled off his broom. His arm was hanging at a very strange angle; riddled with pain, he heard, as though from a distance, a good deal of whistling and shouting. He focused on the Snitch clutched in his good hand.

“Aha,” he said vaguely. “We’ve won.”

And he fainted.

He came around, rain falling on his face, still lying on the field, with someone leaning over him. He saw a glitter of teeth.

“Oh, no, not you,” he moaned.

“Doesn’t know what he’s saying,” said Bright loudly to the anxious crowd of Gryffindors pressing around them. “Not to worry, Eli. I’m about to fix your arm.”

“No!” said Eli. “I’ll keep it like this, thanks. …”

He tried to sit up, but the pain was terrible. He heard a familiar clicking noise nearby.

“I don’t want a photo of this, Colin,” he said loudly.

“Lie back, Eli,” said Bright soothingly. “It’s a simple charm I’ve used countless times —”

“Why can’t I just go to the hospital wing?” said Eli through clenched teeth.

“He should really, Professor,” said a muddy Ash, who couldn’t help grinning even though his Seeker was injured. “Great capture, Eli, really spectacular, your best yet, I’d say —”

Through the thicket of legs around him, Eli spotted Felix and Aiden Brunswick, wrestling the rogue Bludger into a box. It was still putting up a terrific fight.

“Stand back,” said Bright, who was rolling up his jade-green sleeves.

“No — don’t —” said Eli weakly, but Bright was twirling his wand and a second later had directed it straight at Eli’s arm.

A strange and unpleasant sensation started at Eli’s shoulder and spread all the way down to his fingertips. It felt as though his arm was being deflated. He didn’t dare look at what was happening. He had shut his eyes, his face turned away from his arm, but his worst fears were realized as the people above him gasped and Colin Ripple began clicking away madly. His arm didn’t hurt anymore — nor did it feel remotely like an arm.

“Ah,” said Bright. “Yes. Well, that can sometimes happen. But the point is, the bones are no longer broken. That’s the thing to bear in mind. So, Eli, just toddle up to the hospital wing — ah, Mr. Brunswick, Miss Hawke, would you escort him? — and Madam Fern will be able to — er — tidy you up a bit.”

As Eli got to his feet, he felt strangely lopsided. Taking a deep breath he looked down at his right side. What he saw nearly made him pass out again.

Poking out of the end of his robes was what looked like a thick, flesh-colored rubber glove. He tried to move his fingers. Nothing happened.

Bright hadn’t mended Eli’s bones. He had removed them.



Madam Fern wasn’t at all pleased.

“You should have come straight to me!” she raged, holding up the sad, limp remainder of what, half an hour before, had been a working arm. “I can mend bones in a second — but growing them back —”

“You will be able to, won’t you?” said Eli desperately.

“I’ll be able to, certainly, but it will be painful,” said Madam Fern grimly, throwing Eli a pair of pajamas. “You’ll have to stay the night. …”

Isla waited outside the curtain drawn around Eli’s bed while Leo helped him into his pajamas. It took a while to stuff the rubbery, boneless arm into a sleeve.

“How can you stick up for Bright now, Isla, eh?” Leo called through the curtain as he pulled Eli’s limp fingers through the cuff. “If Eli had wanted deboning he would have asked.”

“Anyone can make a mistake,” said Isla. “And it doesn’t hurt anymore, does it, Eli?”

“No,” said Eli, getting into bed. “But it doesn’t do anything else either.”

As he swung himself onto the bed, his arm flapped pointlessly.

Isla and Madam Fern came around the curtain. Madam Fern was holding a large bottle of something labeled Skele-Gro.

“You’re in for a rough night,” she said, pouring out a steaming beakerful and handing it to him. “Regrowing bones is a nasty business.”

So was taking the Skele-Gro. It burned Eli’s mouth and throat as it went down, making him cough and splutter. Still tut-tutting about dangerous sports and inept teachers, Madam Fern retreated, leaving Leo and Isla to help Eli gulp down some water.

“We won, though,” said Leo, a grin breaking across his face. “That was some catch you made. Mortem’s face … he looked ready to kill. …”

“I want to know how he fixed that Bludger,” said Isla darkly.

“We can add that to the list of questions we’ll ask him when we’ve taken the Polyjuice Potion,” said Eli, sinking back onto his pillows. “I hope it tastes better than this stuff. …”

“If it’s got bits of Slytherins in it? You’ve got to be joking,” said Leo.

The door of the hospital wing burst open at that moment. Filthy and soaking wet, the rest of the Gryffindor team had arrived to see Eli.

“Unbelievable flying, Eli,” said Aiden. “I’ve just seen Marcus Flint yelling at Mortem. Something about having the Snitch on top of his head and not noticing. Mortem didn’t seem too happy.”

They had brought cakes, sweets, and bottles of pumpkin juice; they gathered around Eli’s bed and were just getting started on what promised to be a good party when Madam Fern came storming over, shouting, “This boy needs rest, he’s got thirty-three bones to regrow! Out! OUT!”

And Eli was left alone, with nothing to distract him from the stabbing pains in his limp arm.



Hours and hours later, Eli woke quite suddenly in the pitch blackness and gave a small yelp of pain: His arm now felt full of large splinters. For a second, he thought that was what had woken him. Then, with a thrill of horror, he realized that someone was sponging his forehead in the dark.

“Get off!” he said loudly, and then, “Nobby!”

The house-elf’s goggling tennis ball eyes were peering at Eli through the darkness. A single tear was running down his long, pointed nose.

“Eli Ember came back to school,” he whispered miserably. “Nobby warned and warned Eli Ember. Ah sir, why didn’t you heed Nobby? Why didn’t Eli Ember go back home when he missed the train?”

Eli heaved himself up on his pillows and pushed Nobby’s sponge away.

“What’re you doing here?” he said. “And how did you know I missed the train?”

Nobby’s lip trembled and Eli was seized by a sudden suspicion.

“It was you!” he said slowly. “You stopped the barrier from letting us through!”

“Indeed yes, sir,” said Nobby, nodding his head vigorously, ears flapping. “Nobby hid and watched for Eli Ember and sealed the gateway and Nobby had to iron his hands afterward” — he showed Eli ten long, bandaged fingers — “but Nobby didn’t care, sir, for he thought Eli Ember was safe, and never did Nobby dream that Eli Ember would get to school another way!”

He was rocking backward and forward, shaking his ugly head.

“Nobby was so shocked when he heard Eli Ember was back at Hogwarts, he let his master’s dinner burn! Such a flogging Nobby never had, sir. …”

Eli slumped back onto his pillows.

“You nearly got Leo and me expelled,” he said fiercely. “You’d better get lost before my bones come back, Nobby, or I might strangle you.”

Nobby smiled weakly.

“Nobby is used to death threats, sir. Nobby gets them five times a day at home.”

He blew his nose on a corner of the filthy pillowcase he wore, looking so pathetic that Eli felt his anger ebb away in spite of himself.

“Why d’you wear that thing, Nobby?” he asked curiously.

“This, sir?” said Nobby, plucking at the pillowcase. “ ’Tis a mark of the house-elf’s enslavement, sir. Nobby can only be freed if his masters present him with clothes, sir. The family is careful not to pass Nobby even a sock, sir, for then he would be free to leave their house forever.”

Nobby mopped his bulging eyes and said suddenly, “Eli Ember must go home! Nobby thought his Bludger would be enough to make —”

“Your Bludger?” said Eli, anger rising once more. “What d’you mean, your Bludger? You made that Bludger try and kill me?”

“Not kill you, sir, never kill you!” said Nobby, shocked. “Nobby wants to save Eli Ember’s life! Better sent home, grievously injured, than remain here, sir! Nobby only wanted Eli Ember hurt enough to be sent home!”

“Oh, is that all?” said Eli angrily. “I don’t suppose you’re going to tell me why you wanted me sent home in pieces?”

“Ah, if Eli Ember only knew!” Nobby groaned, more tears dripping onto his ragged pillowcase. “If he knew what he means to us, to the lowly, the enslaved, we dregs of the magical world! Nobby remembers how it was when He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named was at the height of his powers, sir! We house-elves were treated like vermin, sir! Of course, Nobby is still treated like that, sir,” he admitted, drying his face on the pillowcase. “But mostly, sir, life has improved for my kind since you triumphed over He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named. Eli Ember survived, and the Dark Lord’s power was broken, and it was a new dawn, sir, and Eli Ember shone like a beacon of hope for those of us who thought the Dark days would never end, sir. … And now, at Hogwarts, terrible things are to happen, are perhaps happening already, and Nobby cannot let Eli Ember stay here now that history is to repeat itself, now that the Chamber of Secrets is open once more —”

Nobby froze, horrorstruck, then grabbed Eli’s water jug from his bedside table and cracked it over his own head, toppling out of sight. A second later, he crawled back onto the bed, cross-eyed, muttering, “Bad Nobby, very bad Nobby …”

“So there is a Chamber of Secrets?” Eli whispered. “And — did you say it’s been opened before? Tell me, Nobby!”

He seized the elf’s bony wrist as Nobby’s hand inched toward the water jug. “But I’m not Muggle-born — how can I be in danger from the Chamber?”

“Ah, sir, ask no more, ask no more of poor Nobby,” stammered the elf, his eyes huge in the dark. “Dark deeds are planned in this place, but Eli Ember must not be here when they happen — go home, Eli Ember, go home. Eli Ember must not meddle in this, sir, ’tis too dangerous —”

“Who is it, Nobby?” Eli said, keeping a firm hold on Nobby’s wrist to stop him from hitting himself with the water jug again. “Who’s opened it? Who opened it last time?”

“Nobby can’t, sir, Nobby can’t, Nobby mustn’t tell!” squealed the elf. “Go home, Eli Ember, go home!”

“I’m not going anywhere!” said Eli fiercely. “One of my best friends is Muggle-born; she’ll be first in line if the Chamber really has been opened —”

“Eli Ember risks his own life for his friends!” moaned Nobby in a kind of miserable ecstasy. “So noble! So valiant! But he must save himself, he must, Eli Ember must not —”

Nobby suddenly froze, his bat ears quivering. Eli heard it, too. There were footsteps coming down the passageway outside.

“Nobby must go!” breathed the elf, terrified. There was a loud crack, and Eli’s fist was suddenly clenched on thin air. He slumped back into bed, his eyes on the dark doorway to the hospital wing as the footsteps drew nearer.

Next moment, Starling was backing into the dormitory, wearing a long woolly dressing gown and a nightcap. He was carrying one end of what looked like a statue. Professor Falconer appeared a second later, carrying its feet. Together, they heaved it onto a bed.

“Get Madam Fern,” whispered Starling, and Professor Falconer hurried past the end of Eli’s bed out of sight. Eli lay quite still, pretending to be asleep. He heard urgent voices, and then Professor Falconer swept back into view, closely followed by Madam Fern, who was pulling a cardigan on over her nightdress. He heard a sharp intake of breath.

“What happened?” Madam Fern whispered to Starling, bending over the statue on the bed.

“Another attack,” said Starling. “Helena found him on the stairs.”

“There was a bunch of grapes next to him,” said Professor Falconer. “We think he was trying to sneak up here to visit Ember.”

Eli’s stomach gave a horrible lurch. Slowly and carefully, he raised himself a few inches so he could look at the statue on the bed. A ray of moonlight lay across its staring face.

It was Colin Ripple. His eyes were wide and his hands were stuck up in front of him, holding his camera.

“Petrified?” whispered Madam Fern.

“Yes,” said Professor Falconer. “But I shudder to think … If Albus hadn’t been on the way downstairs for hot chocolate — who knows what might have —”

The three of them stared down at Colin. Then Starling leaned forward and wrenched the camera out of Colin’s rigid grip.

“You don’t think he managed to get a picture of his attacker?” said Professor Falconer eagerly.

Starling didn’t answer. He opened the back of the camera.

“Good gracious!” said Madam Fern.

A jet of steam had hissed out of the camera. Eli, three beds away, caught the acrid smell of burnt plastic.

“Melted,” said Madam Fern wonderingly. “All melted …”

“What does this mean, Albus?” Professor Falconer asked urgently.

“It means,” said Starling, “that the Chamber of Secrets is indeed open again.”

Madam Fern clapped a hand to her mouth. Professor Falconer stared at Starling.

“But, Albus … surely … who?”

“The question is not who,” said Starling, his eyes on Colin. “The question is, how. …”

And from what Eli could see of Professor Falconer’s shadowy face, she didn’t understand this any better than he did.





Chapter 11



The Dueling Club





Eli woke up on Sunday morning to find the dormitory blazing with winter sunlight and his arm reboned but very stiff. He sat up quickly and looked over at Colin’s bed, but it had been blocked from view by the high curtains Eli had changed behind yesterday. Seeing that he was awake, Madam Fern came bustling over with a breakfast tray and then began bending and stretching his arm and fingers.

“All in order,” she said as he clumsily fed himself porridge left-handed. “When you’ve finished eating, you may leave.”

Eli dressed as quickly as he could and hurried off to Gryffindor Tower, desperate to tell Leo and Isla about Colin and Nobby, but they weren’t there. Eli left to look for them, wondering where they could have got to and feeling slightly hurt that they weren’t interested in whether he had his bones back or not.

As Eli passed the library, Evan Brunswick strolled out of it, looking in far better spirits than last time they’d met.

“Oh, hello, Eli,” he said. “Excellent flying yesterday, really excellent. Gryffindor has just taken the lead for the House Cup — you earned fifty points!”

“You haven’t seen Leo or Isla, have you?” said Eli.

“No, I haven’t,” said Evan, his smile fading. “I hope Leo’s not in another girls’ toilet. …”

Eli forced a laugh, watched Evan walk out of sight, and then headed straight for Wailing Wren’s bathroom. He couldn’t see why Leo and Isla would be in there again, but after making sure that neither Acheron nor any prefects were around, he opened the door and heard their voices coming from a locked stall.

“It’s me,” he said, closing the door behind him. There was a clunk, a splash, and a gasp from within the stall and he saw Isla’s eye peering through the keyhole.

“Eli!” she said. “You gave us such a fright — come in — how’s your arm?”

“Fine,” said Eli, squeezing into the stall. An old cauldron was perched on the toilet, and a crackling from under the rim told Eli they had lit a fire beneath it. Conjuring up portable, waterproof fires was a speciality of Isla’s.

“We’d’ve come to meet you, but we decided to get started on the Polyjuice Potion,” Leo explained as Eli, with difficulty, locked the stall again. “We’ve decided this is the safest place to hide it.”

Eli started to tell them about Colin, but Isla interrupted.

“We already know — we heard Professor Falconer telling Professor Twig this morning. That’s why we decided we’d better get going —”

“The sooner we get a confession out of Mortem, the better,” snarled Leo. “D’you know what I think? He was in such a foul temper after the Quidditch match, he took it out on Colin.”

“There’s something else,” said Eli, watching Isla tearing bundles of knotgrass and throwing them into the potion. “Nobby came to visit me in the middle of the night.”

Leo and Isla looked up, amazed. Eli told them everything Nobby had told him — or hadn’t told him. Isla and Leo listened with their mouths open.

“The Chamber of Secrets has been opened before?” Isla said.

“This settles it,” said Leo in a triumphant voice. “Caspian Mortem must’ve opened the Chamber when he was at school here and now he’s told dear old Jasper how to do it. It’s obvious. Wish Nobby’d told you what kind of monster’s in there, though. I want to know how come nobody’s noticed it sneaking around the school.”

“Maybe it can make itself invisible,” said Isla, prodding leeches to the bottom of the cauldron. “Or maybe it can disguise itself — pretend to be a suit of armor or something — I’ve read about Chameleon Ghouls —”

“You read too much, Isla,” said Leo, pouring dead lacewings on top of the leeches. He crumpled up the empty lacewing bag and looked at Eli.

“So Nobby stopped us from getting on the train and broke your arm. …” He shook his head. “You know what, Eli? If he doesn’t stop trying to save your life he’s going to kill you.”



* * *



The news that Colin Ripple had been attacked and was now lying as though dead in the hospital wing had spread through the entire school by Monday morning. The air was suddenly thick with rumor and suspicion. The first years were now moving around the castle in tight-knit groups, as though scared they would be attacked if they ventured forth alone.

Lila Brunswick, who sat next to Colin Ripple in Charms, was distraught, but Eli felt that Felix and Aiden were going the wrong way about cheering her up. They were taking turns covering themselves with fur or boils and jumping out at her from behind statues. They only stopped when Evan, apoplectic with rage, told them he was going to write to Mrs. Brunswick and tell her Lila was having nightmares.

Meanwhile, hidden from the teachers, a roaring trade in talismans, amulets, and other protective devices was sweeping the school. Nolan Broadfoot bought a large, evil-smelling green onion, a pointed purple crystal, and a rotting newt tail before the other Gryffindor boys pointed out that he was in no danger; he was a pureblood, and therefore unlikely to be attacked.

“They went for Acheron first,” Nolan said, his round face fearful. “And everyone knows I’m almost a Squib.”



In the second week of December Professor Falconer came around as usual, collecting names of those who would be staying at school for Christmas. Eli, Leo, and Isla signed her list; they had heard that Mortem was staying, which struck them as very suspicious. The holidays would be the perfect time to use the Polyjuice Potion and try to worm a confession out of him.

Unfortunately, the potion was only half finished. They still needed the bicorn horn and the boomslang skin, and the only place they were going to get them was from Thorne’s private stores. Eli privately felt he’d rather face Slytherin’s legendary monster than let Thorne catch him robbing his office.

“What we need,” said Isla briskly as Thursday afternoon’s double Potions lesson loomed nearer, “is a diversion. Then one of us can sneak into Thorne’s office and take what we need.”

Eli and Leo looked at her nervously.

“I think I’d better do the actual stealing,” Isla continued in a matter-of-fact tone. “You two will be expelled if you get into any more trouble, and I’ve got a clean record. So all you need to do is cause enough mayhem to keep Thorne busy for five minutes or so.”

Eli smiled feebly. Deliberately causing mayhem in Thorne’s Potions class was about as safe as poking a sleeping dragon in the eye.

Potions lessons took place in one of the large dungeons. Thursday afternoon’s lesson proceeded in the usual way. Twenty cauldrons stood steaming between the wooden desks, on which stood brass scales and jars of ingredients. Thorne prowled through the fumes, making waspish remarks about the Gryffindors’ work while the Slytherins sniggered appreciatively. Jasper Mortem, who was Thorne’s favorite student, kept flicking puffer-fish eyes at Leo and Eli, who knew that if they retaliated they would get detention faster than you could say “Unfair.”

Eli’s Swelling Solution was far too runny, but he had his mind on more important things. He was waiting for Isla’s signal, and he hardly listened as Thorne paused to sneer at his watery potion. When Thorne turned and walked off to bully Nolan, Isla caught Eli’s eye and nodded.

Eli ducked swiftly down behind his cauldron, pulled one of Felix’s Filibuster fireworks out of his pocket, and gave it a quick prod with his wand. The firework began to fizz and sputter. Knowing he had only seconds, Eli straightened up, took aim, and lobbed it into the air; it landed right on target in Gore’s cauldron.

Gore’s potion exploded, showering the whole class. People shrieked as splashes of the Swelling Solution hit them. Mortem got a faceful and his nose began to swell like a balloon; Gore blundered around, his hands over his eyes, which had expanded to the size of a dinner plate — Thorne was trying to restore calm and find out what had happened. Through the confusion, Eli saw Isla slip quietly into Thorne’s office.

“Silence! SILENCE!” Thorne roared. “Anyone who has been splashed, come here for a Deflating Draught — when I find out who did this —”

Eli tried not to laugh as he watched Mortem hurry forward, his head drooping with the weight of a nose like a small melon. As half the class lumbered up to Thorne’s desk, some weighted down with arms like clubs, others unable to talk through gigantic puffed-up lips, Eli saw Isla slide back into the dungeon, the front of her robes bulging.

When everyone had taken a swig of antidote and the various swellings had subsided, Thorne swept over to Gore’s cauldron and scooped out the twisted black remains of the firework. There was a sudden hush.

“If I ever find out who threw this,” Thorne whispered, “I shall make sure that person is expelled.”

Eli arranged his face into what he hoped was a puzzled expression. Thorne was looking right at him, and the bell that rang ten minutes later could not have been more welcome.

“He knew it was me,” Eli told Leo and Isla as they hurried back to Wailing Wren’s bathroom. “I could tell.”

Isla threw the new ingredients into the cauldron and began to stir feverishly.

“It’ll be ready in two weeks,” she said happily.

“Thorne can’t prove it was you,” said Leo reassuringly to Eli. “What can he do?”

“Knowing Thorne, something foul,” said Eli as the potion frothed and bubbled.



A week later, Eli, Leo, and Isla were walking across the entrance hall when they saw a small knot of people gathered around the notice board, reading a piece of parchment that had just been pinned up. Liam Finnegan and Dean Hunter beckoned them over, looking excited.

“They’re starting a Dueling Club!” said Liam. “First meeting tonight! I wouldn’t mind dueling lessons; they might come in handy one of these days. …”

“What, you reckon Slytherin’s monster can duel?” said Leo, but he, too, read the sign with interest.

“Could be useful,” he said to Eli and Isla as they went into dinner. “Shall we go?”

Eli and Isla were all for it, so at eight o’clock that evening they hurried back to the Great Hall. The long dining tables had vanished and a golden stage had appeared along one wall, lit by thousands of candles floating overhead. The ceiling was velvety black once more and most of the school seemed to be packed beneath it, all carrying their wands and looking excited.

“I wonder who’ll be teaching us?” said Isla as they edged into the chattering crowd. “Someone told me Twig was a dueling champion when he was young — maybe it’ll be him.”

“As long as it’s not —” Eli began, but he ended on a groan: Julian Bright was walking onto the stage, resplendent in robes of deep plum and accompanied by none other than Thorne, wearing his usual black.

Bright waved an arm for silence and called, “Gather round, gather round! Can everyone see me? Can you all hear me? Excellent!

“Now, Professor Starling has granted me permission to start this little dueling club, to train you all in case you ever need to defend yourselves as I myself have done on countless occasions — for full details, see my published works.

“Let me introduce my assistant, Professor Thorne,” said Bright, flashing a wide smile. “He tells me he knows a tiny little bit about dueling himself and has sportingly agreed to help me with a short demonstration before we begin. Now, I don’t want any of you youngsters to worry — you’ll still have your Potions master when I’m through with him, never fear!”

“Wouldn’t it be good if they finished each other off?” Leo muttered in Eli’s ear.

Thorne’s upper lip was curling. Eli wondered why Bright was still smiling; if Thorne had been looking at him like that he’d have been running as fast as he could in the opposite direction.

Bright and Thorne turned to face each other and bowed; at least, Bright did, with much twirling of his hands, whereas Thorne jerked his head irritably. Then they raised their wands like swords in front of them.

“As you see, we are holding our wands in the accepted combative position,” Bright told the silent crowd. “On the count of three, we will cast our first spells. Neither of us will be aiming to kill, of course.”

“I wouldn’t bet on that,” Eli murmured, watching Thorne baring his teeth.

“One — two — three —”

Both of them swung their wands above their heads and pointed them at their opponent; Thorne cried: “Expelliarmus!” There was a dazzling flash of scarlet light and Bright was blasted off his feet: He flew backward off the stage, smashed into the wall, and slid down it to sprawl on the floor.

Mortem and some of the other Slytherins cheered. Isla was dancing on tiptoes. “Do you think he’s all right?” she squealed through her fingers.

“Who cares?” said Eli and Leo together.

Bright was getting unsteadily to his feet. His hat had fallen off and his wavy hair was standing on end.

“Well, there you have it!” he said, tottering back onto the platform. “That was a Disarming Charm — as you see, I’ve lost my wand — ah, thank you, Miss Brown — yes, an excellent idea to show them that, Professor Thorne, but if you don’t mind my saying so, it was very obvious what you were about to do. If I had wanted to stop you it would have been only too easy — however, I felt it would be instructive to let them see …”

Thorne was looking murderous. Possibly Bright had noticed, because he said, “Enough demonstrating! I’m going to come amongst you now and put you all into pairs. Professor Thorne, if you’d like to help me —”

They moved through the crowd, matching up partners. Bright teamed Nolan with Justin Winge, but Thorne reached Eli and Leo first.

“Time to split up the dream team, I think,” he sneered. “Brunswick, you can partner Finnigan. Ember —”

Eli moved automatically toward Isla.

“I don’t think so,” said Thorne, smiling coldly. “Mr. Mortem, come over here. Let’s see what you make of the famous Ember. And you, Miss Hawke — you can partner Miss Bulstrode.”

Mortem strutted over, smirking. Behind him walked a Slytherin girl who reminded Eli of a picture he’d seen in Holidays with Hags. She was large and square and her heavy jaw jutted aggressively. Isla gave her a weak smile that she did not return.

“Face your partners!” called Bright, back on the platform. “And bow!”

Eli and Mortem barely inclined their heads, not taking their eyes off each other.

“Wands at the ready!” shouted Bright. “When I count to three, cast your charms to disarm your opponents — only to disarm them — we don’t want any accidents — one … two … three —”

Eli swung his wand high, but Mortem had already started on “two”: His spell hit Eli so hard he felt as though he’d been hit over the head with a saucepan. He stumbled, but everything still seemed to be working, and wasting no more time, Eli pointed his wand straight at Mortem and shouted, “Rictusempra!”

A jet of silver light hit Mortem in the stomach and he doubled up, wheezing.

“I said disarm only!” Bright shouted in alarm over the heads of the battling crowd, as Mortem sank to his knees; Eli had hit him with a Tickling Charm, and he could barely move for laughing. Eli hung back, with a vague feeling it would be unsporting to bewitch Mortem while he was on the floor, but this was a mistake; gasping for breath, Mortem pointed his wand at Eli’s knees, choked, “Tarantallegra!” and the next second Eli’s legs began to jerk around out of his control in a kind of quickstep.

“Stop! Stop!” screamed Bright, but Thorne took charge.

“Finite Incantatem!” he shouted; Eli’s feet stopped dancing, Mortem stopped laughing, and they were able to look up.

A haze of greenish smoke was hovering over the scene. Both Nolan and Justin were lying on the floor, panting; Leo was holding up an ashen-faced Liam, apologizing for whatever his broken wand had done; but Isla and Millicent Bassett were still moving; Millicent had Isla in a headlock and Isla was whimpering in pain; both their wands lay forgotten on the floor. Eli leapt forward and pulled Millicent off. It was difficult: She was a lot bigger than he was.

“Dear, dear,” said Bright, skittering through the crowd, looking at the aftermath of the duels. “Up you go, MacDougal. … Careful there, Miss Lark. … Pinch it hard, it’ll stop bleeding in a second, Buckle —

“I think I’d better teach you how to block unfriendly spells,” said Bright, standing flustered in the midst of the hall. He glanced at Thorne, whose black eyes glinted, and looked quickly away. “Let’s have a volunteer pair — Broadfoot and Finch-Fletchley, how about you —”

“A bad idea, Professor Bright,” said Thorne, gliding over like a large and malevolent bat. “Broadfoot causes devastation with the simplest spells. We’ll be sending what’s left of Finch-Fletchley up to the hospital wing in a matchbox.” Nolan’s round, pink face went pinker. “How about Mortem and Ember?” said Thorne with a twisted smile.

“Excellent idea!” said Bright, gesturing Eli and Mortem into the middle of the hall as the crowd backed away to give them room.

“Now, Eli,” said Bright. “When Jasper points his wand at you, you do this.”

He raised his own wand, attempted a complicated sort of wiggling action, and dropped it. Thorne smirked as Bright quickly picked it up, saying, “Whoops — my wand is a little overexcited —”

Thorne moved closer to Mortem, bent down, and whispered something in his ear. Mortem smirked, too. Eli looked up nervously at Bright and said, “Professor, could you show me that blocking thing again?”

“Scared?” muttered Mortem, so that Bright couldn’t hear him.

“You wish,” said Eli out of the corner of his mouth.

Bright cuffed Eli merrily on the shoulder. “Just do what I did, Eli!”

“What, drop my wand?”

But Bright wasn’t listening.

“Three — two — one — go!” he shouted.

Mortem raised his wand quickly and bellowed, “Serpensortia!”

The end of his wand exploded. Eli watched, aghast, as a long black snake shot out of it, fell heavily onto the floor between them, and raised itself, ready to strike. There were screams as the crowd backed swiftly away, clearing the floor.

“Don’t move, Ember,” said Thorne lazily, clearly enjoying the sight of Eli standing motionless, eye to eye with the angry snake. “I’ll get rid of it. …”

“Allow me!” shouted Bright. He brandished his wand at the snake and there was a loud bang; the snake, instead of vanishing, flew ten feet into the air and fell back to the floor with a loud smack. Enraged, hissing furiously, it slithered straight toward Justin Winge and raised itself again, fangs exposed, poised to strike.

Eli wasn’t sure what made him do it. He wasn’t even aware of deciding to do it. All he knew was that his legs were carrying him forward as though he was on casters and that he had shouted stupidly at the snake, “Leave him alone!” And miraculously — inexplicably — the snake slumped to the floor, docile as a thick, black garden hose, its eyes now on Eli. Eli felt the fear drain out of him. He knew the snake wouldn’t attack anyone now, though how he knew it, he couldn’t have explained.

He looked up at Justin, grinning, expecting to see Justin looking relieved, or puzzled, or even grateful — but certainly not angry and scared.

“What do you think you’re playing at?” he shouted, and before Eli could say anything, Justin had turned and stormed out of the hall.

Thorne stepped forward, waved his wand, and the snake vanished in a small puff of black smoke. Thorne, too, was looking at Eli in an unexpected way: It was a shrewd and calculating look, and Eli didn’t like it. He was also dimly aware of an ominous muttering all around the walls. Then he felt a tugging on the back of his robes.

“Come on,” said Leo’s voice in his ear. “Move — come on —”

Leo steered him out of the hall, Isla hurrying alongside them. As they went through the doors, the people on either side drew away as though they were frightened of catching something. Eli didn’t have a clue what was going on, and neither Leo nor Isla explained anything until they had dragged him all the way up to the empty Gryffindor common room. Then Leo pushed Eli into an armchair and said, “You’re a Parselmouth. Why didn’t you tell us?”

“I’m a what?” said Eli.

“A Parselmouth!” said Leo. “You can talk to snakes!”

“I know,” said Eli. “I mean, that’s only the second time I’ve ever done it. I accidentally set a boa constrictor on my cousin Carson at the zoo once — long story — but it was telling me it had never seen Brazil and I sort of set it free without meaning to — that was before I knew I was a wizard —”

“A boa constrictor told you it had never seen Brazil?” Leo repeated faintly.

“So?” said Eli. “I bet loads of people here can do it.”

“Oh, no they can’t,” said Leo. “It’s not a very common gift. Eli, this is bad.”

“What’s bad?” said Eli, starting to feel quite angry. “What’s wrong with everyone? Listen, if I hadn’t told that snake not to attack Justin —”

“Oh, that’s what you said to it?”

“What d’you mean? You were there — you heard me —”

“I heard you speaking Parseltongue,” said Leo. “Snake language. You could have been saying anything — no wonder Justin panicked, you sounded like you were egging the snake on or something — it was creepy, you know —”

Eli gaped at him.

“I spoke a different language? But — I didn’t realize — how can I speak a language without knowing I can speak it?”

Leo shook his head. Both he and Isla were looking as though someone had died. Eli couldn’t see what was so terrible.

“D’you want to tell me what’s wrong with stopping a massive snake biting off Justin’s head?” he said. “What does it matter how I did it as long as Justin doesn’t have to join the Headless Hunt?”

“It matters,” said Isla, speaking at last in a hushed voice, “because being able to talk to snakes was what Salvatore Serpentine was famous for. That’s why the symbol of Slytherin House is a serpent.”

Eli’s mouth fell open.

“Exactly,” said Leo. “And now the whole school’s going to think you’re his great-great-great-great-grandson or something —”

“But I’m not,” said Eli, with a panic he couldn’t quite explain.

“You’ll find that hard to prove,” said Isla. “He lived about a thousand years ago; for all we know, you could be.”



* * *



Eli lay awake for hours that night. Through a gap in the curtains around his four-poster he watched snow starting to drift past the tower window and wondered …

Could he be a descendant of Salvatore Serpentine? He didn’t know anything about his father’s family, after all. The Powells had always forbidden questions about his wizarding relatives.

Quietly, Eli tried to say something in Parseltongue. The words wouldn’t come. It seemed he had to be face-to-face with a snake to do it.

But I’m in Gryffindor, Eli thought. The Sorting Hat wouldn’t have put me in here if I had Slytherin blood. …

Ah, said a nasty little voice in his brain, but the Sorting Hat wanted to put you in Slytherin, don’t you remember?

Eli turned over. He’d see Justin the next day in Herbology and he’d explain that he’d been calling the snake off, not egging it on, which (he thought angrily, pummeling his pillow) any fool should have realized.



By next morning, however, the snow that had begun in the night had turned into a blizzard so thick that the last Herbology lesson of the term was canceled: Professor Sage wanted to fit socks and scarves on the Mandrakes, a tricky operation she would entrust to no one else, now that it was so important for the Mandrakes to grow quickly and revive Mrs. Whiskers and Colin Ripple.

Eli fretted about this next to the fire in the Gryffindor common room, while Leo and Isla used their time off to play a game of wizard chess.

“For heaven’s sake, Eli,” said Isla, exasperated, as one of Leo’s bishops wrestled her knight off his horse and dragged him off the board. “Go and find Justin if it’s so important to you.”

So Eli got up and left through the portrait hole, wondering where Justin might be.

The castle was darker than it usually was in daytime because of the thick, swirling gray snow at every window. Shivering, Eli walked past classrooms where lessons were taking place, catching snatches of what was happening within. Professor Falconer was shouting at someone who, by the sound of it, had turned his friend into a badger. Resisting the urge to take a look, Eli walked on by, thinking that Justin might be using his free time to catch up on some work, and deciding to check the library first.

A group of the Hufflepuffs who should have been in Herbology were indeed sitting at the back of the library, but they didn’t seem to be working. Between the long lines of high bookshelves, Eli could see that their heads were close together and they were having what looked like an absorbing conversation. He couldn’t see whether Justin was among them. He was walking toward them when something of what they were saying met his ears, and he paused to listen, hidden in the Invisibility section.

“So anyway,” a stout boy was saying, “I told Justin to hide up in our dormitory. I mean to say, if Ember’s marked him down as his next victim, it’s best if he keeps a low profile for a while. Of course, Justin’s been waiting for something like this to happen ever since he let slip to Ember he was Muggle-born. Justin actually told him he’d been down for Eton. That’s not the kind of thing you bandy about with Slytherin’s heir on the loose, is it?”

“You definitely think it is Ember, then, Ernest?” said a girl with blonde pigtails anxiously.

“Hanna,” said the stout boy solemnly, “he’s a Parselmouth. Everyone knows that’s the mark of a Dark wizard. Have you ever heard of a decent one who could talk to snakes? They called Slytherin himself Serpent-tongue.”

There was some heavy murmuring at this, and Ernest went on, “Remember what was written on the wall? Enemies of the Heir, Beware. Ember had some sort of run-in with Acheron. Next thing we know, Acheron’s cat’s attacked. That first year, Ripple, was annoying Ember at the Quidditch match, taking pictures of him while he was lying in the mud. Next thing we know — Ripple’s been attacked.”

“He always seems so nice, though,” said Hanna uncertainly, “and, well, he’s the one who made That-Which-Is-Unnamed disappear. He can’t be all bad, can he?”

Ernest lowered his voice mysteriously, the Hufflepuffs bent closer, and Eli edged nearer so that he could catch Ernest’s words.

“No one knows how he survived that attack by That-Which-Is-Unnamed. I mean to say, he was only a baby when it happened. He should have been blasted into smithereens. Only a really powerful Dark wizard could have survived a curse like that.” He dropped his voice until it was barely more than a whisper, and said, “That’s probably why That-Which-Is-Unnamed wanted to kill him in the first place. Didn’t want another Dark Lord competing with him. I wonder what other powers Ember’s been hiding?”

Eli couldn’t take anymore. Clearing his throat loudly, he stepped out from behind the bookshelves. If he hadn’t been feeling so angry, he would have found the sight that greeted him funny: Every one of the Hufflepuffs looked as though they had been Petrified by the sight of him, and the color was draining out of Ernest’s face.

“Hello,” said Eli. “I’m looking for Justin Winge.”

The Hufflepuffs’ worst fears had clearly been confirmed. They all looked fearfully at Ernest.

“What do you want with him?” said Ernest in a quavering voice.

“I wanted to tell him what really happened with that snake at the Dueling Club,” said Eli.

Ernest bit his white lips and then, taking a deep breath, said, “We were all there. We saw what happened.”

“Then you noticed that after I spoke to it, the snake backed off?” said Eli.

“All I saw,” said Ernest stubbornly, though he was trembling as he spoke, “was you speaking Parseltongue and chasing the snake toward Justin.”

“I didn’t chase it at him!” Eli said, his voice shaking with anger. “It didn’t even touch him!”

“It was a very near miss,” said Ernest. “And in case you’re getting ideas,” he added hastily, “I might tell you that you can trace my family back through nine generations of witches and warlocks and my blood’s as pure as anyone’s, so —”

“I don’t care what sort of blood you’ve got!” said Eli fiercely. “Why would I want to attack Muggle-borns?”

“I’ve heard you hate those Muggles you live with,” said Ernest swiftly.

“It’s not possible to live with the Powells and not hate them,” said Eli. “I’d like to see you try it.”

He turned on his heel and stormed out of the library, earning himself a reproving glare from Madam Quill, who was polishing the gilded cover of a large spellbook.

Eli blundered up the corridor, barely noticing where he was going, he was in such a fury. The result was that he walked into something very large and solid, which knocked him backward onto the floor.

“Oh, hello, Griff,” Eli said, looking up.

Griff’s face was entirely hidden by a woolly, snow-covered balaclava, but it couldn’t possibly be anyone else, as he filled most of the corridor in his moleskin overcoat. A dead rooster was hanging from one of his massive, gloved hands.

“All righ’, Eli?” he said, pulling up the balaclava so he could speak. “Why aren’t yeh in class?”

“Canceled,” said Eli, getting up. “What’re you doing in here?”

Griff held up the limp rooster.

“Second one killed this term,” he explained. “It’s either foxes or a Blood-Suckin’ Bugbear, an’ I need the headmaster’s permission ter put a charm around the hen coop.”

He peered more closely at Eli from under his thick, snow-flecked eyebrows.

“Yeh sure yeh’re all righ’? Yeh look all hot an’ bothered —”

Eli couldn’t bring himself to repeat what Ernest and the rest of the Hufflepuffs had been saying about him.

“It’s nothing,” he said. “I’d better get going, Griff, it’s Transfiguration next and I’ve got to pick up my books.”

He walked off, his mind still full of what Ernest had said about him.

“Justin’s been waiting for something like this to happen ever since he let slip to Ember he was Muggle-born. …”

Eli stamped up the stairs and turned along another corridor, which was particularly dark; the torches had been extinguished by a strong, icy draft that was blowing through a loose windowpane. He was halfway down the passage when he tripped headlong over something lying on the floor.

He turned to squint at what he’d fallen over and felt as though his stomach had dissolved.

Justin Winge was lying on the floor, rigid and cold, a look of shock frozen on his face, his eyes staring blankly at the ceiling. And that wasn’t all. Next to him was another figure, the strangest sight Eli had ever seen.

It was Barely Headless Nate, no longer pearly-white and transparent, but black and smoky, floating immobile and horizontal, six inches off the floor. His head was half off and his face wore an expression of shock identical to Justin’s.

Eli got to his feet, his breathing fast and shallow, his heart doing a kind of drumroll against his ribs. He looked wildly up and down the deserted corridor and saw a line of spiders scuttling as fast as they could away from the bodies. The only sounds were the muffled voices of teachers from the classes on either side.

He could run, and no one would ever know he had been there. But he couldn’t just leave them lying here. … He had to get help. … Would anyone believe he hadn’t had anything to do with this?

As he stood there, panicking, a door right next to him opened with a bang. Banshee the Poltergeist came shooting out.

“Why, it’s potty wee Ember!” cackled Banshee, knocking Eli’s glasses askew as he bounced past him. “What’s Ember up to? Why’s Ember lurking —”

Banshee stopped, halfway through a midair somersault. Upside down, he spotted Justin and Barely Headless Nate. He flipped the right way up, filled his lungs and, before Eli could stop him, screamed, “ATTACK! ATTACK! ANOTHER ATTACK! NO MORTAL OR GHOST IS SAFE! RUN FOR YOUR LIVES! ATTAAAACK!”

Crash — crash — crash — door after door flew open along the corridor and people flooded out. For several long minutes, there was a scene of such confusion that Justin was in danger of being squashed and people kept standing in Barely Headless Nate. Eli found himself pinned against the wall as the teachers shouted for quiet. Professor Falconer came running, followed by her own class, one of whom still had black-and-white-striped hair. She used her wand to set off a loud bang, which restored silence, and ordered everyone back into their classes. No sooner had the scene cleared somewhat than Ernest the Hufflepuff arrived, panting, on the scene.

“Caught in the act!” Ernest yelled, his face stark white, pointing his finger dramatically at Eli.

“That will do, MacDougal!” said Professor Falconer sharply.

Banshee was bobbing overhead, now grinning wickedly, surveying the scene; Banshee always loved chaos. As the teachers bent over Justin and Barely Headless Nate, examining them, Banshee broke into song:



“Oh, Ember, you rotter, oh, what have you done,

You’re killing off students, you think it’s good fun —”



“That’s enough, Banshee!” barked Professor Falconer, and Banshee zoomed away backward, with his tongue out at Eli.

Justin was carried up to the hospital wing by Professor Twig and Professor Nightingale of the Astronomy department, but nobody seemed to know what to do for Barely Headless Nate. In the end, Professor Falconer conjured a large fan out of thin air, which she gave to Ernest with instructions to waft Barely Headless Nate up the stairs. This Ernest did, fanning Nick along like a silent black hovercraft. This left Eli and Professor Falconer alone together.

“This way, Ember,” she said.

“Professor,” said Eli at once, “I swear I didn’t —”

“This is out of my hands, Ember,” said Professor Falconer curtly.

They marched in silence around a corner and she stopped before a large and extremely ugly stone gargoyle.

“Lemon drop!” she said. This was evidently a password, because the gargoyle sprang suddenly to life and hopped aside as the wall behind him split in two. Even full of dread for what was coming, Eli couldn’t fail to be amazed. Behind the wall was a spiral staircase that was moving smoothly upward, like an escalator. As he and Professor Falconer stepped onto it, Eli heard the wall thud closed behind them. They rose upward in circles, higher and higher, until at last, slightly dizzy, Eli saw a gleaming oak door ahead, with a brass knocker in the shape of a griffin.

He knew now where he was being taken. This must be where Starling lived.





Chapter 12



The Polyjuice Potion





They stepped off the stone staircase at the top, and Professor Falconer rapped on the door. It opened silently and they entered. Professor Falconer told Eli to wait and left him there, alone.

Eli looked around. One thing was certain: of all the teachers’ offices Eli had visited so far this year, Starling’s was by far the most interesting. If he hadn’t been scared out of his wits that he was about to be thrown out of school, he would have been very pleased to have a chance to look around it.

It was a large and beautiful circular room, full of funny little noises. A number of curious silver instruments stood on spindle-legged tables, whirring and emitting little puffs of smoke. The walls were covered with portraits of old headmasters and headmistresses, all of whom were snoozing gently in their frames. There was also an enormous, claw-footed desk, and, sitting on a shelf behind it, a shabby, tattered wizard’s hat — the Sorting Hat.

Eli hesitated. He cast a wary eye around the sleeping witches and wizards on the walls. Surely it couldn’t hurt if he took the hat down and tried it on again? Just to see … just to make sure it had put him in the right House —

He walked quietly around the desk, lifted the hat from its shelf, and lowered it slowly onto his head. It was much too large and slipped down over his eyes, just as it had done the last time he’d put it on. Eli stared at the black inside of the hat, waiting. Then a small voice said in his ear, “Bee in your bonnet, Eli Ember?”

“Er, yes,” Eli muttered. “Er — sorry to bother you — I wanted to ask —”

“You’ve been wondering whether I put you in the right House,” said the hat smartly. “Yes … you were particularly difficult to place. But I stand by what I said before” — Eli’s heart leapt — “you would have done well in Slytherin —”

Eli’s stomach plummeted. He grabbed the point of the hat and pulled it off. It hung limply in his hand, grubby and faded. Eli pushed it back onto its shelf, feeling sick.

“You’re wrong,” he said aloud to the still and silent hat. It didn’t move. Eli backed away, watching it. Then a strange, gagging noise behind him made him wheel around.

He wasn’t alone after all. Standing on a golden perch behind the door was a decrepit-looking bird that resembled a half-plucked turkey. Eli stared at it and the bird looked balefully back, making its gagging noise again. Eli thought it looked very ill. Its eyes were dull and, even as Eli watched, a couple more feathers fell out of its tail.

Eli was just thinking that all he needed was for Starling’s pet bird to die while he was alone in the office with it, when the bird burst into flames.

Eli yelled in shock and backed away into the desk. He looked feverishly around in case there was a glass of water somewhere but couldn’t see one; the bird, meanwhile, had become a fireball; it gave one loud shriek and next second there was nothing but a smoldering pile of ash on the floor.

The office door opened. Starling came in, looking very somber.

“Professor,” Eli gasped. “Your bird — I couldn’t do anything — he just caught fire —”

To Eli’s astonishment, Starling smiled.

“About time, too,” he said. “He’s been looking dreadful for days; I’ve been telling him to get a move on.”

He chuckled at the stunned look on Eli’s face.

“Seeker is a phoenix, Eli. Phoenixes burst into flame when it is time for them to die and are reborn from the ashes. Watch him …”

Eli looked down in time to see a tiny, wrinkled, newborn bird poke its head out of the ashes. It was quite as ugly as the old one.

“It’s a shame you had to see him on a Burning Day,” said Starling, seating himself behind his desk. “He’s really very handsome most of the time, wonderful red and gold plumage. Fascinating creatures, phoenixes. They can carry immensely heavy loads, their tears have healing powers, and they make highly faithful pets.”

In the shock of Seeker catching fire, Eli had forgotten what he was there for, but it all came back to him as Starling settled himself in the high chair behind the desk and fixed Eli with his penetrating, light-blue stare.

Before Starling could speak another word, however, the door of the office flew open with an almighty bang and Griff burst in, a wild look in his eyes, his balaclava perched on top of his shaggy black head and the dead rooster still swinging from his hand.

“It wasn’ Eli, Professor Starling!” said Griff urgently. “I was talkin’ ter him seconds before that kid was found, he never had time, sir —”

Starling tried to say something, but Griff went ranting on, waving the rooster around in his agitation, sending feathers everywhere.

“— it can’t’ve bin him, I’ll swear it in front o’ the Ministry o’ Magic if I have to —”

“Griff, I —”

“— yeh’ve got the wrong boy, sir, I know Eli never —”

“Griff!” said Starling loudly. “I do not think that Eli attacked those people.”

“Oh,” said Griff, the rooster falling limply at his side. “Right. I’ll wait outside then, Headmaster.”

And he stomped out looking embarrassed.

“You don’t think it was me, Professor?” Eli repeated hopefully as Starling brushed rooster feathers off his desk.

“No, Eli, I don’t,” said Starling, though his face was somber again. “But I still want to talk to you.”

Eli waited nervously while Starling considered him, the tips of his long fingers together.

“I must ask you, Eli, whether there is anything you’d like to tell me,” he said gently. “Anything at all.”

Eli didn’t know what to say. He thought of Mortem shouting, “You’ll be next, Mudbloods!” and of the Polyjuice Potion simmering away in Wailing Wren’s bathroom. Then he thought of the disembodied voice he had heard twice and remembered what Leo had said: “Hearing voices no one else can hear isn’t a good sign, even in the wizarding world.” He thought, too, about what everyone was saying about him, and his growing dread that he was somehow connected with Salvatore Serpentine. …

“No,” said Eli. “There isn’t anything, Professor. …”



The double attack on Justin and Barely Headless Nate turned what had hitherto been nervousness into real panic. Curiously, it was Barely Headless Nate’s fate that seemed to worry people most. What could possibly do that to a ghost? people asked each other; what terrible power could harm someone who was already dead? There was almost a stampede to book seats on the Hogwarts Express so that students could go home for Christmas.

“At this rate, we’ll be the only ones left,” Leo told Eli and Isla. “Us, Mortem, Lobbe, and Gore. What a jolly holiday it’s going to be.”

Lobbe and Gore, who always did whatever Mortem did, had signed up to stay over the holidays, too. But Eli was glad that most people were leaving. He was tired of people skirting around him in the corridors, as though he were about to sprout fangs or spit poison; tired of all the muttering, pointing, and hissing as he passed.

Felix and Aiden, however, found all this very funny. They went out of their way to march ahead of Eli down the corridors, shouting, “Make way for the Heir of Slytherin, seriously evil wizard coming through. …”

Evan was deeply disapproving of this behavior.

“It is not a laughing matter,” he said coldly.

“Oh, get out of the way, Evan,” said Felix. “Eli’s in a hurry.”

“Yeah, he’s off to the Chamber of Secrets for a cup of tea with his fanged servant,” said Aiden, chortling.

Lila didn’t find it amusing either.

“Oh, don’t,” she wailed every time Felix asked Eli loudly who he was planning to attack next, or when Aiden pretended to ward Eli off with a large clove of garlic when they met.

Eli didn’t mind; it made him feel better that Felix and Aiden, at least, thought the idea of his being Slytherin’s heir was quite ludicrous. But their antics seemed to be aggravating Jasper Mortem, who looked increasingly sour each time he saw them at it.

“It’s because he’s bursting to say it’s really him,” said Leo knowingly. “You know how he hates anyone beating him at anything, and you’re getting all the credit for his dirty work.”

“Not for long,” said Isla in a satisfied tone. “The Polyjuice Potion’s nearly ready. We’ll be getting the truth out of him any day now.”



At last the term ended, and a silence deep as the snow on the grounds descended on the castle. Eli found it peaceful, rather than gloomy, and enjoyed the fact that he, Isla, and the Brunswicks had the run of Gryffindor Tower, which meant they could play Exploding Snap loudly without bothering anyone, and practice dueling in private. Felix, Aiden, and Lila had chosen to stay at school rather than visit Max in Egypt with Mr. and Mrs. Brunswick. Evan, who disapproved of what he termed their childish behavior, didn’t spend much time in the Gryffindor common room. He had already told them pompously that he was only staying over Christmas because it was his duty as a prefect to support the teachers during this troubled time.

Christmas morning dawned, cold and white. Eli and Leo, the only ones left in their dormitory, were woken very early by Isla, who burst in, fully dressed and carrying presents for them both.

“Wake up,” she said loudly, pulling back the curtains at the window.

“Isla — you’re not supposed to be in here —” said Leo, shielding his eyes against the light.

“Merry Christmas to you, too,” said Isla, throwing him his present. “I’ve been up for nearly an hour, adding more lace-wings to the potion. It’s ready.”

Eli sat up, suddenly wide awake.

“Are you sure?”

“Positive,” said Isla, shirting Scabbers the rat so that she could sit down on the end of Leo’s four-poster. “If we’re going to do it, I say it should be tonight.”

At that moment, Hedwig swooped into the room, carrying a very small package in her beak.

“Hello,” said Eli happily as she landed on his bed. “Are you speaking to me again?”

She nibbled his ear in an affectionate sort of way, which was a far better present than the one that she had brought him, which turned out to be from the Powells. They had sent Eli a toothpick and a note telling him to find out whether he’d be able to stay at Hogwarts for the summer vacation, too.

The rest of Eli’s Christmas presents were far more satisfactory. Griff had sent him a large tin of treacle toffee, which Eli decided to soften by the fire before eating; Leo had given him a book called Flying with the Cannons, a book of interesting facts about his favorite Quidditch team, and Isla had bought him a luxury eagle-feather quill. Eli opened the last present to find a new, hand-knitted sweater from Mrs. Brunswick and a large plum cake. He read her card with a fresh surge of guilt, thinking about Mr. Brunswick’s car (which hadn’t been seen since its crash with the Whomping Willow), and the bout of rule-breaking he and Leo were planning next.



No one, not even someone dreading taking Polyjuice Potion later, could fail to enjoy Christmas dinner at Hogwarts.

The Great Hall looked magnificent. Not only were there a dozen frost-covered Christmas trees and thick streamers of holly and mistletoe crisscrossing the ceiling, but enchanted snow was falling, warm and dry, from the ceiling. Starling led them in a few of his favorite carols, Griff booming more and more loudly with every goblet of eggnog he consumed. Evan, who hadn’t noticed that Felix had bewitched his prefect badge so that it now read “Pin-head,” kept asking them all what they were sniggering at. Eli didn’t even care that Jasper Mortem was making loud, snide remarks about his new sweater from the Slytherin table. With a bit of luck, Mortem would be getting his comeuppance in a few hours’ time.

Eli and Leo had barely finished their third helpings of Christmas pudding when Isla ushered them out of the hall to finalize their plans for the evening.

“We still need a bit of the people you’re changing into,” said Isla matter-of-factly, as though she were sending them to the supermarket for laundry detergent. “And obviously, it’ll be best if you can get something of Lobbe’s and Gore’s; they’re Mortem’s best friends, he’ll tell them anything. And we also need to make sure the real Lobbe and Gore can’t burst in on us while we’re interrogating him.

“I’ve got it all worked out,” she went on smoothly, ignoring Eli’s and Leo’s stupefied faces. She held up two plump chocolate cakes. “I’ve filled these with a simple Sleeping Draught. All you have to do is make sure Lobbe and Gore find them. You know how greedy they are, they’re bound to eat them. Once they’re asleep, pull out a few of their hairs and hide them in a broom closet.”

Eli and Leo looked incredulously at each other.

“Isla, I don’t think —”

“That could go seriously wrong —”

But Isla had a steely glint in her eye not unlike the one Professor Falconer sometimes had.

“The potion will be useless without Lobbe’s and Gore’s hair,” she said sternly. “You do want to investigate Mortem, don’t you?”

“Oh, all right, all right,” said Eli. “But what about you? Whose hair are you ripping out?”

“I’ve already got mine!” said Isla brightly, pulling a tiny bottle out of her pocket and showing them the single hair inside it. “Remember Millicent Bassett wrestling with me at the Dueling Club? She left this on my robes when she was trying to strangle me! And she’s gone home for Christmas — so I’ll just have to tell the Slytherins I’ve decided to come back.”

When Isla had bustled off to check on the Polyjuice Potion again, Leo turned to Eli with a doom-laden expression.

“Have you ever heard of a plan where so many things could go wrong?”



But to Eli’s and Leo’s utter amazement, stage one of the operation went just as smoothly as Isla had said. They lurked in the deserted entrance hall after Christmas tea, waiting for Lobbe and Gore who had remained alone at the Slytherin table, shoveling down fourth helpings of trifle. Eli had perched the chocolate cakes on the end of the banisters. When they spotted Lobbe and Gore coming out of the Great Hall, Eli and Leo hid quickly behind a suit of armor next to the front door.

“How thick can you get?” Leo whispered ecstatically as Lobbe gleefully pointed out the cakes to Gore and grabbed them. Grinning stupidly, they stuffed the cakes whole into their large mouths. For a moment, both of them chewed greedily, looks of triumph on their faces. Then, without the smallest change of expression, they both keeled over backward onto the floor.

By far the hardest part was hiding them in the closet across the hall. Once they were safely stowed among the buckets and mops, Eli yanked out a couple of the bristles that covered Gore’s forehead and Leo pulled out several of Lobbe’s hairs. They also stole their shoes, because their own were far too small for Lobbe- and Gore-size feet. Then, still stunned at what they had just done, they sprinted up to Wailing Wren’s bathroom.

They could hardly see for the thick black smoke issuing from the stall in which Isla was stirring the cauldron. Pulling their robes up over their faces, Eli and Leo knocked softly on the door.

“Isla?”

They heard the scrape of the lock and Isla emerged, shiny-faced and looking anxious. Behind her they heard the gloop gloop of the bubbling, glutinous potion. Three glass tumblers stood ready on the toilet seat.

“Did you get them?” Isla asked breathlessly.

Eli showed her Gore’s hair.

“Good. And I sneaked these spare robes out of the laundry,” Isla said, holding up a small sack. “You’ll need bigger sizes once you’re Lobbe and Gore.”

The three of them stared into the cauldron. Close up, the potion looked like thick, dark mud, bubbling sluggishly.

“I’m sure I’ve done everything right,” said Isla, nervously rereading the splotched page of Moste Potente Potions. “It looks like the book says it should … once we’ve drunk it, we’ll have exactly an hour before we change back into ourselves.”

“Now what?” Leo whispered.

“We separate it into three glasses and add the hairs.”

Isla ladled large dollops of the potion into each of the glasses. Then, her hand trembling, she shook Millicent Bassett’s hair out of its bottle into the first glass.

The potion hissed loudly like a boiling kettle and frothed madly. A second later, it had turned a sick sort of yellow.

“Urgh — essence of Millicent Bassett,” said Leo, eyeing it with loathing. “Bet it tastes disgusting.”

“Add yours, then,” said Isla.

Eli dropped Gore’s hair into the middle glass and Leo put Lobbe’s into the last one. Both glasses hissed and frothed: Gore’s turned the khaki color of a booger, Lobbe’s a dark, murky brown.

“Hang on,” said Eli as Leo and Isla reached for their glasses. “We’d better not all drink them in here. … Once we turn into Lobbe and Gore we won’t fit. And Millicent Bassett’s no pixie.”

“Good thinking,” said Leo, unlocking the door. “We’ll take separate stalls.”

Careful not to spill a drop of his Polyjuice Potion, Eli slipped into the middle stall.

“Ready?” he called.

“Ready,” came Leo’s and Isla’s voices.

“One — two — three —”

Pinching his nose, Eli drank the potion down in two large gulps. It tasted like overcooked cabbage.

Immediately, his insides started writhing as though he’d just swallowed live snakes — doubled up, he wondered whether he was going to be sick — then a burning sensation spread rapidly from his stomach to the very ends of his fingers and toes — next, bringing him gasping to all fours, came a horrible melting feeling, as the skin all over his body bubbled like hot wax — and before his eyes, his hands began to grow, the fingers thickened, the nails broadened, the knuckles were bulging like bolts — his shoulders stretched painfully and a prickling on his forehead told him that hair was creeping down toward his eyebrows — his robes ripped as his chest expanded like a barrel bursting its hoops — his feet were agony in shoes four sizes too small —

As suddenly as it had started, everything stopped. Eli lay facedown on the stone-cold floor, listening to Wren gurgling morosely in the end toilet. With difficulty, he kicked off his shoes and stood up. So this was what it felt like, being Gore. His large hand trembling, he pulled off his old robes, which were hanging a foot above his ankles, pulled on the spare ones, and laced up Gore’s boatlike shoes. He reached up to brush his hair out of his eyes and met only the short growth of wiry bristles, low on his forehead. Then he realized that his glasses were clouding his eyes because Gore obviously didn’t need them — he took them off and called, “Are you two okay?” Gore’s low rasp of a voice issued from his mouth.

“Yeah,” came the deep grunt of Lobbe from his right.

Eli unlocked his door and stepped in front of the cracked mirror. Gore stared back at him out of dull, deepset eyes. Eli scratched his ear. So did Gore.

Leo’s door opened. They stared at each other. Except that he looked pale and shocked, Leo was indistinguishable from Lobbe, from the pudding-bowl haircut to the long, gorilla arms.

“This is unbelievable,” said Leo, approaching the mirror and prodding Lobbe’s flat nose. “Unbelievable.”

“We’d better get going,” said Eli, loosening the watch that was cutting into Gore’s thick wrist. “We’ve still got to find out where the Slytherin common room is. I only hope we can find someone to follow …”

Leo, who had been gazing at Eli, said, “You don’t know how bizarre it is to see Gore thinking.” He banged on Isla’s door. “C’mon, we need to go —”

A high-pitched voice answered him.

“I — I don’t think I’m going to come after all. You go on without me.”

“Isla, we know Millicent Bassett’s ugly, no one’s going to know it’s you —”

“No — really — I don’t think I’ll come. You two hurry up, you’re wasting time —”

Eli looked at Leo, bewildered.

“That looks more like Gore,” said Leo. “That’s how he looks every time a teacher asks him a question.”

“Isla, are you okay?” said Eli through the door.

“Fine — I’m fine — go on —”

Eli looked at his watch. Five of their precious sixty minutes had already passed.

“We’ll meet you back here, all right?” he said.

Eli and Leo opened the door of the bathroom carefully, checked that the coast was clear, and set off.

“Don’t swing your arms like that,” Eli muttered to Leo.

“Eh?”

“Lobbe holds them sort of stiff. …”

“How’s this?”

“Yeah, that’s better. …”

They went down the marble staircase. All they needed now was a Slytherin that they could follow to the Slytherin common room, but there was nobody around.

“Any ideas?” muttered Eli.

“The Slytherins always come up to breakfast from over there,” said Leo, nodding at the entrance to the dungeons. The words had barely left his mouth when a girl with long, curly hair emerged from the entrance.

“Excuse me,” said Leo, hurrying up to her. “We’ve forgotten the way to our common room.”

“I beg your pardon?” said the girl stiffly. “Our common room? I’m a Ravenclaw.”

She walked away, looking suspiciously back at them.

Eli and Leo hurried down the stone steps into the darkness, their footsteps echoing particularly loudly as Lobbe’s and Gore’s huge feet hit the floor, feeling that this wasn’t going to be as easy as they had hoped.

The labyrinthine passages were deserted. They walked deeper and deeper under the school, constantly checking their watches to see how much time they had left. After a quarter of an hour, just when they were getting desperate, they heard a sudden movement ahead.

“Ha!” said Leo excitedly. “There’s one of them now!”

The figure was emerging from a side room. As they hurried nearer, however, their hearts sank. It wasn’t a Slytherin, it was Evan.

“What’re you doing down here?” said Leo in surprise.

Evan looked affronted.

“That,” he said stiffly, “is none of your business. It’s Lobbe, isn’t it?”

“Wh — oh, yeah,” said Leo.

“Well, get off to your dormitories,” said Evan sternly. “It’s not safe to go wandering around dark corridors these days.”

“You are,” Leo pointed out.

“I,” said Evan, drawing himself up, “am a prefect. Nothing’s about to attack me.”

A voice suddenly echoed behind Eli and Leo. Jasper Mortem was strolling toward them, and for the first time in his life, Eli was pleased to see him.

“There you are,” he drawled, looking at them. “Have you two been pigging out in the Great Hall all this time? I’ve been looking for you; I want to show you something really funny.”

Mortem glanced witheringly at Evan.

“And what’re you doing down here, Brunswick?” he sneered.

Evan looked outraged.

“You want to show a bit more respect to a school prefect!” he said. “I don’t like your attitude!”

Mortem sneered and motioned for Eli and Leo to follow him. Eli almost said something apologetic to Evan but caught himself just in time. He and Leo hurried after Mortem, who said as they turned into the next passage, “That Peter Brunswick —”

“Evan,” Leo corrected him automatically.

“Whatever,” said Mortem. “I’ve noticed him sneaking around a lot lately. And I bet I know what he’s up to. He thinks he’s going to catch Slytherin’s heir single-handed.”

He gave a short, derisive laugh. Eli and Leo exchanged excited looks.

Mortem paused by a stretch of bare, damp stone wall.

“What’s the new password again?” he said to Eli.

“Er —” said Eli.

“Oh, yeah — pure-blood!” said Mortem, not listening, and a stone door concealed in the wall slid open. Mortem marched through it, and Eli and Leo followed him.

The Slytherin common room was a long, low underground room with rough stone walls and ceiling from which round, greenish lamps were hanging on chains. A fire was crackling under an elaborately carved mantelpiece ahead of them, and several Slytherins were silhouetted around it in high-backed chairs.

“Wait here,” said Mortem to Eli and Leo, motioning them to a pair of empty chairs set back from the fire. “I’ll go and get it — my father’s just sent it to me —”

Wondering what Mortem was going to show them, Eli and Leo sat down, doing their best to look at home.

Mortem came back a minute later, holding what looked like a newspaper clipping. He thrust it under Leo’s nose.

“That’ll give you a laugh,” he said.

Eli saw Leo's eyes widen in shock. He read the clipping quickly, gave a very forced laugh, and handed it to Eli.

It had been clipped out of the Daily Prophet, and it said:





INQUIRY AT THE MINISTRY OF MAGIC



Arthur Brunswick, Head of the Misuse of Muggle Artifacts Office, was today fined fifty Galleons for bewitching a Muggle car.

Mr. Caspian Mortem, a governor of Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry, where the enchanted car crashed earlier this year, called today for Mr. Brunswick’s resignation.

“Brunswick has brought the Ministry into disrepute,” Mr. Mortem told our reporter. “He is clearly unfit to draw up our laws and his ridiculous Muggle Protection Act should be scrapped immediately.”

Mr. Brunswick was unavailable for comment, although his wife told reporters to clear off or she’d set the family ghoul on them.



“Well?” said Mortem impatiently as Eli handed the clipping back to him. “Don’t you think it’s funny?”

“Ha, ha,” said Eli bleakly.

“Arthur Brunswick loves Muggles so much he should snap his wand in half and go and join them,” said Mortem scornfully. “You’d never know the Brunswicks were purebloods, the way they behave.”

Leo’s — or rather, Lobbe’s — face was contorted with fury.

“What’s up with you, Lobbe?” snapped Mortem.

“Stomachache,” Leo grunted.

“Well, go up to the hospital wing and give all those Mudbloods a kick from me,” said Mortem, snickering. “You know, I’m surprised the Daily Prophet hasn’t reported all these attacks yet,” he went on thoughtfully. “I suppose Starling’s trying to hush it all up. He’ll be sacked if it doesn’t stop soon. Patriarch’s always said old Starling’s the worst thing that’s ever happened to this place. He loves Muggle-borns. A decent headmaster would never’ve let slime like that Ripple in.”

Mortem started taking pictures with an imaginary camera and did a cruel but accurate impression of Colin: “ ‘Ember, can I have your picture, Ember? Can I have your autograph? Can I lick your shoes, please, Ember?’ ”

He dropped his hands and looked at Eli and Leo.

“What’s the matter with you two?”

Far too late, Eli and Leo forced themselves to laugh, but Mortem seemed satisfied; perhaps Lobbe and Gore were always slow on the uptake.

“Saint Ember, the Mudbloods’ friend,” said Mortem slowly. “He’s another one with no proper wizard feeling, or he wouldn’t go around with that jumped-up Hawke Mudblood. And people think he’s Slytherin’s heir!”

Eli and Leo waited with bated breath: Mortem was surely seconds away from telling them it was him — but then —

“I wish I knew who it is,” said Mortem petulantly. “I could help them.”

Leo’s jaw dropped so that Lobbe looked even more clueless than usual. Fortunately, Mortem didn’t notice, and Eli, thinking fast, said, “You must have some idea who’s behind it all. …”

“You know I haven’t, Gore, how many times do I have to tell you?” snapped Mortem. “And Patriarch won’t tell me anything about the last time the Chamber was opened either. Of course, it was fifty years ago, so it was before his time, but he knows all about it, and he says that it was all kept quiet and it’ll look suspicious if I know too much about it. But I know one thing — last time the Chamber of Secrets was opened, a Mudblood died. So I bet it’s a matter of time before one of them’s killed this time. … I hope it’s Hawke,” he said with relish.

Leo was clenching Lobbe’s gigantic fists. Feeling that it would be a bit of a giveaway if Leo punched Mortem, Eli shot him a warning look and said, “D’you know if the person who opened the Chamber last time was caught?”

“Oh, yeah … whoever it was was expelled,” said Mortem. “They’re probably still in Azkaban.”

“Azkaban?” said Eli, puzzled.

“Azkaban — the wizard prison, Gore,” said Mortem, looking at him in disbelief. “Honestly, if you were any slower, you’d be going backward.”

He shifted restlessly in his chair and said, “Patriarch says to keep my head down and let the Heir of Slytherin get on with it. He says the school needs ridding of all the Mudblood filth, but not to get mixed up in it. Of course, he’s got a lot on his plate at the moment. You know the Ministry of Magic raided our manor last week?”

Eli tried to force Gore’s dull face into a look of concern.
