“Yeah …” said Mortem. “Luckily, they didn’t find much. Patriarch’s got some very valuable Dark Arts stuff. But luckily, we’ve got our own secret chamber under the drawing-room floor —”

“Ho!” said Leo.

Mortem looked at him. So did Eli. Leo blushed. Even his hair was turning red. His nose was also slowly lengthening — their hour was up, Leo was turning back into himself, and from the look of horror he was suddenly giving Eli, he must be, too.

They both jumped to their feet.

“Medicine for my stomach,” Leo grunted, and without further ado they sprinted the length of the Slytherin common room, hurled themselves at the stone wall, and dashed up the passage, hoping against hope that Mortem hadn’t noticed anything. Eli could feel his feet slipping around in Gore’s huge shoes and had to hoist up his robes as he shrank; they crashed up the steps into the dark entrance hall, which was full of a muffled pounding coming from the closet where they’d locked Lobbe and Gore. Leaving their shoes outside the closet door, they sprinted in their socks up the marble staircase toward Wailing Wren’s bathroom.

“Well, it wasn’t a complete waste of time,” Leo panted, closing the bathroom door behind them. “I know we still haven’t found out who’s doing the attacks, but I’m going to write to Father tomorrow and tell him to check under the Mortems’ drawing room.”

Eli checked his face in the cracked mirror. He was back to normal. He put his glasses on as Leo hammered on the door of Isla’s stall.

“Isla, come out, we’ve got loads to tell you —”

“Go away!” Isla squeaked.

Eli and Leo looked at each other.

“What’s the matter?” said Leo. “You must be back to normal by now, we are —”

But Wailing Wren glided suddenly through the stall door. Eli had never seen her looking so happy.

“Ooooooh, wait till you see,” she said. “It’s awful —”

They heard the lock slide back and Isla emerged, sobbing, her robes pulled up over her head.

“What’s up?” said Leo uncertainly. “Have you still got Millicent’s nose or something?”

Isla let her robes fall and Leo backed into the sink.

Her face was covered in black fur. Her eyes had turned yellow and there were long, pointed ears poking through her hair.

“It was a c-cat hair!” she howled. “M-Millicent Bassett m-must have a cat! And the p-potion isn’t supposed to be used for animal transformations!”

“Uh-oh,” said Leo.

“You’ll be teased something dreadful,” said Wren happily.

“It’s okay, Isla,” said Eli quickly. “We’ll take you up to the hospital wing. Madam Fern never asks too many questions. …”

It took a long time to persuade Isla to leave the bathroom. Wailing Wren sped them on their way with a hearty guffaw. “Wait till everyone finds out you’ve got a tail!”





Chapter 13



The Very Secret Diary





Isla remained in the hospital wing for several weeks. There was a flurry of rumor about her disappearance when the rest of the school arrived back from their Christmas holidays, because of course everyone thought that she had been attacked. So many students filed past the hospital wing trying to catch a glimpse of her that Madam Fern took out her curtains again and placed them around Isla’s bed, to spare her the shame of being seen with a furry face.

Eli and Leo went to visit her every evening. When the new term started, they brought her each day’s homework.

“If I’d sprouted whiskers, I’d take a break from work,” said Leo, tipping a stack of books onto Isla’s bedside table one evening.

“Don’t be silly, Leo, I’ve got to keep up,” said Isla briskly. Her spirits were greatly improved by the fact that all the hair had gone from her face and her eyes were turning slowly back to brown. “I don’t suppose you’ve got any new leads?” she added in a whisper, so that Madam Fern couldn’t hear her.

“Nothing,” said Eli gloomily.

“I was so sure it was Mortem,” said Leo, for about the hundredth time.

“What’s that?” asked Eli, pointing to something gold sticking out from under Isla’s pillow.

“Just a get well card,” said Isla hastily, trying to poke it out of sight, but Leo was too quick for her. He pulled it out, flicked it open, and read aloud:

“To Miss Hawke, wishing you a speedy recovery, from your concerned teacher, Professor Julian Bright, Order of Merlin, Third Class, Honorary Member of the Dark Force Defense League, and five-time winner of Witch Weekly’s Most-Charming-Smile Award.”

Leo looked up at Isla, disgusted.

“You sleep with this under your pillow?”

But Isla was spared answering by Madam Fern sweeping over with her evening dose of medicine.

“Is Bright the smarmiest bloke you’ve ever met, or what?” Leo said to Eli as they left the infirmary and started up the stairs toward Gryffindor Tower. Thorne had given them so much homework, Eli thought he was likely to be in the sixth year before he finished it. Leo was just saying he wished he had asked Isla how many rat tails you were supposed to add to a Hair-Raising Potion when an angry outburst from the floor above reached their ears.

“That’s Acheron,” Eli muttered as they hurried up the stairs and paused, out of sight, listening hard.

“You don’t think someone else’s been attacked?” said Leo tensely.

They stood still, their heads inclined toward Acheron’s voice, which sounded quite hysterical.

“— even more work for me! Mopping all night, like I haven’t got enough to do! No, this is the final straw, I’m going to Starling —”

His footsteps receded along the out-of-sight corridor and they heard a distant door slam.

They poked their heads around the corner. Acheron had clearly been manning his usual lookout post: They were once again on the spot where Mrs. Whiskers had been attacked. They saw at a glance what Acheron had been shouting about. A great flood of water stretched over half the corridor, and it looked as though it was still seeping from under the door of Wailing Wren’s bathroom. Now that Acheron had stopped shouting, they could hear Wren’s wails echoing off the bathroom walls.

“Now what’s up with her?” said Leo.

“Let’s go and see,” said Eli, and holding their robes over their ankles they stepped through the great wash of water to the door bearing its OUT OF ORDER sign, ignored it as always, and entered.

Wailing Wren was crying, if possible, louder and harder than ever before. She seemed to be hiding down her usual toilet. It was dark in the bathroom because the candles had been extinguished in the great rush of water that had left both walls and floor soaking wet.

“What’s up, Wren?” said Eli.

“Who’s that?” glugged Wren miserably. “Come to throw something else at me?”

Eli waded across to her stall and said, “Why would I throw something at you?”

“Don’t ask me,” Wren shouted, emerging with a wave of yet more water, which splashed onto the already sopping floor. “Here I am, minding my own business, and someone thinks it’s funny to throw a book at me. …”

“But it can’t hurt you if someone throws something at you,” said Eli, reasonably. “I mean, it’d just go right through you, wouldn’t it?”

He had said the wrong thing. Wren puffed herself up and shrieked, “Let’s all throw books at Wren, because she can’t feel it! Ten points if you can get it through her stomach! Fifty points if it goes through her head! Well, ha, ha, ha! What a lovely game, I don’t think!”

“Who threw it at you, anyway?” asked Eli.

“I don’t know. … I was just sitting in the U-bend, thinking about death, and it fell right through the top of my head,” said Wren, glaring at them. “It’s over there, it got washed out. …”

Eli and Leo looked under the sink where Wren was pointing. A small, thin book lay there. It had a shabby black cover and was as wet as everything else in the bathroom. Eli stepped forward to pick it up, but Leo suddenly flung out an arm to hold him back.

“What?” said Eli.

“Are you crazy?” said Leo. “It could be dangerous.”

“Dangerous?” said Eli, laughing. “Come off it, how could it be dangerous?”

“You’d be surprised,” said Leo, who was looking apprehensively at the book. “Some of the books the Ministry’s confiscated — Father’s told me — there was one that burned your eyes out. And everyone who read Sonnets of a Sorcerer spoke in limericks for the rest of their lives. And some old witch in Bath had a book that you could never stop reading! You just had to wander around with your nose in it, trying to do everything one-handed. And —”

“All right, I’ve got the point,” said Eli.

The little book lay on the floor, nondescript and soggy.

“Well, we won’t find out unless we look at it,” he said, and he ducked around Leo and picked it up off the floor.

Eli saw at once that it was a diary, and the faded year on the cover told him it was fifty years old. He opened it eagerly. On the first page he could just make out the name “T. M. Cipher” in smudged ink.

“Hang on,” said Leo, who had approached cautiously and was looking over Eli’s shoulder. “I know that name. … T. M. Cipher got an award for special services to the school fifty years ago.”

“How on earth d’you know that?” said Eli in amazement.

“Because Acheron made me polish his shield about fifty times in detention,” said Leo resentfully. “That was the one I burped slugs all over. If you’d wiped slime off a name for an hour, you’d remember it, too.”

Eli peeled the wet pages apart. They were completely blank. There wasn’t the faintest trace of writing on any of them, not even Auntie Mabel’s birthday, or dentist, half-past three.

“He never wrote in it,” said Eli, disappointed.

“I wonder why someone wanted to flush it away?” said Leo curiously.

Eli turned to the back cover of the book and saw the printed name of a variety store on Vauxhall Road, London.

“He must’ve been Muggle-born,” said Eli thoughtfully. “To have bought a diary from Vauxhall Road. …”

“Well, it’s not much use to you,” said Leo. He dropped his voice. “Fifty points if you can get it through Wren’s nose.”

Eli, however, pocketed it.



Isla left the hospital wing, de-whiskered, tail-less, and fur-free, at the beginning of February. On her first evening back in Gryffindor Tower, Eli showed her T. M. Cipher’s diary and told her the story of how they had found it.

“Oooh, it might have hidden powers,” said Isla enthusiastically, taking the diary and looking at it closely.

“If it has, it’s hiding them very well,” said Leo. “Maybe it’s shy. I don’t know why you don’t chuck it, Eli.”

“I wish I knew why someone did try to chuck it,” said Eli. “I wouldn’t mind knowing how Cipher got an award for special services to Hogwarts either.”

“Could’ve been anything,” said Leo. “Maybe he got thirty O.W.L.s or saved a teacher from the giant squid. Maybe he murdered Wren; that would’ve done everyone a favor. …”

But Eli could tell from the arrested look on Isla’s face that she was thinking what he was thinking.

“What?” said Leo, looking from one to the other.

“Well, the Chamber of Secrets was opened fifty years ago, wasn’t it?” he said. “That’s what Mortem said.”

“Yeah …” said Leo slowly.

“And this diary is fifty years old,” said Isla, tapping it excitedly.

“So?”

“Oh, Leo, wake up,” snapped Isla. “We know the person who opened the Chamber last time was expelled fifty years ago. We know T. M. Cipher got an award for special services to the school fifty years ago. Well, what if Cipher got his special award for catching the Heir of Slytherin? His diary would probably tell us everything — where the Chamber is, and how to open it, and what sort of creature lives in it — the person who’s behind the attacks this time wouldn’t want that lying around, would they?”

“That’s a brilliant theory, Isla,” said Leo, “with just one tiny little flaw. There’s nothing written in his diary.”

But Isla was pulling her wand out of her bag.

“It might be invisible ink!” she whispered.

She tapped the diary three times and said, “Aparecium!”

Nothing happened. Undaunted, Isla shoved her hand back into her bag and pulled out what appeared to be a bright red eraser.

“It’s a Revealer, I got it in Diagon Alley,” she said.

She rubbed hard on January first. Nothing happened.

“I’m telling you, there’s nothing to find in there,” said Leo. “Cipher just got a diary for Christmas and couldn’t be bothered filling it in.”



Eli couldn’t explain, even to himself, why he didn’t just throw Cipher’s diary away. The fact was that even though he knew the diary was blank, he kept absentmindedly picking it up and turning the pages, as though it were a story he wanted to finish. And while Eli was sure he had never heard the name T. M. Cipher before, it still seemed to mean something to him, almost as though Cipher was a friend he’d had when he was very small, and had half-forgotten. But this was absurd. He’d never had friends before Hogwarts, Carson had made sure of that.

Nevertheless, Eli was determined to find out more about Cipher, so next day at break, he headed for the trophy room to examine Cipher’s special award, accompanied by an interested Isla and a thoroughly unconvinced Leo, who told them he’d seen enough of the trophy room to last him a lifetime.

Cipher’s burnished gold shield was tucked away in a corner cabinet. It didn’t carry details of why it had been given to him (“Good thing, too, or it’d be even bigger and I’d still be polishing it,” said Leo). However, they did find Cipher’s name on an old Medal for Magical Merit, and on a list of old Head Boys.

“He sounds like Evan,” said Leo, wrinkling his nose in disgust. “Prefect, Head Boy … probably top of every class —”

“You say that like it’s a bad thing,” said Isla in a slightly hurt voice.



The sun had now begun to shine weakly on Hogwarts again. Inside the castle, the mood had grown more hopeful. There had been no more attacks since those on Justin and Barely Headless Nate, and Madam Fern was pleased to report that the Mandrakes were becoming moody and secretive, meaning that they were fast leaving childhood.

“The moment their acne clears up, they’ll be ready for repotting again,” Eli heard her telling Acheron kindly one afternoon. “And after that, it won’t be long until we’re cutting them up and stewing them. You’ll have Mrs. Whiskers back in no time.”

Perhaps the Heir of Slytherin had lost his or her nerve, thought Eli. It must be getting riskier and riskier to open the Chamber of Secrets, with the school so alert and suspicious. Perhaps the monster, whatever it was, was even now settling itself down to hibernate for another fifty years. …

Ernest MacDougal of Hufflepuff didn’t take this cheerful view. He was still convinced that Eli was the guilty one, that he had “given himself away” at the Dueling Club. Banshee wasn’t helping matters; he kept popping up in the crowded corridors singing “Oh, Ember, you rotter …” now with a dance routine to match.

Julian Bright seemed to think he himself had made the attacks stop. Eli overheard him telling Professor Falconer so while the Gryffindors were lining up for Transfiguration.

“I don’t think there’ll be any more trouble, Helena,” he said, tapping his nose knowingly and winking. “I think the Chamber has been locked for good this time. The culprit must have known it was only a matter of time before I caught him. Rather sensible to stop now, before I came down hard on him.

“You know, what the school needs now is a morale-booster. Wash away the memories of last term! I won’t say any more just now, but I think I know just the thing. …”

He tapped his nose again and strode off.

Bright’s idea of a morale-booster became clear at breakfast time on February fourteenth. Eli hadn’t had much sleep because of a late-running Quidditch practice the night before, and he hurried down to the Great Hall, slightly late. He thought, for a moment, that he’d walked through the wrong doors.

The walls were all covered with large, lurid pink flowers. Worse still, heart-shaped confetti was falling from the pale blue ceiling. Eli went over to the Gryffindor table, where Leo was sitting looking sickened, and Isla seemed to have been overcome with giggles.

“What’s going on?” Eli asked them, sitting down and wiping confetti off his bacon.

Leo pointed to the teachers’ table, apparently too disgusted to speak. Bright, wearing lurid pink robes to match the decorations, was waving for silence. The teachers on either side of him were looking stony-faced. From where he sat, Eli could see a muscle going in Professor Falconer’s cheek. Thorne looked as though someone had just fed him a large beaker of Skele-Gro.

“Happy Valentine’s Day!” Bright shouted. “And may I thank the forty-six people who have so far sent me cards! Yes, I have taken the liberty of arranging this little surprise for you all — and it doesn’t end here!”

Bright clapped his hands and through the doors to the entrance hall marched a dozen surly-looking dwarfs. Not just any dwarfs, however. Bright had them all wearing golden wings and carrying harps.

“My friendly, card-carrying cupids!” beamed Bright. “They will be roving around the school today delivering your valentines! And the fun doesn’t stop here! I’m sure my colleagues will want to enter into the spirit of the occasion! Why not ask Professor Thorne to show you how to whip up a Love Potion! And while you’re at it, Professor Twig knows more about Entrancing Enchantments than any wizard I’ve ever met, the sly old dog!”

Professor Twig buried his face in his hands. Thorne was looking as though the first person to ask him for a Love Potion would be force-fed poison.

“Please, Isla, tell me you weren’t one of the forty-six,” said Leo as they left the Great Hall for their first lesson. Isla suddenly became very interested in searching her bag for her schedule and didn’t answer.

All day long, the dwarfs kept barging into their classes to deliver valentines, to the annoyance of the teachers, and late that afternoon as the Gryffindors were walking upstairs for Charms, one of the dwarfs caught up with Eli.

“Oy, you! ’Arry Ember!” shouted a particularly grim-looking dwarf, elbowing people out of the way to get to Eli.

Hot all over at the thought of being given a valentine in front of a line of first years, which happened to include Lila Brunswick, Eli tried to escape. The dwarf, however, cut his way through the crowd by kicking people’s shins, and reached him before he’d gone two paces.

“I’ve got a musical message to deliver to ’Arry Ember in person,” he said, twanging his harp in a threatening sort of way.

“Not here,” Eli hissed, trying to escape.

“Stay still!” grunted the dwarf, grabbing hold of Eli’s bag and pulling him back.

“Let me go!” Eli snarled, tugging.

With a loud ripping noise, his bag split in two. His books, wand, parchment, and quill spilled onto the floor and his ink bottle smashed over everything.

Eli scrambled around, trying to pick it all up before the dwarf started singing, causing something of a holdup in the corridor.

“What’s going on here?” came the cold, drawling voice of Jasper Mortem. Eli started stuffing everything feverishly into his ripped bag, desperate to get away before Mortem could hear his musical valentine.

“What’s all this commotion?” said another familiar voice as Evan Brunswick arrived.

Losing his head, Eli tried to make a run for it, but the dwarf seized him around the knees and brought him crashing to the floor.

“Right,” he said, sitting on Eli’s ankles. “Here is your singing valentine:



His eyes are as green as a fresh pickled toad,

His hair is as dark as a blackboard.

I wish he was mine, he’s really divine,

The hero who conquered the Dark Lord.”



Eli would have given all the gold in Gringotts to evaporate on the spot. Trying valiantly to laugh along with everyone else, he got up, his feet numb from the weight of the dwarf, as Evan Brunswick did his best to disperse the crowd, some of whom were crying with mirth.

“Off you go, off you go, the bell rang five minutes ago, off to class, now,” he said, shooing some of the younger students away. “And you, Mortem —”

Eli, glancing over, saw Mortem stoop and snatch up something. Leering, he showed it to Lobbe and Gore, and Eli realized that he’d got Cipher’s diary.

“Give that back,” said Eli quietly.

“Wonder what Ember’s written in this?” said Mortem, who obviously hadn’t noticed the year on the cover and thought he had Eli’s own diary. A hush fell over the onlookers. Lila was staring from the diary to Eli, looking terrified.

“Hand it over, Mortem,” said Evan sternly.

“When I’ve had a look,” said Mortem, waving the diary tauntingly at Eli.

Evan said, “As a school prefect —” but Eli had lost his temper. He pulled out his wand and shouted, “Expelliarmus!” and just as Thorne had disarmed Bright, so Mortem found the diary shooting out of his hand into the air. Leo, grinning broadly, caught it.

“Eli!” said Evan loudly. “No magic in the corridors. I’ll have to report this, you know!”

But Eli didn’t care, he was one-up on Mortem, and that was worth five points from Gryffindor any day. Mortem was looking furious, and as Lila passed him to enter her classroom, he yelled spitefully after her, “I don’t think Ember liked your valentine much!”

Lila covered her face with her hands and ran into class. Snarling, Leo pulled out his wand, too, but Eli pulled him away. Leo didn’t need to spend the whole of Charms belching slugs.

It wasn’t until they had reached Professor Twig’s class that Eli noticed something rather odd about Cipher’s diary. All his other books were drenched in scarlet ink. The diary, however, was as clean as it had been before the ink bottle had smashed all over it. He tried to point this out to Leo, but Leo was having trouble with his wand again; large purple bubbles were blossoming out of the end, and he wasn’t much interested in anything else.



* * *



Eli went to bed before anyone else in his dormitory that night. This was partly because he didn’t think he could stand Felix and Aiden singing, “His eyes are as green as a fresh pickled toad” one more time, and partly because he wanted to examine Cipher’s diary again, and knew that Leo thought he was wasting his time.

Eli sat on his four-poster and flicked through the blank pages, not one of which had a trace of scarlet ink on it. Then he pulled a new bottle out of his bedside cabinet, dipped his quill into it, and dropped a blot onto the first page of the diary.

The ink shone brightly on the paper for a second and then, as though it was being sucked into the page, vanished. Excited, Eli loaded up his quill a second time and wrote, “My name is Eli Ember.”

The words shone momentarily on the page and they, too, sank without trace. Then, at last, something happened.

Oozing back out of the page, in his very own ink, came words Eli had never written.

“Hello, Eli Ember. My name is Tom Cipher. How did you come by my diary?”

These words, too, faded away, but not before Eli had started to scribble back.

“Someone tried to flush it down a toilet.”

He waited eagerly for Cipher’s reply.

“Lucky that I recorded my memories in some more lasting way than ink. But I always knew that there would be those who would not want this diary read.”

“What do you mean?” Eli scrawled, blotting the page in his excitement.

“I mean that this diary holds memories of terrible things. Things that were covered up. Things that happened at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry.”

“That’s where I am now,” Eli wrote quickly. “I’m at Hogwarts, and horrible stuff’s been happening. Do you know anything about the Chamber of Secrets?”

His heart was hammering. Cipher’s reply came quickly, his writing becoming untidier, as though he was hurrying to tell all he knew.

“Of course I know about the Chamber of Secrets. In my day, they told us it was a legend, that it did not exist. But this was a lie. In my fifth year, the Chamber was opened and the monster attacked several students, finally killing one. I caught the person who’d opened the Chamber and he was expelled. But the headmaster, Professor Plume, ashamed that such a thing had happened at Hogwarts, forbade me to tell the truth. A story was given out that the girl had died in a freak accident. They gave me a nice, shiny, engraved trophy for my trouble and warned me to keep my mouth shut. But I knew it could happen again. The monster lived on, and the one who had the power to release it was not imprisoned.”

Eli nearly upset his ink bottle in his hurry to write back.

“It’s happening again now. There have been three attacks and no one seems to know who’s behind them. Who was it last time?”

“I can show you, if you like,” came Cipher’s reply. “You don’t have to take my word for it. I can take you inside my memory of the night when I caught him.”

Eli hesitated, his quill suspended over the diary. What did Cipher mean? How could he be taken inside somebody else’s memory? He glanced nervously at the door to the dormitory, which was growing dark. When he looked back at the diary, he saw fresh words forming.

“Let me show you.”

Eli paused for a fraction of a second and then wrote two letters.

“OK.”

The pages of the diary began to blow as though caught in a high wind, stopping halfway through the month of June. Mouth hanging open, Eli saw that the little square for June thirteenth seemed to have turned into a minuscule television screen. His hands trembling slightly, he raised the book to press his eye against the little window, and before he knew what was happening, he was tilting forward; the window was widening, he felt his body leave his bed, and he was pitched headfirst through the opening in the page, into a whirl of color and shadow.

He felt his feet hit solid ground, and stood, shaking, as the blurred shapes around him came suddenly into focus.

He knew immediately where he was. This circular room with the sleeping portraits was Starling’s office — but it wasn’t Starling who was sitting behind the desk. A wizened, frail-looking wizard, bald except for a few wisps of white hair, was reading a letter by candlelight. Eli had never seen this man before.

“I’m sorry,” he said shakily. “I didn’t mean to butt in —”

But the wizard didn’t look up. He continued to read, frowning slightly. Eli drew nearer to his desk and stammered, “Er — I’ll just go, shall I?”

Still the wizard ignored him. He didn’t seem even to have heard him. Thinking that the wizard might be deaf, Eli raised his voice.

“Sorry I disturbed you. I’ll go now,” he half-shouted.

The wizard folded up the letter with a sigh, stood up, walked past Eli without glancing at him, and went to draw the curtains at his window.

The sky outside the window was ruby-red; it seemed to be sunset. The wizard went back to the desk, sat down, and twiddled his thumbs, watching the door.

Eli looked around the office. No Seeker the phoenix — no whirring silver contraptions. This was Hogwarts as Cipher had known it, meaning that this unknown wizard was headmaster, not Starling, and he, Eli, was little more than a phantom, completely invisible to the people of fifty years ago.

There was a knock on the office door.

“Enter,” said the old wizard in a feeble voice.

A boy of about sixteen entered, taking off his pointed hat. A silver prefect’s badge was glinting on his chest. He was much taller than Eli, but he, too, had jet-black hair.

“Ah, Cipher,” said the headmaster.

“You wanted to see me, Professor Plume?” said Cipher. He looked nervous.

“Sit down,” said Dippet. “I’ve just been reading the letter you sent me.”

“Oh,” said Cipher. He sat down, gripping his hands together very tightly.

“My dear boy,” said Dippet kindly, “I cannot possibly let you stay at school over the summer. Surely you want to go home for the holidays?”

“No,” said Cipher at once. “I’d much rather stay at Hogwarts than go back to that — to that —”

“You live in a Muggle orphanage during the holidays, I believe?” said Dippet curiously.

“Yes, sir,” said Cipher, reddening slightly.

“You are Muggle-born?”

“Half-blood, sir,” said Cipher. “Muggle father, witch mother.”

“And are both your parents — ?”

“My mother died just after I was born, sir. They told me at the orphanage she lived just long enough to name me — Tom after my father, Marvolo after my grandfather.”

Dippet clucked his tongue sympathetically.

“The thing is, Tom,” he sighed, “special arrangements might have been made for you, but in the current circumstances. …”

“You mean all these attacks, sir?” said Cipher, and Eli’s heart leapt, and he moved closer, scared of missing anything.

“Precisely,” said the headmaster. “My dear boy, you must see how foolish it would be of me to allow you to remain at the castle when term ends. Particularly in light of the recent tragedy … the death of that poor little girl. … You will be safer by far at your orphanage. As a matter of fact, the Ministry of Magic is even now talking about closing the school. We are no nearer locating the — er — source of all this unpleasantness. …”

Cipher’s eyes had widened.

“Sir — if the person was caught — if it all stopped —”

“What do you mean?” said Dippet with a squeak in his voice, sitting up in his chair. “Cipher, do you mean you know something about these attacks?”

“No, sir,” said Cipher quickly.

But Eli was sure it was the same sort of “no” that he himself had given Starling.

Dippet sank back, looking faintly disappointed.

“You may go, Tom. …”

Cipher slid off his chair and slouched out of the room. Eli followed him.

Down the moving spiral staircase they went, emerging next to the gargoyle in the darkening corridor. Cipher stopped, and so did Eli, watching him. Eli could tell that Cipher was doing some serious thinking. He was biting his lip, his forehead furrowed.

Then, as though he had suddenly reached a decision, he hurried off, Eli gliding noiselessly behind him. They didn’t see another person until they reached the entrance hall, when a tall wizard with long, sweeping auburn hair and a beard called to Cipher from the marble staircase.

“What are you doing, wandering around this late, Tom?”

Eli gaped at the wizard. He was none other than a fifty-year-younger Starling.

“I had to see the headmaster, sir,” said Cipher.

“Well, hurry off to bed,” said Starling, giving Cipher exactly the kind of penetrating stare Eli knew so well. “Best not to roam the corridors these days. Not since …”

He sighed heavily, bade Cipher good night, and strode off. Cipher watched him walk out of sight and then, moving quickly, headed straight down the stone steps to the dungeons, with Eli in hot pursuit.

But to Eli’s disappointment, Cipher led him not into a hidden passageway or a secret tunnel but to the very dungeon in which Eli had Potions with Thorne. The torches hadn’t been lit, and when Cipher pushed the door almost closed, Eli could only just see him, standing stock-still by the door, watching the passage outside.

It felt to Eli that they were there for at least an hour. All he could see was the figure of Cipher at the door, staring through the crack, waiting like a statue. And just when Eli had stopped feeling expectant and tense and started wishing he could return to the present, he heard something move beyond the door.

Someone was creeping along the passage. He heard whoever it was pass the dungeon where he and Cipher were hidden. Cipher, quiet as a shadow, edged through the door and followed, Eli tiptoeing behind him, forgetting that he couldn’t be heard.

For perhaps five minutes they followed the footsteps, until Cipher stopped suddenly, his head inclined in the direction of new noises. Eli heard a door creak open, and then someone speaking in a hoarse whisper.

“C’mon … gotta get yeh outta here. … C’mon now … in the box …”

There was something familiar about that voice. …

Cipher suddenly jumped around the corner. Eli stepped out behind him. He could see the dark outline of a huge boy who was crouching in front of an open door, a very large box next to it.

“ ’Evening, Granite,” said Cipher sharply.

The boy slammed the door shut and stood up.

“What yer doin’ down here, Tom?”

Cipher stepped closer.

“It’s all over,” he said. “I’m going to have to turn you in, Granite. They’re talking about closing Hogwarts if the attacks don’t stop.”

“What d’yeh —”

“I don’t think you meant to kill anyone. But monsters don’t make good pets. I suppose you just let it out for exercise and —”

“It never killed no one!” said the large boy, backing against the closed door. From behind him, Eli could hear a funny rustling and clicking.

“Come on, Granite,” said Cipher, moving yet closer. “The dead girl’s parents will be here tomorrow. The least Hogwarts can do is make sure that the thing that killed their daughter is slaughtered. …”

“It wasn’t him!” roared the boy, his voice echoing in the dark passage. “He wouldn’! He never!”

“Stand aside,” said Cipher, drawing out his wand.

His spell lit the corridor with a sudden flaming light. The door behind the large boy flew open with such force it knocked him into the wall opposite. And out of it came something that made Eli let out a long, piercing scream unheard by anyone —

A vast, low-slung, hairy body and a tangle of black legs; a gleam of many eyes and a pair of razor-sharp pincers — Cipher raised his wand again, but he was too late. The thing bowled him over as it scuttled away, tearing up the corridor and out of sight. Cipher scrambled to his feet, looking after it; he raised his wand, but the huge boy leapt on him, seized his wand, and threw him back down, yelling, “NOOOOOOO!”

The scene whirled, the darkness became complete; Eli felt himself falling and, with a crash, he landed spread-eagled on his four-poster in the Gryffindor dormitory, Cipher’s diary lying open on his stomach.

Before he had had time to regain his breath, the dormitory door opened and Leo came in.

“There you are,” he said.

Eli sat up. He was sweating and shaking.

“What’s up?” said Leo, looking at him with concern.

“It was Griff, Leo. Griff opened the Chamber of Secrets fifty years ago.”





Chapter 14



Cornelius Swirl





Eli, Leo, and Isla had always known that Griff had an unfortunate liking for large and monstrous creatures. During their first year at Hogwarts he had tried to raise a dragon in his little wooden house, and it would be a long time before they forgot the giant, three-headed dog he’d christened “Fluffy.” And if, as a boy, Griff had heard that a monster was hidden somewhere in the castle, Eli was sure he’d have gone to any lengths for a glimpse of it. He’d probably thought it was a shame that the monster had been cooped up so long, and thought it deserved the chance to stretch its many legs; Eli could just imagine the thirteen-year-old Griff trying to fit a leash and collar on it. But he was equally certain that Griff would never have meant to kill anybody.

Eli half wished he hadn’t found out how to work Cipher’s diary. Again and again Leo and Isla made him recount what he’d seen, until he was heartily sick of telling them and sick of the long, circular conversations that followed.

“Cipher might have got the wrong person,” said Isla. “Maybe it was some other monster that was attacking people. …”

“How many monsters d’you think this place can hold?” Leo asked dully.

“We always knew Griff had been expelled,” said Eli miserably. “And the attacks must’ve stopped after Griff was kicked out. Otherwise, Cipher wouldn’t have got his award.”

Leo tried a different tack.

“Cipher does sound like Evan — who asked him to squeal on Griff, anyway?”

“But the monster had killed someone, Leo,” said Isla.

“And Cipher was going to go back to some Muggle orphanage if they closed Hogwarts,” said Eli. “I don’t blame him for wanting to stay here. …”

“You met Griff down Knockturn Alley, didn’t you, Eli?”

“He was buying a Flesh-Eating Slug Repellent,” said Eli quickly.

The three of them fell silent. After a long pause, Isla voiced the knottiest question of all in a hesitant voice.

“Do you think we should go and ask Griff about it all?”

“That’d be a cheerful visit,” said Leo. “ ‘Hello, Griff. Tell us, have you been setting anything mad and hairy loose in the castle lately?’ ”

In the end, they decided that they would not say anything to Griff unless there was another attack, and as more and more days went by with no whisper from the disembodied voice, they became hopeful that they would never need to talk to him about why he had been expelled. It was now nearly four months since Justin and Barely Headless Nate had been Petrified, and nearly everybody seemed to think that the attacker, whoever it was, had retired for good. Banshee had finally got bored of his “Oh, Ember, you rotter” song, Ernest MacDougal asked Eli quite politely to pass a bucket of leaping toadstools in Herbology one day, and in March several of the Mandrakes threw a loud and raucous party in greenhouse three. This made Professor Sage very happy.

“The moment they start trying to move into each other’s pots, we’ll know they’re fully mature,” she told Eli. “Then we’ll be able to revive those poor people in the hospital wing.”



The second years were given something new to think about during their Easter holidays. The time had come to choose their subjects for the third year, a matter that Isla, at least, took very seriously.

“It could affect our whole future,” she told Eli and Leo as they pored over lists of new subjects, marking them with checks.

“I just want to give up Potions,” said Eli.

“We can’t,” said Leo gloomily. “We keep all our old subjects, or I’d’ve ditched Defense Against the Dark Arts.”

“But that’s very important!” said Isla, shocked.

“Not the way Bright teaches it,” said Leo. “I haven’t learned anything from him except not to set pixies loose.”

Nolan Broadfoot had been sent letters from all the witches and wizards in his family, all giving him different advice on what to choose. Confused and worried, he sat reading the subject lists with his tongue poking out, asking people whether they thought Arithmancy sounded more difficult than the study of Ancient Runes. Dean Hunter, who, like Eli, had grown up with Muggles, ended up closing his eyes and jabbing his wand at the list, then picking the subjects it landed on. Isla took nobody’s advice but signed up for everything.

Eli smiled grimly to himself at the thought of what Uncle Landon and Aunt Beatrice would say if he tried to discuss his career in wizardry with them. Not that he didn’t get any guidance: Evan Brunswick was eager to share his experience.

“Depends where you want to go, Eli,” he said. “It’s never too early to think about the future, so I’d recommend Divination. People say Muggle Studies is a soft option, but I personally think wizards should have a thorough understanding of the non-magical community, particularly if they’re thinking of working in close contact with them — look at my father, he has to deal with Muggle business all the time. My brother Charlie was always more of an outdoor type, so he went for Care of Magical Creatures. Play to your strengths, Eli.”

But the only thing Eli felt he was really good at was Quidditch. In the end, he chose the same new subjects as Leo, feeling that if he was lousy at them, at least he’d have someone friendly to help him.



Gryffindor’s next Quidditch match would be against Hufflepuff. Ash was insisting on team practices every night after dinner, so that Eli barely had time for anything but Quidditch and homework. However, the training sessions were getting better, or at least drier, and the evening before Saturday’s match he went up to his dormitory to drop off his broomstick feeling Gryffindor’s chances for the Quidditch Cup had never been better.

But his cheerful mood didn’t last long. At the top of the stairs to the dormitory, he met Nolan Broadfoot, who was looking frantic.

“Eli — I don’t know who did it — I just found —”

Watching Eli fearfully, Nolan pushed open the door.

The contents of Eli’s trunk had been thrown everywhere. His cloak lay ripped on the floor. The bedclothes had been pulled off his four-poster and the drawer had been pulled out of his bedside cabinet, the contents strewn over the mattress.

Eli walked over to the bed, openmouthed, treading on a few loose pages of Travels with Trolls. As he and Nolan pulled the blankets back onto his bed, Leo, Dean, and Liam came in. Dean swore loudly.

“What happened, Eli?”

“No idea,” said Eli. But Leo was examining Eli’s robes. All the pockets were hanging out.

“Someone’s been looking for something,” said Leo. “Is there anything missing?”

Eli started to pick up all his things and throw them into his trunk. It was only as he threw the last of the Bright books back into it that he realized what wasn’t there.

“Cipher’s diary’s gone,” he said in an undertone to Leo.

“What?”

Eli jerked his head toward the dormitory door and Leo followed him out. They hurried down to the Gryffindor common room, which was half-empty, and joined Isla, who was sitting alone, reading a book called Ancient Runes Made Easy.

Isla looked aghast at the news.

“But — only a Gryffindor could have stolen — nobody else knows our password —”

“Exactly,” said Eli.



They woke the next day to brilliant sunshine and a light, refreshing breeze.

“Perfect Quidditch conditions!” said Ash enthusiastically at the Gryffindor table, loading the team’s plates with scrambled eggs. “Eli, buck up there, you need a decent breakfast.”

Eli had been staring down the packed Gryffindor table, wondering if the new owner of Cipher’s diary was right in front of his eyes. Isla had been urging him to report the robbery, but Eli didn’t like the idea. He’d have to tell a teacher all about the diary, and how many people knew why Griff had been expelled fifty years ago? He didn’t want to be the one who brought it all up again.

As he left the Great Hall with Leo and Isla to go and collect his Quidditch things, another very serious worry was added to Eli’s growing list. He had just set foot on the marble staircase when he heard it yet again —

“Kill this time … let me rip … tear …”

He shouted aloud and Leo and Isla both jumped away from him in alarm.

“The voice!” said Eli, looking over his shoulder. “I just heard it again — didn’t you?”

Leo shook his head, wide-eyed. Isla, however, clapped a hand to her forehead.

“Eli — I think I’ve just understood something! I’ve got to go to the library!”

And she sprinted away, up the stairs.

“What does she understand?” said Eli distractedly, still looking around, trying to tell where the voice had come from.

“Loads more than I do,” said Leo, shaking his head.

“But why’s she got to go to the library?”

“Because that’s what Isla does,” said Leo, shrugging. “When in doubt, go to the library.”

Eli stood, irresolute, trying to catch the voice again, but people were now emerging from the Great Hall behind him, talking loudly, exiting through the front doors on their way to the Quidditch pitch.

“You’d better get moving,” said Leo. “It’s nearly eleven — the match —”

Eli raced up to Gryffindor Tower, collected his Nimbus Two Thousand, and joined the large crowd swarming across the grounds, but his mind was still in the castle along with the bodiless voice, and as he pulled on his scarlet robes in the locker room, his only comfort was that everyone was now outside to watch the game.

The teams walked onto the field to tumultuous applause. Dorian Ash took off for a warm-up flight around the goal posts; Madam Feather released the balls. The Hufflepuffs, who played in canary yellow, were standing in a huddle, having a last-minute discussion of tactics.

Eli was just mounting his broom when Professor Falconer came half marching, half running across the pitch, carrying an enormous purple megaphone.

Eli’s heart dropped like a stone.

“This match has been canceled,” Professor Falconer called through the megaphone, addressing the packed stadium. There were boos and shouts. Dorian Ash, looking devastated, landed and ran toward Professor Falconer without getting off his broomstick.

“But, Professor!” he shouted. “We’ve got to play — the Cup — Gryffindor —”

Professor Falconer ignored him and continued to shout through her megaphone:

“All students are to make their way back to the House common rooms, where their Heads of Houses will give them further information. As quickly as you can, please!”

Then she lowered the megaphone and beckoned Eli over to her.

“Ember, I think you’d better come with me. …”

Wondering how she could possibly suspect him this time, Eli saw Leo detach himself from the complaining crowd; he came running up to them as they set off toward the castle. To Eli’s surprise, Professor Falconer didn’t object.

“Yes, perhaps you’d better come, too, Brunswick. …”

Some of the students swarming around them were grumbling about the match being canceled; others looked worried. Eli and Leo followed Professor Falconer back into the school and up the marble staircase. But they weren’t taken to anybody’s office this time.

“This will be a bit of a shock,” said Professor Falconer in a surprisingly gentle voice as they approached the infirmary. “There has been another attack … another double attack.”

Eli’s insides did a horrible somersault. Professor Falconer pushed the door open and he and Leo entered.

Madam Fern was bending over a sixth-year girl with long, curly hair. Eli recognized her as the Ravenclaw they’d accidentally asked for directions to the Slytherin common room. And on the bed next to her was —

“Isla!” Leo groaned.

Isla lay utterly still, her eyes open and glassy.

“They were found near the library,” said Professor Falconer. “I don’t suppose either of you can explain this? It was on the floor next to them. …”

She was holding up a small, circular mirror.

Eli and Leo shook their heads, both staring at Isla.

“I will escort you back to Gryffindor Tower,” said Professor Falconer heavily. “I need to address the students in any case.”



“All students will return to their House common rooms by six o’clock in the evening. No student is to leave the dormitories after that time. You will be escorted to each lesson by a teacher. No student is to use the bathroom unaccompanied by a teacher. All further Quidditch training and matches are to be postponed. There will be no more evening activities.”

The Gryffindors packed inside the common room listened to Professor Falconer in silence. She rolled up the parchment from which she had been reading and said in a somewhat choked voice, “I need hardly add that I have rarely been so distressed. It is likely that the school will be closed unless the culprit behind these attacks is caught. I would urge anyone who thinks they might know anything about them to come forward.”

She climbed somewhat awkwardly out of the portrait hole, and the Gryffindors began talking immediately.

“That’s two Gryffindors down, not counting a Gryffindor ghost, one Ravenclaw, and one Hufflepuff,” said the Brunswick twins’ friend Jordan Lee, counting on his fingers. “Haven’t any of the teachers noticed that the Slytherins are all safe? Isn’t it obvious all this stuff’s coming from Slytherin? The Heir of Slytherin, the monster of Slytherin — why don’t they just chuck all the Slytherins out?” he roared, to nods and scattered applause.

Evan Brunswick was sitting in a chair behind Lee, but for once he didn’t seem keen to make his views heard. He was looking pale and stunned.

“Evan’s in shock,” Aiden told Eli quietly. “That Ravenclaw girl — Penelope Springwater — she’s a prefect. I don’t think he thought the monster would dare attack a prefect.”

But Eli was only half-listening. He didn’t seem to be able to get rid of the picture of Isla, lying on the hospital bed as though carved out of stone. And if the culprit wasn’t caught soon, he was looking at a lifetime back with the Powells. Tom Cipher had turned Griff in because he was faced with the prospect of a Muggle orphanage if the school closed. Eli now knew exactly how he had felt.

“What’re we going to do?” said Leo quietly in Eli’s ear. “D’you think they suspect Griff?”

“We’ve got to go and talk to him,” said Eli, making up his mind. “I can’t believe it’s him this time, but if he set the monster loose last time he’ll know how to get inside the Chamber of Secrets, and that’s a start.”

“But Falconer said we’ve got to stay in our tower unless we’re in class —”

“I think,” said Eli, more quietly still, “it’s time to get my dad’s old cloak out again.”



Eli had inherited just one thing from his father: a long and silvery Invisibility Cloak. It was their only chance of sneaking out of the school to visit Griff without anyone knowing about it. They went to bed at the usual time, waited until Nolan, Dean, and Liam had stopped discussing the Chamber of Secrets and finally fallen asleep, then got up, dressed again, and threw the cloak over themselves.

The journey through the dark and deserted castle corridors wasn’t enjoyable. Eli, who had wandered the castle at night several times before, had never seen it so crowded after sunset. Teachers, prefects, and ghosts were marching the corridors in pairs, staring around for any unusual activity. Their Invisibility Cloak didn’t stop them making any noise, and there was a particularly tense moment when Leo stubbed his toe only yards from the spot where Thorne stood standing guard. Thankfully, Thorne sneezed at almost exactly the moment Leo swore. It was with relief that they reached the oak front doors and eased them open.

It was a clear, starry night. They hurried toward the lit windows of Griff’s house and pulled off the cloak only when they were right outside his front door.

Seconds after they had knocked, Griff flung it open. They found themselves face-to-face with him aiming a crossbow at them. Fang the boarhound barked loudly behind him.

“Oh,” he said, lowering the weapon and staring at them. “What’re you two doin’ here?”

“What’s that for?” said Eli, pointing at the crossbow as they stepped inside.

“Nothin’ — nothin’ — “ Griff muttered. “I’ve bin expectin’ — doesn’ matter — Sit down — I’ll make tea —”

He hardly seemed to know what he was doing. He nearly extinguished the fire, spilling water from the kettle on it, and then smashed the teapot with a nervous jerk of his massive hand.

“Are you okay, Griff?” said Eli. “Did you hear about Isla?”

“Oh, I heard, all righ’,” said Griff, a slight break in his voice.

He kept glancing nervously at the windows. He poured them both large mugs of boiling water (he had forgotten to add tea bags) and was just putting a slab of fruitcake on a plate when there was a loud knock on the door.

Griff dropped the fruitcake. Eli and Leo exchanged panic-stricken looks, then threw the Invisibility Cloak back over themselves and retreated into a corner. Griff checked that they were hidden, seized his crossbow, and flung open his door once more.

“Good evening, Griff.”

It was Starling. He entered, looking deadly serious, and was followed by a second, very odd-looking man.

The stranger had rumpled gray hair and an anxious expression, and was wearing a strange mixture of clothes: a pinstriped suit, a scarlet tie, a long black cloak, and pointed purple boots. Under his arm he carried a lime-green bowler.

“That’s Father’s boss!” Leo breathed. “Cornelius Swirl, the Minister of Magic!”

Eli elbowed Leo hard to make him shut up.

Griff had gone pale and sweaty. He dropped into one of his chairs and looked from Starling to Cornelius Swirl.

“Bad business, Griff,” said Fudge in rather clipped tones. “Very bad business. Had to come. Four attacks on Muggle-borns. Things’ve gone far enough. Ministry’s got to act.”

“I never,” said Griff, looking imploringly at Starling. “You know I never, Professor Starling, sir —”

“I want it understood, Cornelius, that Griff has my full confidence,” said Starling, frowning at Fudge.

“Look, Albus,” said Fudge, uncomfortably. “Griff’s record’s against him. Ministry’s got to do something — the school governors have been in touch —”

“Yet again, Cornelius, I tell you that taking Griff away will not help in the slightest,” said Starling. His blue eyes were full of a fire Eli had never seen before.

“Look at it from my point of view,” said Fudge, fidgeting with his bowler. “I’m under a lot of pressure. Got to be seen to be doing something. If it turns out it wasn’t Griff, he’ll be back and no more said. But I’ve got to take him. Got to. Wouldn’t be doing my duty —”

“Take me?” said Griff, who was trembling. “Take me where?”

“For a short stretch only,” said Fudge, not meeting Griff’s eyes. “Not a punishment, Griff, more a precaution. If someone else is caught, you’ll be let out with a full apology —”

“Not Azkaban?” croaked Griff.

Before Fudge could answer, there was another loud rap on the door.

Starling answered it. It was Eli’s turn for an elbow in the ribs; he’d let out an audible gasp.

Mr. Caspian Mortem strode into Griff’s hut, swathed in a long black traveling cloak, smiling a cold and satisfied smile. Fang started to growl.

“Already here, Fudge,” he said approvingly. “Good, good …”

“What’re you doin’ here?” said Griff furiously. “Get outta my house!”

“My dear man, please believe me, I have no pleasure at all in being inside your — er — d’you call this a house?” said Caspian Mortem, sneering as he looked around the small cabin. “I simply called at the school and was told that the headmaster was here.”

“And what exactly did you want with me, Caspian?” said Starling. He spoke politely, but the fire was still blazing in his blue eyes.

“Dreadful thing, Starling,” said Mortem lazily, taking out a long roll of parchment, “but the governors feel it’s time for you to step aside. This is an Order of Suspension — you’ll find all twelve signatures on it. I’m afraid we feel you’re losing your touch. How many attacks have there been now? Two more this afternoon, wasn’t it? At this rate, there’ll be no Muggle-borns left at Hogwarts, and we all know what an awful loss that would be to the school.”

“Oh, now, see here, Caspian,” said Fudge, looking alarmed, “Starling suspended — no, no — last thing we want just now —”

“The appointment — or suspension — of the headmaster is a matter for the governors, Fudge,” said Mr. Mortem smoothly. “And as Starling has failed to stop these attacks —”

“See here, Mortem, if Starling can’t stop them,” said Fudge, whose upper lip was sweating now, “I mean to say, who can?”

“That remains to be seen,” said Mr. Mortem with a nasty smile. “But as all twelve of us have voted —”

Griff leapt to his feet, his shaggy black head grazing the ceiling.

“An’ how many did yeh have ter threaten an’ blackmail before they agreed, Mortem, eh?” he roared.

“Dear, dear, you know, that temper of yours will lead you into trouble one of these days, Griff,” said Mr. Mortem. “I would advise you not to shout at the Azkaban guards like that. They won’t like it at all.”

“Yeh can’ take Starling!” yelled Griff, making Fang the boarhound cower and whimper in his basket. “Take him away, an’ the Muggle-borns won’ stand a chance! There’ll be killin’ next!”

“Calm yourself, Griff,” said Starling sharply. He looked at Caspian Mortem.

“If the governors want my removal, Caspian, I shall of course step aside —”

“But —” stuttered Fudge.

“No!” growled Griff.

Starling had not taken his bright blue eyes off Caspian Mortem’s cold gray ones.

“However,” said Starling, speaking very slowly and clearly so that none of them could miss a word, “you will find that I will only truly have left this school when none here are loyal to me. You will also find that help will always be given at Hogwarts to those who ask for it.”

For a second, Eli was almost sure Starling’s eyes flickered toward the corner where he and Leo stood hidden.

“Admirable sentiments,” said Mortem, bowing. “We shall all miss your — er — highly individual way of running things, Albus, and only hope that your successor will manage to prevent any — ah — killins.”

He strode to the cabin door, opened it, and bowed Starling out. Fudge, fiddling with his bowler, waited for Griff to go ahead of him, but Griff stood his ground, took a deep breath, and said carefully, “If anyone wanted ter find out some stuff, all they’d have ter do would be ter follow the spiders. That’d lead ’em right! That’s all I’m sayin’.”

Fudge stared at him in amazement.

“All right, I’m comin’,” said Griff, pulling on his moleskin overcoat. But as he was about to follow Fudge through the door, he stopped again and said loudly, “An’ someone’ll need ter feed Fang while I’m away.”

The door banged shut and Leo pulled off the Invisibility Cloak.

“We’re in trouble now,” he said hoarsely. “No Starling. They might as well close the school tonight. There’ll be an attack a day with him gone.”

Fang started howling, scratching at the closed door.





Chapter 15



Arachne





Summer was creeping over the grounds around the castle; sky and lake alike turned periwinkle blue and flowers large as cabbages burst into bloom in the greenhouses. But with no Griff visible from the castle windows, striding the grounds with Fang at his heels, the scene didn’t look right to Eli; no better, in fact, than the inside of the castle, where things were so horribly wrong.

Eli and Leo had tried to visit Isla, but visitors were now barred from the hospital wing.

“We’re taking no more chances,” Madam Fern told them severely through a crack in the infirmary door. “No, I’m sorry, there’s every chance the attacker might come back to finish these people off. …”

With Starling gone, fear had spread as never before, so that the sun warming the castle walls outside seemed to stop at the mullioned windows. There was barely a face to be seen in the school that didn’t look worried and tense, and any laughter that rang through the corridors sounded shrill and unnatural and was quickly stifled.

Eli constantly repeated Starling’s final words to himself. “I will only truly have left this school when none here are loyal to me. … Help will always be given at Hogwarts to those who ask for it.” But what good were these words? Who exactly were they supposed to ask for help, when everyone was just as confused and scared as they were?

Griff’s hint about the spiders was far easier to understand — the trouble was, there didn’t seem to be a single spider left in the castle to follow. Eli looked everywhere he went, helped (rather reluctantly) by Leo. They were hampered, of course, by the fact that they weren’t allowed to wander off on their own but had to move around the castle in a pack with the other Gryffindors. Most of their fellow students seemed glad that they were being shepherded from class to class by teachers, but Eli found it very irksome.

One person, however, seemed to be thoroughly enjoying the atmosphere of terror and suspicion. Jasper Mortem was strutting around the school as though he had just been appointed Head Boy. Eli didn’t realize what he was so pleased about until the Potions lesson about two weeks after Starling and Griff had left, when, sitting right behind Mortem, Eli overheard him gloating to Lobbe and Gore.

“I always thought Patriarch might be the one who got rid of Starling,” he said, not troubling to keep his voice down. “I told you he thinks Starling’s the worst headmaster the school’s ever had. Maybe we’ll get a decent headmaster now. Someone who won’t want the Chamber of Secrets closed. Falconer won’t last long, she’s only filling in. …”

Thorne swept past Eli, making no comment about Isla’s empty seat and cauldron.

“Sir,” said Mortem loudly. “Sir, why don’t you apply for the headmaster’s job?”

“Now, now, Mortem,” said Thorne, though he couldn’t suppress a thin-lipped smile. “Professor Starling has only been suspended by the governors. I daresay he’ll be back with us soon enough.”

“Yeah, right,” said Mortem, smirking. “I expect you’d have Patriarch’s vote, sir, if you wanted to apply for the job — I’ll tell Patriarch you’re the best teacher here, sir —”

Thorne smirked as he swept off around the dungeon, fortunately not spotting Liam Finnegan, who was pretending to vomit into his cauldron.

“I’m quite surprised the Mudbloods haven’t all packed their bags by now,” Mortem went on. “Bet you five Galleons the next one dies. Pity it wasn’t Hawke —”

The bell rang at that moment, which was lucky; at Mortem’s last words, Leo had leapt off his stool, and in the scramble to collect bags and books, his attempts to reach Mortem went unnoticed.

“Let me at him,” Leo growled as Eli and Dean hung onto his arms. “I don’t care, I don’t need my wand, I’m going to kill him with my bare hands —”

“Hurry up, I’ve got to take you all to Herbology,” barked Thorne over the class’s heads, and off they marched, with Eli, Leo, and Dean bringing up the rear, Leo still trying to get loose. It was only safe to let go of him when Thorne had seen them out of the castle and they were making their way across the vegetable patch toward the greenhouses.

The Herbology class was very subdued; there were now two missing from their number, Justin and Isla.

Professor Sage set them all to work pruning the Abyssinian Shrivelfigs. Eli went to tip an armful of withered stalks onto the compost heap and found himself face-to-face with Ernest MacDougal. Ernest took a deep breath and said, very formally, “I just want to say, Eli, that I’m sorry I ever suspected you. I know you’d never attack Isla Hawke, and I apologize for all the stuff I said. We’re all in the same boat now, and, well —”

He held out a pudgy hand, and Eli shook it.

Ernest and his friend Hanna came to work at the same Shrivelfig as Eli and Leo.

“That Jasper Mortem character,” said Ernest, breaking off dead twigs, “he seems very pleased about all this, doesn’t he? D’you know, I think he might be Slytherin’s heir.”

“That’s clever of you,” said Leo, who didn’t seem to have forgiven Ernest as readily as Eli.

“Do you think it’s Mortem, Eli?” Ernest asked.

“No,” said Eli, so firmly that Ernest and Hanna stared.

A second later, Eli spotted something.

Several large spiders were scuttling over the ground on the other side of the glass, moving in an unnaturally straight line as though taking the shortest route to a prearranged meeting. Eli hit Leo over the hand with his pruning shears.

“Ouch! What’re you —”

Eli pointed out the spiders, following their progress with his eyes screwed up against the sun.

“Oh, yeah,” said Leo, trying, and failing, to look pleased. “But we can’t follow them now —”

Ernest and Hanna were listening curiously.

Eli’s eyes narrowed as he focused on the spiders. If they pursued their fixed course, there could be no doubt about where they would end up.

“Looks like they’re heading for the Forbidden Forest. …”

And Leo looked even unhappier about that.

At the end of the lesson Professor Sage escorted the class to their Defense Against the Dark Arts lesson. Eli and Leo lagged behind the others so they could talk out of earshot.

“We’ll have to use the Invisibility Cloak again,” Eli told Leo. “We can take Fang with us. He’s used to going into the forest with Griff, he might be some help.”

“Right,” said Leo, who was twirling his wand nervously in his fingers. “Er — aren’t there — aren’t there supposed to be werewolves in the forest?” he added as they took their usual places at the back of Bright’s classroom.

Preferring not to answer that question, Eli said, “There are good things in there, too. The centaurs are all right, and the unicorns …”

Leo had never been into the Forbidden Forest before. Eli had entered it only once and had hoped never to do so again.

Bright bounded into the room and the class stared at him. Every other teacher in the place was looking grimmer than usual, but Bright appeared nothing short of buoyant.

“Come now,” he cried, beaming around him. “Why all these long faces?”

People swapped exasperated looks, but nobody answered.

“Don’t you people realize,” said Bright, speaking slowly, as though they were all a bit dim, “the danger has passed! The culprit has been taken away —”

“Says who?” said Dean Hunter loudly.

“My dear young man, the Minister of Magic wouldn’t have taken Griff if he hadn’t been one hundred percent sure that he was guilty,” said Bright, in the tone of someone explaining that one and one made two.

“Oh, yes he would,” said Leo, even more loudly than Dean.

“I flatter myself I know a touch more about Griff’s arrest than you do, Mr. Brunswick,” said Bright in a self-satisfied tone.

Leo started to say that he didn’t think so, somehow, but stopped in midsentence when Eli kicked him hard under the desk.

“We weren’t there, remember?” Eli muttered.

But Bright’s disgusting cheeriness, his hints that he had always thought Griff was no good, his confidence that the whole business was now at an end, irritated Eli so much that he yearned to throw Gadding with Ghouls right in Bright’s stupid face. Instead he contented himself with scrawling a note to Leo: Let’s do it tonight.

Leo read the message, swallowed hard, and looked sideways at the empty seat usually filled by Isla. The sight seemed to stiffen his resolve, and he nodded.



The Gryffindor common room was always very crowded these days, because from six o’clock onward the Gryffindors had nowhere else to go. They also had plenty to talk about, with the result that the common room often didn’t empty until past midnight.

Eli went to get the Invisibility Cloak out of his trunk right after dinner, and spent the evening sitting on it, waiting for the room to clear. Felix and Aiden challenged Eli and Leo to a few games of Exploding Snap, and Lila sat watching them, very subdued in Isla’s usual chair. Eli and Leo kept losing on purpose, trying to finish the games quickly, but even so, it was well past midnight when Felix, Aiden, and Lila finally went to bed.

Eli and Leo waited for the distant sounds of two dormitory doors closing before seizing the cloak, throwing it over themselves, and climbing through the portrait hole.

It was another difficult journey through the castle, dodging all the teachers. At last they reached the entrance hall, slid back the lock on the oak front doors, squeezed between them, trying to stop any creaking, and stepped out into the moonlit grounds.

“ ’Course,” said Leo abruptly as they strode across the black grass, “we might get to the forest and find there’s nothing to follow. Those spiders might not’ve been going there at all. I know it looked like they were moving in that sort of general direction, but …”

His voice trailed away hopefully.

They reached Griff’s house, sad and sorry-looking with its blank windows. When Eli pushed the door open, Fang went mad with joy at the sight of them. Worried he might wake everyone at the castle with his deep, booming barks, they hastily fed him treacle toffee from a tin on the mantelpiece, which glued his teeth together.

Eli left the Invisibility Cloak on Griff’s table. There would be no need for it in the pitch-dark forest.

“C’mon, Fang, we’re going for a walk,” said Eli, patting his leg, and Fang bounded happily out of the house behind them, dashed to the edge of the forest, and lifted his leg against a large sycamore tree.

Eli took out his wand, murmured, “Lumos!” and a tiny light appeared at the end of it, just enough to let them watch the path for signs of spiders.

“Good thinking,” said Leo. “I’d light mine, too, but you know — it’d probably blow up or something. …”

Eli tapped Leo on the shoulder, pointing at the grass. Two solitary spiders were hurrying away from the wandlight into the shade of the trees.

“Okay,” Leo sighed as though resigned to the worst, “I’m ready. Let’s go.”

So, with Fang scampering around them, sniffing tree roots and leaves, they entered the forest. By the glow of Eli’s wand, they followed the steady trickle of spiders moving along the path. They walked behind them for about twenty minutes, not speaking, listening hard for noises other than breaking twigs and rustling leaves. Then, when the trees had become thicker than ever, so that the stars overhead were no longer visible, and Eli’s wand shone alone in the sea of dark, they saw their spider guides leaving the path.

Eli paused, trying to see where the spiders were going, but everything outside his little sphere of light was pitch-black. He had never been this deep into the forest before. He could vividly remember Griff advising him not to leave the forest path last time he’d been in here. But Griff was miles away now, probably sitting in a cell in Azkaban, and he had also said to follow the spiders.

Something wet touched Eli’s hand and he jumped backward, crushing Leo’s foot, but it was only Fang’s nose.

“What d’you reckon?” Eli said to Leo, whose eyes he could just make out, reflecting the light from his wand.

“We’ve come this far,” said Leo.

So they followed the darting shadows of the spiders into the trees. They couldn’t move very quickly now; there were tree roots and stumps in their way, barely visible in the near blackness. Eli could feel Fang’s hot breath on his hand. More than once, they had to stop, so that Eli could crouch down and find the spiders in the wandlight.

They walked for what seemed like at least half an hour, their robes snagging on low-slung branches and brambles. After a while, they noticed that the ground seemed to be sloping downward, though the trees were as thick as ever.

Then Fang suddenly let loose a great, echoing bark, making both Eli and Leo jump out of their skins.

“What?” said Leo loudly, looking around into the pitch-dark, and gripping Eli’s elbow very hard.

“There’s something moving over there,” Eli breathed. “Listen … sounds like something big. …”

They listened. Some distance to their right, the something big was snapping branches as it carved a path through the trees.

“Oh, no,” said Leo. “Oh, no, oh, no, oh —”

“Shut up,” said Eli frantically. “It’ll hear you.”

“Hear me?” said Leo in an unnaturally high voice. “It’s already heard Fang!”

The darkness seemed to be pressing on their eyeballs as they stood, terrified, waiting. There was a strange rumbling noise and then silence.

“What d’you think it’s doing?” said Eli.

“Probably getting ready to pounce,” said Leo.

They waited, shivering, hardly daring to move.

“D’you think it’s gone?” Eli whispered.

“Dunno —”

Then, to their right, came a sudden blaze of light, so bright in the darkness that both of them flung up their hands to shield their eyes. Fang yelped and tried to run, but got lodged in a tangle of thorns and yelped even louder.

“Eli!” Leo shouted, his voice breaking with relief. “Eli, it’s our car!”

“What?”

“Come on!”

Eli blundered after Leo toward the light, stumbling and tripping, and a moment later they had emerged into a clearing.

Mr. Brunswick’s car was standing, empty, in the middle of a circle of thick trees under a roof of dense branches, its headlights ablaze. As Leo walked, openmouthed, toward it, it moved slowly toward him, exactly like a large, turquoise dog greeting its owner.

“It’s been here all the time!” said Leo delightedly, walking around the car. “Look at it. The forest’s turned it wild. …”

The sides of the car were scratched and smeared with mud. Apparently it had taken to trundling around the forest on its own. Fang didn’t seem at all keen on it; he kept close to Eli, who could feel him quivering. His breathing slowing down again, Eli stuffed his wand back into his robes.

“And we thought it was going to attack us!” said Leo, leaning against the car and patting it. “I wondered where it had gone!”

Eli squinted around on the floodlit ground for signs of more spiders, but they had all scuttled away from the glare of the headlights.

“We’ve lost the trail,” he said. “C’mon, let’s go and find them.”

Leo didn’t speak. He didn’t move. His eyes were fixed on a point some ten feet above the forest floor, right behind Eli. His face was livid with terror.

Eli didn’t even have time to turn around. There was a loud clicking noise and suddenly he felt something long and hairy seize him around the middle and lift him off the ground, so that he was hanging facedown. Struggling, terrified, he heard more clicking, and saw Leo’s legs leave the ground, too, heard Fang whimpering and howling — next moment, he was being swept away into the dark trees.

Head hanging, Eli saw that what had hold of him was marching on six immensely long, hairy legs, the front two clutching him tightly below a pair of shining black pincers. Behind him, he could hear another of the creatures, no doubt carrying Leo. They were moving into the very heart of the forest. Eli could hear Fang fighting to free himself from a third monster, whining loudly, but Eli couldn’t have yelled even if he had wanted to; he seemed to have left his voice back with the car in the clearing.

He never knew how long he was in the creature’s clutches; he only knew that the darkness suddenly lifted enough for him to see that the leaf-strewn ground was now swarming with spiders. Craning his neck sideways, he realized that they had reached the ridge of a vast hollow, a hollow that had been cleared of trees, so that the stars shone brightly onto the worst scene he had ever laid eyes on.

Spiders. Not tiny spiders like those surging over the leaves below. Spiders the size of carthorses, eight-eyed, eight-legged, black, hairy, gigantic. The massive specimen that was carrying Eli made its way down the steep slope toward a misty, domed web in the very center of the hollow, while its fellows closed in all around it, clicking their pincers excitedly at the sight of its load.

Eli fell to the ground on all fours as the spider released him. Leo and Fang thudded down next to him. Fang wasn’t howling anymore, but cowering silently on the spot. Leo looked exactly like Eli felt. His mouth was stretched wide in a kind of silent scream and his eyes were popping.

Eli suddenly realized that the spider that had dropped him was saying something. It had been hard to tell, because he clicked his pincers with every word he spoke.

“Arachne!” it called. “Arachne!”

And from the middle of the misty, domed web, a spider the size of a small elephant emerged, very slowly. There was gray in the black of his body and legs, and each of the eyes on his ugly, pincered head was milky white. He was blind.

“What is it?” he said, clicking his pincers rapidly.

“Men,” clicked the spider who had caught Eli.

“Is it Griff?” said Arachne, moving closer, his eight milky eyes wandering vaguely.

“Strangers,” clicked the spider who had brought Leo.

“Kill them,” clicked Arachne fretfully. “I was sleeping. …”

“We’re friends of Griff’s,” Eli shouted. His heart seemed to have left his chest to pound in his throat.

Click, click, click went the pincers of the spiders all around the hollow.

Arachne paused.

“Griff has never sent men into our hollow before,” he said slowly.

“Griff’s in trouble,” said Eli, breathing very fast. “That’s why we’ve come.”

“In trouble?” said the aged spider, and Eli thought he heard concern beneath the clicking pincers. “But why has he sent you?”

Eli thought of getting to his feet but decided against it; he didn’t think his legs would support him. So he spoke from the ground, as calmly as he could.

“They think, up at the school, that Griff’s been setting a — a — something on students. They’ve taken him to Azkaban.”

Arachne clicked his pincers furiously, and all around the hollow the sound was echoed by the crowd of spiders; it was like applause, except applause didn’t usually make Eli feel sick with fear.

“But that was years ago,” said Arachne fretfully. “Years and years ago. I remember it well. That’s why they made him leave the school. They believed that I was the monster that dwells in what they call the Chamber of Secrets. They thought that Griff had opened the Chamber and set me free.”

“And you … you didn’t come from the Chamber of Secrets?” said Eli, who could feel cold sweat on his forehead.

“I!” said Arachne, clicking angrily. “I was not born in the castle. I come from a distant land. A traveler gave me to Griff when I was an egg. Griff was only a boy, but he cared for me, hidden in a cupboard in the castle, feeding me on scraps from the table. Griff is my good friend, and a good man. When I was discovered, and blamed for the death of a girl, he protected me. I have lived here in the forest ever since, where Griff still visits me. He even found me a wife, Mosag, and you see how our family has grown, all through Griff’s goodness. …”

Eli summoned what remained of his courage.

“So you never — never attacked anyone?”

“Never,” croaked the old spider. “It would have been my instinct, but out of respect for Griff, I never harmed a human. The body of the girl who was killed was discovered in a bathroom. I never saw any part of the castle but the cupboard in which I grew up. Our kind like the dark and the quiet. …”

“But then … Do you know what did kill that girl?” said Eli. “Because whatever it is, it’s back and attacking people again —”

His words were drowned by a loud outbreak of clicking and the rustling of many long legs shifting angrily; large black shapes shifted all around him.

“The thing that lives in the castle,” said Arachne, “is an ancient creature we spiders fear above all others. Well do I remember how I pleaded with Griff to let me go, when I sensed the beast moving about the school.”

“What is it?” said Eli urgently.

More loud clicking, more rustling; the spiders seemed to be closing in.

“We do not speak of it!” said Arachne fiercely. “We do not name it! I never even told Griff the name of that dread creature, though he asked me, many times.”

Eli didn’t want to press the subject, not with the spiders pressing closer on all sides. Arachne seemed to be tired of talking. He was backing slowly into his domed web, but his fellow spiders continued to inch slowly toward Eli and Leo.

“We’ll just go, then,” Eli called desperately to Arachne, hearing leaves rustling behind him.

“Go?” said Arachne slowly. “I think not. …”

“But — but —”

“My sons and daughters do not harm Griff, on my command. But I cannot deny them fresh meat, when it wanders so willingly into our midst. Good-bye, friend of Griff.”

Eli spun around. Feet away, towering above him, was a solid wall of spiders, clicking, their many eyes gleaming in their ugly black heads.

Even as he reached for his wand, Eli knew it was no good, there were too many of them, but as he tried to stand, ready to die fighting, a loud, long note sounded, and a blaze of light flamed through the hollow.

Mr. Brunswick’s car was thundering down the slope, headlights glaring, its horn screeching, knocking spiders aside; several were thrown onto their backs, their endless legs waving in the air. The car screeched to a halt in front of Eli and Leo and the doors flew open.

“Get Fang!” Eli yelled, diving into the front seat; Leo seized the boarhound around the middle and threw him, yelping, into the back of the car — the doors slammed shut — Leo didn’t touch the accelerator but the car didn’t need him; the engine roared and they were off, hitting more spiders. They sped up the slope, out of the hollow, and they were soon crashing through the forest, branches whipping the windows as the car wound its way cleverly through the widest gaps, following a path it obviously knew.

Eli looked sideways at Leo. His mouth was still open in the silent scream, but his eyes weren’t popping anymore.

“Are you okay?”

Leo stared straight ahead, unable to speak.

They smashed their way through the undergrowth, Fang howling loudly in the back seat, and Eli saw the side mirror snap off as they squeezed past a large oak. After ten noisy, rocky minutes, the trees thinned, and Eli could again see patches of sky.

The car stopped so suddenly that they were nearly thrown into the windshield. They had reached the edge of the forest. Fang flung himself at the window in his anxiety to get out, and when Eli opened the door, he shot off through the trees to Griff’s house, tail between his legs.
