Eli got out too, and after a minute or so, Leo seemed to regain the feeling in his limbs and followed, still stiff-necked and staring. Eli gave the car a grateful pat as it reversed back into the forest and disappeared from view.

Eli went back into Griff’s cabin to get the Invisibility Cloak. Fang was trembling under a blanket in his basket. When Eli got outside again, he found Leo being violently sick in the pumpkin patch.

“Follow the spiders,” said Leo weakly, wiping his mouth on his sleeve. “I’ll never forgive Griff. We’re lucky to be alive.”

“I bet he thought Arachne wouldn’t hurt friends of his,” said Eli.

“That’s exactly Griff’s problem!” said Leo, thumping the wall of the cabin. “He always thinks monsters aren’t as bad as they’re made out, and look where it’s got him! A cell in Azkaban!” He was shivering uncontrollably now. “What was the point of sending us in there? What have we found out, I’d like to know?”

“That Griff never opened the Chamber of Secrets,” said Eli, throwing the cloak over Leo and prodding him in the arm to make him walk. “He was innocent.”

Leo gave a loud snort. Evidently, hatching Arachne in a cupboard wasn’t his idea of being innocent.

As the castle loomed nearer Eli twitched the cloak to make sure their feet were hidden, then pushed the creaking front doors ajar. They walked carefully back across the entrance hall and up the marble staircase, holding their breath as they passed corridors where watchful sentries were walking. At last they reached the safety of the Gryffindor common room, where the fire had burned itself into glowing ash. They took off the cloak and climbed the winding stair to their dormitory.

Leo fell onto his bed without bothering to get undressed. Eli, however, didn’t feel very sleepy. He sat on the edge of his fourposter, thinking hard about everything Arachne had said.

The creature that was lurking somewhere in the castle, he thought, sounded like a sort of monster Mistborn — even other monsters didn’t want to name it. But he and Leo were no closer to finding out what it was, or how it Petrified its victims. Even Griff had never known what was in the Chamber of Secrets.

Eli swung his legs up onto his bed and leaned back against his pillows, watching the moon glinting at him through the tower window.

He couldn’t see what else they could do. They had hit dead ends everywhere. Cipher had caught the wrong person, the Heir of Slytherin had got off, and no one could tell whether it was the same person, or a different one, who had opened the Chamber this time. There was nobody else to ask. Eli lay down, still thinking about what Arachne had said.

He was becoming drowsy when what seemed like their very last hope occurred to him, and he suddenly sat bolt upright.

“Leo,” he hissed through the dark, “Leo —”

Leo woke with a yelp like Fang’s, stared wildly around, and saw Eli.

“Leo — that girl who died. Arachne said she was found in a bathroom,” said Eli, ignoring Nolan’s snuffling snores from the corner. “What if she never left the bathroom? What if she’s still there?”

Leo rubbed his eyes, frowning through the moonlight. And then he understood, too.

“You don’t think — not Wailing Wren?”





Chapter 16



The Chamber of Secrets





All those times we were in that bathroom, and she was just three toilets away,” said Leo bitterly at breakfast next day, “and we could’ve asked her, and now …”

It had been hard enough trying to look for spiders. Escaping their teachers long enough to sneak into a girls’ bathroom, the girls’ bathroom, moreover, right next to the scene of the first attack, was going to be almost impossible.

But something happened in their first lesson, Transfiguration, that drove the Chamber of Secrets out of their minds for the first time in weeks. Ten minutes into the class, Professor Falconer told them that their exams would start on the first of June, one week from today.

“Exams?” howled Liam Finnegan. “We’re still getting exams?”

There was a loud bang behind Eli as Nolan Broadfoot’s wand slipped, vanishing one of the legs on his desk. Professor Falconer restored it with a wave of her own wand, and turned, frowning, to Liam.

“The whole point of keeping the school open at this time is for you to receive your education,” she said sternly. “The exams will therefore take place as usual, and I trust you are all studying hard.”

Studying hard! It had never occurred to Eli that there would be exams with the castle in this state. There was a great deal of mutinous muttering around the room, which made Professor Falconer scowl even more darkly.

“Professor Starling’s instructions were to keep the school running as normally as possible,” she said. “And that, I need hardly point out, means finding out how much you have learned this year.”

Eli looked down at the pair of white rabbits he was supposed to be turning into slippers. What had he learned so far this year? He couldn’t seem to think of anything that would be useful in an exam.

Leo looked as though he’d just been told he had to go and live in the Forbidden Forest.

“Can you imagine me taking exams with this?” he asked Eli, holding up his wand, which had just started whistling loudly.



Three days before their first exam, Professor Falconer made another announcement at breakfast.

“I have good news,” she said, and the Great Hall, instead of falling silent, erupted.

“Starling’s coming back!” several people yelled joyfully.

“You’ve caught the Heir of Slytherin!” squealed a girl at the Ravenclaw table.

“Quidditch matches are back on!” roared Ash excitedly.

When the hubbub had subsided, Professor Falconer said, “Professor Sage has informed me that the Mandrakes are ready for cutting at last. Tonight, we will be able to revive those people who have been Petrified. I need hardly remind you all that one of them may well be able to tell us who, or what, attacked them. I am hopeful that this dreadful year will end with our catching the culprit.”

There was an explosion of cheering. Eli looked over at the Slytherin table and wasn’t at all surprised to see that Jasper Mortem hadn’t joined in. Leo, however, was looking happier than he’d looked in days.

“It won’t matter that we never asked Wren, then!” he said to Eli. “Isla’ll probably have all the answers when they wake her up! Mind you, she’ll go crazy when she finds out we’ve got exams in three days’ time. She hasn’t studied. It might be kinder to leave her where she is till they’re over.”

Just then, Lila Brunswick came over and sat down next to Leo. She looked tense and nervous, and Eli noticed that her hands were twisting in her lap.

“What’s up?” said Leo, helping himself to more porridge.

Lila didn’t say anything, but glanced up and down the Gryffindor table with a scared look on her face that reminded Eli of someone, though he couldn’t think who.

“Spit it out,” said Leo, watching her.

Eli suddenly realized who Lila looked like. She was rocking backward and forward slightly in her chair, exactly like Nobby did when he was teetering on the edge of revealing forbidden information.

“I’ve got to tell you something,” Lila mumbled, carefully not looking at Eli.

“What is it?” said Eli.

Lila looked as though she couldn’t find the right words.

“What?” said Leo.

Lila opened her mouth, but no sound came out. Eli leaned forward and spoke quietly, so that only Lila and Leo could hear him.

“Is it something about the Chamber of Secrets? Have you seen something? Someone acting oddly?”

Lila drew a deep breath and, at that precise moment, Evan Brunswick appeared, looking tired and wan.

“If you’ve finished eating, I’ll take that seat, Lila. I’m starving, I’ve only just come off patrol duty.”

Lila jumped up as though her chair had just been electrified, gave Evan a fleeting, frightened look, and scampered away. Evan sat down and grabbed a mug from the center of the table.

“Evan!” said Leo angrily. “She was just about to tell us something important!”

Halfway through a gulp of tea, Evan choked.

“What sort of thing?” he said, coughing.

“I just asked her if she’d seen anything odd, and she started to say —”

“Oh — that — that’s nothing to do with the Chamber of Secrets,” said Evan at once.

“How do you know?” said Leo, his eyebrows raised.

“Well, er, if you must know, Lila, er, walked in on me the other day when I was — well, never mind — the point is, she spotted me doing something and I, um, I asked her not to mention it to anybody. I must say, I did think she’d keep her word. It’s nothing, really, I’d just rather —”

Eli had never seen Evan look so uncomfortable.

“What were you doing, Evan?” said Leo, grinning. “Go on, tell us, we won’t laugh.”

Evan didn’t smile back.

“Pass me those rolls, Eli, I’m starving.”



Eli knew the whole mystery might be solved tomorrow without their help, but he wasn’t about to pass up a chance to speak to Wren if it turned up — and to his delight it did, midmorning, when they were being led to History of Magic by Julian Bright.

Bright, who had so often assured them that all danger had passed, only to be proved wrong right away, was now wholeheartedly convinced that it was hardly worth the trouble to see them safely down the corridors. His hair wasn’t as sleek as usual; it seemed he had been up most of the night, patrolling the fourth floor.

“Mark my words,” he said, ushering them around a corner. “The first words out of those poor Petrified people’s mouths will be ‘It was Griff.’ Frankly, I’m astounded Professor Falconer thinks all these security measures are necessary.”

“I agree, sir,” said Eli, making Leo drop his books in surprise.

“Thank you, Eli,” said Bright graciously while they waited for a long line of Hufflepuffs to pass. “I mean, we teachers have quite enough to be getting on with, without walking students to classes and standing guard all night. …”

“That’s right,” said Leo, catching on. “Why don’t you leave us here, sir, we’ve only got one more corridor to go —”

“You know, Brunswick, I think I will,” said Bright. “I really should go and prepare my next class —”

And he hurried off.

“Prepare his class,” Leo sneered after him. “Gone to curl his hair, more like.”

They let the rest of the Gryffindors draw ahead of them, then darted down a side passage and hurried off toward Wailing Wren’s bathroom. But just as they were congratulating each other on their brilliant scheme —

“Ember! Brunswick! What are you doing?”

It was Professor Falconer, and her mouth was the thinnest of thin lines.

“We were — we were —” Leo stammered. “We were going to — to go and see —”

“Isla,” said Eli. Leo and Professor Falconer both looked at him.

“We haven’t seen her for ages, Professor,” Eli went on hurriedly, treading on Leo’s foot, “and we thought we’d sneak into the hospital wing, you know, and tell her the Mandrakes are nearly ready and, er, not to worry —”

Professor Falconer was still staring at him, and for a moment, Eli thought she was going to explode, but when she spoke, it was in a strangely croaky voice.

“Of course,” she said, and Eli, amazed, saw a tear glistening in her beady eye. “Of course, I realize this has all been hardest on the friends of those who have been … I quite understand. Yes, Ember, of course you may visit Miss Hawke. I will inform Professor Shades where you’ve gone. Tell Madam Fern I have given my permission.”

Eli and Leo walked away, hardly daring to believe that they’d avoided detention. As they turned the corner, they distinctly heard Professor Falconer blow her nose.

“That,” said Leo fervently, “was the best story you’ve ever come up with.”

They had no choice now but to go to the hospital wing and tell Madam Fern that they had Professor Falconer’s permission to visit Isla.

Madam Fern let them in, but reluctantly.

“There’s just no point talking to a Petrified person,” she said, and they had to admit she had a point when they’d taken their seats next to Isla. It was plain that Isla didn’t have the faintest inkling that she had visitors, and that they might just as well tell her bedside cabinet not to worry for all the good it would do.

“Wonder if she did see the attacker, though?” said Leo, looking sadly at Isla’s rigid face. “Because if he sneaked up on them all, no one’ll ever know. …”

But Eli wasn’t looking at Isla’s face. He was more interested in her right hand. It lay clenched on top of her blankets, and bending closer, he saw that a piece of paper was scrunched inside her fist.

Making sure that Madam Fern was nowhere near, he pointed this out to Leo.

“Try and get it out,” Leo whispered, shifting his chair so that he blocked Eli from Madam Fern’s view.

It was no easy task. Isla’s hand was clamped so tightly around the paper that Eli was sure he was going to tear it. While Leo kept watch he tugged and twisted, and at last, after several tense minutes, the paper came free.

It was a page torn from a very old library book. Eli smoothed it out eagerly and Leo leaned close to read it, too.



Of the many fearsome beasts and monsters that roam our land, there is none more curious or more deadly than the Basilisk, known also as the King of Serpents. This snake, which may reach gigantic size and live many hundreds of years, is born from a chicken’s egg, hatched beneath a toad. Its methods of killing are most wondrous, for aside from its deadly and venomous fangs, the Basilisk has a murderous stare, and all who are fixed with the beam of its eye shall suffer instant death. Spiders flee before the Basilisk, for it is their mortal enemy, and the Basilisk flees only from the crowing of the rooster, which is fatal to it.



And beneath this, a single word had been written, in a hand Eli recognized as Isla’s. Pipes.

It was as though somebody had just flicked a light on in his brain.

“Leo,” he breathed. “This is it. This is the answer. The monster in the Chamber’s a basilisk — a giant serpent! That’s why I’ve been hearing that voice all over the place, and nobody else has heard it. It’s because I understand Parseltongue. …”

Eli looked up at the beds around him.

“The basilisk kills people by looking at them. But no one’s died — because no one looked it straight in the eye. Colin saw it through his camera. The basilisk burned up all the film inside it, but Colin just got Petrified. Justin … Justin must’ve seen the basilisk through Barely Headless Nate! Nick got the full blast of it, but he couldn’t die again … and Isla and that Ravenclaw prefect were found with a mirror next to them. Isla had just realized the monster was a basilisk. I bet you anything she warned the first person she met to look around corners with a mirror first! And that girl pulled out her mirror — and —”

Leo’s jaw had dropped.

“And Mrs. Whiskers?” he whispered eagerly.

Eli thought hard, picturing the scene on the night of Halloween.

“The water …” he said slowly. “The flood from Wailing Wren’s bathroom. I bet you Mrs. Whiskers only saw the reflection. …”

He scanned the page in his hand eagerly. The more he looked at it, the more it made sense.

“… The crowing of the rooster … is fatal to it!” he read aloud. “Griff’s roosters were killed! The Heir of Slytherin didn’t want one anywhere near the castle once the Chamber was opened! Spiders flee before it! It all fits!”

“But how’s the basilisk been getting around the place?” said Leo. “A giant snake … Someone would’ve seen …”

Eli, however, pointed at the word Isla had scribbled at the foot of the page.

“Pipes,” he said. “Pipes … Leo, it’s been using the plumbing. I’ve been hearing that voice inside the walls. …”

Leo suddenly grabbed Eli’s arm.

“The entrance to the Chamber of Secrets!” he said hoarsely. “What if it’s a bathroom? What if it’s in —”

“— Wailing Wren’s bathroom,” said Eli.

They sat there, excitement coursing through them, hardly able to believe it.

“This means,” said Eli, “I can’t be the only Parselmouth in the school. The Heir of Slytherin’s one, too. That’s how he’s been controlling the basilisk.”

“What’re we going to do?” said Leo, whose eyes were flashing. “Should we go straight to Falconer?”

“Let’s go to the staffroom,” said Eli, jumping up. “She’ll be there in ten minutes. It’s nearly break.”

They ran downstairs. Not wanting to be discovered hanging around in another corridor, they went straight into the deserted staffroom. It was a large, paneled room full of dark, wooden chairs. Eli and Leo paced around it, too excited to sit down.

But the bell to signal break never came.

Instead, echoing through the corridors came Professor Falconer’s voice, magically magnified.

“All students to return to their House dormitories at once. All teachers return to the staffroom. Immediately, please.”

Eli wheeled around to stare at Leo.

“Not another attack? Not now?”

“What’ll we do?” said Leo, aghast. “Go back to the dormitory?”

“No,” said Eli, glancing around. There was an ugly sort of wardrobe to his left, full of the teachers’ cloaks. “In here. Let’s hear what it’s all about. Then we can tell them what we’ve found out.”

They hid themselves inside it, listening to the rumbling of hundreds of people moving overhead, and the staffroom door banging open. From between the musty folds of the cloaks, they watched the teachers filtering into the room. Some of them were looking puzzled, others downright scared. Then Professor Falconer arrived.

“It has happened,” she told the silent staffroom. “A student has been taken by the monster. Right into the Chamber itself.”

Professor Twig let out a squeal. Professor Sage clapped her hands over her mouth. Thorne gripped the back of a chair very hard and said, “How can you be sure?”

“The Heir of Slytherin,” said Professor Falconer, who was very white, “left another message. Right underneath the first one. ‘Her skeleton will lie in the Chamber forever.’ ”

Professor Twig burst into tears.

“Who is it?” said Madam Feather, who had sunk, weak-kneed, into a chair. “Which student?”

“Lila Brunswick,” said Professor Falconer.

Eli felt Leo slide silently down onto the wardrobe floor beside him.

“We shall have to send all the students home tomorrow,” said Professor Falconer. “This is the end of Hogwarts. Starling always said …”

The staffroom door banged open again. For one wild moment, Eli was sure it would be Starling. But it was Bright, and he was beaming.

“So sorry — dozed off — what have I missed?”

He didn’t seem to notice that the other teachers were looking at him with something remarkably like hatred. Thorne stepped forward.

“Just the man,” he said. “The very man. A girl has been snatched by the monster, Bright. Taken into the Chamber of Secrets itself. Your moment has come at last.”

Bright blanched.

“That’s right, Julian,” chipped in Professor Sage. “Weren’t you saying just last night that you’ve known all along where the entrance to the Chamber of Secrets is?”

“I — well, I —” sputtered Bright.

“Yes, didn’t you tell me you were sure you knew what was inside it?” piped up Professor Twig.

“D-did I? I don’t recall —”

“I certainly remember you saying you were sorry you hadn’t had a crack at the monster before Griff was arrested,” said Thorne. “Didn’t you say that the whole affair had been bungled, and that you should have been given a free rein from the first?”

Bright stared around at his stony-faced colleagues.

“I — I really never — you may have misunderstood —”

“We’ll leave it to you, then, Julian,” said Professor Falconer. “Tonight will be an excellent time to do it. We’ll make sure everyone’s out of your way. You’ll be able to tackle the monster all by yourself. A free rein at last.”

Bright gazed desperately around him, but nobody came to the rescue. He didn’t look remotely handsome anymore. His lip was trembling, and in the absence of his usually toothy grin, he looked weak-chinned and feeble.

“V-very well,” he said. “I’ll — I’ll be in my office, getting — getting ready.”

And he left the room.

“Right,” said Professor Falconer, whose nostrils were flared, “that’s got him out from under our feet. The Heads of Houses should go and inform their students what has happened. Tell them the Hogwarts Express will take them home first thing tomorrow. Will the rest of you please make sure no students have been left outside their dormitories.”

The teachers rose and left, one by one.



It was probably the worst day of Eli’s entire life. He, Leo, Felix, and Aiden sat together in a corner of the Gryffindor common room, unable to say anything to each other. Evan wasn’t there. He had gone to send an owl to Mr. and Mrs. Brunswick, then shut himself up in his dormitory.

No afternoon ever lasted as long as that one, nor had Gryffindor Tower ever been so crowded, yet so quiet. Near sunset, Felix and Aiden went up to bed, unable to sit there any longer.

“She knew something, Eli,” said Leo, speaking for the first time since they had entered the wardrobe in the staffroom. “That’s why she was taken. It wasn’t some stupid thing about Evan at all. She’d found out something about the Chamber of Secrets. That must be why she was —” Leo rubbed his eyes frantically. “I mean, she was a pureblood. There can’t be any other reason.”

Eli could see the sun sinking, blood-red, below the skyline. This was the worst he had ever felt. If only there was something they could do. Anything.

“Eli,” said Leo. “D’you think there’s any chance at all she’s not — you know —”

Eli didn’t know what to say. He couldn’t see how Lila could still be alive.

“D’you know what?” said Leo. “I think we should go and see Bright. Tell him what we know. He’s going to try and get into the Chamber. We can tell him where we think it is, and tell him it’s a basilisk in there.”

Because Eli couldn’t think of anything else to do, and because he wanted to be doing something, he agreed. The Gryffindors around them were so miserable, and felt so sorry for the Brunswicks, that nobody tried to stop them as they got up, crossed the room, and left through the portrait hole.

Darkness was falling as they walked down to Bright’s office. There seemed to be a lot of activity going on inside it. They could hear scraping, thumps, and hurried footsteps.

Eli knocked and there was a sudden silence from inside. Then the door opened the tiniest crack and they saw one of Bright’s eyes peering through it.

“Oh — Mr. Ember — Mr. Brunswick —” he said, opening the door a bit wider. “I’m rather busy at the moment — if you would be quick —”

“Professor, we’ve got some information for you,” said Eli. “We think it’ll help you.”

“Er — well — it’s not terribly —” The side of Bright’s face that they could see looked very uncomfortable. “I mean — well — all right —”

He opened the door and they entered.

His office had been almost completely stripped. Two large trunks stood open on the floor. Robes, jade-green, lilac, midnight-blue, had been hastily folded into one of them; books were jumbled untidily into the other. The photographs that had covered the walls were now crammed into boxes on the desk.

“Are you going somewhere?” said Eli.

“Er, well, yes,” said Bright, ripping a life-size poster of himself from the back of the door as he spoke and starting to roll it up. “Urgent call — unavoidable — got to go —”

“What about my sister?” said Leo jerkily.

“Well, as to that — most unfortunate —” said Bright, avoiding their eyes as he wrenched open a drawer and started emptying the contents into a bag. “No one regrets more than I —”

“You’re the Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher!” said Eli. “You can’t go now! Not with all the Dark stuff going on here!”

“Well — I must say — when I took the job —” Bright muttered, now piling socks on top of his robes. “nothing in the job description — didn’t expect —”

“You mean you’re running away? said Eli disbelievingly. “After all that stuff you did in your books —”

“Books can be misleading,” said Bright delicately.

“You wrote them!” Eli shouted.

“My dear boy,” said Bright, straightening up and frowning at Eli. “Do use your common sense. My books wouldn’t have sold half as well if people didn’t think I’d done all those things. No one wants to read about some ugly old Armenian warlock, even if he did save a village from werewolves. He’d look dreadful on the front cover. No dress sense at all. And the witch who banished the Bandon Banshee had a hairy chin. I mean, come on —”

“So you’ve just been taking credit for what a load of other people have done?” said Eli incredulously.

“Eli, Eli,” said Bright, shaking his head impatiently, “it’s not nearly as simple as that. There was work involved. I had to track these people down. Ask them exactly how they managed to do what they did. Then I had to put a Memory Charm on them so they wouldn’t remember doing it. If there’s one thing I pride myself on, it’s my Memory Charms. No, it’s been a lot of work, Eli. It’s not all book signings and publicity photos, you know. You want fame, you have to be prepared for a long hard slog.”

He banged the lids of his trunks shut and locked them.

“Let’s see,” he said. “I think that’s everything. Yes. Only one thing left.”

He pulled out his wand and turned to them.

“Awfully sorry, boys, but I’ll have to put a Memory Charm on you now. Can’t have you blabbing my secrets all over the place. I’d never sell another book —”

Eli reached his wand just in time. Bright had barely raised his, when Eli bellowed, “Expelliarmus!”

Bright was blasted backward, falling over his trunk; his wand flew high into the air; Leo caught it, and flung it out of the open window.

“Shouldn’t have let Professor Thorne teach us that one,” said Eli furiously, kicking Bright’s trunk aside. Bright was looking up at him, feeble once more. Eli was still pointing his wand at him.

“What d’you want me to do?” said Bright weakly. “I don’t know where the Chamber of Secrets is. There’s nothing I can do.”

“You’re in luck,” said Eli, forcing Bright to his feet at wandpoint. “We think we know where it is. And what’s inside it. Let’s go.”

They marched Bright out of his office and down the nearest stairs, along the dark corridor where the messages shone on the wall, to the door of Wailing Wren’s bathroom.

They sent Bright in first. Eli was pleased to see that he was shaking.

Wailing Wren was sitting on the tank of the end toilet.

“Oh, it’s you,” she said when she saw Eli. “What do you want this time?”

“To ask you how you died,” said Eli.

Wren’s whole aspect changed at once. She looked as though she had never been asked such a flattering question.

“Ooooh, it was dreadful,” she said with relish. “It happened right in here. I died in this very stall. I remember it so well. I’d hidden because Olive Thistle was teasing me about my glasses. The door was locked, and I was crying, and then I heard somebody come in. They said something funny. A different language, I think it must have been. Anyway, what really got me was that it was a boy speaking. So I unlocked the door, to tell him to go and use his own toilet, and then —” Wren swelled importantly, her face shining. “I died.”

“How?” said Eli.

“No idea,” said Wren in hushed tones. “I just remember seeing a pair of great, big, yellow eyes. My whole body sort of seized up, and then I was floating away. …” She looked dreamily at Eli. “And then I came back again. I was determined to haunt Olive Thistle, you see. Oh, she was sorry she’d ever laughed at my glasses.”

“Where exactly did you see the eyes?” said Eli.

“Somewhere there,” said Wren, pointing vaguely toward the sink in front of her toilet.

Eli and Leo hurried over to it. Bright was standing well back, a look of utter terror on his face.

It looked like an ordinary sink. They examined every inch of it, inside and out, including the pipes below. And then Eli saw it: Scratched on the side of one of the copper taps was a tiny snake.

“That tap’s never worked,” said Wren brightly as he tried to turn it.

“Eli,” said Leo. “Say something. Something in Parseltongue.”

“But —” Eli thought hard. The only times he’d ever managed to speak Parseltongue were when he’d been faced with a real snake. He stared hard at the tiny engraving, trying to imagine it was real.

“Open up,” he said.

He looked at Leo, who shook his head.

“English,” he said.

Eli looked back at the snake, willing himself to believe it was alive. If he moved his head, the candlelight made it look as though it were moving.

“Open up,” he said.

Except that the words weren’t what he heard; a strange hissing had escaped him, and at once the tap glowed with a brilliant white light and began to spin. Next second, the sink began to move; the sink, in fact, sank, right out of sight, leaving a large pipe exposed, a pipe wide enough for a man to slide into.

Eli heard Leo gasp and looked up again. He had made up his mind what he was going to do.

“I’m going down there,” he said.

He couldn’t not go, not now they had found the entrance to the Chamber, not if there was even the faintest, slimmest, wildest chance that Lila might be alive.

“Me too,” said Leo.

There was a pause.

“Well, you hardly seem to need me,” said Bright, with a shadow of his old smile. “I’ll just —”

He put his hand on the door knob, but Leo and Eli both pointed their wands at him.

“You can go first,” Leo snarled.

White-faced and wandless, Bright approached the opening.

“Boys,” he said, his voice feeble. “Boys, what good will it do?”

Eli jabbed him in the back with his wand. Bright slid his legs into the pipe.

“I really don’t think —” he started to say, but Leo gave him a push, and he slid out of sight. Eli followed quickly. He lowered himself slowly into the pipe, then let go.

It was like rushing down an endless, slimy, dark slide. He could see more pipes branching off in all directions, but none as large as theirs, which twisted and turned, sloping steeply downward, and he knew that he was falling deeper below the school than even the dungeons. Behind him he could hear Leo, thudding slightly at the curves.

And then, just as he had begun to worry about what would happen when he hit the ground, the pipe leveled out, and he shot out of the end with a wet thud, landing on the damp floor of a dark stone tunnel large enough to stand in. Bright was getting to his feet a little ways away, covered in slime and white as a ghost. Eli stood aside as Leo came whizzing out of the pipe, too.

“We must be miles under the school,” said Eli, his voice echoing in the black tunnel.

“Under the lake, probably,” said Leo, squinting around at the dark, slimy walls.

All three of them turned to stare into the darkness ahead.

“Lumos!” Eli muttered to his wand and it lit again. “C’mon,” he said to Leo and Bright, and off they went, their footsteps slapping loudly on the wet floor.

The tunnel was so dark that they could only see a little distance ahead. Their shadows on the wet walls looked monstrous in the wandlight.

“Remember,” Eli said quietly as they walked cautiously forward, “any sign of movement, close your eyes right away. …”

But the tunnel was quiet as the grave, and the first unexpected sound they heard was a loud crunch as Leo stepped on what turned out to be a rat’s skull. Eli lowered his wand to look at the floor and saw that it was littered with small animal bones. Trying very hard not to imagine what Lila might look like if they found her, Eli led the way forward, around a dark bend in the tunnel.

“Eli — there’s something up there —” said Leo hoarsely, grabbing Eli’s shoulder.

They froze, watching. Eli could just see the outline of something huge and curved, lying right across the tunnel. It wasn’t moving.

“Maybe it’s asleep,” he breathed, glancing back at the other two. Bright’s hands were pressed over his eyes. Eli turned back to look at the thing, his heart beating so fast it hurt.

Very slowly, his eyes as narrow as he could make them and still see, Eli edged forward, his wand held high.

The light slid over a gigantic snake skin, of a vivid, poisonous green, lying curled and empty across the tunnel floor. The creature that had shed it must have been twenty feet long at least.

“Blimey,” said Leo weakly.

There was a sudden movement behind them. Julian Bright’s knees had given way.

“Get up,” said Leo sharply, pointing his wand at Bright.

Bright got to his feet — then he dived at Leo, knocking him to the ground.

Eli jumped forward, but too late — Bright was straightening up, panting, Leo’s wand in his hand and a gleaming smile back on his face.

“The adventure ends here, boys!” he said. “I shall take a bit of this skin back up to the school, tell them I was too late to save the girl, and that you two tragically lost your minds at the sight of her mangled body — say good-bye to your memories!”

He raised Leo’s Spellotaped wand high over his head and yelled, “Obliviate!”

The wand exploded with the force of a small bomb. Eli flung his arms over his head and ran, slipping over the coils of snake skin, out of the way of great chunks of tunnel ceiling that were thundering to the floor. Next moment, he was standing alone, gazing at a solid wall of broken rock.

“Leo!” he shouted. “Are you okay? Leo!”

“I’m here!” came Leo’s muffled voice from behind the rock-fall. “I’m okay — this git’s not, though — he got blasted by the wand —”

There was a dull thud and a loud “ow!” It sounded as though Leo had just kicked Bright in the shins.

“What now?” Leo’s voice said, sounding desperate. “We can’t get through — it’ll take ages. …”

Eli looked up at the tunnel ceiling. Huge cracks had appeared in it. He had never tried to break apart anything as large as these rocks by magic, and now didn’t seem a good moment to try — what if the whole tunnel caved in?

There was another thud and another “ow!” from behind the rocks. They were wasting time. Lila had already been in the Chamber of Secrets for hours. … Eli knew there was only one thing to do.

“Wait there,” he called to Leo. “Wait with Bright. I’ll go on. … If I’m not back in an hour …”

There was a very pregnant pause.

“I’ll try and shift some of this rock,” said Leo, who seemed to be trying to keep his voice steady. “So you can — can get back through. And, Eli —”

“See you in a bit,” said Eli, trying to inject some confidence into his shaking voice.

And he set off alone past the giant snake skin.

Soon the distant noise of Leo straining to shift the rocks was gone. The tunnel turned and turned again. Every nerve in Eli’s body was tingling unpleasantly. He wanted the tunnel to end, yet dreaded what he’d find when it did. And then, at last, as he crept around yet another bend, he saw a solid wall ahead on which two entwined serpents were carved, their eyes set with great, glinting emeralds.

Eli approached, his throat very dry. There was no need to pretend these stone snakes were real; their eyes looked strangely alive.

He could guess what he had to do. He cleared his throat, and the emerald eyes seemed to flicker.

“Open,” said Eli, in a low, faint hiss.

The serpents parted as the wall cracked open, the halves slid smoothly out of sight, and Eli, shaking from head to foot, walked inside.





Chapter 17



The Heir of Slytherin





He was standing at the end of a very long, dimly lit chamber. Towering stone pillars entwined with more carved serpents rose to support a ceiling lost in darkness, casting long, black shadows through the odd, greenish gloom that filled the place.

His heart beating very fast, Eli stood listening to the chill silence. Could the basilisk be lurking in a shadowy corner, behind a pillar? And where was Lila?

He pulled out his wand and moved forward between the serpentine columns. Every careful footstep echoed loudly off the shadowy walls. He kept his eyes narrowed, ready to clamp them shut at the smallest sign of movement. The hollow eye sockets of the stone snakes seemed to be following him. More than once, with a jolt of the stomach, he thought he saw one stir.

Then, as he drew level with the last pair of pillars, a statue high as the Chamber itself loomed into view, standing against the back wall.

Eli had to crane his neck to look up into the giant face above: It was ancient and monkeyish, with a long, thin beard that fell almost to the bottom of the wizard’s sweeping stone robes, where two enormous gray feet stood on the smooth Chamber floor. And between the feet, facedown, lay a small, black-robed figure with flaming-red hair.

“Lila!” Eli muttered, sprinting to her and dropping to his knees. “Lila — don’t be dead — please don’t be dead —” He flung his wand aside, grabbed Lila’s shoulders, and turned her over. Her face was white as marble, and as cold, yet her eyes were closed, so she wasn’t Petrified. But then she must be —

“Lila, please wake up,” Eli muttered desperately, shaking her. Lila’s head lolled hopelessly from side to side.

“She won’t wake,” said a soft voice.

Eli jumped and spun around on his knees.

A tall, black-haired boy was leaning against the nearest pillar, watching. He was strangely blurred around the edges, as though Eli were looking at him through a misted window. But there was no mistaking him —

“Tom — Tom Cipher?”

Cipher nodded, not taking his eyes off Eli’s face.

“What d’you mean, she won’t wake?” Eli said desperately. “She’s not — she’s not — ?”

“She’s still alive,” said Cipher. “But only just.”

Eli stared at him. Tom Cipher had been at Hogwarts fifty years ago, yet here he stood, a weird, misty light shining about him, not a day older than sixteen.

“Are you a ghost?” Eli said uncertainly.

“A memory,” said Cipher quietly. “Preserved in a diary for fifty years.”

He pointed toward the floor near the statue’s giant toes. Lying open there was the little black diary Eli had found in Wailing Wren’s bathroom. For a second, Eli wondered how it had got there — but there were more pressing matters to deal with.

“You’ve got to help me, Tom,” Eli said, raising Lila’s head again. “We’ve got to get her out of here. There’s a basilisk … I don’t know where it is, but it could be along any moment. … Please, help me —”

Cipher didn’t move. Eli, sweating, managed to hoist Lila half off the floor, and bent to pick up his wand again.

But his wand had gone.

“Did you see — ?”

He looked up. Cipher was still watching him — twirling Eli’s wand between his long fingers.

“Thanks,” said Eli, stretching out his hand for it.

A smile curled the corners of Cipher’s mouth. He continued to stare at Eli, twirling the wand idly.

“Listen,” said Eli urgently, his knees sagging with Lila’s dead weight. “We’ve got to go! If the basilisk comes —”

“It won’t come until it is called,” said Cipher calmly.

Eli lowered Lila back onto the floor, unable to hold her up any longer.

“What d’you mean?” he said. “Look, give me my wand, I might need it —”

Cipher’s smile broadened.

“You won’t be needing it,” he said.

Eli stared at him.

“What d’you mean, I won’t be — ?”

“I’ve waited a long time for this, Eli Ember,” said Cipher. “For the chance to see you. To speak to you.”

“Look,” said Eli, losing patience, “I don’t think you get it. We’re in the Chamber of Secrets. We can talk later —”

“We’re going to talk now,” said Cipher, still smiling broadly, and he pocketed Eli’s wand.

Eli stared at him. There was something very funny going on here. …

“How did Lila get like this?” he asked slowly.

“Well, that’s an interesting question,” said Cipher pleasantly. “And quite a long story. I suppose the real reason Lila Brunswick’s like this is because she opened her heart and spilled all her secrets to an invisible stranger.”

“What are you talking about?” said Eli.

“The diary,” said Cipher. “My diary. Little Lila’s been writing in it for months and months, telling me all her pitiful worries and woes — how her brothers tease her, how she had to come to school with secondhand robes and books, how” — Cipher’s eyes glinted — “how she didn’t think famous, good, great Eli Ember would ever like her. …”

All the time he spoke, Cipher’s eyes never left Eli’s face. There was an almost hungry look in them.

“It’s very boring, having to listen to the silly little troubles of an eleven-year-old girl,” he went on. “But I was patient. I wrote back. I was sympathetic, I was kind. Lila simply loved me. No one’s ever understood me like you, Tom. … I’m so glad I’ve got this diary to confide in. … It’s like having a friend I can carry around in my pocket. …”

Cipher laughed, a high, cold laugh that didn’t suit him. It made the hairs stand up on the back of Eli’s neck.

“If I say it myself, Eli, I’ve always been able to charm the people I needed. So Lila poured out her soul to me, and her soul happened to be exactly what I wanted. … I grew stronger and stronger on a diet of her deepest fears, her darkest secrets. I grew powerful, far more powerful than little Miss Brunswick. Powerful enough to start feeding Miss Brunswick a few of my secrets, to start pouring a little of my soul back into her …”

“What d’you mean?” said Eli, whose mouth had gone very dry.

“Haven’t you guessed yet, Eli Ember?” said Cipher softly. “Lila Brunswick opened the Chamber of Secrets. She strangled the school roosters and daubed threatening messages on the walls. She set the Serpent of Slytherin on four Mudbloods, and the Squib’s cat.”

“No,” Eli whispered.

“Yes,” said Cipher, calmly. “Of course, she didn’t know what she was doing at first. It was very amusing. I wish you could have seen her new diary entries … far more interesting, they became. … Dear Tom,” he recited, watching Eli’s horrified face, “I think I’m losing my memory. There are rooster feathers all over my robes and I don’t know how they got there. Dear Tom, I can’t remember what I did on the night of Halloween, but a cat was attacked and I’ve got paint all down my front. Dear Tom, Evan keeps telling me I’m pale and I’m not myself. I think he suspects me. … There was another attack today and I don’t know where I was. Tom, what am I going to do? I think I’m going mad. … I think I’m the one attacking everyone, Tom!”

Eli’s fists were clenched, the nails digging deep into his palms.

“It took a very long time for stupid little Lila to stop trusting her diary,” said Cipher. “But she finally became suspicious and tried to dispose of it. And that’s where you came in, Eli. You found it, and I couldn’t have been more delighted. Of all the people who could have picked it up, it was you, the very person I was most anxious to meet. …”

“And why did you want to meet me?” said Eli. Anger was coursing through him, and it was an effort to keep his voice steady.

“Well, you see, Lila told me all about you, Eli,” said Cipher. “Your whole fascinating history.” His eyes roved over the lightning scar on Eli’s forehead, and their expression grew hungrier. “I knew I must find out more about you, talk to you, meet you if I could. So I decided to show you my famous capture of that great oaf, Griff, to gain your trust —”

“Griff’s my friend,” said Eli, his voice now shaking. “And you framed him, didn’t you? I thought you made a mistake, but —”

Cipher laughed his high laugh again.

“It was my word against Griff’s, Eli. Well, you can imagine how it looked to old Armando Plume. On the one hand, Tom Cipher, poor but brilliant, parentless but so brave, school prefect, model student … on the other hand, big, blundering Griff, in trouble every other week, trying to raise werewolf cubs under his bed, sneaking off to the Forbidden Forest to wrestle trolls … but I admit, even I was surprised how well the plan worked. I thought someone must realize that Griff couldn’t possibly be the Heir of Slytherin. It had taken me five whole years to find out everything I could about the Chamber of Secrets and discover the secret entrance … as though Griff had the brains, or the power!

“Only the Transfiguration teacher, Starling, seemed to think Griff was innocent. He persuaded Dippet to keep Griff and train him as gamekeeper. Yes, I think Starling might have guessed. … Starling never seemed to like me as much as the other teachers did. …”

“I bet Starling saw right through you,” said Eli, his teeth gritted.

“Well, he certainly kept an annoyingly close watch on me after Griff was expelled,” said Cipher carelessly. “I knew it wouldn’t be safe to open the Chamber again while I was still at school. But I wasn’t going to waste those long years I’d spent searching for it. I decided to leave behind a diary, preserving my sixteen-year-old self in its pages, so that one day, with luck, I would be able to lead another in my footsteps, and finish Salvatore Serpentine’s noble work.”

“Well, you haven’t finished it,” said Eli triumphantly. “No one’s died this time, not even the cat. In a few hours the Mandrake Draught will be ready and everyone who was Petrified will be all right again —”

“Haven’t I already told you,” said Cipher quietly, “that killing Mudbloods doesn’t matter to me anymore? For many months now, my new target has been — you.”

Eli stared at him.

“Imagine how angry I was when the next time my diary was opened, it was Lila who was writing to me, not you. She saw you with the diary, you see, and panicked. What if you found out how to work it, and I repeated all her secrets to you? What if, even worse, I told you who’d been strangling roosters? So the foolish little brat waited until your dormitory was deserted and stole it back. But I knew what I must do. It was clear to me that you were on the trail of Slytherin’s heir. From everything Lila had told me about you, I knew you would go to any lengths to solve the mystery — particularly if one of your best friends was attacked. And Lila had told me the whole school was buzzing because you could speak Parseltongue. …

“So I made Lila write her own farewell on the wall and come down here to wait. She struggled and cried and became very boring. But there isn’t much life left in her. … She put too much into the diary, into me. Enough to let me leave its pages at last. … I have been waiting for you to appear since we arrived here. I knew you’d come. I have many questions for you, Eli Ember.”

“Like what?” Eli spat, fists still clenched.

“Well,” said Cipher, smiling pleasantly, “how is it that you — a skinny boy with no extraordinary magical talent — managed to defeat the greatest wizard of all time? How did you escape with nothing but a scar, while Mistborn’s powers were destroyed?”

There was an odd red gleam in his hungry eyes now.

“Why do you care how I escaped?” said Eli slowly. “Mistborn was after your time. …”

“Mistborn,” said Cipher softly, “is my past, present, and future, Eli Ember. …”

He pulled Eli’s wand from his pocket and began to trace it through the air, writing three shimmering words:

tom marvolo riddle

Then he waved the wand once, and the letters of his name rearranged themselves:

i am lord voldemort

“You see?” he whispered. “It was a name I was already using at Hogwarts, to my most intimate friends only, of course. You think I was going to use my filthy Muggle father’s name forever? I, in whose veins runs the blood of Salvatore Serpentine himself, through my mother’s side? I, keep the name of a foul, common Muggle, who abandoned me even before I was born, just because he found out his wife was a witch? No, Eli — I fashioned myself a new name, a name I knew wizards everywhere would one day fear to speak, when I had become the greatest sorcerer in the world!”

Eli’s brain seemed to have jammed. He stared numbly at Cipher, at the orphaned boy who had grown up to murder Eli’s own parents, and so many others. … At last he forced himself to speak.

“You’re not,” he said, his quiet voice full of hatred.

“Not what?” snapped Cipher.

“Not the greatest sorcerer in the world,” said Eli, breathing fast. “Sorry to disappoint you and all that, but the greatest wizard in the world is Zephyr Starling. Everyone says so. Even when you were strong, you didn’t dare try and take over at Hogwarts. Starling saw through you when you were at school and he still frightens you now, wherever you’re hiding these days —”

The smile had gone from Cipher’s face, to be replaced by a very ugly look.

“Starling’s been driven out of this castle by the mere memory of me!” he hissed.

“He’s not as gone as you might think!” Eli retorted. He was speaking at random, wanting to scare Cipher, wishing rather than believing it to be true —

Cipher opened his mouth, but froze.

Music was coming from somewhere. Cipher whirled around to stare down the empty Chamber. The music was growing louder. It was eerie, spine-tingling, unearthly; it lifted the hair on Eli’s scalp and made his heart feel as though it was swelling to twice its normal size. Then, as the music reached such a pitch that Eli felt it vibrating inside his own ribs, flames erupted at the top of the nearest pillar.

A crimson bird the size of a swan had appeared, piping its weird music to the vaulted ceiling. It had a glittering golden tail as long as a peacock’s and gleaming golden talons, which were gripping a ragged bundle.

A second later, the bird was flying straight at Eli. It dropped the ragged thing it was carrying at his feet, then landed heavily on his shoulder. As it folded its great wings, Eli looked up and saw it had a long, sharp golden beak and a beady black eye.

The bird stopped singing. It sat still and warm next to Eli’s cheek, gazing steadily at Cipher.

“That’s a phoenix. …” said Cipher, staring shrewdly back at it.

“Seeker?” Eli breathed, and he felt the bird’s golden claws squeeze his shoulder gently.

“And that —” said Cipher, now eyeing the ragged thing that Seeker had dropped, “that’s the old school Sorting Hat —”

So it was. Patched, frayed, and dirty, the hat lay motionless at Eli’s feet.

Cipher began to laugh again. He laughed so hard that the dark Chamber rang with it, as though ten Ciphers were laughing at once —

“This is what Starling sends his defender! A songbird and an old hat! Do you feel brave, Eli Ember? Do you feel safe now?”

Eli didn’t answer. He might not see what use Seeker or the Sorting Hat were, but he was no longer alone, and he waited for Cipher to stop laughing with his courage mounting.

“To business, Eli,” said Cipher, still smiling broadly. “Twice — in your past, in my future — we have met. And twice I failed to kill you. How did you survive? Tell me everything. The longer you talk,” he added softly, “the longer you stay alive.”

Eli was thinking fast, weighing his chances. Cipher had the wand. He, Eli, had Seeker and the Sorting Hat, neither of which would be much good in a duel. It looked bad, all right … but the longer Cipher stood there, the more life was dwindling out of Lila … and in the meantime, Eli noticed suddenly, Cipher’s outline was becoming clearer, more solid. … If it had to be a fight between him and Cipher, better sooner than later.

“No one knows why you lost your powers when you attacked me,” said Eli abruptly. “I don’t know myself. But I know why you couldn’t kill me. Because my mother died to save me. My common Muggle-born mother,” he added, shaking with suppressed rage. “She stopped you killing me. And I’ve seen the real you, I saw you last year. You’re a wreck. You’re barely alive. That’s where all your power got you. You’re in hiding. You’re ugly, you’re foul —”

Cipher’s face contorted. Then he forced it into an awful smile.

“So. Your mother died to save you. Yes, that’s a powerful counter-charm. I can see now … there is nothing special about you, after all. I wondered, you see. There are strange likenesses between us, after all. Even you must have noticed. Both half-bloods, orphans, raised by Muggles. Probably the only two Parselmouths to come to Hogwarts since the great Slytherin himself. We even look something alike … but after all, it was merely a lucky chance that saved you from me. That’s all I wanted to know.”

Eli stood, tense, waiting for Cipher to raise his wand. But Cipher’s twisted smile was widening again.

“Now, Eli, I’m going to teach you a little lesson. Let’s match the powers of Mistborn, Heir of Salvatore Serpentine, against famous Eli Ember, and the best weapons Starling can give him. …”

He cast an amused eye over Seeker and the Sorting Hat, then walked away. Eli, fear spreading up his numb legs, watched Cipher stop between the high pillars and look up into the stone face of Slytherin, high above him in the half-darkness. Cipher opened his mouth wide and hissed — but Eli understood what he was saying. …

“Speak to me, Slytherin, greatest of the Hogwarts Four.”

Eli wheeled around to look up at the statue, Seeker swaying on his shoulder.

Slytherin’s gigantic stone face was moving. Horrorstruck, Eli saw his mouth opening, wider and wider, to make a huge black hole.

And something was stirring inside the statue’s mouth. Something was slithering up from its depths.

Eli backed away until he hit the dark Chamber wall, and as he shut his eyes tight he felt Seeker’ wing sweep his cheek as he took flight. Eli wanted to shout, “Don’t leave me!” but what chance did a phoenix have against the king of serpents?

Something huge hit the stone floor of the Chamber. Eli felt it shudder — he knew what was happening, he could sense it, could almost see the giant serpent uncoiling itself from Slytherin’s mouth. Then he heard Cipher’s hissing voice:

“Kill him.”

The basilisk was moving toward Eli; he could hear its heavy body slithering heavily across the dusty floor. Eyes still tightly shut, Eli began to run blindly sideways, his hands outstretched, feeling his way — Mistborn was laughing —

Eli tripped. He fell hard onto the stone and tasted blood — the serpent was barely feet from him, he could hear it coming —

There was a loud, explosive spitting sound right above him, and then something heavy hit Eli so hard that he was smashed into the wall. Waiting for fangs to sink through his body he heard more mad hissing, something thrashing wildly off the pillars —

He couldn’t help it — he opened his eyes wide enough to squint at what was going on.

The enormous serpent, bright, poisonous green, thick as an oak trunk, had raised itself high in the air and its great blunt head was weaving drunkenly between the pillars. As Eli trembled, ready to close his eyes if it turned, he saw what had distracted the snake.

Seeker was soaring around its head, and the basilisk was snapping furiously at him with fangs long and thin as sabers —

Seeker dived. His long golden beak sank out of sight and a sudden shower of dark blood spattered the floor. The snake’s tail thrashed, narrowly missing Eli, and before Eli could shut his eyes, it turned — Eli looked straight into its face and saw that its eyes, both its great, bulbous yellow eyes, had been punctured by the phoenix; blood was streaming to the floor, and the snake was spitting in agony.

“NO!” Eli heard Cipher screaming. “LEAVE THE BIRD! LEAVE THE BIRD! THE BOY IS BEHIND YOU! YOU CAN STILL SMELL HIM! KILL HIM!”

The blinded serpent swayed, confused, still deadly. Seeker was circling its head, piping his eerie song, jabbing here and there at its scaly nose as the blood poured from its ruined eyes.

“Help me, help me,” Eli muttered wildly, “someone — anyone —”

The snake’s tail whipped across the floor again. Eli ducked. Something soft hit his face.

The basilisk had swept the Sorting Hat into Eli’s arms. Eli seized it. It was all he had left, his only chance — he rammed it onto his head and threw himself flat onto the floor as the basilisk’s tail swung over him again.

Help me — help me — Eli thought, his eyes screwed tight under the hat. Please help me —

There was no answering voice. Instead, the hat contracted, as though an invisible hand was squeezing it very tightly.

Something very hard and heavy thudded onto the top of Eli’s head, almost knocking him out. Stars winking in front of his eyes, he grabbed the top of the hat to pull it off and felt something long and hard beneath it.

A gleaming silver sword had appeared inside the hat, its handle glittering with rubies the size of eggs.

“KILL THE BOY! LEAVE THE BIRD! THE BOY IS BEHIND YOU! SNIFF — SMELL HIM!”

Eli was on his feet, ready. The basilisk’s head was falling, its body coiling around, hitting pillars as it twisted to face him. He could see the vast, bloody eye sockets, see the mouth stretching wide, wide enough to swallow him whole, lined with fangs long as his sword, thin, glittering, venomous —

It lunged blindly — Eli dodged and it hit the Chamber wall. It lunged again, and its forked tongue lashed Eli’s side. He raised the sword in both his hands —

The basilisk lunged again, and this time its aim was true — Eli threw his whole weight behind the sword and drove it to the hilt into the roof of the serpent’s mouth —

But as warm blood drenched Eli’s arms, he felt a searing pain just above his elbow. One long, poisonous fang was sinking deeper and deeper into his arm and it splintered as the basilisk keeled over sideways and fell, twitching, to the floor.

Eli slid down the wall. He gripped the fang that was spreading poison through his body and wrenched it out of his arm. But he knew it was too late. White-hot pain was spreading slowly and steadily from the wound. Even as he dropped the fang and watched his own blood soaking his robes, his vision went foggy. The Chamber was dissolving in a whirl of dull color.

A patch of scarlet swam past, and Eli heard a soft clatter of claws beside him.

“Seeker,” said Eli thickly. “You were fantastic, Seeker. …” He felt the bird lay its beautiful head on the spot where the serpent’s fang had pierced him.

He could hear echoing footsteps and then a dark shadow moved in front of him.

“You’re dead, Eli Ember,” said Cipher’s voice above him. “Dead. Even Starling’s bird knows it. Do you see what he’s doing, Ember? He’s crying.”

Eli blinked. Seeker’s head slid in and out of focus. Thick, pearly tears were trickling down the glossy feathers.

“I’m going to sit here and watch you die, Eli Ember. Take your time. I’m in no hurry.”

Eli felt drowsy. Everything around him seemed to be spinning.

“So ends the famous Eli Ember,” said Cipher’s distant voice. “Alone in the Chamber of Secrets, forsaken by his friends, defeated at last by the Dark Lord he so unwisely challenged. You’ll be back with your dear Mudblood mother soon, Eli. … She bought you twelve years of borrowed time … but Mistborn got you in the end, as you knew he must. …”

If this is dying, thought Eli, it’s not so bad.

Even the pain was leaving him. …

But was this dying? Instead of going black, the Chamber seemed to be coming back into focus. Eli gave his head a little shake and there was Seeker, still resting his head on Eli’s arm. A pearly patch of tears was shining all around the wound — except that there was no wound —

“Get away, bird,” said Cipher’s voice suddenly. “Get away from him — I said, get away —”

Eli raised his head. Cipher was pointing Eli’s wand at Seeker; there was a bang like a gun, and Seeker took flight again in a whirl of gold and scarlet.

“Phoenix tears …” said Cipher quietly, staring at Eli’s arm. “Of course … healing powers … I forgot …”

He looked into Eli’s face. “But it makes no difference. In fact, I prefer it this way. Just you and me, Eli Ember … you and me. …”

He raised the wand —

Then, in a rush of wings, Seeker had soared back overhead and something fell into Eli’s lap — the diary.

For a split second, both Eli and Cipher, wand still raised, stared at it. Then, without thinking, without considering, as though he had meant to do it all along, Eli seized the basilisk fang on the floor next to him and plunged it straight into the heart of the book.

There was a long, dreadful, piercing scream. Ink spurted out of the diary in torrents, streaming over Eli’s hands, flooding the floor. Cipher was writhing and twisting, screaming and flailing and then —

He had gone. Eli’s wand fell to the floor with a clatter and there was silence. Silence except for the steady drip drip of ink still oozing from the diary. The basilisk venom had burned a sizzling hole right through it.

Shaking all over, Eli pulled himself up. His head was spinning as though he’d just traveled miles by Floo powder. Slowly, he gathered together his wand and the Sorting Hat, and, with a huge tug, retrieved the glittering sword from the roof of the basilisk’s mouth.

Then came a faint moan from the end of the Chamber. Lila was stirring. As Eli hurried toward her, she sat up. Her bemused eyes traveled from the huge form of the dead basilisk, over Eli, in his blood-soaked robes, then to the diary in his hand. She drew a great, shuddering gasp and tears began to pour down her face.

“Eli — oh, Eli — I tried to tell you at b-breakfast, but I c-couldn’t say it in front of Evan — it was me, Eli — but I — I s-swear I d-didn’t mean to — R-Cipher made me, he t-took me over — and — how did you kill that — that thing? W-where’s Cipher? The last thing I r-remember is him coming out of the diary —”

“It’s all right,” said Eli, holding up the diary, and showing Lila the fang hole, “Cipher’s finished. Look! Him and the basilisk. C’mon, Lila, let’s get out of here —”

“I’m going to be expelled!” Lila wept as Eli helped her awkwardly to her feet. “I’ve looked forward to coming to Hogwarts ever since B-Max came and n-now I’ll have to leave and — w-what’ll Mother and Father say?”

Seeker was waiting for them, hovering in the Chamber entrance. Eli urged Lila forward; they stepped over the motionless coils of the dead basilisk, through the echoing gloom, and back into the tunnel. Eli heard the stone doors close behind them with a soft hiss.

After a few minutes’ progress up the dark tunnel, a distant sound of slowly shifting rock reached Eli’s ears.

“Leo!” Eli yelled, speeding up. “Lila’s okay! I’ve got her!”

He heard Leo give a strangled cheer, and they turned the next bend to see his eager face staring through the sizable gap he had managed to make in the rockfall.

“Lila!” Leo thrust an arm through the gap in the rock to pull her through first. “You’re alive! I don’t believe it! What happened? How — what — where did that bird come from?”

Seeker had swooped through the gap after Lila.

“He’s Starling’s,” said Eli, squeezing through himself.

“How come you’ve got a sword?” said Leo, gaping at the glittering weapon in Eli’s hand.

“I’ll explain when we get out of here,” said Eli with a sideways glance at Lila, who was crying harder than ever.

“But —”

“Later,” Eli said shortly. He didn’t think it was a good idea to tell Leo yet who’d been opening the Chamber, not in front of Lila, anyway. “Where’s Bright?”

“Back there,” said Leo, still looking puzzled but jerking his head up the tunnel toward the pipe. “He’s in a bad way. Come and see.”

Led by Seeker, whose wide scarlet wings emitted a soft golden glow in the darkness, they walked all the way back to the mouth of the pipe. Julian Bright was sitting there, humming placidly to himself.

“His memory’s gone,” said Leo. “The Memory Charm backfired. Hit him instead of us. Hasn’t got a clue who he is, or where he is, or who we are. I told him to come and wait here. He’s a danger to himself.”

Bright peered good-naturedly up at them all.

“Hello,” he said. “Odd sort of place, this, isn’t it? Do you live here?”

“No,” said Leo, raising his eyebrows at Eli.

Eli bent down and looked up the long, dark pipe.

“Have you thought how we’re going to get back up this?” he said to Leo.

Leo shook his head, but Seeker the phoenix had swooped past Eli and was now fluttering in front of him, his beady eyes bright in the dark. He was waving his long golden tail feathers. Eli looked uncertainly at him.

“He looks like he wants you to grab hold …” said Leo, looking perplexed. “But you’re much too heavy for a bird to pull up there —”

“Seeker,” said Eli, “isn’t an ordinary bird.” He turned quickly to the others. “We’ve got to hold on to each other. Lila, grab Leo’s hand. Professor Bright —”

“He means you,” said Leo sharply to Bright.

“You hold Lila’s other hand —”

Eli tucked the sword and the Sorting Hat into his belt, Leo took hold of the back of Eli’s robes, and Eli reached out and took hold of Seeker’s strangely hot tail feathers.

An extraordinary lightness seemed to spread through his whole body and the next second, in a rush of wings, they were flying upward through the pipe. Eli could hear Bright dangling below him, saying, “Amazing! Amazing! This is just like magic!” The chill air was whipping through Eli’s hair, and before he’d stopped enjoying the ride, it was over — all four of them were hitting the wet floor of Wailing Wren’s bathroom, and as Bright straightened his hat, the sink that hid the pipe was sliding back into place.

Wren goggled at them.

“You’re alive,” she said blankly to Eli.

“There’s no need to sound so disappointed,” he said grimly, wiping flecks of blood and slime off his glasses.

“Oh, well … I’d just been thinking … if you had died, you’d have been welcome to share my toilet,” said Wren, blushing silver.

“Urgh!” said Leo as they left the bathroom for the dark, deserted corridor outside. “Eli! I think Wren’s grown fond of you! You’ve got competition, Lila!”

But tears were still flooding silently down Lila’s face.

“Where now?” said Leo, with an anxious look at Lila. Eli pointed.

Seeker was leading the way, glowing gold along the corridor. They strode after him, and moments later, found themselves outside Professor Falconer’s office.

Eli knocked and pushed the door open.





Chapter 18



Nobby's Reward





For a moment there was silence as Eli, Leo, Lila, and Bright stood in the doorway, covered in muck and slime and (in Eli’s case) blood. Then there was a scream.

“Lila!”

It was Mrs. Brunswick, who had been sitting crying in front of the fire. She leapt to her feet, closely followed by Mr. Brunswick, and both of them flung themselves on their daughter.

Eli, however, was looking past them. Professor Starling was standing by the mantelpiece, beaming, next to Professor Falconer, who was taking great, steadying gasps, clutching her chest. Seeker went whooshing past Eli’s ear and settled on Starling’s shoulder, just as Eli found himself and Leo being swept into Mrs. Brunswick’s tight embrace.

“You saved her! You saved her! How did you do it?”

“I think we’d all like to know that,” said Professor Falconer weakly.

Mrs. Brunswick let go of Eli, who hesitated for a moment, then walked over to the desk and laid upon it the Sorting Hat, the ruby-encrusted sword, and what remained of Cipher’s diary.

Then he started telling them everything. For nearly a quarter of an hour he spoke into the rapt silence: He told them about hearing the disembodied voice, how Isla had finally realized that he was hearing a basilisk in the pipes; how he and Leo had followed the spiders into the forest, that Arachne had told them where the last victim of the basilisk had died; how he had guessed that Wailing Wren had been the victim, and that the entrance to the Chamber of Secrets might be in her bathroom. …

“Very well,” Professor Falconer prompted him as he paused, “so you found out where the entrance was — breaking a hundred school rules into pieces along the way, I might add — but how on earth did you all get out of there alive, Ember?”

So Eli, his voice now growing hoarse from all this talking, told them about Seeker’s timely arrival and about the Sorting Hat giving him the sword. But then he faltered. He had so far avoided mentioning Cipher’s diary — or Lila. She was standing with her head against Mrs. Brunswick’s shoulder, and tears were still coursing silently down her cheeks. What if they expelled her? Eli thought in panic. Cipher’s diary didn’t work anymore. … How could they prove it had been he who’d made her do it all?

Instinctively, Eli looked at Starling, who smiled faintly, the firelight glancing off his half-moon spectacles.

“What interests me most,” said Starling gently, “is how Mistborn managed to enchant Lila, when my sources tell me he is currently in hiding in the forests of Albania.”

Relief — warm, sweeping, glorious relief — swept over Eli.

“W-what’s that?” said Mr. Brunswick in a stunned voice. “That-Which-Is-Unnamed? En-enchant Lila? But Lila’s not … Lila hasn’t been … has she?”

“It was this diary,” said Eli quickly, picking it up and showing it to Starling. “Cipher wrote it when he was sixteen. …”

Starling took the diary from Eli and peered keenly down his long, crooked nose at its burnt and soggy pages.

“Brilliant,” he said softly. “Of course, he was probably the most brilliant student Hogwarts has ever seen.” He turned around to the Brunswicks, who were looking utterly bewildered.

“Very few people know that Mistborn was once called Tom Cipher. I taught him myself, fifty years ago, at Hogwarts. He disappeared after leaving the school … traveled far and wide … sank so deeply into the Dark Arts, consorted with the very worst of our kind, underwent so many dangerous, magical transformations, that when he resurfaced as Mistborn, he was barely recognizable. Hardly anyone connected Mistborn with the clever, handsome boy who was once Head Boy here.”

“But, Lila,” said Mrs. Brunswick. “What’s our Lila got to do with — with — him?”

“His d-diary!” Lila sobbed. “I’ve b-been writing in it, and he’s been w-writing back all year —”

“Lila!” said Mr. Brunswick, flabbergasted. “Haven’t I taught you anything? What have I always told you? Never trust anything that can think for itself if you can’t see where it keeps its brain. Why didn’t you show the diary to me, or your mother? A suspicious object like that, it was clearly full of Dark Magic —”

“I d-didn’t know,” sobbed Lila. “I found it inside one of the books Mother got me. I th-thought someone had just left it in there and forgotten about it —”

“Miss Brunswick should go up to the hospital wing right away,” Starling interrupted in a firm voice. “This has been a terrible ordeal for her. There will be no punishment. Older and wiser wizards than she have been hoodwinked by Mistborn.” He strode over to the door and opened it. “Bed rest and perhaps a large, steaming mug of hot chocolate. I always find that cheers me up,” he added, twinkling kindly down at her. “You will find that Madam Fern is still awake. She’s just giving out Mandrake juice — I daresay the basilisk’s victims will be waking up any moment.”

“So Isla’s okay!” said Leo brightly.

“There has been no lasting harm done, Lila,” said Starling.

Mrs. Brunswick led Lila out, and Mr. Brunswick followed, still looking deeply shaken.

“You know, Helena,” Professor Starling said thoughtfully to Professor Falconer, “I think all this merits a good feast. Might I ask you to go and alert the kitchens?”

“Right,” said Professor Falconer crisply, also moving to the door. “I’ll leave you to deal with Ember and Brunswick, shall I?”

“Certainly,” said Starling.

She left, and Eli and Leo gazed uncertainly at Starling. What exactly had Professor Falconer meant, deal with them? Surely — surely — they weren’t about to be punished?

“I seem to remember telling you both that I would have to expel you if you broke any more school rules,” said Starling.

Leo opened his mouth in horror.

“Which goes to show that the best of us must sometimes eat our words,” Starling went on, smiling. “You will both receive Special Awards for Services to the School and — let me see — yes, I think two hundred points apiece for Gryffindor.”

Leo went as brightly pink as Bright’s valentine flowers and closed his mouth again.

“But one of us seems to be keeping mightily quiet about his part in this dangerous adventure,” Starling added. “Why so modest, Julian?”

Eli gave a start. He had completely forgotten about Bright. He turned and saw that Bright was standing in a corner of the room, still wearing his vague smile. When Starling addressed him, Bright looked over his shoulder to see who he was talking to.

“Professor Starling,” Leo said quickly, “there was an accident down in the Chamber of Secrets. Professor Bright —”

“Am I a professor?” said Bright in mild surprise. “Goodness. I expect I was hopeless, was I?”

“He tried to do a Memory Charm and the wand backfired,” Leo explained quietly to Starling.

“Dear me,” said Starling, shaking his head, his long silver mustache quivering. “Impaled upon your own sword, Julian!”

“Sword?” said Bright dimly. “Haven’t got a sword. That boy has, though.” He pointed at Eli. “He’ll lend you one.”

“Would you mind taking Professor Bright up to the infirmary, too?” Starling said to Leo. “I’d like a few more words with Eli. …”

Bright ambled out. Leo cast a curious look back at Starling and Eli as he closed the door.

Starling crossed to one of the chairs by the fire.

“Sit down, Eli,” he said, and Eli sat, feeling unaccountably nervous.

“First of all, Eli, I want to thank you,” said Starling, eyes twinkling again. “You must have shown me real loyalty down in the Chamber. Nothing but that could have called Seeker to you.”

He stroked the phoenix, which had fluttered down onto his knee. Eli grinned awkwardly as Starling watched him.

“And so you met Tom Cipher,” said Starling thoughtfully. “I imagine he was most interested in you. …”

Suddenly, something that was nagging at Eli came tumbling out of his mouth.

“Professor Starling … Cipher said I’m like him. Strange likenesses, he said. …”

“Did he, now?” said Starling, looking thoughtfully at Eli from under his thick silver eyebrows. “And what do you think, Eli?”

“I don’t think I’m like him!” said Eli, more loudly than he’d intended. “I mean, I’m — I’m in Gryffindor, I’m …”

But he fell silent, a lurking doubt resurfacing in his mind.

“Professor,” he started again after a moment. “The Sorting Hat told me I’d — I’d have done well in Slytherin. Everyone thought I was Slytherin’s heir for a while … because I can speak Parseltongue. …”

“You can speak Parseltongue, Eli,” said Starling calmly, “because Mistborn — who is the last remaining descendant of Salvatore Serpentine — can speak Parseltongue. Unless I’m much mistaken, he transferred some of his own powers to you the night he gave you that scar. Not something he intended to do, I’m sure. …”

“Mistborn put a bit of himself in me?” Eli said, thunderstruck.

“It certainly seems so.”

“So I should be in Slytherin,” Eli said, looking desperately into Starling’s face. “The Sorting Hat could see Slytherin’s power in me, and it —”

“Put you in Gryffindor,” said Starling calmly. “Listen to me, Eli. You happen to have many qualities Salvatore Serpentine prized in his hand-picked students. His own very rare gift, Parseltongue — resourcefulness — determination — a certain disregard for rules,” he added, his mustache quivering again. “Yet the Sorting Hat placed you in Gryffindor. You know why that was. Think.”

“It only put me in Gryffindor,” said Eli in a defeated voice, “because I asked not to go in Slytherin. …”

“Exactly,” said Starling, beaming once more. “Which makes you very different from Tom Cipher. It is our choices, Eli, that show what we truly are, far more than our abilities.” Eli sat motionless in his chair, stunned. “If you want proof, Eli, that you belong in Gryffindor, I suggest you look more closely at this.”

Starling reached across to Professor Falconer’s desk, picked up the blood-stained silver sword, and handed it to Eli. Dully, Eli turned it over, the rubies blazing in the firelight. And then he saw the name engraved just below the hilt.

Godric Gryffindor.

“Only a true Gryffindor could have pulled that out of the hat, Eli,” said Starling simply.

For a minute, neither of them spoke. Then Starling pulled open one of the drawers in Professor Falconer’s desk and took out a quill and a bottle of ink.

“What you need, Eli, is some food and sleep. I suggest you go down to the feast, while I write to Azkaban — we need our gamekeeper back. And I must draft an advertisement for the Daily Prophet, too,” he added thoughtfully. “We’ll be needing a new Defense Against the Dark Arts teacher. … Dear me, we do seem to run through them, don’t we?”

Eli got up and crossed to the door. He had just reached for the handle, however, when the door burst open so violently that it bounced back off the wall.

Caspian Mortem stood there, fury in his face. And cowering behind his legs, heavily wrapped in bandages, was Nobby.

“Good evening, Caspian,” said Starling pleasantly.

Mr. Mortem almost knocked Eli over as he swept into the room. Nobby went scurrying in after him, crouching at the hem of his cloak, a look of abject terror on his face.

The elf was carrying a stained rag with which he was attempting to finish cleaning Mr. Mortem’s shoes. Apparently Mr. Mortem had set out in a great hurry, for not only were his shoes half-polished, but his usually sleek hair was disheveled. Ignoring the elf bobbing apologetically around his ankles, he fixed his cold eyes upon Starling.

“So!” he said “You’ve come back. The governors suspended you, but you still saw fit to return to Hogwarts.”

“Well, you see, Caspian,” said Starling, smiling serenely, “the other eleven governors contacted me today. It was something like being caught in a hailstorm of owls, to tell the truth. They’d heard that Arthur Brunswick’s daughter had been killed and wanted me back here at once. They seemed to think I was the best man for the job after all. Very strange tales they told me, too. … Several of them seemed to think that you had threatened to curse their families if they didn’t agree to suspend me in the first place.”

Mr. Mortem went even paler than usual, but his eyes were still slits of fury.

“So — have you stopped the attacks yet?” he sneered. “Have you caught the culprit?”

“We have,” said Starling, with a smile.

“Well?” said Mr. Mortem sharply. “Who is it?”

“The same person as last time, Caspian,” said Starling. “But this time, Mistborn was acting through somebody else. By means of this diary.”

He held up the small black book with the large hole through the center, watching Mr. Mortem closely. Eli, however, was watching Nobby.

The elf was doing something very odd. His great eyes fixed meaningfully on Eli, he kept pointing at the diary, then at Mr. Mortem, and then hitting himself hard on the head with his fist.

“I see …” said Mr. Mortem slowly to Starling.

“A clever plan,” said Starling in a level voice, still staring Mr. Mortem straight in the eye. “Because if Eli here” — Mr. Mortem shot Eli a swift, sharp look — “and his friend Leo hadn’t discovered this book, why — Lila Brunswick might have taken all the blame. No one would ever have been able to prove she hadn’t acted of her own free will. …”

Mr. Mortem said nothing. His face was suddenly masklike.

“And imagine,” Starling went on, “what might have happened then. … The Brunswicks are one of our most prominent pure-blood families. Imagine the effect on Arthur Brunswick and his Muggle Protection Act, if his own daughter was discovered attacking and killing Muggle-borns. … Very fortunate the diary was discovered, and Cipher’s memories wiped from it. Who knows what the consequences might have been otherwise. …”

Mr. Mortem forced himself to speak.

“Very fortunate,” he said stiffly.

And still, behind his back, Nobby was pointing, first to the diary, then to Caspian Mortem, then punching himself in the head.

And Eli suddenly understood. He nodded at Nobby, and Nobby backed into a corner, now twisting his ears in punishment.

“Don’t you want to know how Lila got hold of that diary, Mr. Mortem?” said Eli.

Caspian Mortem rounded on him.

“How should I know how the stupid little girl got hold of it?” he said.

“Because you gave it to her,” said Eli. “In Flourish and Blotts. You picked up her old Transfiguration book and slipped the diary inside it, didn’t you?”

He saw Mr. Mortem’s white hands clench and unclench.

“Prove it,” he hissed.

“Oh, no one will be able to do that,” said Starling, smiling at Eli. “Not now that Cipher has vanished from the book.
